Yves Lavandier

PREFACE
"Art is not pure expression, it is elaborated expression."
(Jean-François Zygel [97]')
"It is not the outpourings of the unconscious but rather the mastery of unconscious tendencies, the submission of the creative faculty to strong aesthetic discipline that alone allows works of art."
(Bruno Bettelheim [08])
Channeling the gushes of blood
Red Smith [74], an American sports journalist, once famously said: "Writing is easy, you just sit down at your typewriter and open a vein." The phrase is charming but incomplete. It's not enough to open a vein; you also have to channel the flow. Many artists have expressed this idea of mastering the flow in their own way. For Jorge Luis Borges, art was a mix of fire and algebra. Billy Wilder [93] said that a writer shouldn't just have hundreds of ideas; he should also be their accountant. Hitchcock [41] told Truffaut "that a mass of ideas, however good, is not enough to make a successful film. They must be carefully presented with a constant awareness of the overall configuration." In other words, your left hemisphere must continually sort and organize the outpourings of your right hemisphere.
Interest in coherence?
You might wonder if it's really necessary to put order into your gushes of blood. It's true that an artist creates with both their skills and their clumsiness. Flaws and mistakes are also part of the work. It's even said that if Ingres' famous Grande Odalisque didn't have three extra vertebrae, the painting wouldn't be a masterpiece. In the same spirit, it seems some artists add "false notes" to their works or remove one or two pieces of the puzzle to make it "artistic"! All this seems exaggerated and especially inauthentic to me.

1. The numbers in brackets [ ] refer to the bibliography at the end of the volume.

Moreover, the idea that genius—if one has it—will necessarily shine through, no matter what, is a cliché. In 1896, regarding a selection of the many scores sent to him, Johannes Brahms [12] noted: "Some convey real inspiration but lack structure. Others have structure, but they lack inspiration. As I've already said, no composition can be lasting if it isn't made of both inspiration and serious craftsmanship."
I believe that, in all arts, talent and genius need a lot of work and a minimum of rigor. Not rigidity, rigor. Hence the importance of knowing your intention (see chapter 2) and respecting a few rules.
Vade retro
The taboo word: "rules." To avoid offending artists allergic to rules, some use another term: guides, principles, etc. It's as effective as calling a cleaning lady a surface technician. I assert that dramaturgy is governed by rules, whether in life or fiction, and there's no reason to despair about it, quite the contrary. Rules are our friends. They help us convey our thoughts to others. I elaborate on these ideas in the introduction to La dramaturgie [51].
Note that the word "rules" refers to two different notions, logically linked. First, they are the constants whose omnipresence is observed in the repertoire. These fundamental rules then become writing guides. For example, if by observing great works, I see they contain conflict and understand its validity, when I become an author, I set myself the rule of including conflict in what I write.
Abracadabra
I began to take an interest in the rules of storytelling in 1980, as an apprentice filmmaker, not as a theorist. In the West, the first screenplay treatise is none other than Aristotle's Poetics [02]. After him, Horace, Nicolas Boileau, Denis Diderot, William Archer, Lajos Egri, Edward Mabley, and many others enriched the reflection over the centuries. And then suddenly, in 1979, for reasons that escape me, the reflection considerably narrowed with the publication of a book that quickly became very influential in the United States: Screenplay [28] by Syd Field. Suddenly, to write a screenplay, one had to fit into a definitive formula, otherwise, you were done for. In the decades that followed, many Anglo-Saxon theorists elaborated on the model proposed by Syd Field and each arrived at a complicated and rigid structural theory. Miraculously, the great masterpieces of cinema (preferably American) conform exactly to the model. Neither the viewer nor the screenwriter that I am ever found themselves in it.
When I see a clever diagram with arrows, ups and downs, and the "proof" by example that goes with it, I can't help but think of two images. First, the stepsisters of Cinderella, in the Grimm brothers' tale (Cinderella), who mutilate their feet to fit into the golden slipper. Initially, the prince is fooled but when he sees the blood, he repudiates the impostors. I feel that some story theorists are like Cinderella's stepsisters: they want at all costs for their foot (inadequate model) to fit the golden slipper (the entirety of great stories). In my opinion, they get big blisters but they still manage to damage the works and deceive many authors (see pages 104-105).
Partly, moreover, for the following reason. It's the second image that comes to mind, that of an illusionist performing a sleight of hand. Like all magicians, he needs smoke and mirrors, he needs a clever and complicated formula to convince. Because humans tend to find sophisticated models more intelligent and therefore more credible than simple models (see Alex Bavelas' experiment described in Evaluating a Screenplay [50]). I must admit that compared to some paradigms, my conception of the three acts is banal to tears (see pages 176-177). It is not impressive. But—and this is where I'm getting at—know that my goal in this book is not to impress you, it is to be in agreement with the facts—those that are said to be stubborn—and to help you write. In short, to find the right map for the territory. I'm not saying I achieve it 100% but, in any case, it's my only motivation. It's also an author's motivation. Over the years, I've sought the theory and method that seemed to me the most logical, the most faithful to reality, and the most practical for writing myself.
Now, if rigid and sophisticated models help you write, by all means, don't hesitate. We count on you to tell us beautiful stories. Because, in the end, that's all that matters.

Be autonomous
In life, whatever the field and whatever the rules, there are three possible attitudes towards them: submission, rebellion, or autonomy. Like La dramaturgie [51], this book is full of rules. They weren't decreed by Hollywood. For the most part, they are linked to the life of living organisms and have existed since the dawn of time. I invite you to follow them, or not, being neither in submission nor rebellion, but in autonomy.
The method proposed in this book logically follows from the storytelling rules detailed in La dramaturgie. Constructing a Story is to guides what La dramaturgie is to constants. It is therefore not a summary of La dramaturgie. It is the development of the former chapters 16, 17, and 22 of its predecessor. It aims to be concrete and methodological. Moreover, I have strived not to repeat myself and never to cite the same examples (or not in the same way). That's why I will regularly refer to the theory present in La dramaturgie.
My ambition is to help you organize the outpourings of your right hemisphere and to allow authors who want to tell a story to convey their thoughts and universe while captivating the receiver of their art. The method also stems from my personal experience. My suggestions are worth what they're worth but at least I'm the first to apply them. All the recommendations that nourish this book have proven themselves in the workshops I've led. I readily admit they don't suit everyone. But they work with some authors. So each one must find what can help them.
The "four-quarters" rule
If a book invites its reader to mix, in equal weight, eggs, flour, sugar, and butter and put it all in an oven at 180°, for 45 minutes, there's a good chance everyone will get a delicious pound cake. Not because the rule's statement is simple but because it is easy to apply. The rules that appear in this book may sometimes seem just as simple. You must keep in mind that they are much more difficult to implement. It's not writing that's difficult, it's writing well. It's not understanding the rules that requires talent, it's their application. Understanding is one thing, learning is another. The application of the rules requires not only talent but also a lot of time and practice. As we know, success is 5% inspiration and 95% perspiration. That's the limit of this book: it's not enough to have read and understood it to immediately become a good dramatic author or a good script doctor (see Evaluating a Screenplay [50]). It would be too easy.
Terminological questions
Behind the words "pitch," "sequence," or "exposition," each theorist offers their definitions and they don't always match. If you're writing a personal project, I invite you to focus on the concepts defined in this book rather than the words that designate them. But if you're doing commissioned work and your interlocutor uses a given vocabulary, ask for clarifications. What does he mean by "sequence" or "treatment"? What does he want to see in it? How many pages? A glossary at the end of the volume will help you recall all the notions covered in the book.
Hats off to the screenwriters!
In March 2015, I created a web series titled Hats off to the screenwriters! It is in English, subtitled in French, and available on YouTube. Each episode is between three and six minutes long and illustrates a remarkable storytelling element. It is therefore a visual complement to this book and La dramaturgie [51]. It is also and above all a tribute to all the great storytellers, to those artists who invent stories, characters, fictional universes, structures, and... meaning.
Yves Lavandier (May 2019)

1. THE BASIC MOTIF

"I will do everything to discover the author of this murder."

"You will learn nothing from me."

(Oedipus, Oedipus Rex)

(Tiresias, Oedipus Rex)

I am often asked what a story is (in the sense of a narrative). More precisely, what a good story is. My definition is simple; it encompasses all good stories, whether it's The Cherry Orchard or Terminator. A good story is a difficult human action. N.B. The word "human" is to be taken in the broad sense, in the anthropomorphic sense. One can tell stories of animals, puppets, machines, corks, as long as they make one think of humans. The four words matter in my definition: "action," "human," "difficult," but also "a." If you tell a difficult human action, there's a good chance you'll end up with a narrative. However, this definition does not reveal the basic motif of every story. A good story is a difficult human action, agreed, but what tool allows for producing a difficult human action? Before getting into the concrete methodology, it seems necessary to answer this important question and provide some insights into the basic motif of the narrative.
Objective versus obstacles
To uncover the basic motif of every story, one can either start from the definition of the word "drama" or focus on the basic element of dramaturgy: conflict. In both cases, the result is the same.
"Drama" means "action" in Greek. At the source of every action undertaken by a living organism, there is an impulse, a desire, an objective, conscious or unconscious. This objective can be, at choice, easy to achieve or difficult to achieve. In fiction, an objective easy to achieve interests no one and tells almost nothing. The objective must therefore be difficult to achieve. The only way to confer this status is to oppose it to various and diverse forces that can be called "obstacles."

If we now start from conflict, and more particularly dynamic conflict, we understand that it is created by the opposition between an objective and an obstacle. I assert that every action of a living organism can be reduced to an objective, conscious or unconscious, versus one or more obstacles.
Conflict and obstacle
Even though they are linked, conflict and obstacle are two distinct notions that should not be confused. Everyone sees what a conflict is, but it doesn't seem easy to define. Dictionaries merely explain that conflict results from opposition. We know what creates conflict, but we still don't know exactly what it is. I propose the following definition: conflict is a difficult circumstance of life, always associated with a feeling of frustration and often with a feeling of anxiety. Does this mean there are difficult life circumstances without frustration or anxiety? Yes. When a difficult situation is met by an individual with humor or as a means to grow and gain experience, then it is not experienced as a source of problems. It is rare for a human being to react with such detachment or serenity in the face of a challenge, but it happens. In all other cases, the difficulty turns into conflict and generates unpleasant feelings.
The obstacle, on the other hand, is an element that opposes a movement, therefore an action, a desire, a want, an objective (conscious or unconscious). This opposing element can be an object, an animal, a person, a natural element, another objective, a quality, a flaw, an emotion, etc. But it is clear that a precise objective is needed to confer the status of an obstacle on an element. A force 5 wind is an obstacle for someone who wants to play outdoor ping-pong, but the same force 5 wind is not at all an obstacle for an experienced windsurfer. Similarly, a wall can be, depending on the circumstances, an obstacle, a help, or neither. A ticket inspector, acting exactly the same way, will be an obstacle for someone who cheats, not for someone who pays for their transport.
Imagine now that you are playing outdoor ping-pong. Suddenly, the wind picks up and starts blowing at twenty knots. Don't you feel frustration? Imagine you are on a train without a ticket and you see an inspector approaching. Don't you feel a twinge of anxiety? And when the inspector issues you a fine, don't you feel frustration? If you answer no to these three questions, it's because the wind and the inspector are not obstacles for you, and therefore they don't cause you conflict.
A scene from White Heat presents a conflict that turns out to serve as an obstacle to action. In prison, Cody (James Cagney) is informed that his escape is organized for that very evening. It's his local objective. A few minutes later, he learns that his beloved mother has died. He experiences an intense static conflict. Mad with grief, he starts breaking everything, hits several guards, and ends up in solitary confinement for an indefinite time, an obstacle that ruins his escape plan.
Fundamental mechanisms as a consequence
Objective-obstacles logically imply character, characterization, conflict, protagonist, stakes, arena, three-act structure, inciting incident, climax, crescendo, dramatic question, suspense, unity, and preparation. Quite a list!
Character because an objective doesn't float in the ether; it necessarily has an owner.
Characterization because the character has characteristics, a life routine, needs, specificities.
Conflict because, as we've just seen, the objective-obstacles opposition generates conflict.
Protagonist because the character who experiences the most conflict is the one whose story is told. We call them the "protagonist."
Stakes because the protagonist didn't set this objective without reason. They have motivations and something to lose or gain in the operation.
Arena because often, either the protagonist comes from a meaningful setting or universe, or their objective leads them into a meaningful setting or universe.
Three-act structure because as soon as a character adopts an objective, they create three logical phases: before the objective, during the objective, and after the objective. I call these phases "launch-action-fallout."
Inciting incident because an objective doesn't come out of nowhere. An event is needed to provoke it.
Climax because an objective is not eternal. There comes a moment when either the protagonist achieves their objective or abandons it. This moment that concludes the second act is called the "climax" in English. In French, we could say the "great resolution scene."
Crescendo because in fiction, it is logical and effective for the strongest obstacles to be placed at the end.

Dramatic question because the protagonist and the viewer wonder if the protagonist will achieve their objective.
Suspense because if the obstacles are well-balanced, neither too weak nor too strong, the dramatic response is uncertain. The viewer is torn between hope and fear.
Unity because a given objective generates one and only one action.
Preparation because as soon as a character declares their objective, they make a double announcement, linked both to the character's characteristics and their objective. Everything that follows must depend on this gigantic preparation.
All these fundamental notions logically stem from the objective-obstacles pair. The only important mechanism in dramaturgy not induced by this basic motif is dramatic irony. Dramatic irony, which we will return to, is closely linked to the mode of perception of all fiction.
Unconscious objectives
I distinguish two types of unconscious objectives. The first type is reflexive. It is often an instinctive protection objective. When, inadvertently, you put your hand on hot metal, you immediately, without thinking, have the objective of preserving your hand. When an explosion sounds, you don't take two minutes to analyze the situation and declare an objective; you immediately duck and run for cover. When a nuisance invades the life of a misanthropic adventurer, he seeks peace without necessarily being aware of his objective. This is what Martin (Yves Montand) does in Le Sauvage. When you experience professional, sexual, and emotional misery, like Clara (Emmanuelle Devos) in Sur mes lèvres, you try to get out of it without necessarily articulating it clearly. This first type of unconscious objective falls into the category of dramatic objectives. We will return to this with the notions of sub-objectives and motivations (pages 162-165).
The second type of unconscious objective is a transformation objective. I call it the "trajectory objective" as it corresponds to the psychological trajectory of one of the characters. It is always a declination of a universal objective, common to all living organisms: to grow, to gain resilience, sometimes humanity. For humans, this can mean: being less venal, shedding omnipotence, mourning, becoming more generous, gaining lucidity, freeing oneself from inner demons, countering the barbarity of our cells, etc.
You will notice that the first objective is a reaction to external obstacles or internal-origin obstacles and is concrete. While the second is closely linked to an internal obstacle. We will see in chapters 4 and 5 that this distinction is important and will discuss the trajectory objective in detail in chapter 5.

Life is Aristotelian

In the documentary "A Childhood Under Occupation," a witness of the time, Roger Emmenecker, recounts that his father was a teacher in Alsace during World War II and that Roger was a student in his class. His father was also part of the resistance and used the school's radio to listen to the BBC. One day, after secretly listening to Radio London, the father forgot to reset the dial to an authorized frequency. When a German inspector turned on the radio the next day, the whole class heard English voices. A moment of hesitation. Roger's father then turned to his son, slapped him, and accused him of playing with the radio. Roger understood the maneuver, confirmed he was the culprit, and his father was not troubled.

Everything is in this true story. An internal obstacle, conflict, suspense, dramatic irony, a piece of activity, and a resourceful protagonist. And above all, yet another confirmation that dramaturgy is not only in theater or cinema, but also in life. All living organisms, plants, animals, or humans, are programmed to propagate the species. This is one of the great unconscious goals of life. As soon as a living organism undertakes something (consciously or unconsciously) and the result is uncertain, there is dramaturgy.

- Will the superorganism pass natural selection? Suspense...
- Will the seed carried by the wind germinate and give birth to a plant? Suspense...
- Will the baby turtle just hatched at the top of the beach reach the sea safely? Suspense...
- Will my childbirth go well? Suspense...
- Will I win my trial? Suspense...
- Will I manage to learn piano or blacksmithing? Suspense...
- Will I get a salary raise? Suspense...
- Will I manage to write a beautiful story? Suspense...

In humans, the principle also works on an unconscious level.
- Will I finally understand that I have trouble - check the box - letting go, admitting my mistakes, getting to work, settling down, accepting imperfection, accepting death, respecting others, respecting my body, being authentic, etc.? Suspense...
- Will I grow, transform for the better or, on the contrary, let the barbarity within me express and/or develop? Suspense...

The principle also works when we identify with a human being, real or fictional, or when we consider that the actions of some reflect on us.
- Will my child pass their exam? Suspense...
- Will my favorite candidate be elected? Suspense...
- Will the team I support win the World Cup? Suspense...
- Will my compatriot taken hostage on the other side of the planet be released? Suspense...
- Will French cinema and audiovisual finally understand the importance of the script (in the sense of narrative) and put screenwriters (in the sense of storytellers) at the center of the process? Mega suspense...

If the narrative were a plant...
... it would be a Romanesco broccoli. Indeed, this objective-obstacle principle is the basic form of a fractal structure. In other words, the narrative is the combination, at different scales, of thousands of objective-obstacle pairs.

But, you may wonder, if every narrative can be reduced to such a simple pattern, how is it that in the end, narratives are so different from one another? There are six major reasons for this.

1. First, a narrative can be composed of major objectives of different natures: dramatic, trajectory, ironic. A single general dramatic objective is advised, but there can be several trajectory and/or ironic objectives that overlay the dramatic objective.

2. Then, there is an infinite number of possible combinations available to authors. An infinite number of possible protagonists, characterizations, arenas, triggering incidents, obstacles, and climaxes. However, even if dramatic objectives can be diverse and varied, it cannot be said that there is an infinite number of motivations and objectives.

In life as in fiction, the same forces always move living organisms.
Imagine that on the scale of the entire work, you settle on a protagonist, a triggering incident, a general objective, obstacles, all very specific. From this seemingly unique set, you can still write thirty-six different narratives. This brings us to the four other factors of differentiation.

3. As mentioned earlier, the basic pattern is found at different scales: the entire work but also the series, the episode, the act (dramatic or logistical), the sequence, the scene, sometimes even a line of dialogue. The entire first act of "Les Compères," for example, has a different protagonist from the general protagonist. It is Christine (Anny Duperey) whose objective is to find her runaway son. Once she has passed her objective to Pignon (Pierre Richard) and Lucas (Gérard Depardieu), we enter the second act. The climax of "Ben-Hur" is the famous chariot race. It is a sequence (that is, a set of scenes) that lasts 25 minutes and contains three acts and a climax. The second act of this sequence begins when the chariots set off. The climax of the climax of "Ben-Hur" is the moment when Ben-Hur (Charlton Heston) and Messala (Stephen Boyd) are at the head of the race and finalize their confrontation by intensifying it. The third act of the climax includes the crowning of the winner and the death of the loser.

4. The thousands of basic patterns combined to obtain a narrative do not always concern the general protagonist of the work. In one of the scenes of "North by Northwest," a secondary character, Leonard (Martin Landau), tries to convince his boss (James Mason) that the woman his boss loves has betrayed them. The general protagonist of the film (Cary Grant) witnesses the scene as a hidden observer but is not the local protagonist.

5. One can play with the basic structure. Some elements can be hidden, revealed later, only part of the side stories shown, add one or more subplots, create surprises or mystery, place elements out of order. And also choose to develop the trajectory objective more than the dramatic objective. When I say that a narrative is a fractal mix, it is not a regular mix where each mini-narrative appears in order and in full. A narrative is an arbitrary and partial fractal mix. In the short film "Omnibus," for example, the protagonist's life routine - usually part of the first act - is found in the second act. It is used as an argument by the protagonist (Daniel Rialet). "Once Upon a Time in the West" is a beautiful example of an arbitrary and partial fractal mix. The triggering incident is the massacre of the McBain family by Frank (Henry Fonda), an immoral gang leader working for a corrupt industrialist named Morton (Gabriele Ferzetti). This triggering incident affects two important characters: Jill (Claudia Cardinale), McBain's wife, who arrives from New Orleans after the massacre, and Cheyenne (Jason Robards) whom Frank frames for his heinous crimes. Jill initially aims to return from where she came, but when she understands what her husband wanted to do - build a station at a strategic location for the railroad under construction - she decides to stay and counter Frank and Morton. As for Cheyenne, he has two objectives: not to pay for Frank's crimes and to take revenge. He also has a secondary objective to help Jill. A fourth important character in the film has the same secondary objective as Cheyenne. It is Harmonica (Charles Bronson). His main objective is to take revenge on Frank. In Harmonica's story, the triggering incident dates back many years when Frank killed Harmonica's brother in a sadistic way. This triggering incident is the subject of a famous flashback at the end of the film. In short, take the five main characters of "Once Upon a Time in the West," each with their triggering incident, their main and secondary objectives, make them cross and recross, and you get the cocktail we know. It is a fractal structure that respects two fundamental mechanisms: conflict and unity. Unity is embodied by Frank. Because the objectives of the other characters are all related to him.

6. Finally, the last criterion and not the least, this fractal combination that is the narrative is never as perfectly constructed as a Romanesco broccoli. One must account for failures, approximations, exceptions, rebellions (conscious or not), in short, what some call "poetic licenses."

History and narrative

The preceding points, particularly the fifth, lead me to discuss the difference between history and narrative. History is the chronological sequence of events relating to a given subject. In life, events are in order. Moreover, we give the important events of human life the name of History. And in history class, more or less, teachers recount events in chronological order. The remarkable documentary titled "Apocalypse: The Second World War" recounts all the events that constituted this war and does so in order. One can perfectly tell a fiction by respecting the chronology. But one can also choose a different order. This order is not random; it is arbitrary. It often depends on the author's intention and the effects they want to create in the viewer. "A dozen scenes can be assembled in a thousand and one different ways," writes Frank Capra, "but only one or two will give a narrative. (...) A teacher once brought us twenty shots representing a dog show in which his daughter had entered her dog. I edited the sequence in chronological order. (...) It was more boring than rain. I needed a narrative, an angle. I re-edited the shots from the winning dog's point of view. Better, but tricky. I then decided to edit the sequence from the girl's point of view. (...) The film immediately blossomed, provoking laughter and tears. I was discovering another principle of entertainment: what interests people most are people."

Capra was also discovering the difference between history and narrative. Because the narrative, precisely, is the arrangement of the events of a story in a chosen and specific order.

2. MEANING

"If for young children, the one who educates is the schoolmaster, for the older ones, it is the poet."
(Aeschylus in "The Frogs")
"There is no better tool in the world for solving social problems than telenovelas."
(Miguel Sabido)
"You don't write to say something, you write because you have something to say."
(Francis Scott Fitzgerald)
"... do not write an unnecessary word in the interest of form but bend the form in the interest of what is to be said."
(Arthur Miller)
"Every author must start by asking what they want to say, but they should never feel they have a mission."
(Krzysztof Piesiewicz)

A. THE FIRST POINT OF THE LINE

I see many authors who don't know how to approach writing, how to continue, and how to finish. They feel (rightly) that there are many elements to consider, so many that they will never get through them. So they decide to play the spontaneity card, jot down what comes to mind, and move forward blindly. In short, they make a gigantic bet on their instinct and genius. The image that comes to me every time is that of an individual who takes a dozen points in space and tries to draw a line through them. Unless by miraculous accident, it results in anything but a line.

A better chance of obtaining a line is to reduce the number of points, that is, to sort through all their ideas, to channel the bursts of blood. A better chance still is to proceed in order and logically. This is the method I propose in this book. If you take a first point and a direction, you have a chance of obtaining a line. The second point will logically depend on the first, the third on the first and second, etc., etc. Of course, this will not give a perfect line. And perhaps that's for the best. Small imperfections sometimes contribute to the beauty and uniqueness of a work. But it can help avoid major imperfections.

One might think that the first point of the line is the dramatic pitch, or one of the themes, or the subject, or the premises. I don't believe so. In my opinion, the first point of the line is your general intention, that is, the meaning you want your narrative to convey. This meaning is often unconscious, but it necessarily exists. All the reflections in this chapter have one goal: to convince you to understand your general intention to write better.

Be careful not to confuse meaning and internal trajectory. Evolving a character can enrich a narrative and/or give it depth. But that will give a meaning (a utility) to your narrative, not necessarily meaning (a purpose). Conversely, there are works in which no character evolves and yet they say something. "No Man's Land" is a beautiful illustration. No one grows, but the film conveys a powerful message.

The spark of departure

In April 1968, Martin Luther King Jr. is assassinated on the balcony of his motel by a shooter posted in a hotel room across the street. Upon hearing the news, Francis Veber visualizes the scene and wonders what would have happened if the shooter had been disturbed by the hotel maid. From this question were born the play "Le Contrat" (1969) and its film adaptation "L'Emmerdeur" (1973). In 1978, Stephen King's cat is run over and buried in a pet cemetery created by children. King then wonders what would happen if his cat came back to life. This led to "Pet Sematary." In 1980, Bob Gale flips through his father's high school album and wonders: "What if I had been in the same class as my father, would we have been friends?" This question eventually gave rise to "Back to the Future."

The reasons that lead an author to want to tell a story are multiple and varied. It can be a news item (as for Francis Veber), an experience lived by the author (as for Stephen King), a historical character, a message to convey to the world, a desire to challenge the zeitgeist, a paradoxical question (the famous "What if...?" of Bob Gale and strong premises in general), etc.

Each time, a small spark ignites the author's desire to embark on writing. I don't think there are more noble or desirable sparks than others. They are all respectable. And they all have a common faculty: they make the author vibrate. The process is partly unconscious. But each spark speaks enough to the author for them to want to spend a year or more on a specific universe and characters. However, a starting point does not seem sufficient to me to embark on writing. The starting point is not the first point of the line I mentioned earlier, even if it often contains it unconsciously.

Starting idea and point of view

Imagine you find a great idea for a short story: the story of a photographer, immobilized in his apartment by an accident, who starts observing his neighbors through the window and one day believes he discovers that one of his neighbors is a murderer. Tempting, isn't it?

In fact, what you have just found is not an idea for a short story but a starting idea for a short story. You think you have a subject when you only have premises. Suppose that, without further ado, you undertake the writing of this film. It is clear that with this same starting point, you can end up with thirty-six different works. "Rear Window" is one of them. But one can also imagine that the neighbor is not a murderer, and by snooping around, the photographer completely ruins his life. Not only is it not the same narrative at all (with, nevertheless, the same starting point), but the moral is not the same either. William Irish's short story shows us that curiosity and interference are legitimate even if they can prove dangerous. The second scenario shows us that curiosity is a bad trait.

Who says that by plunging headlong into writing your starting idea, you will obtain the work that suits you, the work that most deeply corresponds to your personality? In other words, if you do not ask yourself why you want to tell this story and not another, what deeply interests you in such or such a starting point, you risk being guided by your unconscious.

Unconscious: Advantages and Disadvantages

I am intimately convinced that every human being, whether author or spectator, unconsciously knows the great principles of narrative. More so, I think that an author who starts writing a subject has within them all the ingredients to make a good narrative. I have observed this many times in my workshops: everything is there, in the head of each author. The whole difficulty lies in correctly bringing out what exists unconsciously and translating under a form acceptable to others the ideal narrative you have in your head. Because the unconscious often plays tricks on us, and letting it run free can pose several problems.

First, the unconscious contains the inherent barbarity in each of us. This is how we see works that are clearly misogynistic, racist, sadistic, militaristic, homophobic, etc., whose authors vehemently defend themselves from being driven by such intentions. They are often sincere. They just forgot that the barbaric core in question can express itself unconsciously in multiple human activities (including artistic creation). From this point of view, one can say that a work of art, and a fortiori a discursive work like a novel, a play, or a screenplay, is always a magnificent Freudian slip. That is, the symptom of the author's beliefs, fantasies, and concerns.

Second, the unconscious tends to mix everything and does not always express its ideas appropriately. The idea emerges in the conscious in its raw state. When it deigns to emerge. Thus, it may suddenly occur to you that your protagonist could do this or that when in fact the idea would be much better suited to the main opponent. We will return to this phenomenon with what I call the "trick of permutation."

Third, in our unconscious, there is what is called an "internal saboteur" that has been passed down to us by our educators and sometimes prevents us from being in harmony with our deep self - which is also unconscious, which does not make things easier.

Fourth, the unconscious of a dramatic author is partially nourished by clichés and situations seen in others. On this subject, a funny story circulates in cinema circles. Hitchcock tells it to Truffaut, but it is likely that he did not invent it. It is the story of a screenwriter who wakes up one morning and realizes he dreamed of an absolutely brilliant film subject. Unfortunately, he forgot it. He thinks he will manage to dream it again and remember it. But the next day, the same story. He dreamed of this brilliant subject again but still cannot remember it. He then understands what he must do: force himself to wake up at the end of the dream, jot down the subject on a notepad at hand, and go back to sleep peacefully. That's what he does. The next day, he remembers he dreamed but still does not remember the subject. He begins to curse himself when he realizes that this time, he took notes on a notepad in the middle of the night. He goes to his bedside table, all excited, and reads: "Boy falls in love with a girl."

B. THE INTEREST OF DETERMINING MEANING

Meaning before the tool

All the mechanisms of dramaturgy, starting with conflict, generate emotion, thus interest for the spectator. But they also generate meaning. This is the case, for example, with characterization. Depending on whether your photographer's neighbor, in the courtyard opposite, is a murderer or not, the narrative does not have the same meaning.

All these tools are at your disposal to help you translate your thoughts and make them accessible. But before knowing which tool to use and how, it is necessary to know the meaning you want to create, whether locally or generally. It is therefore necessary to know your intention, your thought. How can you determine the characterization of the protagonist or the dramatic response if you do not know what you want to tell? In the example of the photographer and his murderous neighbor, you will be tempted to take the photographer as the protagonist. It is clear that this works in the first case ("Rear Window"). But not at all in the second. If the neighbor's life is ruined by the photographer, the protagonist - the one who will experience the most conflict - will rather be the neighbor. Isn't it preferable that the point of view, the meaning, the thought, the intention impose the choice of tools rather than the reverse?

Moreover, the author's point of view is a formidable sorting agent. It is easier to keep or discard an idea (of conflict, character, scene, activity, etc.) when you know what you want to say. And this proves absolutely essential when writing with others.

On the power of drama and images

Some may wonder if it is really necessary to have a clear point of view since, anyway, it will not change the spectators. Regarding "La Grande Illusion," which attempts to show that the gap is
greater between social classes than between nations, Jean Renoir expressed his bitterness: he made his film in 1937, and the war still happened. He concluded that cinema has no power. This is doubly naive (or incredibly pretentious). It is naive to believe that a film—or a play (cf. The Trojan War Will Not Take Place, which references the imminence of World War II)—can stop the gigantic forces leading to war, and when it doesn't work, to immediately conclude that drama has no influence. Drama surely has an influence, but it is almost impossible to measure. One thing is certain: experience, and particularly emotion, transforms a human being more surely than a speech. An "affective awareness" is much more effective than a rational awareness. Jean Piaget demonstrated that it is concrete experience that determines a change in our way of perceiving and reacting to reality and leads us to modify our thinking, not the other way around. The spectator of a dramatic work experiences an affective experience by identifying with the protagonist and living through some of their conflicts. It certainly doesn't have the strength of a real experience, but it is likely to leave traces. In his adaptation of Tartuffe, Murnau expresses the idea that theater or cinema can enrich people. We see a disinherited grandson (André Mattoni) project a film within the film (Tartuffe) to his grandfather (Hermann Picha) to open his eyes to the hypocrisy of his servant (Rosa Valetti). Murnau does not go so far as to show that the grandfather is transformed by watching a film, but it makes him doubt and he dismisses the schemer.

If Murnau had shot Tartuffe two years later, after Sunrise, he might have gone further. The filmmaker indeed experienced an edifying story upon the release of his first American film. Sunrise tells the story of a farmer (George O'Brien) who considers killing his wife (Janet Gaynor) to live with another woman. Ultimately, he understands his mistake and returns to the right path. Upon its release, Murnau received a package containing revolver bullets and a letter. A viewer, who was in the same situation as the film's protagonist, had realized his mistake and thanked Murnau for opening his eyes.

In the same vein, we can also cite what David Phillips called the "Werther effect." The publication of Goethe's The Sorrows of Young Werther had such a real census in Europe (at the end of the 18th century) that it provoked a wave of suicides (mirroring Werther's unfortunate end). In The Art of Change, Nardone and Watzlawick explain that a literary or dramatic narrative can strongly influence certain individuals and can be used for therapeutic purposes. Conversely, it is known that hearing about insecurity or terrorism every day on television can end up making people fearful and, for example, influence their vote. In Jai Santoshi Maa, the deity recreated for the film's needs pleased the audience so much that a cult is now dedicated to her in India! Bill Plympton claims that his mother quit smoking after watching his short film 25 Ways to Quit Smoking—which is so delirious that one might forget the seriousness of the subject. There is no doubt that documentaries like Fahrenheit 9/11, Super Size Me, or The Corporation have an influence. After watching The Corporation, some viewers decided to stop drinking milk (full of antibiotics) or to boycott certain brands. The manga Say Hello to Black Jack prompted Japanese authorities to revalue the salaries of medical interns. The broadcast of The Rage of the Young on Iranian television led to an increase in toothpaste sales. Finally, Jaws, which, during the summer of its release (1975), encouraged many swimmers to stay safely on the sand. In short, a narrative or images can have an influence. Just because it is difficult to measure doesn't mean it should be neglected. On another, even deeper power of the image, one can read with interest How Hitchcock Taught Me by Serge Tisseron.

For all that, does a human being go to the cinema or theater to change? Hard to say. Perhaps unconsciously. Growing is one of the major concerns of human beings. But it also depends on what they go to see. There is little chance that Funny Games (1997) or Hannibal (2001) will make us more human, but we can appreciate that Oedipus Rex or It's a Wonderful Life (1946) enrich us.

Social education on a large scale

A Mexican screenwriter, Miguel Sabido, deserves a moment's attention for his work. In the mid-60s, he launched a movement that still inspires many authors worldwide. Convinced that popular television series are the ideal vehicle for advancing social causes, Sabido developed a system based on the learning theories of psychologist Albert Bandura. These theories essentially postulate that a human being changes their behavior by observing and imitating others, thus engaging their mirror neurons. Sabido thought that a television series could change viewers' behaviors if they identify with characters facing social problems and evolving. He launched soap operas addressing delicate themes: female genital mutilation, homosexuality, domestic violence, illiteracy, HIV, malaria, etc. Quickly, positive effects were felt. Accompany Me, for example, which deals with contraception, led to a decrease in birth rates in Mexico. Immediately, many authors were inspired by Sabido's work. Hum Log, one of the first Indian soap operas, successfully campaigned against forced marriage. Taru, a radio soap opera, advocated for equal treatment between girls and boys and led to the creation of schools for disadvantaged children. A South African doctor, Garth Japhet, convinced that "telling stories to convey wisdom and teach people to live better is as old as the world," launched a series called Soul City to improve the quality of life of his fellow citizens. The results are there.

The "I" is likable

However, in my opinion, it is not primarily to change the viewer that it is important for an author to be aware of their work, to take a stand, and to have a point of view. It is to:

1. Create a personal, strictly personal work;
2. Have the first point of their line and choose the most appropriate form. Meaning before the tool rather than the tool before the meaning, as we have just seen;
3. Have a standard, a tool for sorting. Not just to sort the wheat from the chaff, the cortical from the reptilian. But also to sort the tools themselves;
4. Possibly, write with others. Collaborations rarely fail for artisanal reasons. When they fail, it's because the authors didn't realize they didn't want to say the same thing;
5. Be honest with the viewer: by saying "I" and not "we." This goes beyond point number 1 (satisfying personal interest). Here, it is about respecting the viewer's interest.

The viewer appreciates knowing or feeling the author's point of view, even if it differs from their own. Because a fully assumed point of view allows the viewer to form their own opinion. It leaves the viewer free. "The assumed point of view inevitably leads to dialectics," wrote Jérôme Soubeyrand in 2004. "We all remember, after leaving the cinema with friends, confronting our points of view and arguing until late at night. It's hard to experience something like that after watching an episode of a French TV series, an American blockbuster, or a CNC-labeled art film. Their representations of reality have taken care to lift the point of view as much as possible. This fiction, I call it 'veritarist' because it wants to make me believe that what I saw is not a 'point of view' but 'The Real.'"

Is it because systems like French television, Hollywood studios, or the National Center for Cinematography tend to play ideological Procrustes that authors don't ask themselves enough about meaning?

As soon as works of art start to resemble many of their neighbors, when decision-makers claim to know what viewers want, when selection committees favor a single thought, in short, when a smaller common denominator prevails, authors end up no longer saying "I" but "we." The narrative has a strong symbolic function, a function of representing reality, which makes storytellers responsible to society. One of their very first responsibilities is not to deprive viewers of their free will. To do this, one must have a personal point of view, one must say "I."

In 1964, to engaged journalists who thought that the world at the time required an ambiguous point of view and that all works should reflect this, Orson Welles replied: "I believe I have never made a film without having a solid ethical point of view on its story. (...) The characters are ambiguous, but the meaning of the work is not." Everything is said.

An underexploited potential

Knowing what you are telling can also prevent you from frustrating or disappointing the viewer. We have all had, in cinema, theater, comics, television, the impression that authors found a great subject or premise and forgot to exploit it, or didn't dare to go all the way, perhaps due to not having done a maturation work before writing. This is the case, in my opinion, of The Castafiore Emerald in which Hergé has the originality of taking Haddock and not Tintin as the protagonist but doesn't go all the way with his audacity (cf. Dramaturgy, chapter 5). It's also the case with The Big Blue and Multiplicity. But the examples are legion. Upon the release of The Big Blue, Luc Besson repeatedly stated in the press that he wanted to oppose, in his film, the spirit of competition to fraternity: "On land, there is suspicion, sideways glances, jealousy, deceit, hatred. Underwater, outside of meals, everyone mingles without aggression." In the film, it's the character of Jacques Mayol (Jean-Marc Barr) who embodies the values of the sea. However, at the end of the film, his competition with Enzo (Jean Reno) resumes. It's about who will dive the deepest in apnea. (N.B. Warning, what follows reveals the end of the film.) Jacques knows they are at the limit of what is humanly possible. He also knows that Enzo won't give up, precisely because Enzo represents the "values of the land," he has a competitor's mentality. The doctors have asked them to stop; it becomes too dangerous. What should Jacques do if he truly represents the values of the sea? And what should Luc Besson do if he wants to show that these values are harmonious? In my opinion, Jacques should have broken the record but kept it to himself. Besson and his co-writers should have shown us the record plaque before Jacques lets it sink and then surfaces pretending he didn't succeed, thus refusing competition and its ravages, and leaving victory (and life) to Enzo. But that's not what Jacques does. He surfaces with the plaque. Enzo is stung in his pride, decides to dive, and kills himself. If it was really necessary for Enzo to die at the end—to show, for example, that the feeling of omnipotence can be fatal—we can imagine that Jacques pretends he failed, that Enzo doesn't believe him and dives anyway. Or that Jacques, who at one point says to Enzo "I don't cheat with you," is torn between telling the truth and wanting to protect his friend, that this dilemma is dramatized. But we have none of that, and at the end of The Big Blue, it seems to me that Besson missed not an enticing or debatable point (depending on taste) but what he declared to be his point.

Multiplicity tells the misadventures of Doug (Michael Keaton), a construction contractor whose main characteristic is being overwhelmed. One day, by chance, he meets a scientist (Harris Yulin) capable of cloning a human being. In fact, the scientist has cloned himself and lives with his double. He offers Doug to duplicate him to solve his overloaded schedule problem. Doug agrees. The scientist then creates a first double, then a second, then a third. The premises are enticing. They constitute a golden metaphor to talk about two great classics of human mental illnesses: workaholism and ultrasolutions. The word "workaholism" is an English neologism constructed from "work" and "alcoholism." It refers to this well-known neurosis worldwide that consists of losing oneself in work. We could translate "workaholic" as "workaholic." Like Doug, workaholics are overwhelmed, accept more things to do than their schedule allows, and expend enormous energy to ensure. Like Doug, they secretly aspire to do less but rarely succeed. The problem with Multiplicity is that it doesn't really present Doug's issue as a compulsion for which he is responsible. While Doug is lying on a couch, ready to self-examine, the wise cloner even goes so far as to tell him that his problem is not psychological. At that moment, a great opportunity to address the subject in depth is missed. We could have imagined, for example, that with one clone at work and another at home, Doug would have a hard time enjoying his free time. Because, for a workaholic, spending an hour doing nothing is terribly anxiety-inducing. But that's not the case. He plays golf and sails between two superficial adventures.

Still in this vein, Doug would have eventually realized that his problem doesn't disappear with clones and that more and more solutions feed the problem instead of solving it. This is one of the forms of ultrasolution, a phenomenon theorized by Paul Watzlawick. When the wise cloner in Multiplicity presents his own clone and suggests to Doug that it has improved his life, the authors clearly announce that they won't have the ambition to address the problem of ultrasolutions. It would have been necessary, for that, for Doug to go find the scientist, for the latter to have never cloned human beings, and for Doug to insist on being cloned, against the scientist's advice, the latter anticipating the difficulties to come.

In short, instead of making the equivalent of Groundhog Day on workaholism and ultrasolutions, the authors of Multiplicity made an amusing and superficial film. They missed their subject. Americans do this regularly. They set up an inventive and meaningful technology and, instead of exploiting its social, moral, or political consequences, instead of questioning said technology or even their own system, they serve us a thriller that ends in cliché (Minority Report) or an adventure film with lots of shootouts (Inception). The premises are enticing, the universe is spectacular, but the treatment ends up being basic or even childish. I think of an exception, though: Final Cut.

Finding your general intention

Where does meaning lie?

Meaning is everywhere in a dramatic work. It is found particularly in:

- The characterization of the protagonist and their journey. Cf. above;
- The objective. Both in the content of the objective and in the perception the viewer has of it. When you determine an objective, local or general, always ask yourself if you want the viewer to agree or not with the holder of this objective. Do you want your viewer to approve the objective? Do you want the viewer to hope for a positive dramatic response? All answers are valid. Simply, it will not be experienced the same way by the viewer and therefore will not create the same narrative. We will return to this on page 150;
- The motivations. Stealing money to buy a third Ferrari does not have the same meaning as stealing money to pay for an eye operation for a poor blind person (cf. City Lights);
- The actions and reactions. Showing humans reacting in such or such a way is necessarily a discourse on human nature;
- The endings: climax, dramatic response, third act. If you tell the story of a teenager from a disadvantaged background who aims to get out of drugs, your point, that is, the meaning you give to your narrative, will not be the same if the teenager achieves their goal or not. Pages 281-282, I cite works whose endings are remarkable and... significant;
- The setups. Preparation is a powerful generator of meaning. A simple pebble (Departures), a mere broken mirror (The Apartment), a tiny bell sound (The Life of Galileo), can mean a lot if they have been carefully prepared;

and investigates to understand what is happening to him. In the middle of the film, a crime—that has been predicted and should occur because its perpetrator has not been preventively arrested—is not committed. The victim dies, certainly, but not at the hands of the announced murderer. This important twist should lead Anderton to question his profession and the system he serves. Could there be innocents among the people he has arrested and imprisoned? If predicted and unprevented crimes ultimately do not occur, there is a serious flaw in the prediction system. But neither the film nor Philip K. Dick's original story addresses this question. It is simply mentioned in the first act, before the narrative turns into yet another chase. As often in American cinema, the problem comes from the villain, hence a rotten apple, not the system itself which is untouchable.

- Comedy. All comedy is an invitation to let go as well as a discourse on human limitations and the relatively limited relevance of our free will. That's why when a critic says of The Dinner Game "We laugh but we only laugh," they are gravely missing the point. They haven't understood the film, as they say. Not only is laughter a remedy that deserves all honors, but also a good comedy is always a fair look at life and human nature. A joke, for example, always has significance. That's why it's futile to tell someone who got offended: "But I'm joking! It was for fun!" Unconsciously, people know there is meaning behind every joke. At most, one could reproach a good comedy for reinventing the wheel and always saying the same thing about humans. A dangerous trial because authors have been saying roughly the same thing about humans for thirty centuries, whether their treatment is serious or comedic. When James Cameron shows the cowardice of some and the courage of others in Titanic, one can acknowledge him for making a nice discourse on human nature. One can also say that he shows what we have already seen in Macbeth, Platoon, or The Wages of Fear.

The difficulty of knowing one's intention

Many authors do not seek to know their intention, perhaps because it requires courage and energy. Not only can it be frightening to delve deep within oneself, but also finding one's deep motivations is not always an easy task. For some, it is even extremely difficult. It sometimes takes several years and several works for an author to finally realize what motivates them and what they are telling. But it is also, for us authors, one of the great interests of narrative activity: to get to know ourselves better.

On the difficulty of identifying one's deep motivations, an experiment deserves to be reported. An individual is placed under hypnosis. They are given the instruction to open their umbrella that evening at 8 p.m. wherever they are, whatever the circumstances. Then they are brought out of their hypnotic state, taking care to make them forget the instruction. The day goes by normally, sunny, and at exactly 8 p.m., while in their living room, the individual gets up, grabs their umbrella, and opens it. Their wife, astonished, asks what is going on. Wisdom would suggest that our character questions their motivations and responds: "You're right, what am I doing? What I just did is absurd." But not at all. Our man confidently finds a good reason for his gesture. He will explain, for example, that it might rain tomorrow.
and he wanted to check if his umbrella was working properly. Our subject was what Freud called "rationalization." Many psychologists believe that we act like this every day in response to inappropriate unconscious commands that stem from our reptilian brain or are inherited from our upbringing. Every day, we open umbrellas in our living room, and every day, we invent an excuse to justify our action. This shows how difficult it is to truly understand what motivates us.

Pure entertainment?
Another possibility is that authors have no intention at all, their sole aim being to entertain. And yet... We've seen purely commercial films, produced to entertain the masses, develop a point of view. It's even one of the specialties of American cinema. In The Poseidon Adventure, a typical Hollywood disaster film, the protagonist (Gene Hackman) is a priest. His goal is to survive the sinking of a ship. His means: to go ahead and seek help. At the beginning of the film, he fights against passengers who prefer to wait quietly for assistance. Events will prove them wrong. In short, The Poseidon Adventure is not just an entertaining interlude; it illustrates a famous adage: "Help yourself, and Heaven will help you." It's no coincidence that the author of the novel chose to make his protagonist a priest—a characterization exploited towards the end. Of course, it's not a very deep message likely to shake humanity. But it proves that you can entertain people without being completely hollow. Regarding Uranus, whose action takes place in France after the Liberation and features communists, resistance fighters, liberals, Christians, and former collaborators, Claude Berri declared he had no point of view, not wanting to take sides with any of the characters because no one is black or white. But what is this position if not precisely a point of view?

I remember the release of Jurassic Park in 1993. It was all about special effects, digital images, world box office records, weak storytelling, and the commercialization of merchandise. It's true that, structurally, the script resembles a pretext for a big roller coaster ride. Yet, it was forgotten to mention that the film written by Michael Crichton and David Koepp develops a point of view: "You shouldn't play with nature; life always finds a way." This point of view is both stated in two scenes and shown by the entire film. Who can say that the decision-makers of tomorrow won't have been (partly) sensitized to the risks of genetics by Jurassic Park?

American special effects films often suffer this fate. The Mask similarly received many comments about its connections to Tex Avery or its digital tricks. Who pointed out that the story dealt with the mask everyone wears within themselves? It's superficial, certainly, but it's still mentioned. Back to the Future gently addresses the Oedipus complex since, after going back thirty years, Marty (Michael Fox) aims to push his future mother into the arms of his future father. He thus finds himself facing his teenage mother (Lea Thompson) who tries to seduce him. In Time After Time, H.G. Wells (Malcolm McDowell) and Jack the Ripper (David Warner) are propelled into American society in 1979. The latter adapts much faster and more easily than the former. Isn't that meaningful? Yes, of course. But some persist in seeing these films as mere distractions. Implying for Theodor Adorno and his heirs: unworthy products.

The saying or scene that summarizes the general intention
A trick can be useful to help you know your general intention: find a saying, a proverb, that summarizes or illustrates your story. It's not as difficult as it seems, since there is a proverb to justify everything and its opposite.

Another trick: look for the scene that summarizes or illustrates the work's message. It sometimes exists. In Time After Time, it's the scene where Jack turns on the television, shows Wells the horrors, wars, and violence of our time, and says: "I belong here." In No Man's Land, it's the last scene and even the last image, terrible. In Victor Victoria, it's a discussion scene between Victoria (Julie Andrews) and King Marchan (James Garner), in the latter's room. She asks him to let her continue to disguise herself because it's her only way to work and be someone. He wants her to stop because he doesn't want people to think he's in love with a transvestite. The discussion revolves around image issues, the gaze of others, the duality of femininity/virility within each of us. This scene summarizes the entire point of view of the film directed by Blake Edwards.

Ikiru tells the story of a dull bureaucrat, Watanabe (Takashi Shimura), who one day learns he has terminal cancer. He decides not to return to work, takes some "good" time before deciding to do something with the months he has left. (N.B. Beware, what follows reveals the end of the film.) At the end of the second act, a voice-over announces that Watanabe is dead. The third act is quite long. It shows Watanabe's wake during which his colleagues discuss his determination, leading to many small flashbacks. We thus learn how Watanabe spent his last months. He fought to have a playground installed in his town. The colleagues eventually understand that knowing his imminent death gave Watanabe the strength to mobilize against corruption and bureaucracy, which they don't have the courage to do. It's then that one of them says: "We are all mortal." Silence... This is the moment that summarizes the general intention of Ikiru.

Hamlet contains several key scenes of this type. I'll highlight two. The one where Hamlet jokes about Polonius's death (dining with worms) and explains to the king the cycle "beggar eats fish, fish eats worm, worm eats king" (IV/3). In other words, a king can end up in a beggar's intestine. And the often-illustrated one where Hamlet talks about Yorick, the king's jester, while looking at his skull (V/1): cheerful one day, cadaverous the next.

In the middle of Groundhog Day, Phil (Bill Murray) has almost succeeded in seducing Rita (Andie MacDowell). She and he have fun having a snowball fight with children. But Phil overdoes it. Rita realizes he's not sincere. She ends up slapping him. Then follows a dozen slaps in quick succession. The film's point of view being that to escape the repetitive psychological games that poison our lives, one must be authentic, we see that this scene expresses it negatively.

Beware, even though I just used it, the expression "key scene" can be misleading. Because there are all sorts of key scenes in a dramatic work, starting with climaxes and resolutions of dramatic irony. These are not necessarily the scenes where the author's point of view is developed.

The pot of intimacy and authenticity
Sometimes this scene of general intention is the hardest to write or even the one the author unconsciously avoids. Generally, it's because it's in this scene that the author is truly authentic or vulnerable (or both). And it's scary to expose oneself, to be truly oneself. But stopping to beat around the bush and facing this scene is extremely beneficial, for the work as well as for the author. Because missing the pot means missing the work.

One day, in a workshop, I forced an author to write the scene he had been avoiding for several sessions. I told him to put his two characters face to face and say what they had to say. No matter if it was clumsy. No matter if the scene wasn't to remain in the final story. But the abscess had to be lanced. I added that I wouldn't start any other work of this author until this scene was written. When he arrived at the workshop the next day, he had changed physically. It was spectacular. He had written the scene. He read it to us. It was moving. The author had finally unblocked his project.

It's because I've often seen screenwriters refuse to enter the pot and embark on writing without knowing what they really wanted to tell that I insist so much on the question of hidden and deep meaning. And this phenomenon is not only seen in writing workshops. Every week, plays or films lacking coherence are released. Conversely, I've observed that in-depth and conscious work on intention and motivations can improve writing—whether at the development stage or during a script doctoring session on a complete draft.

First draft of an intention note
The intention note is a document often required by reading committees. The natural tendency is to write it when the story is finished and ready to be sent to the commission. What if you wrote your intention note before even writing a line of text?

Indeed, an intention note often includes two parts: one dedicated to the why and one to the how. The second part is usually written by the director. They will talk about the form they envision to embody the story. But the first part, which is best written by the author, can very well be written before the story is written. Of course, it can be amended once the text is finished.

The trash test
In some cases, the author doesn't lack intention. On the contrary, they are motivated by several elements. They want to say this, but there's also that character that interests them. And then there's also this. And if they could include that... Again, the image that comes to mind is that of an individual trying to draw a single line through four or five points in space. A good way to know the one and only point from which everything else will be built is to pass the so-called "trash test." Simply answer honestly the question: "If, in everything that motivates you, you had to throw everything away, absolutely everything, and keep only one element, what would it be?" It's just a simulation. It's often painful because choices have to be made. But it's always instructive. And useful. Once you've decided on the element to keep at all costs, and the message that goes with it, you take it as a starting point and create your line from it.

Know how to recognize the best subject
There is another scenario, rarer but just as damaging, which consists of missing not the subject but a more exciting subject than the one being treated. When the viewer realizes this, their frustration at seeing a good subject slip away will be all the more intense because the work that alludes to it is not captivating.

If you feel you have an even stronger subject than the one you're writing, one of two things: either you refrain from mentioning it in your story to avoid whetting the viewer's appetite, or you completely abandon what you're writing to embark on developing the stronger subject.

N.B. On this scenario, I will not provide examples. If you allow me, I prefer to keep to myself the lovely untreated subjects I've spotted here and there.

Be aware of potential pitfalls
The problem of didacticism
Some claim that starting a story with a preconceived idea can be dangerous and lead straight to didacticism. Certainly, you must avoid reducing your story to explaining an idea, to demonstrating a thesis. But if you don't forget to create emotion and interest in the viewer (protagonist - objective - obstacles, for example), you have little chance of falling into didacticism.

Sartre achieves this wonderfully with No Exit—whether one agrees with his philosophy or not. Chaplin has a very clear point of view in The Great Dictator and only falls into didacticism in the speech scene at the end. In Right You Are (If You Think So), Pirandello has one of his characters say what his play shows very well. It's didactic and unnecessary. In these last two cases, if the authors had removed the didactic scenes, their message would have remained intact. Waiting for Godot, Rhinoceros, The Trojan War Will Not Take Place, Modern Times, It's a Wonderful Life (1946), or The Journey of Mr. Perrichon, to take a few examples among many others, clearly convey a point of view and never fall into didacticism. Danis Tanović thinks that prevention is the solution against war. It's with this point of view that he says he undertook No Man's Land. It resulted in a powerful, entertaining, and not at all didactic outcome.

Engaged but not didactic
It's hard to imagine Aristophanes, Capra, Chaplin, Costa-Gavras, Eisenstein, Ibsen, Molière, or Sartre starting a work without knowing what they want to put into it. In the case of Brecht and Shaw, it's even more evident. They wrote clearly to say something. Or, at least, as Francis Scott Fitzgerald said, because they had something to say.

Through epic theater, Brecht sought to educate the viewer. And he didn't hide it. The prologue of The Caucasian Chalk Circle, often omitted from performances, presents two groups of peasants disputing the exploitation of a land. To whom should it belong? To those who have a title deed? Or to those who will make the best use of it? To answer this question, a singer appears and proposes to tell a Chinese legend, which constitutes the rest of the play. How to say more explicitly that theater can convey an enlightening point of view and even that it is made for that?

As for Shaw, he declared loudly that he didn't believe in construction, despite his admiration for Ibsen, and that only ideas deserved attention. One of his main arguments was that people would listen to a one-and-a-half-hour lecture, so why not a play full of ideas? Let's be honest, the argument isn't very good. First, the audience for lectures isn't the same as for theaters, and even if it were, one doesn't expect from a play what one expects from a lecture.

Moreover, thinking that a lecture doesn't need to be structured (and even sexy) can lead to serious disillusionment. But, fortunately for us, there is, as with Brecht, a Shaw theorist and a Shaw playwright. The theorist opposed ideas and construction as if they were incompatible. The playwright wanted to stand out from Sardou and well-made plays. This didn't prevent him, like Molière or Ibsen, from brilliantly using the mechanisms of drama to say what he wanted to say and thus... avoid didacticism.

I find it more surprising to note that artists like Milos Forman, Woody Allen, Steven Spielberg, or Jirô Taniguchi—who are not openly militant—have also been driven by an intention before undertaking some of their works. When The Firemen's Ball was released in Czechoslovakia in 1967, the firemen threatened to resign because, they said, they were mocked in it. During a special meeting, Milos Forman explained to them that it was a misunderstanding: in his mind, The Firemen's Ball was not a film about firemen but about the government. Forman wanted to criticize his administration and chose a metaphor to do so.

In a 2002 documentary, Woody Allen explains what motivated him to write his films. We learn with interest that he rarely embarks on a story blindly, relying on his unconscious to give it meaning. At the start of most of his films, Woody Allen has a clear point of view, almost a thesis to illustrate. With Zelig, for example, he wanted to mock those people who don't hesitate to renounce their opinion to avoid making waves and to belong to a group. Watching Zelig, one might think Woody Allen found a strong premise (the story of a chameleon man) and just found it amusing to treat it. Well, no, he had something to say, and he chose the metaphor of Zelig for that.

I thought Close Encounters of the Third Kind was the film of a gifted child playing with his obsessions and favorite themes. Until I heard Steven Spielberg declare in 2007: "In 1975-76, when I was writing Close Encounters of the Third Kind, I wasn't as politically aware as I am today. And yet the Cold War, the lack of dialogue between the Russians and the Americans, Watergate, Nixon's impeachment, in short, everything that was happening at that time gave me the impression of a huge communication abyss. It's gotten worse since. You could say that Close Encounters of the Third Kind was my first attempt to deliver a message: if we can communicate with aliens, why can't we communicate with each other?"

Jirô Taniguchi thinks that some experiences can change our way of living. In the afterword to A Distant Neighborhood, he explains that he wanted to show that every human being, during their life, can discover the few essential things that really matter to them. He then imagined a story in which a man haunts the body of a teenager, the time to erase the slate he has with his wife and daughter. It results in a manga so poignant that it's impossible, between sobs, to think that the author is telling us something. And yet...

These four works, The Firemen's Ball, Zelig, Close Encounters of the Third Kind, and A Distant Neighborhood, are metaphorical narratives. The Anglo-Saxons have long mastered the art of conceiving modern allegories. It's one of the reasons why they are the kings of fantasy and science fiction. It's also one of the reasons why they can entertain while saying something. Like a good ointment: a quality active ingredient in an effective excipient.

An example of didactic construction
One can be didactic through dialogue (cf. The Great Dictator or Right You Are (If You Think So), above). One can also be didactic through the very structure of the narrative.

In Mély-mélodrame, a couple abandons their dog on the vacation road. The dog believes it fell out of the car and seeks to rejoin its owners—thought bubbles allow us to understand what the dog thinks. After various adventures, it ends up finding them at the bottom of a ravine, victims of a car accident. It goes to find the police, thus helping to save its owners' lives. They then regret abandoning it.

This short story has everything to work: a triggering incident, a protagonist (the dog) and its goal, obstacles, a climax, etc. And yet, it is clearly didactic. Moreover, the author acknowledges it: "It's melodramatic, it's true!... But we dedicate this story to all those who take an animal as a toy," writes Mittéï in the afterword to his story. But it is not didactic because the author has a point of view. It is because the events (in particular, the discovery of the accident) are not there due to the logic of the action but due to the author's will. They are placed to demonstrate and lack preparation. Didacticism, whether in Mély-mélodrame or in general, does not come from the author having something to say but from saying it clumsily.

This is how one can recognize the off-putting aspect of didacticism while defending the necessity of having a point of view. This is what La Fontaine does in The Shepherd and the Lion: "A bare moral brings boredom; the tale conveys the precept with it. In these kinds of fables, one must instruct and please, and telling for the sake of telling seems to me of little matter." The two notions, instructing and pleasing, are therefore not incompatible. Many board games are both entertaining and educational, and no one complains. On the contrary, it is likely that this dual aspect responds to a need in both the player and the viewer.

Point of view, message, and propaganda
The word "point of view" sometimes scares a little. The proponents of "art for art's sake" ("Three words absolutely devoid of meaning" according to Dumas fils) don't want to hear about it, and some American producers or filmmakers have launched this famous quip: "When I have a message to deliver, I go to the post office." Despite what they say, an author always has at least one point of view: their perception, if not their conception, of the world and aesthetics. This point of view is often unconscious, it sometimes exists despite them, but it exists. All this to say that the word "point of view" does not necessarily designate an ideological message.

You will have understood that in this chapter, I am not inviting you to have a point of view since you necessarily have one, even if only unconsciously. I invite you to know your point of view to better convey it.

The point of view of It's a Wonderful Life (1946), for example, is: "A man who has friends cannot be a failure." It is said once in the film but, above all, it is shown by the entire film. Tennessee Williams was convinced that all his works carried a message. And, according to him, this message was the following: "The tearing need, the screaming need for a gigantic universal effort to better know ourselves and to better know others, or at least to understand that no man has the monopoly on good and virtue any more than any man has the privilege of duplicity, evil, etc."
