In many fairy tales or in certain fables like The Lion and the Mouse, the protagonist helps a character for free who returns the favor some time later. This simple motif already contains a point of view: "Help others, they will help you." Moreover, fairy tales are
far from being mere products of entertainment. They convey numerous messages. Here are a few:

- Your negative emotions (fear, jealousy, anger, frustration, sadness, guilt...) are legitimate;
- Nothing shines without friction, but efforts always pay off in the end;
- It's better to choose the long term over the short term;
- It's better to prioritize the reality principle over the pleasure principle;
- We need help; we can't do everything alone;
- Patience is necessary; we can't have everything immediately;
- We can love, we can change, and we can succeed.

E. BEYOND THE GENERAL INTENTION
Bending the rules for a reason

The preliminary work on personal intention discussed in this chapter can be useful if you feel the urge to play with the rules—while remaining Aristotelian, that is, always with the intention of telling a story. For authors who want to deconstruct, here are a few suggestions.

1. First, know the rules well, not just in theory. In other words, master them, as Picasso or Schoenberg mastered those of their respective arts before creating cubism or dodecaphonism.
2. Question your motivations. To the question: "Why do I want to break the rules, deconstruct, put the end at the beginning, remove a piece of the puzzle?" many authors will immediately respond: "Because it will be great" or "Because that's how my story needs to be told." Chances are this is rationalization (like "I wanted to check my umbrella," see above). By digging a little deeper, you can arrive at more sincere answers: "Because I want to emulate a great artist" or "Because I want to stand out" or "To avoid resembling the commercial cinema I despise" or "Because I lack confidence, fear being mediocre if I write a simple story with a beginning, middle, and end, and fear everyone will notice." To those who have the honesty to give these answers, I strongly advise starting by respecting the rules and adopting a simple form. Not only might they be surprised by the result—fear is not always justified—but they will also learn more about life and themselves than by standing on their heads.
3. Ensure that the subject justifies and even demands deconstruction. This is the case in Memento. When you want to tell the story of a character suffering from short-term memory loss, it makes sense to tell the story backward. This is the case in Maus. When you want to work on memory and the importance of bearing witness to a Holocaust survivor, it makes sense to use incessant flashbacks. This is the case in À la folie... pas du tout. When you want to portray an erotomaniac and show that a romantic relationship can be experienced completely differently by each partner, it makes sense to tell the story twice. This is not the case, in my opinion, in Pulp Fiction, Irreversible, or The Three Burials (see pages 241-242). The risk, when deconstructing without being driven by the subject, is to no longer speak about life but about narrative techniques. And while this discourse may sometimes be spectacular and impressive, it is rarely profound.

The question of originality

Another crucial question often linked to meaning: what is the originality of your subject or the way you exploit it? What are you saying, what are you showing that hasn't already been said or shown? Jean-Marie Roth [70] has a lovely phrase: "Everything has already been written but not yet by you." He is right to remind us that all subjects have already been addressed and that everything depends on how they are exploited. He is right to put authors at ease. And at the same time, the mere desire to express oneself does not justify asking financial partners for five million euros and asking thousands of spectators to devote two hours of their lives to watching your work.

If everything has already been told, what angle should you choose to tell your subject differently? Will your work merely fill pipes or enrich humanity's heritage? Be careful, choosing a new angle does not mean everything must be original. If the protagonist, their goal, the inciting incident, and the obstacles are all unique, there is a strong chance you will offer the viewer an alienating experience and lose them. Generally, be original on one or two elements and classic on the rest. In short, follow the often-heard advice in Hollywood: "Do the same thing but different."

If there are authors who have understood the need to stand out from the existing repertoire, they are the creators of police series. Since Nostradamus Agency and Badge 714 in the early 1950s, there have been so many investigative series on television, and their structure is so constant and simplistic that one is forced to find an original angle when creating the thousand-and-first police series. So, we imagine the investigator is talented but full of quirks (Monk) or set the investigation in the Middle Ages (Cadfael) or decide to show crimes from multiple perspectives, a bit like Rashomon (Boomtown). Or, we imagine the criminal is a homeostatic disruptor and the investigator a doctor (Dr. House).

For Fabien Vehlmann [87], a good way to be original is to constantly expand one's general knowledge. "I often use the image of the brain as a vast peanut warehouse where everything is weightless. Each new piece of information received by watching a film, reading a book... are peanuts entering your brain. These peanuts float weightlessly. If you only have three, they never collide. Yet, an idea is two peanuts colliding. So, the more general culture peanuts you accumulate in all possible fields, the more they bump into each other. And the more they come from varied domains, the richer the creations because they don't come from the same semantic field. (...) The problem with many aspiring screenwriters is that they either lack general culture—they loved Tolkien and want to do Tolkien... which can't work—or they have the same culture as ten million people in the world: they've seen Star Wars, Harry Potter, Matrix... It's not a personal culture."

A game of Russian dolls

The act of creation also greatly contributes to discovering meaning. We write better because we know what we want to say, and we understand better what we want to say by writing. Uncovering one's intention is like handling Russian dolls. Each time you open one, there's another, smaller one. For each answer to "Why do I want to tell this?" there's another question: "Yes, but why this answer?"

It's almost impossible to open all the dolls. Even with the finished work, you'll still discover things about your deep motivations, the unconscious reason for this or that element. You might even be surprised by what your story says. Should you undergo psychotherapy before starting to write? No, of course not. But I suggest opening enough Russian dolls, delving deep enough into the maturity of a subject, before starting to compose.

Constantly question your subject and ideas

As we've just seen, the question of meaning arises upstream. An author will undertake a story because they want to mock their government (Aufou, the Firemen) or invite humans to talk to each other (Close Encounters of the Third Kind).

But the question of meaning also arises downstream. It even imposes itself. At each stage, each idea, however small or local it may be, you should ask yourself what it says, if it's consistent with the rest. Because, if an idea doesn't come to you by chance, it still has its own coherence, which may not align with the coherence of your project.

In truth, the question of meaning arises continuously, before the tool as well as after the tool, and at all scales. I can understand that you may not want to question your intention and initial motivations because you lack the energy or fear it will stifle your creativity. Let's admit it. But you must work with a minimum of awareness. Look at Shakespeare's plays. There's a philosophical subject every ten lines. Watch or rewatch American films from the 30s, 40s, and 50s, written at a time when censorship and taboos were powerful, and you'll see how much the authors knew what they were setting up. The allusions and innuendos are countless, even in B-movies.

I strongly encourage you to know what you want to tell and to understand, at every moment and until the end, what you are telling.

The legs?

Another interest in knowing why you want to tell a story and what meaning lies behind this desire is the opportunity to test your motivation. When you find a story idea, it's common to be enthusiastic. That's normal and for the best. But very quickly, two essential questions deserve to be asked: 1 - does my subject have legs, and 2 - do I have legs?

1. Some subjects, especially strong premises, seem exciting at first glance but don't have the potential to fill a second act. It's often the second half of the second act that is likely to be lacking. Before embarking on several months of writing, try to imagine what your subject can yield. You must feel an entire and solid tree behind what is currently just a seed.
2. It takes time and fierce energy to complete a story. And even more to complete a quality story. The causes of discouragement are countless. I believe one of the most powerful motivations to go the distance and to rework twenty times is the desire for the vision and message conveyed by the story to be communicated to the world. Hence the interest in knowing this vision and message.

3. MAJOR CHOICES
Before writing, learn to think.
(Nicolas Boileau [10])
"I don't believe at all in scripts that start without knowing where they're going. (...) It requires a steel framework."
(Suso Cecchi d'Amico [15])
"I then made a mistake often made by young authors: starting to write without having finished building."
(Francis Veber on The Kidnapping [86])

In order

Once you know what you want to tell and why (see chapter 2), it's in your best interest to proceed in order. The metaphor has been used a lot, but it's still useful: you don't start a house with the wallpaper. A play or a feature film script is too rich and complex a creation to be fully grasped by a human brain and justify immediately diving into writing dialogues. It's preferable to start with what we'll call the "foundations," then a list of milestones, then a scene-by-scene (or sequence outline), then a treatment (or non-dialogue continuity), finally arriving at the dialogue continuity. All these tools have three functions. They are first guides for building the story. They also help explore and mature the project. Finally, they are very useful for rewriting, each with its specific function.

If you are a complete beginner, I advise starting with writing short stories or even scenes (see chapter 14) rather than immediately diving into writing a long work. The ideal is to do the same work as on a long work but on a smaller scale. This is entirely possible since the rules contained in this book apply to all stories, regardless of their length. It's one of the applications of the fractal principle.

What exactly are the foundations?

Telling a story, whatever it is, and whatever its quality, is making millions of choices, taking millions of decisions. Some of these decisions are tiny, others a bit more important, up to some that are major. Fundamental. Foundational.

The foundations are simply the sum of the major choices of your story among an infinity of possibilities.

All the stories in the world, good or bad, have foundations. Give me a finished story, any story, and I'll make a list of its major choices. This list may not be entirely coherent, it may have some gaps, some uncertainties, but it will be possible to produce because a self-respecting story necessarily contains major orientations. All the narrators in the world do this work, consciously or unconsciously. The big question is not whether you can do without it, it's impossible when telling a story. The question is whether it's better to make these major choices at the beginning of writing or as you go along, at the whim of circumstances and inspiration, at the risk of being incoherent, getting discouraged, or writing tons of scenes for nothing. You guessed my answer.

Beware, I'm not saying this preliminary work should be set in stone and become untouchable. Anyway, it's not possible. Throughout writing, an author enriches and refines the foundations of their story. But I think it's wise to have a base, a solid direction, to start.

The most important element of the foundations is the dramatic pitch. I dedicate chapter 4 to it. Sometimes, a character in the story transforms. This evolution cannot be done lightly. It's the subject of chapter 5. Finally, we will address all the rest of the foundational work in chapter 6.

A springboard for the imagination

In reading these three chapters and even the one dedicated to structure (chapter 8), some may be tempted to think that all this work is a cerebral activity. Nothing could be further from the truth. Intellectual, yes, necessarily, but not cerebral. Nor theoretical. Answering all the questions posed by the foundations requires creativity and spontaneity, generates emotions in the author, activates their right hemisphere as much as their left hemisphere, and can provide genuine enjoyment, like when you find an exciting idea, a brilliant dialogue, or a powerful payoff. In other words, when I use the term "think" in these chapters, I'm talking about creative reflection, not reasoning or cold, clinical calculation. Enjoy yourself and put your inventiveness to work in creating your foundations and structure.

I add, to reassure artists frightened by these unbearable "formats," that this work on the foundations will never prevent chance, accidents, and poetic licenses. The reason is simple: whatever method is used, an author's unconscious never loses its vigor.

Finally, I point out that the foundations are also a springboard for the reader's imagination. When I read complete and coherent foundations, I see as much of a story as when I read a synopsis.

Research in all directions

As I explained in chapter 2, the first point of the line is meaning. When you know what you want to say, a good way to nourish your project (and therefore the foundations) is to document yourself. This can take various forms. If you have a specific arena (historical or technical, for example), it's imperative to know it well. Hence investigations, readings, visits, interviews...

Regarding the creation of the Buck Danny series, Jean-Michel Charlier [18] stated: "I quickly realized that you couldn't validly tell an aviation story if you didn't pilot yourself. Hence the desire for Hubinon (the illustrator) and me to get our pilot licenses." Excessive professional conscience? No, simple evidence. An author can't know everything, but they must know what they're talking about. Those who, like me, are fascinated by the documentary dimension and the spectacular veracity of The Wire won't be surprised to learn that its creator, David Simon, accompanied police officers for months in the field when he worked for the Baltimore Sun.

We see authors whose only references are the works of others. Thus, in some French police stories, the police and justice borrow the jargon and procedures of their American counterparts. Let it be known, in France, witnesses do not swear on the Bible, and high schools do not have cheerleader clubs.

The source of any story should be life. To avoid inbreeding and gain credibility, you must document yourself. Not only is it one of the riches of our profession to be able to discover foreign universes, but it also gives ideas that nourish development.

Another type of research is to become aware of what has already been done on subjects or genres close to your project. This allows you to see what works or not. That's why you're not obliged to limit yourself to watching or rewatching the great masterpieces whose subject has some resemblance to yours. Flops are also instructive.

All this research has a huge advantage: it gives ideas. I'm not talking about copying what's already been done; I'm talking about new ideas found in reaction to others' works. Moreover, seeing what's already been done forces you to be original, to stand out, to offer something personal, to say "I" and not "We."

The genre

Becoming aware of existing stories on a subject similar to yours also allows you to familiarize yourself with the conventions of your project's genre. Note that genre is not the only categorical criterion for classifying works. If, for example, your story deals with the mother-daughter relationship, this theme is present in many different genres (from Carrie to Steel Magnolias, through Autumn Sonata). If you're writing a story with elderly people, I suggest watching or rewatching Cocoon, Harold and Maude, About Schmidt, The Straight Story, Tough Guys, etc., even if "old people's films" are not yet considered a genre.

This may come one day because the question of genre obsesses American screenplay theorists and Hollywood decision-makers. I invite you to consult them because I don't have much to say about genre, except for the few paragraphs that follow. I'm probably wrong. I should be interested in genre as an author. However, as a spectator, I think I'm definitively vaccinated on the subject since 1986. At the time, I loved pirate stories (among other genres). Largely thanks to the comic book series Redbeard. When the film Pirates came out, I rushed to a cinema and was terribly disappointed. I realized that what I loved most in Redbeard was not so much the arena and its codes as the narrative genius of Jean-Michel Charlier. Boarding, wooden legs, and treasure hunts are of little interest if they don't fit into a solid story. Already, in my teenage cinephilia years, I had noticed that some films directed by Alfred Hitchcock or some films with Laurel and Hardy were less successful than others. Since then, I wonder how someone can claim to love revenge films when it can yield both The Sting and Eye for an Eye. If asked whether I enjoy westerns, I don't know what to answer. I can't say I love westerns in absolute terms. I adore some westerns (Blueberry (comic series, albums 1 to 22), Buddy
Longway (albums 1 to 16), The Human Target, The Desert Without Light, Little Big Man, Rio Bravo, High Noon) and I dispute quite a few. The reason is simple: the genre I prefer, far ahead of all others, is that of well-told stories. I don't care about their setting or category. I regret that broadcasters and magazines classify films by genre and not by the quality of the story. "Honey, what do you want to watch tonight? A thriller or a romantic comedy?" "A well-written story!" I think I'm not alone in this, and the first criterion for deciding to spend a few hours on a story is not the genre but word of mouth—which is often due to the quality of the story.

A small terminological clarification: I'm talking about genre in the general sense of type of story. Thriller, biopic, epic, disaster film, etc. In France, "genre film" sometimes refers to films belonging to a handful of highly coded genres in which Anglo-Saxons specialize and in which French cinema is underrepresented: survival, fantasy, horror, and science fiction. In the United States, a distinction is made between "film genre," which deals with film genres, and "genre film," which often refers to a B-movie belonging to a particular genre.

Theorists will tell you that a genre is both a promise and a system of codes—these codes can be of different natures: setting, props, themes, tone, characters, plot, symbols, etc. Theorists add that you have an interest in respecting both. First, it makes your task easier. Second, if you don't just reproduce the codes, it can help you avoid clichés and offer something refreshing (and thus avoid producing a "genre film"). Third, it prevents the viewer from being disoriented. It's not just script theorists and Hollywood decision-makers who are fond of genres; it seems that many viewers are too.

In short, if your story fits into a genre, it's a good idea to know its conventions. For example, in a romantic comedy, a famous American script theorist explains, the kiss is one of the obligatory passages. Often at the end. Logical, right? Except... The Apartment is one of the most beautiful romantic comedies in the repertoire, and it doesn't contain a kiss. I don't see a kiss in City Lights, Bringing Up Baby, or My Fair Lady either. Nor in most musicals (and romantic ones) starring Fred Astaire. You might retort that my examples are somewhat old and that genre codes evolve. That's true. So I'll cite the following romantic comedies. They are recent and don't contain a kiss: Salmon Fishing in the Yemen (2012), If You Are the One (2008), This Is Not a Love Story (2015), This Is Not What I Expected (2017). I conclude that you can break the codes of a genre, even the most important ones, without the world collapsing. But, as with all rules, it's better to bend them knowingly.

Some genre specialists think that all stories necessarily belong to a genre, and often to several—the most "discreet" or "neutral" genre being the psychological drama. It seems that authors have an interest in mixing genres, not just sticking to one. This is surely why we see a romantic subplot in many action or adventure films. The interest of having multiple genres is perhaps to enrich the story, to add thematic and dramatic layers. In any case, if you feel your story lacks strength, one option is to add a genre to it. And exploit it, of course. Thus, you may discover that your subject will be more powerful if you transpose it to a distant era or add a criminal investigation or include elements of black comedy. Or if you give it the appearance of a mockumentary like Zelig.

Genre, especially when it's exotic, is also sometimes considered a symbolic tool for addressing a subject. If, for example, you want to talk about a subject that concerns you personally, it can be fruitful not to address it directly, frontally, but to use a metaphor, a strong premise, or a genre. It's a way to detach from your subject. It may also help the receiver better understand the message unconsciously. Fairy tales perfectly illustrate this principle. By exploiting a medieval, fantastic, and Manichean setting, fairy tales symbolically speak to the child about their unconscious fears and desires.

Others will tell you that you have an interest in specializing. If you're good at horror films, only write horror films. Why not. It's true that the profession tends to label artists, to want to fit them into small boxes. I still wonder how Billy Wilder managed to co-write and direct grim films like The Lost Weekend and comedies like Some Like It Hot. I also think that an author is not a painter. When a painter approaches a gallery owner, they come with several paintings, and they better manifest a unique style. But a screenwriter or novelist never sends a dozen stories to a decision-maker at once. It's already a challenge to get a single project read by a decision-maker. So five! Moreover, will a decision-maker charmed by your social comedy want to ensure you only write social comedies?

Since I'm talking about social comedy, it's time to clarify that, in my opinion, comedy is not a genre but a super-genre. And above all, a treatment, one of the only two possible treatments of life's matters. Whatever the subject, there are only two ways to talk about it: serious or mocking, straightforward or offbeat. Comedy represents far too many works in the repertoire to be reduced to a genre. Slapstick, parody, social comedy, romantic comedy, musical comedy, black comedy, on the other hand, are genres.

Final thought for the road. There is a genre that seems more delicate than all others: anticipation, what Anglo-Saxons call "social science fiction." First, because with each new work, the setting must be presented. It's never the same. In a western, an epic, a pirate story, the setting is already known, already coded. Even in classic science fiction, you can reuse codes proposed by others. In anticipation, everything must be invented and presented each time. Corollary, it takes time. So, it lengthens the first act. And above all, it needs to be credible. And even indisputable. Many anticipation works fail, in my opinion, because they can't convince the viewer from the start. Hence the temptation for some authors to create mystery, a way to postpone the moment when they have to explain their setting. In my opinion, this solves nothing and makes reception even more painful. Let's be clear, I'm not saying to avoid anticipation. It's one of the most useful genres and has some lovely successes (1984 or Black Mirror, for example). I'm just saying it's a genre that requires particular care.

4. THE DRAMATIC PITCH

"Twenty-five words, max."
(Griffin Mill (Tim Robbins), The Player)
"When I'm given a subject, (...) I like to see it on a sheet of paper. (...) You don't have to write much. Just that a man meets a woman somewhere and something happens. (...) A viewer sees the film and, back home, is likely to talk about it to his wife. She wants to know how it was, so he tells her it's the story of a man who meets a woman—and what he tells his wife is what you should have written on that sheet of paper from the start."
(Alfred Hitchcock in 1939 [42])
"I fight against him, I fight against you, I fight for Mom's rights to the house."
(Eben, Desire Under the Elms)

A. PROTAGONIST-OBJECTIVE-OBSTACLES
One of the first operations to carry out when you have a sufficiently good idea of what you want to tell (see chapter 2) is to choose the most suitable protagonist, the most suitable objective, and the most suitable obstacles to correctly translate your thought.

A simple example, if you want to show that poverty leads to crime, it's better to choose a poor protagonist rather than a rich one. A bit less obvious, if you think curiosity is a bad trait, it's better to take the neighbor rather than the photographer as the protagonist (see pages 25 and 27).

On the importance of the nature of the protagonist
The choice of protagonist is not trivial. It largely determines the point of view developed by the work. Take the example of Dances with Wolves. Much ink has been spilled on the film's humanism. What perhaps hasn't been said enough is that by taking a kind soldier as the protagonist, the authors condemned the genocide of Native Americans while giving today's viewers a clear conscience. Because the film doesn't just say that Native Americans were wrongly massacred.

It also says that among the people of the massacres, there were humane and understanding people. No doubt every American viewer recognized their ancestor in John Dunbar (Kevin Costner). To this, one could add that the film paradoxically shows a certain ostracism towards the Pawnees, ostracism it claims to condemn elsewhere. Is it because there must be "villains" in a story at all costs? Even villains have reasons. Yet Dances with Wolves hardly cares about the reasons of the Pawnees. Is this truly the best way to be humanistic? By definition, humanism aims for the human person and their flourishing. For the human being to flourish, improve, and help future generations do the same, they need to understand themselves. It's the Socratic wish: "Know thyself." To understand, for example, what led them to massacre Native Americans. It's great to recognize the genocide of Native Americans, it's great to condemn it, but in my opinion, it's even greater to understand why. Because it's the only way to prevent another genocide. Yet, it's not by taking a kind soldier as the protagonist that we invite the viewer to effectively understand the problem. For Dances with Wolves to be truly, deeply humanistic, the protagonist would have needed a moral flaw, to be, for example, a "bad" soldier whose reasons and motivations the authors would have explained, reminding us that barbarism—which, among other things, leads to genocide—exists in each of us. Of course, it would have been less successful. I'm not saying that Dances with Wolves is a bad film from a craftsmanship point of view. Simply, it's not obvious that it is, as has often been said, a very humanistic film.

Angelicism
In fact, Dances with Wolves is representative of a trend that regularly runs through Western cinema, which could be called "angelicism" and consists of only telling stories of positive heroes. Is it due to a public need? To authors' modesty fearing to make their protagonists unsympathetic? To political correctness? To producers who want positive heroes to, they imagine, attract audiences? To actors who fear for their image? It's likely that an explanation also lies in the social context. In times of crisis, we need positive and all-powerful saviors. The first American superheroes were born in the 1930s, after the 1929 crisis.

Anyway, we are far today from the inglorious characters of the 1970s, those protagonists with a beautiful dark side called "anti-heroes" (see Ugly, Dirty and Bad, Aguirre, the Wrath of God, The Old Lady's Money, Harold and Maude, The Tenant, Midnight Cowboy, Midnight Express, Scent of a Woman, Série noire, Taxi Driver, Dog Day Afternoon, One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, etc.). In Saturday Night Fever, which is far from being a confidential film, the protagonist Tony Manero (John Travolta) is presented as particularly sexist. In the middle of the film, he starts making love in a car with a friend (Donna Pescow). Suddenly, he stops and asks her if she's protected. She replies no. He then declares he doesn't want to go further and simply asks her to perform oral sex. Sic! Could we see such a scene in a mainstream American film today?

Billy Wilder said in 1970 [94]: "We can make superficial films about show business (...) where the star breaks a leg and the understudy triumphs. But the understudy is actually a boring character. The interesting character not treated is the star who broke a leg and sees the unknown take their place." For Billy Wilder, cinema should be more interested in the unsympathetic characters played by Jean Hagen in Singin' in the Rain or Bebe Daniels in 42nd Street. Note that today, it's Anglo-Saxon television that has taken up the torch of 1970s cinema. Gray characters thrive much more in TV series than in cinema.

In short, as you can see, the choice of protagonist is far from a trivial and innocent operation. I remind you that the protagonist may not be the main character of the work—that is, the character who embodies the subject or one of the major themes—but is the one who conveys the author's intention (see Tartuffe or The Journey of Mr. Perrichon). Molière didn't take Tartuffe as the protagonist because he doesn't seek to understand hypocrisy, but simply to condemn it. To show that we are sorcerer's apprentices, that it takes a tremendous amount of energy against the machines we ourselves created, James Cameron doesn't take the evil exterminator robot (Arnold Schwarzenegger) as the protagonist but as the antagonist in Terminator.

Everyone has their reasons
I wrote earlier that the nature of the protagonist depends on your intention. Some works adopt a very particular structure that consists of not clearly choosing a single protagonist and focusing on the actions of several characters. If you have multiple protagonists, each with a different objective, you simply end up with a choral narrative. This can work if you alternate the plots. But it can become very complicated if your different protagonists oppose each other, in other words, if they are each other's respective obstacles. This is the case in Cops and Robbers and A Separation. The (almost philosophical) interest of this type of structure is to show that everyone has their reasons and that it's unfair to take sides categorically. But the (craftsmanship) risk of this type of structure is to prevent identification and keep the viewer at a distance. The first season of a series like Spartacus: Blood and Sand magnificently manages to focus on several antagonistic characters without breaking identification. The trick is to designate a clear and distinct protagonist (Spartacus (Andy Whitfield), in this case) and then develop the actions of secondary characters, including antagonists. In Spartacus: Blood and Sand, we abundantly follow the paths of Batiatus (John Hannah) and Crixus (Manu Bennett) even though they are often opponents of Spartacus. It's no coincidence that I cite a series as an example. It's likely that the second trick to successfully handling multiple antagonistic actions is to have space. Cops and Robbers and A Separation are films. If, at the beginning of a feature film, you clearly identify a protagonist and then start focusing on the actions of opponents, not only do you make identification difficult, but you can also give the impression of not respecting the unity of action.

B. THE LOGLINE
Sometimes associated with the setting, the inciting incident, and the obstacles, the protagonist-objective pair can be formulated in a sentence called the "pitch." Among other things, the word "pitch" means "launch" in English. In baseball, the pitcher is the player positioned at the center of the field, who throws the ball to the batter. In dramaturgy, the pitcher throws an idea to a decision-maker to sell it. But, as we will see, the pitch is much more than a sales tool reserved for Hollywood transactions. It's also a formidable writing tool. And even, as we will see with the elements of the first act, a logical tool, an evidence.

Tagline and logline
We distinguish several types of pitch. The two main ones are the tagline and the logline (or one-line pitch or dramatic pitch), whose functions are different. The tagline is a slogan. It gives a good idea of the spirit of the work, its atmosphere, but reveals little about the story itself. Examples:
A. In space, no one can hear you scream.
B. Troubles?... Confusions?... Anxieties?... Meet real professionals.
C. Just when you thought it was safe to go back in the water.
D. Life is in their hands. Death is in their minds.

The tagline is essentially a sales tool intended for viewers, and if it addresses a decision-maker, the idea is to interest them in the subject, certainly not to tell them the story. It can be fun for a screenwriter to come up with a tagline for their project (for example, on the cover page of their script), but it remains accessory. Moreover, most taglines are conceived once the film is made, to be put on the poster.

The logline or one-line pitch—"one-line" meaning both a sentence and a guiding line—is a summary of the story in one line. The principle can be applied to theater as well as cinema. Examples:
E. A young prince battles his own doubts to avenge his father.
F. A military man full of panache and poetry but self-conscious about a physical flaw tries to woo a precious woman through a handsome intermediary.
G. A tramp does everything to help a young blind florist who mistakes him for a millionaire.
H. A scientist confronts the intolerance and obscurantism of his time to assert his revolutionary theories.
I. An ordinary composer seeks to destroy the musical genius who fascinates him.
J. To catch a cruelly inventive serial killer, an FBI trainee takes the risk of consulting a manipulative and even more dangerous psychopath.

The one-line pitch resembles the more or less successful film summaries found in TV magazines. Logically, a well-conceived dramatic pitch at the start doesn't change during writing. Of course, a dramatic pitch is extremely reductive. The examples given above do not account for all the richness of the corresponding works. But that's not their goal. The interest is to define the base of the story, the main idea of the narrative, the point on which the entire pyramid must rest.

It's an obvious sales tool. You go to a decision-maker and summarize your subject in a few words: "It's the story of a self-centered weather presenter trying to escape the endless repetition of a grim day in his life (K)." Point. Immediately, the decision-maker asks for more. How does this day repeat? What does the presenter do to escape it? Does he succeed? Is it a thriller or a comedy? What do you want to say or show with this story? Etc. The discussion can then last an hour, you can tell as many details as you want, the decision-maker has a base to which all the narrative information can be attached. They will have a clear idea of the subject as well as the story.

In the United States, pitching is the subject of entire chapters in books dedicated to screenwriting. They explain how to prepare and conduct a pitching session. Some common-sense rules emerge. The most well-known is to know the person you are pitching to. You don't pitch a sitcom project to a producer who makes melodramatic unitary films.

The pitch as a writing and evaluation tool
But, again, the one-line pitch is not just a sales tool. It's also a valuable writing tool. Because, by constituting a guiding line for the story, even reductive, it allows the author not to stray, to respect the unity of action, and to clarify their ideas and intentions. It also forces one to be concise, a quality that many authors lack.

That's why, in addition to being a sales tool and a writing tool, the dramatic pitch has a third characteristic: it is a competence revealer. When I hear a pitch that works, I don't have the certainty that its author will be able to develop the story, but I at least feel that they know where they are going, that they know what they are telling. I have the (very pleasant) impression that there will be a pilot in the plane. When I encounter a shaky pitch, I tell myself that the author is already struggling to organize their ideas, and I remember Boileau's words [10]: "What is well conceived is clearly stated, and the words to say it come easily." Organizing one's ideas, finding the words to say it, is one of the major activities of any author.

You now understand why the pitch is so important. In my opinion, it is an essential step. And a compass for the author, until the end.
C. DESIGNING YOUR DRAMATIC PITCH

A standard dramatic pitch necessarily includes the protagonist, the essence of their characterization, and their goal. Example: "A famous but cowardly singer struggles to hide his bisexuality."

You'll notice the use of a verb expressing a difficult-to-achieve goal. "Struggles...", "Goes all out to...", "Makes every effort...", "Endeavors to...", "Does his utmost to...". In a pitch, one doesn't merely charm; one moves heaven and earth to charm. One doesn't find; one searches. My preferred formula is "Fights to...". This is an important rule. A good pitch is enticing. Therefore, a good pitch highlights a challenging action. It should exude both suspense (in the broad sense) and the protagonist's fierce determination.

In a pitch, you might also find:
- An inciting incident. Example: "Denounced on France Culture by a jealous composer, a famous but cowardly singer fights to hide his bisexuality";
- An idea of the obstacles. Example: "A famous but cowardly singer fights to hide his bisexuality from his catechist wife";
- An arena, meaning a significant setting, which we'll return to. Example: "In 1950s Japan, a famous but cowardly singer fights to hide his bisexuality";
- A major dramatic irony. In my example, it is implied by the verb "hide". Expressions like "who thinks he is", "without knowing that", or "unbeknownst to" immediately evoke dramatic irony.

To summarize, the standard formula for a dramatic pitch is: "In such an arena, following such an inciting incident, such a character fights against such obstacles to achieve such a goal." Let's revisit the pitch example above: "To catch a cruelly inventive serial killer, an FBI trainee takes the risk of consulting a manipulative and even more dangerous psychopath." We are not far from the ideal formula. The inciting incident is mentioned—a serial killer is on the loose—the protagonist has three characteristics (female, cop, rookie), her goal is clear (catch the serial killer), her greatest sub-goal is indicated (consult a psychopath), and we have a good idea of the danger and obstacles—the psychopath is manipulative and dangerous, not to mention the serial killer who also seems vicious.

A logical trio
There is always a logical link between the protagonist, the inciting incident, and their goal, especially if the future protagonist is part of the inciting incident. Imagine the principal of your son's school calls you in and tells you your son is showing worrying signs of academic delay. What do you do? Will you immediately try to seduce your neighbor or build a house? No, you will adopt a goal related to the inciting incident and your personality.

If you put Jean-Claude Dusse (Michel Blanc in Les Bronzés and Les Bronzés font du ski) in Cyrano de Bergerac's situation, Edmond Rostand's play lasts ten minutes. Jean-Claude will immediately imagine he has an opening with Roxane, try to make a move, and get rejected. Curtain. If an uncertain young man learns that his father was killed by his uncle, it can lead to a three-hour play (Hamlet, for example). But if it's Rodrigue (Le Cid) who experiences the same inciting incident, the uncle gets skewered at the first opportunity, and the story also lasts about ten minutes.

It's because there is a logical link between the protagonist, the inciting incident, and the goal that the inciting incident is such a valuable tool. It helps the audience understand the future protagonist's motivations. In short, unless it makes sense for your story, don't deprive yourself of the inciting incident.

The formulation of a pitch
Even if it's a good sign, it's not absolutely imperative that the pitch fits into a single sentence. It must give a single direction, a single line of writing, but it doesn't mean you should perform grammatical acrobatics to fit everything into one sentence. Example: "In the year 2530, law enforcement and justice are handled by robots. Following a divorce, the corrupt leader of an ecological party fights for custody of her children." This makes two sentences and thirty-two words. I think Griffin Mill (Tim Robbins), the protagonist producer of The Player, wouldn't hold it against me.

Moreover, a pitch should not contain:
- Names. It doesn't matter if the popular singer is named Jiro or Akira. A pitch should only contain proper names if your story involves a historical figure;
- The arena, if it is not significant. Generally, a contemporary Western city is not interesting enough to feature in a dramatic pitch;
- Elements that are too vague. "The corrupt leader of an ecological party" is a more meaningful phrase than "A 40-year-old woman";
- Too many details. Especially when the pitch is the very beginning of a conversation (with a friend, a decision-maker, etc.). It's better to launch a lean pitch and add details later based on questions. In short, you must be precise and specific but not drown yourself and us in details;
- The dramatic response or the ending. We've already discussed this above. The pitch must create uncertainty, thus suspense. To generate a mix of hope and fear in the listener, the pitch must not reveal whether the protagonist succeeds or not.

Pitch and central conflict
A good dramatic pitch gives a clear idea of what the central conflict of the story will be. Conversely, if you know your central conflict, you shouldn't have difficulty finding the pitch.

But how do you design your pitch when you already have plenty of leads and twenty pages of notes? The trash test, mentioned in chapter 2, proves invaluable. You have to make choices, reduce the subject to its essence, and find the central conflict. The central conflict is both the strongest and most present conflict in the work, and the one that gives an idea of the main action. Be careful, it must be a dynamic conflict (not static) and a conflict sufficiently balanced (neither too strong nor too weak). What a character cannot do at all cannot constitute a central conflict. When you have found your central conflict, you have logically found your protagonist and their goal.

In Groundhog Day, for example, the central conflict is not that Phil Connors (Bill Murray) relives the same dismal day over and over. This cursed repetition is a conflict, certainly, but it's a static conflict. It is part of the inciting incident and, once it has launched the action, we consider it a given. The central (and dynamic) conflict of Groundhog Day is Phil's difficulty in escaping this curse.

In case of choral work
If you are writing a choral work with several protagonists, you need a dramatic pitch for each plot. Ideally, the plots should be linked to each other. Either thematically or dramatically. In the latter case, one of the plots constitutes, directly or indirectly, obstacles for another plot. Of course, you can also propose both thematic and dramatic links.

A little advice on choral work: make sure it's not avoidance and/or filler, that it makes sense in your project. And also, make sure all the plots are interesting. Remember The Taste of Others. The main plot, with this business owner (Jean-Pierre Bacri) who falls in love and becomes interested in culture, is delightful. But everything revolving around Bruno (Alain Chabat), Franck (Gérard Lanvin), and Manie (Agnès Jaoui) is less exciting. This is partly because this subplot is not constructed, its protagonist is not clearly identified, and it lacks conflict. It's no coincidence that anyone summarizing the film only talks about the main plot. Yet the subplot takes up space. Since the film lasts an hour and fifty minutes, the main plot is not as developed and accomplished as it could have been. At the limit, the authors should have treated the same subject with the second plot, but seen from another angle. They do so with a third, lighter plot, that of Mrs. Castella (Christiane Millet) decorating her sister-in-law's (Brigitte Catillon) apartment.

In contrast, this is fully achieved by the authors of Portrait craché d'une famille modèle. There are four plots, all dealing with parenthood in various forms. Here are the pitches: A - A stressed salesman does his utmost to support his 9-year-old son lacking self-confidence; B - A divorced and sexually frustrated mother struggles to control her two teenage children; C - A teacher confronts her demanding husband about the education of their 4-year-old genius daughter; D - The father of the three, an insensitive patriarch, fights to save his fourth child, a 25-year-old prodigal loser.

Of course, if you have a single plot with several co-protagonists, just include the co-protagonists in the sentence. Example: "Following a misunderstanding, two clumsy house painters, a confident big guy and a whiny skinny guy, are mistaken for police officers. They strive to enforce the law." But in this case, you don't have a choral work. You just have several protagonists with the same goal.

In case of a serialized series
In the case of series (TV or comics) with non-closed episodes, the principle is the same, depending on the format of the series. Some series offer a major inciting incident and a protagonist per season or album cycle (Blueberry, Breaking Bad, 24, Prison Break). The general pitch is obvious. "In 1867, in the American West, a hot-headed cavalry lieutenant fights to pacify the Native American territory where a railroad is being built" (cf. the four albums of the Iron Horse cycle). If there are subplots, each has its pitch. Some series offer several major plots that run alternately and sometimes intersect. Each has its inciting incident and protagonist (A French Village, Downton Abbey, Desperate Housewives). This results in several pitches, as many as there are plots, exactly like in the example of Portrait craché d'une famille modèle above.

The first season of The Bureau offers an interesting alternative to these two major serial principles. Because it contains a main protagonist (Malotru (Mathieu Kassovitz)) and three inciting incidents all concerning the same character. This results in three pitches. Or, if preferred, a triple pitch: "A brilliant DGSE member must train a novice, manage his love for a former target without it being known, and find an agent missing in Algeria under worrying conditions." Of course, there is a link between the three plots: they are all related to Malotru's espionage activity. The first season of The Bureau would work much less well if Éric Rochant had given Malotru the triple goal of finding the missing agent, learning piano, and adopting a child. Just as finding a missing agent in Algeria, a missing agent in Hungary, and a missing agent in Mexico would not have been a very good idea either. It is also noted that the protagonist is surrounded by co-protagonists. Thus, Malotru starts training the novice (Sara Giraudeau) before quickly passing the baton to a colleague (Florence Loiret Caille).

The strong postulate
The Americans call it a "high concept". It's a strong premise whose presentation makes one salivate. Not all pitches contain the seeds of exciting action. When you hear: "In 1879, in Norway, an infantilized woman does everything to prevent her condescending husband from knowing her secret (A Doll's House)", you have to trust the author to envision an exciting story. But there are cases where the pitch immediately announces a beautiful central conflict, a paradoxical situation, or original premises.

If you want to find a strong premise, you can think of discrepancy, inversion, irony, spectacle, and... the prospect of conflict. Examples:
N. A professional robber determined to go straight is taken hostage by an amateur robber. He fights to prove his innocence.
O. A policeman does everything to neutralize the bomb on a bus that will explode if the bus goes below 50 mph.
P. During Prohibition, two musicians disguise themselves as women to escape mobsters. They strive to manage the situation.
Q. Mistaken for a non-existent spy, a carefree advertiser tries to save his skin.
R. A 49-year-old man transported into the body of the teenager he was at 14 does everything to prevent his father from leaving his family.

That said, I'm not sure that authors of strong premises immediately think of a properly formulated dramatic pitch. The key to a strong premise often holds in two words: "What if...?" What if a professional robber was taken hostage by an amateur robber? (N). What if a virtual spy incarnated in a guy who asked for nothing? (Q). What if Shakespeare was a victim of writer's block? (Shakespeare in Love). What if scientists managed to bring dinosaurs back to life? (Jurassic Park). What if a teenager went back in time and met his future parents at the same age as him? (Back to the Future). What if an unemployed actor disguised himself as a woman to find work? (Tootsie). What if the consciousness of a 40-year-old man, just deceased, entered the body of a 17-year-old teenager (A Radiant Sky)? The "What if...?" approach is seductive, but you can't stop there. You must then present the subject in the form of an effective dramatic pitch.

Danny Rubin has often explained how the idea for Groundhog Day came to him. He wondered: "What if an individual was immortal, if he could live for a very long time, how would he change?" Rubin was excited by this "What if...?" but immediately encountered a problem: how to show someone living for a very long time without making a historical or science fiction film spanning several centuries? Rubin then remembered an idea he had a few years earlier, about a guy whose day repeats endlessly, which can lead to a form of eternity. Rubin thus moved from a strong premise to a pitch.

Be careful, developing a strong premise is as difficult, if not more, than writing an apparently banal subject. For two reasons. First, the strong premise creates an expectation in the audience, making them more demanding. Second, if the strong premise immediately provides you with a handful of powerful, amusing scenes, you must manage to insert these scenes into a coherent and solid whole. Don't just repeat the original conflict throughout the second act, as is sometimes seen (cf. Esmera or The Visitors).

Neither the result nor the intention nor the atmosphere
A pitch must summarize the action, not the meaning of the story, nor the author's intention, nor, as we've seen, the final result. "Following the death of his guinea pig, a child learns the meaning of life" or "It's the story of a hairdresser who embarks on a journey of self-discovery" are not good dramatic pitches because they do not account for the concrete dynamics of the work. They describe more the result (also very vague) than the way the protagonist achieves it.

The two examples above are close to what could be called the "trajectory pitch", echoing the trajectory goal we discussed in chapter 1. That is, the sentence that summarizes the internal trajectory of the protagonist or another character. This subject is the focus of the next chapter.

Some examples of pitches that, in my opinion, don't work. In any case, that don't entice me. I'll let you guess why for each of them.
- To earn a living, two losers, Jean-René and Kevin, embark on an adventure as dangerous as it is hilarious.
- It's the story of an ambitious and manipulative woman who wants to become famous and ends up collapsing, swept away by her megalomania.
- Amnesia, gastronomy, and state secrets.
- A young group of friends gets lost in a hostile land. But they find the idea that will allow them to get out.
- There are secrets better left unknown. What is the price to pay for ethics? And for freedom?
- Boniface, 20, has always lived in his housing project, in the suburbs, with the same friends. But it's starting to weigh on him. He dreams of other horizons.
- The lives of a painter, an executive, and a drug dealer intertwine in a story of violence and redemption.
- Angélique is devastated by her brother's death. Can hope take the form of a charming exorcist?
- Film title tells the story of a rebirth. When you're at the bottom of the pool, you can only rise.
- Ouagadougou, 1960. Little Ousmane dreams of leaving his village to see the world. The arrival of a white teacher will shake up his daily life.
- Around a child with leukemia, a tragedy unfolds, involving two women with opposing values.

D. PITCH AND FIRST ACT
We sometimes hear about the "dictate of the pitch". As if condensing a story wasn't a legitimate aspiration. As if a good story could do without a pitch. Or as if anything with an Anglo-Saxon name was suspect. I explained in La dramaturgie that cliffhangers, milking, and script doctoring were invented by ancient and classical playwrights, not Hollywood.

And then, recently, I made a discovery. I considered the first act of very diverse works: L'argent de la vieille, The Sting, Breaking Bad, Les Compères, How to Train Your Dragon, The School for Wives, Jean de Florette, Lysistrata, A Doll's House, Miracle in Alabama, Mustang, Where Is My Friend's House?, The Story of Qiu Ju, A Radiant Sky, Groundhog Day, The Lives of Others. I even summarized them on an editing table. And I realized that all these first acts were nothing more than the presentation of the elements of their respective pitch. At the end of a respectable first act, that is, at the moment of the declaration of the goal, the audience knows what the action will be and has all the components of the pitch in mind.

What do we see, for example, in the first act of The Story of Qiu Ju? That we are in a poor Chinese countryside, that Qiu Ju (Gong Li) is a determined and justice-loving woman, that she seeks reparation for an assault (in the lower abdomen) her husband (Liu Peiqi) was a victim of, that local justice is bureaucratic, that the aggressor (Lei Kesheng) refuses to apologize and even humiliates Qiu Ju. And what is the pitch of The Story of Qiu Ju? "In the Chinese countryside of 1990, a proud, stubborn, and legalistic peasant woman fights to obtain an apology from the proud village chief who struck her husband in the lower abdomen."

Look at the first act of The School for Wives. You find all the elements of its pitch: "In the 17th century, a sexist and all-powerful old man, haunted by the fear of being deceived, fights to thwart a budding romance between his ward, raised in ignorance, and a young man who takes the old man for a confidant." Look at the first act of Miracle in Alabama. And compare it with the pitch: "A young educator, a former blind person and just graduated, fights to teach a deaf-mute-blind teenager to communicate." A Radiant Sky: "Following a traffic accident, the consciousness of a forty-year-old finds itself in the body of a teenager. He fights to say sorry and goodbye to his family before his consciousness disappears." Mustang: "In Turkey, an 11-year-old rebellious girl and her four sisters resist the growing obscurantism and confinement of their family."

Be careful, I'm not saying that the pitch is a summary of the first act. By definition, the pitch gives an overview of the action, therefore of the second act. But since the first act's function is to launch the action, we can say that at the end of the first act, the audience has a good idea of the pitch. They have grasped that, following the inciting incident, such a character will fight to achieve such a goal against such obstacles. If you change "fights" to "will fight" or "decides to fight" in a pitch, you get a summary of the first act.

One will agree that the works cited above are not formatted products, basely commercial works. Yet you can test with all the first acts of thousands of stories of all kinds and origins, you will see that they present more or less successfully the elements of the pitch: the arena, the characterization of the future protagonist and main characters, the inciting incident, the declaration of the goal, and a general idea of the obstacles to come. In other words, the pitch is not a dictate but a narrative obviousness.

And it's even a logical obviousness. I remind you that the word "pitch", borrowed from baseball, means "launch". So what is a first act? Precisely the launch of a story.

To convince yourself, take the pitches listed above and see if they give you enough to fill a first act. See if they give you a good idea of what the action will be. Isn't such a pitch too vague? Another boastful? Yet another incomplete? Isn't there a declaration of the goal missing here, an inciting incident there? In short, those who talk about the dictate of the pitch could just as well talk to us about the dictate of a well-designed first act or even the dictate of a good story. Dictates that billions of humans have been accommodating very well for millennia.
E. PLOT-DRIVEN AND CHARACTER-DRIVEN NARRATIVES

A plot-driven narrative emphasizes action and conscious objectives. A character-driven narrative focuses more on the portrait or psychological trajectory of a character.

Of course, most narratives blend plot and characterization. We are dealing with a spectrum. At one end, we have Duel, North by Northwest, Terminator, An Italian Straw Hat, 24, where characterization is sometimes almost nonexistent. At the other end, we have Amadeus, The Cherry Orchard, Don Juan, The Easy Life, The Misanthrope, About Schmidt, A Streetcar Named Desire, One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, where the structure is particularly loose. But many narratives successfully mix plot and characterization. I think of Cyrano de Bergerac, The School for Wives, The Apartment, Hamlet, A Doll's House, Martha Jane Cannary, Oedipus Rex, Othello, A Distant Neighborhood, Back to the Future, The Sopranos, Groundhog Day, It's a Wonderful Life (1946).

As an author, you surely have your preferences. Without realizing it, your tastes will lead you toward one type of narrative over another. It's interesting to become aware of your preferences. Because where you place the cursor depends not only on your tastes but also on your intention.

Characterization in Plot-Driven Narratives

If you choose a plot-driven narrative, you will focus on the twists and turns. However, I encourage you to characterize your characters. What's the point, you might ask? I believe that good characterizations make a narrative more accurate, richer, and more interesting. Don't you find Haddock, Calculus, Thomson and Thompson, and Castafiore more credible and endearing than Tintin? Tintin is so smooth that he becomes non-human. Giving your characters rough edges is both realistic and more enjoyable for the audience.

Action or Characterization First?

Dear reader, if you know Groundhog Day, I propose a little game. Imagine you are presenting the film to a friend who has never heard of it. You want to pitch the subject. What do you say, exactly, in one or two sentences maximum? Note the answer somewhere. Then answer the following question.

Did you present the film with a sentence like: 1 - "It's the story of a self-centered weatherman who fights to escape the endless repetition of a grim day in his life"? Or like this: 2 - "It's the story of a self-centered weatherman who learns to become human and authentic"? Or rather like this: 3 - "It's the story of a self-centered weatherman who fights to escape the endless repetition of a grim day in his life and learns to become human and authentic"? The first summary is the dramatic pitch. The second is what I called earlier the "trajectory pitch" (see chapter 5). The film is the sum of both. Even if some viewers mainly remember Phil Connors' (Bill Murray) journey, Groundhog Day is not just a character-driven narrative. It's also a plot. In short, the best summary, in my opinion, is number 3.

N.B. In some cases, it's good to indicate the genre if the pitch elements don't make it clear. Here, for example, one could say: "It's a romantic comedy. The story of a self-centered weatherman who fights to escape the endless repetition of a grim day in his life and learns to become human and authentic." If you want to preserve suspense about the internal trajectory, you can indicate it with uncertainty. "It's the story of a self-centered weatherman who fights to escape the endless repetition of a grim day in his life. Will he understand that the solution is to become human and authentic?"

By definition, a plot is a causal succession of events. Many believe that the most important thing in a narrative is not the plot but what it provokes in (at least) one of the characters, how it transforms them. Some even go so far as to think that it's the characters who make the plot and not the other way around. I think both (plot and character) are interconnected, that one doesn't go without the other. And vice versa. As mentioned above, if you put Jean-Claude Dusse in Cyrano's situation, you don't get the same story. In this case, it's the way a different character (Dusse instead of Cyrano) responds to the same situation that changes the narrative. But the reverse also works. Keep the same character, Cyrano, and this time change the situation: instead of hearing Roxane say she's in love with Christian, Cyrano learns that Le Bret has been cowardly assassinated. Again, the resulting story is no longer the same. Modify a character or an event, and you change the narrative. I insist: plot and character are closely intertwined, and both are desirable.

The Basic Structure in Character-Driven Narratives

If you choose to write a character-driven narrative, you will focus on characterization, possibly on a character's evolution, but this should not exempt you from having a dramatic pitch. I don't believe for a second in 100% "character-driven" narratives. I think there always needs to be a plot, even if it's a pretext. I doubt Sophocles' motivation for writing Oedipus Rex was to show how Oedipus eliminates the plague ravaging his city. He was probably more interested in Oedipus's journey, wanting to show how Oedipus learns the truth about himself. In fact, the major stake of the play is Oedipus's identity, not the survival of the Thebans. Nevertheless, Sophocles needed a guiding thread, a dramatic question, a pretext plot: eradicating the plague in Thebes. And it's by fighting to achieve this goal that Oedipus discovers who he is. The same principle applies in Groundhog Day (see above). It's by trying to escape the curse that Phil transforms.

Successful character-driven narratives have a classic structure, at least an inciting incident and a climax. (N.B. Warning, I reveal the endings of the following works.)

In the play Amadeus, the inciting incident is Salieri's discovery of Mozart, and the climax is Mozart's madness. Salieri's dramatic objective is to escape the jealousy consuming him by destroying the "Creature." In Don Juan, the inciting incident is Done Elvire's demand for explanations—she even curses him regarding his tragic flaw ("Know that your crime will not go unpunished and that the same Heaven you mock will avenge your perfidy")—Don Juan's dramatic objective is to live his libertine life as he sees fit, and the climax is Don Juan's death. In The Easy Life, the inciting incident is the meeting between Bruno (Vittorio Gassman) and Roberto (Jean-Louis Trintignant), Roberto's dramatic objective is to get home, and the climax is the car accident. In The Misanthrope, there is no inciting incident, but Alceste has a general objective: to get Célimène to stop flirting with everyone and take a clear stand. The climax is when Célimène refuses to follow Alceste into his "desert" (V/4). In About Schmidt, the inciting incident is Mr. Schmidt's (Jack Nicholson) retirement, and the climax is his speech at his daughter's (Hope Davis) wedding. Schmidt's dramatic objective is to make himself useful. It may not be conscious, but it's clear. In The Cherry Orchard, the inciting incident is when we learn that the family property will be auctioned in three months, Lioubov's dramatic objective is to find a solution to the property's problem, and the climax is when Lopakhine announces he has bought the estate. In A Streetcar Named Desire, the inciting incident is Blanche's arrival at Stella's. Blanche's dramatic objective is to catch her breath. The climax is the rape. In One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, the inciting incident is not shown; it's the scene that decides McMurphy (Jack Nicholson) to pretend he's insane. But it's narrated in the interview with the director (Dean R. Brooks), and we see its consequences (McMurphy's arrival in this new universe). McMurphy's dramatic objective is to be seen favorably by the authorities to avoid prison. The climax is McMurphy's attempted murder of Nurse Ratched (Louise Fletcher).

Note that, in some cases, the protagonist may be someone other than the character you are portraying, who is often the main character of the work. This is the case in Amadeus and The Easy Life above, as well as in Being There, My Uncle, The Perfume of the Woman in Black, and Tartuffe. In these works, the protagonist's action gives the author the opportunity to characterize an important character on the sidelines of the protagonist.

In all scenarios, if you choose to create a portrait and even if plot-driven narratives don't motivate you, you must design a sufficiently solid framework for your characters. Otherwise, you risk losing the audience and missing out on your remarkable portrait. We will return in chapter 8 to the importance of having what Gustave Flaubert [30] called a thread.

F. TEST YOUR PITCH CONSTANTLY

Once you have your pitch, ask yourself three questions. 1. Is it well-formulated? 2. Does it excite me, make me want to spend a year on it? 3. Will it allow me to convey my message, does it correspond to what I want to say and show? If you answer yes to all three questions, you can move on.

Of course, the pitch is a necessary but not sufficient condition. You must also exploit it, meaning you must fulfill its promises. Hence obstacles, sub-objectives, trajectory conflicts, dramatic knots, and all that jazz. In other words, don't write a pitch to please me and then do whatever you want. Throughout the development of your narrative, I invite you to regularly check that you respect your dramatic pitch and that you are digging into what constitutes one of your main writing tools. If not, if you feel you're straying, I invite you to reformulate the pitch and examine its coherence as well as your interest in this new formulation. It must correspond to both what you want to write and what you are telling.

5. THE INTERNAL TRAJECTORY

"You make me want to be a better man."
(Melvin [Jack Nicholson], As Good as It Gets)

"Having listened to you for these thousand and one nights, I leave with a profoundly changed soul."
(Shahryar, One Thousand and One Nights)

"With Qiu Ju, a Chinese woman, I wanted to say that every Chinese person must do the same thing: fight to get their way and discover themselves in the process they have engaged in to achieve their goal."
(Zhang Yimou [961])

"It's all well and good to have fun all the time, but I would like you to grow up a bit."
(Kazué, A Distant Neighborhood)

"Rejecting a loyal friend is actually depriving oneself of a part of one's own life. (...) It takes time to learn this for sure."
(Creon, Oedipus Rex)

The vast majority of narratives are not loops where one returns to the starting point or parentheses without consequences. They lead one or more characters from one point to another, most often through causal relationships. We go from A to Z via B, C, etc. All well-designed narratives thus depict an evolution. And often, several evolutions of different natures. We go from A1 to Z1, from A2 to P2, from O3 to T3, etc. These are journeys, paths, trajectories in the broad sense. But when we talk about trajectory in the art of storytelling, we refer more specifically to an internal and personal journey, a psychological or moral change experienced by one or more characters. Most often, this journey is for the better. However, sometimes the transformation goes in the wrong direction.

A. THREE EMOTIONAL QUESTIONS

By definition, the protagonist has a general dramatic objective, motivations, and several sub-objectives. They are not alone. All worthy characters have one or more objectives. They want something. In parallel and in addition to these desires, some characters are sometimes presented with a need, often an unconscious need. It is sometimes called the "dramatic need." I will call it here the "trajectory objective." The Anglo-Saxons sometimes speak of "want" and "need" to designate these two objectives. Or of external story and internal story. The names change, but the idea is the same.

A Doll's House and Rain Man offer two beautiful examples of this pair of objectives. In Henrik Ibsen's play, Nora's general objective is to prevent her husband from discovering a compromising secret. Moreover, we feel that she would benefit from not letting herself be infantilized by her husband. We therefore attribute this trajectory objective to her. In the film written by Barry Morrow and Ronald Bass, Charlie (Tom Cruise) has the general objective of getting a share of the inheritance. We feel that he also needs to grow emotionally, to be less venal and more warm.

The trajectory objective is the second type of unconscious objective I mentioned in chapter 1 (pages 16-17). It corresponds to a universal need, that of gaining humanity, and thus can constitute an additional identification factor. As soon as the trajectory objective is perceived by the audience, it raises a question in their mind, which I call the "trajectory question": "Will the character achieve their trajectory objective and if so, how?" Or if you prefer: "Will the character see their need resolved and if so, how?" In short, we wonder if the character will grow.

You will notice that if the need is unconscious and the audience perceives it unbeknownst to the character, we automatically have a case of dramatic irony. And thus, an ironic question, whose general formulation "Will the victim discover, when and how, what they don't know?" becomes: "Will the character discover, when and how, that they had a lack, a personal problem to solve?" In fact, fully realized internal trajectories often come with an awareness. But not always. We will come back to this.

In any narrative, suspense, the audience's emotional anticipation, can therefore be created by three questions: dramatic, ironic, and trajectory. Namely:

- Will the protagonist succeed?
- Will the character who is unaware learn what we know?
- Will the character who needs to grow change?

Of course, it is important that there is a link between the protagonist's desire and one of the characters' needs. This is one of the conditions for the narrative to have coherence, an organic dimension. It makes no sense for a character to undertake a difficult action on their own while, elsewhere, a character transforms. In general, the link is as follows: in seeking to achieve their dramatic objective, the protagonist encounters obstacles and generates conflicts that lead one of the characters (often the protagonist themselves but not always, see below) to experience a personality change. I say "in general" because, sometimes, as we will see, the link is reversed. Because a character changes, an action is initiated. But in both cases, there is a link between the dramatic objective and the trajectory objective.

This chapter is intended to help you construct one or more internal trajectories. (N.B. Warning, all the examples cited below reveal important moments of the corresponding works, often the endings. Skip the works you don't know if you don't want their discovery to be spoiled.)

But before delving into the details of creating an internal trajectory, I have several crucial preliminary remarks to make.

B. BEWARE OF DOGMAS

Personality Change, Not Status Change

Preliminary Remark No. 1. I believe that a character's trajectory is more powerful and meaningful if it is moral or psychological, rather than social or biological. Some observers want characters to change so much (preferably for the better) that anything is good to spot a change, sometimes at the expense of the work (see the analysis of Tootsie below). Even when a protagonist overcomes their internal flaw at the moment of achieving their objective, they cry out for change. Except that if the protagonist still possesses their internal flaw, they haven't changed, at least regarding that flaw. They may have just managed to cope with it. Success is not a personality change. A revelation is not a personality change. An evolution is not a personality change. A blossoming is not a personality change. An individual who finds a job or starts feeling love can keep the same character. One can be stupid poor, become rich, and remain stupid. Even the fact that a character goes from life to death may not be felt as such a big change by the audience. After all, people die a lot in drama, as in life. Dying will appear as a more significant change if the deceased learned a lesson just before dying. Like Lear and Othello but unlike Macbeth.

Another change that doesn't appear as a valid change is that of characters who are forced to evolve because circumstances impose it on them. (N.B. Warning, major spoiler.) In One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, McMurphy (Jack Nicholson) goes from rebel to submissive, and even 100% submissive. But he has no choice. He is forcibly lobotomized. In reality, McMurphy is rebellious to the end, until neutralization, and he learns no lesson. Nor does he lose one. Because a character can change for the worse, shrink instead of grow. But, in any case, they must do it of their own free will, remain responsible for what they become.

In fact, the most interesting changes are those that depict an appearance of free will, where the character who changes clearly has two paths before them and chooses (consciously or not) the one of transformation. We will come back to this.

Another scenario not to be confused with a change: that of characters whose author suddenly reveals an additional personality trait (see the example of Mr. Languedoc in La Cage aux Folles, page 215). This enriches them, gives them depth, and that's all the better, but it doesn't mean they change. An additional trait is not a modified trait. In life, it happens that a charming and attentive man becomes a monster of jealousy and abuse once he has seduced the woman he desired. This is not a personality change. It's the revelation of a hidden facet.

Why do I insist so much on these different types of evolution? Because they are not of the same value and do not require the same work. Transforming a character from cowardly to courageous or from selfish to altruistic requires much more attention than making them go from life to death, from poor to rich, or from alone to accompanied. This work of justifying the internal trajectory is delicate, and it's the whole purpose of this chapter.

Dramatic Protagonist and Trajectory Protagonist

Preliminary Remark No. 2. Despite the heavy insistence of some screenplay theorists, the character who evolves may not be the protagonist. In this case, we can then consider that there are two protagonists: one for the dramatic question and one for the trajectory question.

Armand is the dramatic protagonist of The Journey of Mr. Perrichon (dramatic objective (dram. obj.): to woo Perrichon's daughter) and Perrichon is the trajectory protagonist (trajectory objective (traj. obj.): to learn to express gratitude). Anne is the dramatic protagonist of The Miracle Worker (dram. obj.: to teach Helen to communicate) and Helen is the trajectory protagonist (traj. obj.: to be less wild). Billy (Jamie Bell) is the dramatic protagonist of Billy Elliot (dram. obj.: to become a professional dancer) and his father (Gary Lewis) is the trajectory protagonist (traj. obj.: to be more tolerant). Harold is the dramatic protagonist of How to Train Your Dragon (dram. obj.: to be accepted as he is) and his entire people are the trajectory protagonist (traj. obj.: to be less primitive). See also, below, the examples of Asterix and the Normans, Cyrano de Bergerac, One Thousand and One Nights, Toy Story, The Lives of Others, and One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest. In Ratatouille, it's not even an important character in the story who transforms; it's a secondary character: the critic Anton Ego.

It's not the co-protagonists who change in Romeo and Juliet. It's Capulet and Montague when they put an end to their ancestral hatred (V/3). In some of Molière's plays (The Miser, The Imaginary Invalid, The Tricks of Scapin), the young people and their servants are the dramatic co-protagonists when the potential trajectory protagonists are the old men. I write "potential" because Harpagon, Argan, Argante, and Géronte hardly evolve. They are more the subjects of the portraits painted by Molière. Tartuffe is the most complete play in this regard. Dorine, Mariane, and a good part of the family constitute the dramatic protagonist (objective: to prevent Tartuffe from harming). Orgon is the trajectory protagonist (objective: to be less fanatical). He is the one who evolves. And Tartuffe is the subject of the portrait (and the main character).
