In the category "The character who changes is neither the protagonist nor the main character," we must also mention all those narratives where the main character is constant but serves as inspiration for others: Being There, Mr. Deeds Goes to Town, Babette's Feast, Forrest Gump, Mary Poppins, etc. They are sometimes called "catalytic characters."

This tendency to confuse dramatic protagonist and trajectory protagonist leads some theorists to abusively attribute the status of dramatic protagonist to the character who changes. In The Godfather, for example, Michael (Al Pacino) is clearly the trajectory protagonist.
Michael is clearly the trajectory protagonist. But it's much less evident that he is the dramatic protagonist. When I watch The Godfather, I perceive the Corleone family as the protagonist. More specifically Vico (Marlon Brando), Sonny (James Caan), and Michael. Moreover, the latter takes action very late in the film, well after the second act has begun.

Changing from the start of the action

Preliminary Remark No. 3. It is tempting to think that it is the journey undertaken by your protagonist that will make them grow. Therefore, the external trajectory should generate the internal trajectory. If this is the case, logically, the change should occur at the end of the journey. However, there are a significant number of works where the change happens at the beginning of the second act. Often, it is this transformation that pushes the protagonist to take action.

The Flowers of War provides a striking example. John Miller (Christian Bale), an embalmer by trade, finds himself in a church in Nanking during the massacre of the city in 1937. Schoolgirls ask him to help them escape, but John doesn't care. He is only interested in alcohol, money, and paid sex. When Japanese soldiers burst into the church and start savagely attacking the schoolgirls, John realizes he has more important things to do than hide. He pretends to be the priest of the place and embarks on the rescue. He is no longer the selfish jerk from the beginning.

The Nightmare Before Christmas offers another example. At the start, Jack, the grand orchestrator of Halloween, feels jaded and empty. Suddenly, he discovers Christmas Town. It's such an epiphany for him that he immediately goes from depressed to joyful and decides to take care of Christmas. Of course, his conception of the holiday doesn't fit Christmas. He will eventually understand his mistake and return to what he knows best. But he doesn't regain life and warmth at the end. He changes from the start.

In Ikiru, Watanabe (Takashi Shimura) is a bland bureaucrat with a low opinion of himself and his usefulness on Earth. One day, he learns he has terminal cancer. After some wandering and hesitation, he decides to serve the world, give his life meaning, and fight to create a safe children's park in the city center.

The very first episode of Breaking Bad introduces us to Walter White (Bryan Cranston). He is a chemistry teacher bullied by some of his students. He supplements his income at a car wash where the boss, Bogdan (Marius Stan), exploits him without Walter having the courage to protest. When one of his students discovers him cleaning a car, Walter suffers yet another humiliation. Then one day, a triggering incident occurs: Walter collapses and learns he has terminal cancer. In the following scenes, Walter tells Bogdan off and attacks young people mocking his disabled son (RJ Mitte). In other words, from the very first episode of the series, the protagonist goes from passive to assertive, from dominated to dominant. This is not the only evolution Walter White experiences in the series, but it is clear that the intense conflict of the cancer diagnosis causes an immediate psychological transformation in him.

And let's not forget one of the major redemption fictions: Les Misérables. At the beginning of the novel, Jean Valjean's heart is full of hatred. Because he is released from prison, he is rejected by everyone. Only a generous bishop welcomes him. Valjean finds nothing better to do than steal from the holy man! Nevertheless, a few adventures later, he permanently moves to the good side and, under the name of M. Madeleine, starts doing good around him. And we are still at the beginning of the novel.

To these examples, we could add cases where the transformation occurs neither at the end nor the beginning but in the middle. This is the case in Schindler's List, Ninotchka, Toy Story, or The Lives of Others, analyzed below.

No one changes

Preliminary Remark No. 4. Despite what some screenplay theorists, mostly American, say, you can have a story in which no character changes. Asterix is indefinitely the same. Charlie Chaplin's Tramp never evolves in his films, neither within a given film nor from one film to another. To convince us, Chaplin shows this with a wink at the end of The Gold Rush, where the "Little Man" experiences a major social change: he becomes a millionaire. Despite wearing a top hat and fur coats, he can't help picking up a cigar butt. Inside, the Tramp hasn't changed. At the end of Big Deal on Madonna Street, all the protagonists are exactly the same as at the beginning: losers. Peppe (Vittorio Gassman) agrees to work, but is it because he has become honest or because he desperately needs to eat? Who changes personality in North by Northwest? Or in Amadeus, Some Like It Hot, Children of Paradise, Fargo, Once Upon a Time in the West, Memento?

Life of Galileo, a sumptuous play written by Bertolt Brecht, is an amusing example. From the start, Galileo is presented as a clever and stubborn man. Throughout the play, he opposes the Catholic Church. He recants before the Inquisition at the end of the thirteenth scene. The spectator who doesn't know the real story of the Italian scientist might be disappointed, but they don't think Galileo has changed personality, from brave to cowardly. Because the spectator understands, at a minimum, that Galileo was forced to recant. And then, in the third act, we discover that Galileo hasn't changed one bit. In reality, he made the Church believe he was changing his position to achieve his goal (to assert his revolutionary theories) clandestinely. The second element that lends a smile (or reflection) in Brecht's play, related to the subject at hand, is the fact that the Church will take more than two centuries to recognize its astronomical error. In short, in Life of Galileo, neither Galileo nor the Church changes!

Why grow?

So why, despite this impressive array of counterexamples, do many Americans insist on trajectory and, worse, on a well-defined type of trajectory? Some even demand that all characters transform except the villains—as if a Hollywood script were aimed at four-year-olds. Could this omnipresence of trajectory be due to an obsessive need to demonstrate that free will exists? Could it be due to confusion between the necessity for a story to be an eventful trajectory in itself and the idea that a character must have an internal trajectory? Or could it be due to religious influence? In Christian theology, the human being is born corrupted by original sin and must work towards salvation through redemption. In many religions, one must suffer to get better. A Christmas Carol, an extremely popular and influential tale in the Anglo-Saxon world, tells how a vile miser, an "old sinner" named Scrooge, transforms into a generous and compassionate man. The tale is a massive deus ex machina (cf. analysis below), an XXL moral lesson that many Anglo-Saxon parents must have imposed on their children. It may be encouraging for all those who want to save their souls, but is it essential that all stories depict one or even several redemptions? More generally, does a story necessarily have to involve a change? Biological life constantly evolves, certainly. Living organisms are born, live, and die. Inevitably, a human being is therefore not quite the same from one minute to the next. But this change is purely biological. We see naive, stubborn, or lazy people who remain naive, stubborn, or lazy all their lives and die naive, stubborn, or lazy. You might say that fiction doesn't have to be a copy-paste of life. Perhaps it has the obligation to show us the way to grow? I think, indeed, that this is clearly one of its functions. Except that... A story can show us the way to grow without any of its characters growing! I assert that one can interest a spectator with a story in which no one learns a lesson. And this for a fundamental reason: if someone must grow, it's the spectator, not necessarily one of the characters.

No one grows in Platoon or No Man's Land, but we, the spectators, are shaken and can hope to leave the screening with a better understanding of human barbarity. In Waiting for Godot, it is imperative that Vladimir and Estragon remain the same and do not change one bit. This is one of the elements that allows Beckett to convey his point of view to the spectators. The same goes for Antigone, The Cherry Orchard, Don Juan, The Misanthrope, or The Government Inspector. In all these cases, the fact that the characters remain the same until the end gives meaning to the work.

It should be noted that stories in which no one changes are all the more powerful if the circumstances presented would allow one or more characters to change. The strength of the works cited above is to plunge the characters into provocative situations and see that they do nothing, that they remain the same until the end. The lesson, if there is one, is for the spectator. The Remains of the Day offers another beautiful example of this principle.

The right measure

In short, beware of dogmas about character trajectory. There comes a point where the exceptions are so numerous that they invalidate the rule instead of confirming it.

Moreover, growing is not the only function of the story. Since myths and fairy tales, the story is also there to help us live and interpret the world around us. In other words, it serves to tame our emotions and make sense of things. And then it also serves to entertain us. For all these reasons, a well-conducted adventure film is just as respectable as a portrait full of humanity. Not to mention comedy! In many comedies, no character evolves. This sometimes contributes to enhancing the comedy and making it more meaningful. Comedy, any comedy, helps us accept our human limitations. If it doesn't suggest ways for us to grow, it clearly helps us live.

The measure with which to assess the interest of having a character who changes is your intention. It may make sense for another character than the protagonist to evolve. It may make sense for them to change at the beginning or in the middle and not at the end. It may make sense for there to be no character change at all. In the first two cases, work on the trajectory of the concerned character. In the last case, don't insist on the internal trajectory.

C. FOUR ESSENTIAL ELEMENTS

If, despite all the preceding precautions, you have decided to have a character transform during the action, you must design several narrative elements. They are essential for creating a worthy internal trajectory. They are also strongly recommended if you want to avoid some of your receivers (the spiritual children of Cinderella's stepsisters, cf. page 9) taking their subjective feelings about your work for an objective analysis. In order, you need:

1 - a scene showing the character trait that will be the subject of evolution. This moment sometimes features a second character that highlights (for the spectator) the lack of the trajectory protagonist. A sort of moral conscience or reasonable contradictor;

2 - the cause of the change, what I call the "trajectory conflict." This can be a particular conflict, a painful failure, an awakening, an accumulation of drops in a vase, another change, a mirror effect, etc. We will return to this extensively below. These conflicts are not necessarily created by the opposition between the protagonist's goal and obstacles—especially if it is another character than the protagonist who evolves. These are elements that attack one of the character traits of the trajectory protagonist;

3 - the moment when the character shifts, which I call the "trajectory climax." This moment is all the more effective if the character faces a choice (and even a dilemma), if we feel they take responsibility for their transformation, instead of being the plaything of external forces. We have just seen that the trajectory climax could be placed very early, in the first act, and even be confused with the triggering incident. Most often, however, it is found at the end of the story. It can then be before the (dramatic) climax, at the same time as the climax, or after the climax. Some theorists claim that the trajectory climax must be before the dramatic climax because it is the personal transformation of the protagonist that allows them to achieve their dramatic goal. This is true in Star Wars (1977). It is because Luke (Mark Hamill) accepts to trust the Force—and thus renounces rationality and technology—that he achieves his goal. But The Apartment, A Doll's House, and A Distant Neighborhood—excuse the little!—prove the fragility of this dogma. In these works, it is not because the protagonist changes that they can succeed, it is because they fail that they are led to change (cf. the analyses of these three works below). Another case, illustrated by Casablanca and Rain Man: because the protagonist changes, they renounce their goal. In short, on this subject as on so many others, let's remain open and flexible. All scenarios can work, and the right measure is always your intention;

4 - finally, a scene showing the new character trait. This fourth phase should not be neglected. Seeing the trajectory protagonist with their new humanity is always a joy for the receiver. It deserves to be assumed and clear.

The two most delicate phases are phases 2 and 3. It is not very complicated to imagine an evolution and show two opposing character traits (1 and 4). However, justifying a profound personality change and clearly showing the moment when this change occurs are two much more delicate matters. I find, moreover, that they are often handled lightly in many works. I see trajectory conflicts that are too weak or unconvincing—Lethal Weapon and Good Will Hunting are "remarkable" in this regard. I see trajectory climaxes where authors let the spectator guess what is happening in the character's mind. Presenting phases 1, 2, and 4 does not compensate for the absence of phase 3. You need all four phases, and they must all be clear and convincing.

Analyses of works from the perspective of internal trajectory

Let's examine various changes in the repertoire. The examples proposed below offer a range of transformations and means to achieve them. I think they are inspiring, including by their possible shortcomings.

(N.B. Attention, once again, I reveal important dramatic knots of the works in question. If you don't know them, I invite you to see them before reading what follows.)

OEDIPUS REX (c. 430 BC) and OEDIPUS AT COLONUS (406 BC)

The protagonist is Oedipus. He is also the character who changes. Oedipus has several characteristics. He is skillful, courageous, determined, compassionate, responsible. But the two character traits that interest us in this chapter are his blindness and irritability. I see three different forms of blindness in Oedipus. Before the scene with Tiresias, at the beginning of the second act of Oedipus Rex, Oedipus is consciously and unconsciously unaware of who Laius's murderer is. For spectators who know his legend from the beginning of the play, Oedipus is therefore the victim of a great dramatic irony. But after Tiresias, blindness no longer has the same meaning. Because the seer has given the protagonist the solution. It is certainly unbearable, which explains why Oedipus refuses to face it, but he has the answer within him. One can say that from the scene with Tiresias, the external obstacle that was Oedipus's ignorance has become internal. Finally, the third form of blindness in Oedipus's journey begins when he blinds himself (third act of Oedipus Rex).

Oedipus's irritability is first expressed with Creon, when he accuses his uncle/brother-in-law of plotting against him. Then the story, by Oedipus himself, of his confrontation with Laius shows that he easily loses his temper. Later still, Oedipus threatens the old shepherd several times with death. Oedipus clearly has a hot temper. At the climax, the great dramatic irony of the play is resolved, Oedipus's psychological blindness ceases. It is replaced by physiological blindness. Does Oedipus's impetuosity cease for all that? Not really. Oedipus is still hot-headed. The fact that he blinds himself is quite eloquent in this regard. In short, at the end of Oedipus Rex, the protagonist has gained in lucidity but is still irascible. It is Oedipus at Colonus that shows a change has occurred. Oedipus is much less angry there. "The sufferings and long days I have lived," he declares, "teach me not to be demanding." Certainly, he still has character, but he is a bit more humble. And even, at times, plaintive. N.B. By focusing on the old blind and exiled Oedipus, Oedipus at Colonus can be considered a sequel to Oedipus Rex. However, the two plays, written about twenty years apart, were not part of the same trilogy.

Conclusion. I wrote earlier that the mere resolution of a dramatic irony does not necessarily induce a personality change. Certainly, the ex-victim advances in knowledge, enriches themselves, but they can remain the same. If Oedipus Rex constitutes a valid exception, it is because the dramatic irony concerns nothing less than the intimate identity of the trajectory protagonist. One can remain the same when discovering they are deceived or have a hidden child. Or even that they have an unresolved dramatic need. However, one can hardly remain the same when discovering they have killed their father and married their mother. One would waver at less! Indeed, before the obligatory scene, Oedipus was reasonably content with himself. At the climax, he is consumed by shame. He has clearly changed. The trajectory conflict is essentially constituted by the discovery of the atrocious truth. And the trajectory climax coincides with the obligatory scene.

It should be noted that Tiresias and Creon play the role of reasonable contradictors. "Characters like yours are scourges to themselves," says Creon. "You reproach me for my furious stubbornness," says Tiresias, "while you cannot see the one who lodges with you." In a single sentence, Tiresias has just highlighted both Oedipus's impetuosity and his blindness.

THE THOUSAND AND ONE NIGHTS (13th century)

There are several versions of this major traditional ensemble. But the principle that interests us here is the same in all translations. The character who changes in the external story is Shahryar—while Scheherazade is the dramatic protagonist. In truth, Shahryar changes twice. At the beginning of the tale, the sultan has ruled his country justly for twenty years. Then he experiences two events in quick succession. First, he is betrayed by his wife. Then (in Joseph-Charles Mardrus's translation), he encounters a curious adolescent who advises him to beware of women. In Antoine Galland's translation, it is a lady who collects lovers. From that moment, every night, Shahryar brings a virgin, takes her virginity, and kills her in the morning. I am not exaggerating in saying that this is character-defining. After a while, Scheherazade, the eldest daughter of Shahryar's vizier, offers herself as a wife. She clearly states her goal: "I intend to stop the course of this barbarity that the sultan exercises on the families of this city." For a thousand and one nights, Scheherazade prevents Shahryar from killing her by telling him stories and taking care not to finish them when the morning light appears. Shahryar thus experiences a thousand and one nights of distraction, suspense, and frustration, a thousand and one more or less powerful hooks. In the end, Scheherazade presents the sultan with the three children they have had together and whom she has given birth to without his knowledge. Shahryar is moved to tears. He explains to Scheherazade's father that, thanks to the young woman, repentance has entered his heart. Not only does he renounce executing her, but when he explains what he has experienced to his brother, the latter abandons the same sexist barbarity and decides to marry Scheherazade's sister. Finally, the sultan has the stories told by his wife written and printed to edify future generations. Even today, some would do well to reread The Thousand and One Nights.

Conclusion. Phases 1 and 4 (before-after) are clear. The trajectory climax is, of course, the morning of the last night when Scheherazade has finished her tales and presents her children to the sultan. The trajectory conflict is a sum of elements. Shahryar experiences a long softening, punctuated by numerous mirror effects. For Scheherazade sometimes tells edifying stories about adultery, the power of women, and gender inequality. The complicated story of the sympathetic adulterer, for example, allows Shahryar to feel empathy for an adulterous sultana. In short, as Shahryar says, Scheherazade calms his heart while instructing his mind.

THE TAMING OF THE SHREW (1592)

The protagonist is Petruchio. The character who changes is Katharina. From her entrance, it is clear that Katharina does not hold her tongue. She refuses to let her father tell her what to do. At the beginning of Act II, she argues with her sister and ends up hitting her (a gesture indicated by Shakespeare). The trajectory conflict is interesting because Petruchio uses an original technique to tame Katharina. It consists of saying the opposite of what is. Petruchio explains it in a monologue (lines 169-180 of II/1): "If she complains, I'll say she sings like a nightingale. If she says nothing, I'll praise her eloquence..." In Ericksonian hypnosis, this technique aims to provoke confusion in the other and bypass the conscious blocks. Once married, Petruchio continues his reverse psychology. He starves his wife, prevents her from sleeping, and denies her the finest clothes, all in the name of his love and attention for her. Katharina is therefore thoroughly mistreated and in an extreme state of confusion. In scene IV/5, she finally submits to her husband's extravagances. Since he speaks of the Moon while pointing at the Sun, then it is indeed the Moon. Hortensio remarks to Petruchio that he has won. But Petruchio does not yet claim victory. Old Vincencio arrives. Petruchio describes him as a woman. Katharina does not contradict him.
opposition and joins in the game. Scene V/1 is the first moment that shows Katharina has changed. She agrees to kiss Petruchio in public and, for the first time, calls him "Love." In scene V/2, Hortensio's wife speaks ill of Petruchio. Katharina does not appreciate it at all. Then comes a bet. The winner will be the one whose wife proves the most docile. Against all odds, Katharina is the only wife to obey her husband. Finally, she delivers a speech whose scent of submission to the master-husband still sparks controversy today. Among other things, she declares that anger makes a woman lose her beauty and that she was wrong to be shrewish.

Conclusion. Phases 1 and 4 are evident. The trajectory conflict (described above) is original and effective. However, I find that the trajectory climax is not impactful enough. When Katharina agrees to go along with her husband (IV/5), her motivations are unclear. Does she do it, as Hortensio suggests, to avoid prolonging their journey? Similarly, when she agrees to kiss Petruchio in public, it's under the threat that they will immediately return to Verona and Katharina won't see if her sister marries the one she loves. In their 1967 adaptation, Franco Zeffirelli, Suso Cecchi d'Amico, and Paul Dehn show Katharina (Elizabeth Taylor) kissing Petruchio (Richard Burton) on the nose and not on the mouth! It's even worse than in the play. One is tempted to think that Katharina has still not yielded and even that she is preparing a trick for her husband.

BEAUTY AND THE BEAST (1757)
There are many versions of this tale. I analyze here the one by Jeanne-Marie Leprince de Beaumont. The protagonist is Beauty. The character who changes is the Beast. Beauty does not change; she is generous and honest from beginning to end. Her two sisters are mean and envious and remain so, even transformed into statues. The Beast has three characteristics: it is terribly ugly, kind, and not very intelligent. Throughout the time it hosts Beauty in its castle, the Beast asks Beauty to marry it, but she refuses. Beauty returns to her father. Seeing her sisters' husbands, she understands that beauty and intelligence are nothing compared to kindness. Moreover, she misses the Beast. She returns to the castle to find the Beast dying. Not seeing Beauty return, the Beast had decided to stop eating. Beauty then shows her love and finally agrees to marry it, which transforms the Beast into a magnificent prince, the one it was before being cursed.

Conclusion. All phases are present in the tale. Phase 3 (the trajectory conflict) deserves attention. Beauty's repeated refusals to marry the Beast generate frustration for the Beast, of course, like any obstacle, but they are also a source of humiliation and suffering. These are powerful "You are worth nothing" messages that the Beast receives for three months and endures without flinching. It is interesting that this tale does not rely on magical power to justify the change (as in Riquet with the Tuft). The Beast first undergoes a painful ordeal before transforming.

I wrote earlier that Beauty does not change. It's more subtle than that. Certainly, on a realistic level, she has the same personality, that of a compassionate young woman, from beginning to end. But let's not forget that we are dealing with a fairy tale. Mme Leprince de Beaumont's version symbolically describes how a child successfully navigates their Oedipal period, moving from love for a father to love for a man. From this perspective, there is no doubt that Beauty grows. She was childish, she becomes mature.
first. We don't want Rick to succeed in staying neutral; we prefer that he break through his shell and reconnect with his feelings.

THE APARTMENT (1960)

The character who changes is the protagonist: C.C. Baxter (Jack Lemmon). At the beginning, it's clear that he lets people walk all over him. One might also think he's an opportunist since he accepts being exploited with the specific goal of climbing the corporate ladder. That said, there might be other ways to advance in a company than by being mistreated like that. Baxter is the type who doesn't know how to say no. He is also incredibly kind. In the second half, when he could take advantage of the disastrous image Sheldrake (Fred MacMurray) has with Fran (Shirley MacLaine), he does everything to excuse Sheldrake and get him back with the young woman. Baxter is almost a pushover. Fortunately, his kindness doesn't go unnoticed by Fran. The worst blow comes to Baxter at the climax. He goes to see Sheldrake to tell him he has won Fran's heart and is leaving with her, and Sheldrake proves the exact opposite. At that moment, Baxter abandons his dramatic goal (to win Fran). We enter the third act. Only then, for the first time in the story, does Baxter say stop. He stands up as a man and tells his boss off.

Conclusion. Phases 1 and 4 are evident. The trajectory climax is the immediate consequence of the dramatic climax. The trajectory conflict is also linked to the dramatic conflict. Baxter is humiliated for much of the story. And, moreover, in a sensitive area: romantic feelings. In this regard, the scene where Baxter realizes that the woman he loves is sleeping with another man in his own apartment is a terrible blow. At the trajectory climax, the idea of continuing to lend his apartment to house Fran and Sheldrake's romance is unbearable to Baxter. However, he makes a choice. After all, even if he has lost love, he could keep the deputy director position that cost him so much. Besides, Diamond and Wilder make us believe for a moment that this is the option Baxter chooses. A false lead. In truth, the cup has overflowed. No one will wipe their feet on Baxter anymore.

There is a moral conscience in the film. It is embodied by Dr. Dreyfuss (Jack Kruschen). The great originality of this moral conscience is that it is mistaken about Baxter. Dreyfuss believes his neighbor is a complete playboy when he is not. And yet, when Dreyfuss suggests Baxter be a "mensch," paradoxically, we agree with the doctor. Certainly, Baxter doesn't need to learn to be a "mensch" by treating women correctly and abstaining, but he must learn to be a "mensch" by saying stop to those who abuse him. Logically, Baxter can't hear Dreyfuss since he knows Dreyfuss is mistaken about him. Dr. Dreyfuss may well be, in the entire repertoire, the only example of a reasonable contradictor who is wrong while being right.

ASTERIX AND THE NORMANS (1966)

Asterix and Obelix are the protagonists. The characters who change are Goudurix and the Normans. From his arrival in the village (on a flashy chariot), Goudurix considers he has landed among bumpkins and makes some disdainful remarks. When he sees a drakkar approaching the coast, he is seized with terror. From that moment, he becomes quieter, but the relaxed reaction of the Gauls confirms to him that he is among madmen. The Normans, on the other hand, do not know fear and are victims of great dramatic irony: they believe that fear gives the power to fly. They have therefore decided to visit a land where fear is known to acquire its secret. When Asterix learns the reason for their presence on Gaulish soil, he has the idea to use Assurancetourix, whose singing annoys even cattle. The tactic works. The Normans are so traumatized by the bard's first songs that they dread hearing more. Their study trip is a success; they now know what fear is. They even become a bit fearful. Moreover, they realize that fear does not give wings.

Conclusion. Like many series, Asterix has two good reasons not to offer a trajectory protagonist: it's pure comedy, and it's a series, like a sitcom, where the main characters must remain the same from one episode to another. Asterix and the Normans is thus a remarkable exception. Even though, of course, it is secondary characters, unique to this episode, who transform. The biggest change here is made by the Normans. Their trajectory conflict is original and convincing. For eight albums, Goscinny has shown us how traumatizing Assurancetourix can be. But very cleverly, Goscinny doesn't just rely on our possible knowledge of the series; he clearly prepares, at the beginning of this ninth adventure, the fact that the bard sings poorly. Goudurix also evolves. He starts snobbish and seems more humble at the end. The trajectory conflict in this evolution is extreme terror and the company of bumpkins who stand up to the Normans. He also goes from cowardly to courageous, but it's much more artificial. On page 39, he is chattering his teeth. A few panels later, he rejoices at seeing Asterix attack the Normans and tries to join the fight. He ends the album repeating, "Let them come!" But it didn't take much for him to become daring. It's easy to be brave when you're on the side of the strongest. And if his newfound courage was just another bluff? We would need to see Goudurix in Lutetia to know if he has truly evolved in this regard.

ONE FLEW OVER THE CUCKOO'S NEST (1975)

The character who changes is Chief (Will Sampson), while the protagonist is McMurphy (Jack Nicholson). Chief is a discreet patient, whose restraint is largely due to being deaf and mute. But McMurphy notices him immediately because of his size. Chief is a giant. McMurphy then begins to introduce Chief to basketball. Chief eventually enjoys it. At 34 minutes into the film, McMurphy explains to the patients how he plans to escape to watch his baseball game: by tearing out a hydrotherapy console and smashing a window with it. Chief listens attentively and then sees McMurphy fail. Chief is also present when McMurphy gets excited in front of a blank TV screen (cf. pages 342-343). Later, a fight breaks out between McMurphy and an orderly (Nathan George). Chief intervenes. At 80 minutes, in the hallway where Chief and McMurphy await electroshock therapy, McMurphy discovers that Chief is neither deaf nor mute. After the shock and excitement, McMurphy proposes to Chief that they escape. Chief mentions Canada. At 88 minutes, McMurphy tells Chief he can't take it anymore and must escape. Chief replies that he can't follow him. "You're much bigger than me," he tells McMurphy. Chief recounts that his father was big, but the institution broke him, just as it tries to break McMurphy. At 120 minutes, after several violent moments, Chief discovers that McMurphy has been lobotomized. He then tells him that he is ready to escape and that they will leave together. Chief kills McMurphy, smashes a window with the hydrotherapy console, and escapes.

Conclusion. The four phases are present in the film. Chief goes from submissive to autonomous. The twist at the 80th minute proves that Chief has long had a rebellious streak and dreams of leaving, but until now, he hasn't felt up to it. The trajectory conflict consists of several elements: McMurphy's friendship, the mirror effect McMurphy provokes, and the accumulation of violence triggered by the troublemaker's arrival in this balanced arena that is the psychiatric hospital. The trajectory climax is Chief's discovery that McMurphy has been lobotomized. This creates a salutary shock in him, leading him to "free" McMurphy and then free himself.

TOOTSIE (1982)

I read everywhere that Michael Dorsey (Dustin Hoffman) is a macho and that his trajectory is to learn to respect women. There must be two 1982 films titled Tootsie. In the one I saw, could someone explain in which scene(s) Michael is clearly presented as a macho who abuses women? At the beginning of the film, a friend of his, Sandy (Teri Garr), publicly says that Michael is a great guy. A few minutes later, during his birthday party, Michael gently flirts with two guests and seems to be sweet-talking a third. Not very subtle, sure, but not enough to call Simone de Beauvoir to the rescue. At 21 minutes into the film, Michael, disguised as Dorothy, calls the director (Dabney Coleman) a macho, not without reason. At 33 minutes, entering the women's dressing room, disguised as Dorothy, Michael is embarrassed to find April (Geena Davis) scantily clad. It's clear he's not in his Dorothy character and reacts as Michael. If he were truly macho, I'd see him taking the opportunity to ogle. Or he could brush against April's bra while pretending to be interested in the quality of the underwires. Instead, he's embarrassed. At 39 minutes, Michael explains to Jeff (Bill Murray) that he doesn't understand why Julie (Jessica Lange) is dating the director. Because it's clear he doesn't treat her right. Could Michael be a macho who finds another macho shocking? A subtle macho, in a way. Later, when Dorothy tells the director that he understands his machismo better than he thinks, is that proof that Michael was as abusive a macho as the director? Moreover, we must not forget that a good part of Michael's reactions and motivations are linked to the film's major dramatic irony. When he undresses to try on Sandy's dress and she catches him in his underwear, he prefers to make her believe he desires her rather than confess what he wanted to do. And if this fling has no future, it's more due to Sandy than to Michael.

In short, sorry, but I see Michael as a rather sensitive guy, not wildly feminist, of course, but not macho either. As a drama teacher, he inspires confidence in me, but I certainly wouldn't enroll in his phallocracy classes. In the third act, Michael tells Julie that he was a better man with her, disguised as a woman, than he had ever been with another woman as a man. The phrase is nice, except that it's said and not shown. Moreover, even if it's true, it's not enough for there to be a phase 4 in a story to retrospectively create a phase 1. Just as it's not enough to obsessively defend the internal trajectory to see it where there is little. If the authors of Tootsie wanted to show the transformation of a macho condemned to disguise himself as a woman to find work and understand in his flesh how painful sexism can be (a very beautiful subject), they should have characterized Michael as they characterized the director. In fact, if you want to see a macho in Tootsie, a real one, you should turn to him. Or to the old actor (George Gaynes), nicknamed "The Tongue," who almost assaults Dorothy towards the end. But maybe Hollywood didn't want such a radical protagonist.

One can also consider that the interest of the story is precisely to take an ordinary man, vaguely flirtatious but not excessively, superficial in his relationship with women (due to clumsiness, loneliness, alcohol abuse, or mild ideology), and make him understand that even when you respect women, it's more comfortable to be in a man's skin than in a woman's. I hear this argument. Even if, again, it doesn't seem to me that Michael is characterized at the start as someone who has no empathy for the female condition. He would have had to be more macho than that at the beginning, a light macho. Or he would have had to be very superficial in his relationships with women, but not just on the night he celebrates his birthday. Or he would have had to see no difference between how women and men are treated. For example, Sandy could have said to him: "Women are much more mistreated than men in our profession." And he would have replied: "What are you talking about? As long as you have talent..." And by disguising himself as a woman, he would have understood that indeed, actresses are not treated the same way as actors. But as it stands, I'm sorry, we don't have any of that. I don't see a remarkable internal trajectory in Tootsie, and while the film has many qualities, I don't find it exemplary on the subject that interests us here.

BACK TO THE FUTURE (1985)

The character who changes is George McFly (Crispin Glover), while the protagonist is Marty (Michael J. Fox), his son. At the beginning of the story, set in 1985, George is clearly characterized as a weakling who lets himself be bullied by his superior, Biff (Thomas F. Wilson). Unlike many trajectory protagonists, he acknowledges he has a trajectory flaw but finds lame excuses. When Marty teleports to 1955, he encounters the same George (30 years younger), already bullied by the same Biff (also 30 years younger). To convince George to talk to Lorraine (Lea Thompson), Marty's future mother, Marty scares George. But George fails, despite Marty's coaching. Marty then presents a plan to George: on the night of the dance, Marty will act too forward with Lorraine, George will intervene to defend her, and pretend to beat up Marty, which will cast George in a heroic light. George agrees to participate in this setup. When the time comes, he intervenes as planned but encounters Biff instead of Marty. He eventually finds the courage to hit Biff and save Lorraine. Then, during the dance, George asserts himself with Lorraine and kisses her. When Marty returns to 1985, the present has been altered. George is no longer cowardly, and Biff is at his service.

Conclusion. Phases 1 and 4 are evident. George is clearly cowardly at the beginning, clearly transformed at the end. The trajectory climax is easy to spot; it's the moment when George finds the courage to hit Biff. It's on the trajectory conflict that one might nitpick. What causes George's transformation? His love for Lorraine? Marty's coaching? The fact that George was ready to hit Marty (for show) and can't back down from Biff? Probably a combination of all these factors. But it must be acknowledged that his change is a bit easy. Note that Bob Gale and Robert Zemeckis at least have the honesty to justify George's change other than by the alteration of the future operated by Marty and Doc (Christopher Lloyd): George has already changed in 1955 (certainly with Marty's help).

RAIN MAN (1988)

The character who changes is the protagonist: Charlie (Tom Cruise). Charlie sells luxury cars, but business hasn't been going well lately. When he learns of his father's death - the inciting incident - he shows no emotion, much to the surprise of his girlfriend Susanna (Valeria Golino). Charlie then explains. He and his father had been estranged for years, and his father was quite odious when Charlie was a teenager. For much of the second act, Charlie finds it difficult to tolerate the company of his autistic older brother Raymond (Dustin Hoffman), whom he has kidnapped to obtain half of the inheritance. A significant turning point occurs at 70 minutes into the film. Thanks to a photo, Charlie discovers that he spent the first few years of his life with Raymond - whom Charlie then called Rain Man - before Raymond was institutionalized. Their father feared Raymond might harm little Charlie. From that moment, Charlie stops seeing his brother as a burden. But he is still interested in the inheritance. A second turning point occurs when, financially cornered, Charlie has the idea to exploit Raymond's gift to win money at the casino. It works magnificently. Relieved, Charlie begins to find qualities in his brother. He teaches him to dance and even expresses the desire to hold him. The dramatic climax goes almost unnoticed and would have deserved more emphasis. It must be said that the authors unfortunately neglected the dramatic question throughout the film and focused mainly on the trajectory question. As a result, there is not enough conflict in the second half. At the climax, in a scene inexplicably poorly filmed, the estate administrator (Jerry Molen) explains that he has full power over the inheritance distribution and is not legally obliged to give anything to Charlie. Charlie thus understands that he will not achieve his goal. However, the administrator offers Charlie a $250,000 check if he renounces seeking custody of Raymond. Charlie refuses the check. The next scene is an interview with a psychiatric expert (Barry Levinson) to decide Raymond's fate. Charlie declares that he has managed to bond with his brother and wants to continue taking care of him. An emotional image shows them side by side, head to head.

Conclusion. Phase 1 could have been more pronounced. At the beginning of the story, we see that Charlie is venal and not inclined to compassion, but we can find excuses for him. Note that Susanna serves somewhat as a moral conscience without it being emphasized. Phase 4 is clear. At the end, Charlie doesn't care about the money and expresses affection. The originality of Rain Man lies in the fact that Charlie changes in two personality traits, not just one. He could very well become compassionate while continuing to be interested in money. He could very well no longer be venal without enjoying the relationship he has with his autistic brother. On this subject of double change, the phases 2 deserve attention. The change in compassion is due to a laborious taming, which is a classic cause. However, the change in venality is precisely due to the first change. It's because Charlie has become fond of his brother that he is no longer interested in money. In other words, the first change (from cold to compassionate) serves as a trajectory conflict for the second change (from venal to disinterested).

If there are two changes, there must logically be two trajectory climaxes. The scene with the $250,000 check is clearly one. It concerns venality. However, the trajectory climax related to the lack of compassion does not exist. It could be the scene where Charlie discovers the photo of Raymond and him when he was three years old if Charlie radically changed towards Raymond at that moment. But that's not really the case. This scene only initiates Charlie's softening. On the trajectory of compassion, we are therefore dealing with a gradual change. This aligns with the idea of taming but makes the change difficult to appreciate.

GROUNDHOG DAY (1993)

The character who changes is the protagonist: Phil Connors (Bill Murray). At the beginning, Phil is clearly characterized as arrogant and jaded. Rita (Andie MacDowell) and Larry (Chris Elliott), the cameraman, serve as moral consciences, but it's clear that their perspective doesn't matter to Phil. At 33 minutes into the film, Phil realizes he can break the law without consequences. He takes advantage of it. He gorges himself, flirts, steals money, punches Ned (Stephen Tobolowsky), and begins to court Rita by manipulating her. But at 55 minutes, he gets a flurry of slaps. Rita has realized that Phil is not genuine. Phil then enters a period of despair. He tries to kill the groundhog and then to commit suicide in a thousand and one ways. In vain. At 64 minutes, Phil must face the fact: he is immortal, like a god. At 68 minutes, we understand that Phil is in love with Rita. Moreover, he acknowledges that he is a jerk. He no longer needs a reasonable contradictor. Rita tells him that what is happening to him may not be a curse, that there may be another way to see things. In the next scene, at 72 minutes, Phil acts like a saint. He gives all his cash to the homeless man (Les Podewell), brings coffee to Larry, saves a child (Shaun Chaiyabhac), etc.

Conclusion. Phases 1 and 4 are evident. The trajectory climax is the series of vain suicides. From there, Phil is no longer arrogant. However, he hasn't become helpful and genuine. He is mostly lost. He feels both immortal and totally powerless. It's Rita's reflection that will show him the path to redemption. The trajectory conflict is the immense frustration generated by the accumulation of failures. Romantic failures that culminate with the flurry of slaps. And failures in the face of the desire for death, which also conclude with a montage sequence. This trajectory conflict is particularly powerful. Already, it's no small thing to get rejected or not be able to die. But, in addition, these failures
reconnu qu'il avait besoin d'aide et a accepté de se laisser porter par sa famille. Dwayne a retrouvé la parole et a compris qu'il pouvait compter sur les autres. Olive, elle, a appris qu'elle pouvait être elle-même sans craindre le jugement des autres. Le climax de Little Miss Sunshine est un moment de libération collective où chaque membre de la famille accepte ses failles et ses forces. La danse finale symbolise cette acceptation et cette solidarité retrouvée. C'est un moment de joie et de réconciliation qui, bien que bref, laisse entrevoir un avenir plus lumineux pour chacun d'eux.
looks better, but is he really safe from another romantic failure, another depression, and another suicide attempt? Shortly before the climax, he sees an ad in a newspaper about a rival colleague, called the "No. 1 specialist on Marcel Proust," while Frank claims this title. He closes the newspaper without clearly telling us if it affects him or if he doesn't care. It's a shame. This moment was a great opportunity to show us if he has changed.

In fact, if we absolutely want to find a trajectory protagonist in Little Miss Sunshine, we must consider the entire family. They start dysfunctional and end a bit less dysfunctional. At the beginning of the film, each character is in their own neurosis. By the end, they have somewhat come out of their bubble and have united. The trajectory conflicts on this transformation are quite lovely. Several elements force the Hoovers to stick together, starting with the van they have to push to start. Their complicity is also expressed in the removal of the grandfather's body. Finally, when they all go on stage, they are more united than ever. But even on this trajectory, I still find that the authors cut too early. I would have liked to see, in the third act, some signs that this newfound family solidarity will last and wasn't just circumstantial.

THE LIVES OF OTHERS (2006)

The protagonist is Georg Dreyman (Sebastian Koch) while the character who changes is Captain Wiesler (Ulrich Mühe). In the first scene, Wiesler conducts an interrogation. In the second, he gives a lecture at the Stasi university. His ruthlessness and lack of humanity are evident. When a student finds the interrogation inhumane, Wiesler discreetly marks a small cross next to the student's name. At the theater, Wiesler suspects the playwright Dreyman of being too polite to be honest and suggests he be monitored. Surveillance begins. But Wiesler quickly understands that it was ordered by Minister Hempf (Thomas Thieme) for personal, not political, reasons. Wiesler lives alone in a grim building. He calls on a prostitute (Gabi Fleming) who cannot stay after the act because she has strict hours. You can sense that Wiesler would like a bit of human warmth. Moreover, he reads "Memory of Marie A.," a romantic poem by Bertolt Brecht. Dreyman learns that his director friend (Volkmar Kleinert) has committed suicide. He plays a piece that this friend had given him: "The Sonata of the Good Man." Listening to it, Wiesler sheds a big tear. Dreyman then quotes Lenin: "If I listen to Beethoven's Appassionata, I won't finish the revolution." Then Dreyman comments: "Can someone who listens to this music, who really listens to it, be a bad man?" At 53 minutes into the film, in an elevator, Wiesler meets a child who tells him that, according to his father, the Stasi is made up of bad people who imprison others. Wiesler goes to ask the child his father's name but refrains. Later, Wiesler meets actress Christa-Maria Sieland (Martina Gedeck) in a café, reassures her about her talent, and invites her to be authentic. He then learns that, thanks to him, Sieland has decided not to lose herself and has returned to her partner. At 72 minutes, Wiesler decides not to denounce Dreyman and notes "Nothing to Report" in his report.

Conclusion. Phases 1 and 4 are clearly present. At the beginning, Wiesler is sinister and dangerous. He sends chills down your spine. When he refrains from arresting the child's father and starts falsifying reports, he has clearly become more human. The trajectory climax is the scene where Wiesler listens to music so moving that it brings him to tears. What troubles me in The Lives of Others is the trajectory conflict. Or, if you prefer, the cause of the change. That Wiesler disapproves of his minister's attitude is not enough. Hempf cannot be the first to show corruption. Henckel von Donnersmarck wants us to believe that a humanist sonata is enough to transform someone (or slow down a revolution). Is it really sufficient? Music soothes the soul, certainly, but perhaps not to that extent. In Manhattan Murder Mystery, Larry (Woody Allen) says that listening to Wagner makes him want to invade Poland.

Does Dreyman change? At the beginning, one of his friends, Hauser (Hans-Uwe Bauer), tells him he must take a stand if he is a man. Indeed, Dreyman doesn't make waves; he adapts to the East German dictatorship. When his director friend commits suicide, he decides to take action. Does he go from cowardly to courageous? If so, it's not spectacular. Dreyman would be cowardly if he didn't defend his disgraced friends. Yet, he does so from the start. It seems to me that Dreyman's trajectory, if there is one, is much lighter than Wiesler's.

DEPARTURES (2008)

The character who evolves is the protagonist: Daigo (Masahiro Motoki). For almost an hour, Daigo is ashamed of his new job as a mortician. To the point that he hides what he does from his wife, Mika (Ryôko Hirosue). At 51 minutes into the film, his boss (Tsutomu Yamazaki) delicately prepares a deceased person. Daigo observes him. In voice-over, we then hear him express all his admiration for this work. "All his gestures seemed sublime to me," he concludes. At 61 minutes, Daigo talks about his father: "If I see him again, I'll beat him up." At 82 minutes, Daigo recounts that his father left with his mistress, that he was the worst father, and that he doesn't even remember his face. At 104 minutes, we understand that Daigo and his father had exchanged a stone. Shortly after, Daigo learns that his father has just died but refuses to deal with it. His friendly secretary (Kimiko Yo) reveals that she too abandoned her 6-year-old son to leave with a lover. Daigo accuses her of being as irresponsible as his father but eventually agrees to see the deceased. At first, he doesn't recognize him. When he sees how funeral home employees botch the work, he decides to take care of it himself. In his father's hand, he then discovers the stone Daigo had given him as a child. The memory of his father's younger face returns to him.

Conclusion. Daigo evolves on two levels. First, he changes his mind about his job. But it's not a change of personality. Moreover, this change of position on the mortician's job is also experienced by two other characters later in the film, when they attend a delicately conducted ceremony by Daigo.

The other evolution of Daigo, more profound, concerns the slate he has with his father. This evolution is undeniable. Daigo had resentment towards his father. After the trajectory climax, that is, the moment when he prepares his father's body, Daigo is at peace. In my eyes, it's not a change of character. Daigo is still the same. But it's an evolution. The only element that seems missing in the film is what triggers the click for Daigo. The fact that Daigo learns to appreciate his job and helps others to grieve is an element that prepares him to grieve himself, not for his father's death but for the abandonment he suffered and hasn't yet digested. The secretary also serves as a mirror character. Despite all this, after the secretary's speech, Daigo is still angry. The author could have explained what finally decides him to see the deceased.

Teachings

You see that there are roughly two significant types of trajectory. The one where the trajectory protagonist modifies a trait of their personality. Shahryar (One Thousand and One Nights) goes from barbaric to human. Nora (A Doll's House) goes from childish to adult. Baxter (The Apartment) goes from cowardly to courageous. In this case, the trajectory protagonist grows because they improve, morally or psychologically. Or the opposite. They worsen, so they degrade. The second type of trajectory is where the trajectory protagonist cleans up their life. Oedipal (Oedipus Rex) understands who he is. Rick (Casablanca) gets rid of his ghost. Daigo (Departures) reconciles with his father. In this case, the trajectory protagonist starts by clearing something that encumbered them, which often leads, as a bonus, to a change in characterization. Oedipal gains humility. Rick gets involved for others. Daigo is less angry.

In both cases, we have seen that a serious ordeal is needed to provoke this change. In other words, you must conceive a powerful trajectory conflict and exploit it.

CREATION OF THE INTERNAL TRAJECTORY

Phases 1 and 4

In Bullets Over Broadway, David (John Cusack) is both the dramatic protagonist and the trajectory protagonist. He goes from pretentious to humble. Symbolically, the very first dialogue of the film is: "I am an artist!" And the penultimate dialogue: "I am not an artist." Of course, Woody Allen and Douglas McGrath show what these two dialogues summarize. To create an internal trajectory, you must first determine the starting state of the character who changes and their end state. Be careful, the character does not change completely, they do not become someone else. They transform on one character trait and one only. Or they clean up one aspect of their life and one only.

A spectrum

A character does not have to go from one thing to its opposite, especially if they start from an extreme position. From idealist, for example, they would become cynical. They can just go from idealist to realist. In other words, they can evolve without necessarily reaching the other end of the spectrum. I invite you to draw the spectrum of the character who transforms. This will help you choose the right starting position and the right ending position for your project. Some examples of spectrums:

Gullible-Credulous-Confident-Cautious-Skeptical-Suspicious Stingy-Thrifty-Economical-Unselfish-Generous-Extravagant Devout-Practicing-Non-practicing-Agnostic-Atheist-Impious Dimwitted-Stupid-Limited-Sensible-Intelligent-Gifted

Rebellious-Rebellious-Defiant-Composed-Flexible-Docile-Submissive Corrupt-Corrupt-Correct-Honest-Virtuous-Legalistic-Incorruptible Coward-Cowardly-Timid-Cautious-Courageous-Rash-Fearless

You see that making one of your characters go from dimwitted to gifted might seem a bit artificial. "Limited to intelligent" already seems more plausible. At the same time, the journey must be real, significant. "Thrifty to economical" is not a remarkable evolution. So it's a matter of balance. The criteria for finding the right path are therefore:

1 - once again, the meaning you want to give to your story;
2 - the strength of the change. It must be sufficiently marked but not excessive;
3 - the credibility of the change. Cf. the work on phase 2, below.

A lack at the start

You will also note that if the starting position is median (unselfish, agnostic, sensible, composed, modest, etc.), there is a good chance that the audience will not perceive your character's need to evolve. We must feel that your character has a flaw, a clear defect. More generally, the idea is to show that the trajectory protagonist is not a complete human being. Lisa (Grace Kelly in Rear Window) offers a subtle example. From afar, she seems perfect. But we understand that she has never strayed from Park Avenue. She is perfect on a magazine cover. She needs to step out of her comfort zone, to know how to take risks to be whole. Lisa's apparent perfection hides her incompleteness and deprives her of part of her own life.

Blindness as an additional flaw

The character who transforms often has less perspective than the audience on their condition. We talked about this in the introduction. This blindness can take two forms.

1. The character is not even aware of having a questionable character trait. Phil Connors (Bill Murray in Groundhog Day) thinks he is normal. It's not him who is cynical, it's the others who are hicks or naive.
2. The character is aware of being different but does not understand that it costs them. Rick (Humphrey Bogart) knows he is self-centered, that he doesn't get involved for anyone, but he doesn't see the point of changing. Katharina (The Taming of the Shrew) knows very well that she has a strong character. What she probably doesn't know is how much it would benefit her to soften.

N.B. Not all characters are blind. Some know they have a flaw, they even sometimes know that it would do them good to get rid of it but they haven't yet encountered the right opportunity to change. Or they don't have the courage, like George (Crispin Glover) in Back to the Future (cf. analysis above). Brody (Roy Scheider in Jaws) is also in this case with his aquaphobia - even if this issue is quite light in the film.

What triggers the change?

The transformation of a character cannot just be a pious wish. Once you have an idea of the path you want your character to take, you need to imagine what will concretely transform them. What brings a human being to change psychologically? What can be the trajectory conflict? I see seven major avenues.

1. The character benefits from a successful therapy, whether it's psychotherapy, an exorcism, or a shamanic experience. But this takes up space and significantly directs your story. That's why it's rare for a work of fiction to describe these processes. However, there are works that come close to the psychotherapeutic journey. I think of Oedipus Rex, of course, The Taming of the Shrew (cf. above) or My Father's Journal.
2. The character benefits from learning (Pygmalion, Miracle in Milan, The Empire Strikes Back, The King's Speech). If the learning is successful, it is logical that the student transforms. That said, not all learning necessarily provokes a change in personality. One can master a language or a craft, for example, and remain naive, unstable, or selfish.
3. The character experiences an extreme experience. A coma, a car accident, a kidnapping, a dehumanizing confinement, a war scene, a near-death experience. Or even a divine vision. When you hate Christians and meet Jesus on the road to Damascus, you have valid reasons to convert and go from the wicked Saul, stoner of Stephen, to the kind Paul, propagator of the Christian doctrine to polytheists. In One Thousand and One Nights, the discovery of his wife's adultery makes Shahryar go from good to cruel. In La Strada, Gelsomina (Giulietta Masina) goes mad after witnessing a murder. It is the tragic death of their respective child that pushes Capulet and Montague to reconcile (Romeo and Juliet). In The Dark Knight, Harvey Dent (Aaron Eckhart) goes from legalistic to criminal after the unjust death of the woman he loves. That said, he already had schizoid tendencies and was already nicknamed Two-Face by his fellow students. In the climax of Tartuffe, Orgon, hidden under a table, hears his darling Tartuffe, who was supposed to marry his daughter, making an assiduous court to his wife. This stinging slap cleans Orgon of his fanatical blindness.

But, beware, not every trauma, however intense, is a cause of transformation. In Maus, Vladek goes through the Holocaust and survives. One might think that having been a victim of racism and dehumanization made him tolerant. Well, no. Forty years later, Vladek discriminates against Blacks.

In the examples of The Apartment, A Doll's House, Distant Neighborhood, or Groundhog Day above, we see that the extreme experience can take the form of a crushing failure. In these works, the protagonist spends a lot of energy to achieve their goal. When they understand that it is in vain and abandon their goal, they are ripe for transformation. The definitive failure acts as a trauma. I see in this case proof that conflict is not necessarily a source of unhappiness and destruction. It can push an individual to let go and open up to new mental structures.
4. The character experiences a catharsis or an epiphany. The catharsis is a hard, emotional moment that cleanses the character of what was burdening them (a ghost, a past trauma, a childhood wound). Casablanca offers a beautiful example. The epiphany can be created by a phrase, an image, a smell, a sound, which allows the scattered elements of a character's life to suddenly make sense. As if the pieces of a puzzle suddenly assembled to reveal the image. In Ratatouille, it's a magnificent elastic, like Proust's madeleine, that triggers Anton Ego's transformation.
5. The character faces a symbolic mirror. Either a mirror character or a mirror situation. One Thousand and One Nights and Distant Neighborhood use this process abundantly. It is also found in One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, in the sense that McMurphy serves as a model, an inspiration, to Chief. As mentioned above, Jean Valjean's transformation occurs at the beginning of Les Misérables. In the chapter titled "Petit-Gervais," Victor Hugo explains that the bishop served as a mirror character for the protagonist: "The priest's forgiveness was the greatest assault and the most formidable attack."
6. Another technique, close to the mirror effect, is to label (generally positively) the character who must change. That is, a character reflects an image to the trajectory protagonist that does not necessarily correspond to them but can push them to evolve. In One Thousand and One Nights, Scheherazade calls Shahryar a magnanimous king endowed with good manners. In The Taming of the Shrew, Petruchio speaks highly of Katharina, even when she is unbearable. In Schindler's List, Lôwenstein and later Regina Perlman come to tell Schindler that he is good.
7. The character endures conflict after conflict or obstacle after obstacle, which often generates an accumulation of frustrations. The moment their cup overflows can lead them to let go and change.

You will note that all the processes described here are as valid in life as in fiction. Labeling (6), for example, is a classic. It is very effective, although unfortunately, it is often negative and therefore counterproductive. If you want the other to change, you must attribute a positive label to them, even if it does not correspond to what they truly are.

All these processes can be combined, of course. Be careful, some require preparation work. If there is a ghost, for example, we must know it before the trajectory protagonist purges it.

The general idea of the trajectory conflict is that evolution is done in pain, certainly not in ease. You must make your trajectory protagonist suffer, push them to their limits, twist the knife in their wound. Even if they benefit from a final circumstance that makes them change, it is preferable that they have suffered well before. Magical conversions do not work in fiction.

Sudden or progressive?

I have never been convinced by progressive changes. It may work in life but, in my opinion, not in fiction. Even in the learning cases mentioned above, the student fails, resists, makes mistakes, until finally mastering what they are supposed to learn. The same goes for taming stories. Most often, the characters oppose each other until the end, until finally falling into each other's arms. Rain Man, analyzed above, is an exception, which I do not find, by the way, completely successful. Moreover, Rain Man can afford it because it has two changes, the second not being progressive.

Not only does it seem more credible to me that the change occurs at a specific moment but, in addition, a progressive change, if well arranged, risks being invisible - since the evolution is done in small touches. By contrast, a sudden change has the advantage of being clear. Of course, this does not exclude preparation elements. Just because a change occurs at a specific moment does not mean it should not be justified beforehand. This is one of the major functions of the trajectory conflict, which acts on the trajectory protagonist long before they transform.

I see an exception to the principle of sudden change: it is an Italian comedy from 1971 titled The Witness. Let's see why the progressive change is justified there. The Witness tells the story of a prudish woman, Isolina (Monica Vitti), who gets to know a pimp, Marino (Ugo Tognazzi), in a legal case. After causing his incarceration, she visits Marino in prison and gets closer to him. Isolina and Marino soon marry. Then begins, on Marino's part, a subtle conditioning. Little by little, he leads his wife to loosen up, and eventually to prostitute herself. He goes gently, asking her for more and more at each step. This "learning" is accompanied, of course, by dramatic irony. Because, unlike Isolina, we understand what Marino is doing. As you can see, in The Witness, the progressive change is justified. It is obvious that Isolina's transformation, from chaste woman to woman of easy virtue, could not happen abruptly. If Marino had asked Isolina to prostitute herself bluntly, she would have categorically refused. And if he had insisted and insisted and insisted hoping she would eventually crack, I doubt it would have worked better.

It is no coincidence that I mention dramatic irony in the example of The Witness. Because progressive changes have an even more serious disadvantage than the clarity problem mentioned above. When you create a progressive change and make sure that this progression is perceived by the audience, you announce the end. Suppose you start a story with a violent character. After a few scenes, they are only agitated. A few more scenes and they become merely emotional. Then relatively composed. Then calm. You see that the audience has understood that your character will end up gentle or affectionate. In other words, progressive change is only acceptable if your character...
will end badly, because then you create a crescendo. You announce, certainly, but you announce a dark ending. Whereas if the viewer anticipates a change for the better, you deflate the suspense linked to the internal trajectory. You turn the announcement into a phone call, which is never a good idea. If Super Witness works, it's both because it's the only credible transformation and because Isolina ends badly.

Physical vs. psychological transformation. A progressive physiological transformation seems more credible or effective to me than a progressive psychological transformation. That said, physical transformations can also be sudden. We saw this with Beauty and the Beast earlier. At the very beginning of The Metamorphosis, Gregor wakes up transformed into an insect. The change happened overnight. In The Fly, André (David Hedison) ends up with a giant fly head right after his teleportation. In contrast, in the 1986 remake (The Fly), Seth's (Jeff Goldblum) physical transformation is progressive. After his teleportation, he just has a few coarse hairs on his back and extraordinary athletic abilities. Gradually, he metamorphoses into a monster. But it's interesting to note that Seth's psychological transformation is not progressive. From the moment of teleportation, Seth is no longer the same; he becomes wild, mystical, and irritable. A little later, a major dramatic irony is resolved. Seth understands what we have known for twenty-five minutes, namely that he has fused with a fly. He immediately becomes attentive again, less wild. He asks Veronica (Geena Davis) for help.

At the beginning of The Picture of Dorian Gray, Dorian listens favorably to the cynical Lord Henry, but Lord Henry does not change Dorian because he is already ready to sell his soul. He thus wishes that his portrait ages in his place. Then he embarks on a life of debauchery, while the painting begins to show the stigmas of Dorian's vices. The portrait evolves progressively, but not Dorian. He is, however, torn. He sometimes regrets having such a dark soul. That doesn't stop him from continuing to do evil. (Note: Warning, I reveal the ending.) Dorian eventually understands that his guilt and his attempts at repentance were mostly motivated by vanity. He stabs the portrait, which has the effect of returning the painting to its original state and physically transforming Dorian, who goes from a handsome young man to a hideous corpse. In Oscar Wilde's novel, Dorian does not transform psychologically. And he only transforms physically and brutally at the end, after living twenty years without aging. The authors of the most famous film adaptation, the one from 1945, introduced a semblance of redemption into the story. Dorian (Hurd Hatfield) gives up marrying Gladys (Donna Reed) to avoid harming her. Gladys, whom he knew as a child and who is probably the only person he cares about. He notes that this renunciation slightly and favorably alters his hideous portrait. He seems determined to pursue the path of redemption. But in wanting to kill the portrait, he hastens his end.

Physical metamorphoses can thus serve as metaphors to signify psychological evolution, but not necessarily. They can also serve as curses, like Gregor's in The Metamorphosis, or as rewards, like the transformation of Pygmalion's ivory statue into a young woman. They can also serve as trials, like Midas's ability to turn everything he touches into gold. In The Metamorphoses, Ovid tells that Tiresias was transformed into a woman for seven years before regaining his male body. This allowed him to understand the differences between the two sexes from the inside, but it is not said that it made him grow. Then Hera made him blind, and Zeus gave him the gift of divination. Three major metamorphoses, undoubtedly. Did they alter the character's characterization? Ovid does not indicate.

Character narrative and internal trajectory

The notion of internal trajectory is more important if you are writing a character-driven story or a story mixing plot and character than if you are writing a pure plot-driven story (cf. page 75). Very schematically, the work of the author of a plot-driven story consists of placing obstacles on the path to the goal and sub-goals. While the work of the author of a character-driven story is to make the protagonist experience conflicts that push them to their limits, reveal their personality, and sometimes force them to change, become aware, and make a choice. In both cases, you will note that fortunately, there is conflict.

Be careful, a portrait does not necessarily imply an internal trajectory. Don Juan is a portrait, but no one evolves in the play. Don Juan remains the same until death: an impious libertine. The same goes for The Cherry Orchard. The same goes for About Schmidt. Throughout the film, Warren Schmidt (Jack Nicholson) receives messages "You don't exist," some very violent, until Ndugu's letter finally proves to him that he is worth something. It is such a relief that Warren bursts into tears. However, he does not change. One cannot say that he goes from someone who has no self-esteem to the opposite. From the beginning of the film, Warren is convinced that he is worth something. If he goes to his former company to offer his help, it is because he thinks he can be useful. It is the events that keep sending him the opposite signal.

Moreover, in some stories that can be described as portraits, it is not the character who is the subject of the portrait that changes. We talked about this on pages 78 and 82-84.

Trajectory question and chorality

If you have several protagonists, whether co-protagonists with the same goal (The Odd Couple or The Dirty Dozen) or several protagonists in a choral story, you can design a trajectory for each character. The three protagonists of We All Loved Each Other So Much do not evolve in the same way. They all start the film with the same character trait: idealism. In the end, Antonio (Nino Manfredi) has not changed; he is still as naive, while Nicola (Stefano Satta Flores) is disillusioned, and Gianni (Vittorio Gassman) is corrupt. You can even have a non-choral story, with a single protagonist, and nevertheless several characters who change, each in their own way. This is the case in the example of Culbuto, proposed on pages 200-203, in which the protagonist and another important character transform. Of course, for both characters, it is the protagonist's action that causes this change.

An emotional learning

Think of the internal trajectory as emotional learning, not intellectual learning. Characters do not learn an idea or information (as after a fascinating discussion or reading a good book); they learn to act and react differently. At the end of The Wizard of Oz, Dorothy (Judy Garland) has not only understood that there is no place like home, she has also learned it. Or, if you prefer, she has understood it in her flesh.

Above all, no "trajectory phone"

I have already written this in the previous chapter, but I insist: in my opinion, a story that offers only an internal trajectory does not work. You cannot simply present a character who needs to change and then opportunely place elements and characters on their path that will help them without your protagonist undertaking anything. And this even if they experience conflict. Because it is not credible. The deus ex machina is obvious. You can feel the author behind, arranging things for their protagonist. In life, when you are stingy and bitter, no moralizing spirit comes to visit you to put you on the path to redemption (cf. the analysis of A Christmas Carol above). When you are suicidal, in debt, and blind to your inner beauty, no angel comes to save you (until, it seems, prostituting themselves for you!), as in Angel-A. You will tell me that what is true in life may not be true in fiction and vice versa. And that, moreover, some fictions are metaphors. Certainly. But in fiction, an angel, a coach, or a spirit cannot have the sole and unique objective of saving the protagonist. In It's a Wonderful Life (1946), mentioned above, Clarence (Henry Travers), a second-class angel, does not aim to save George (James Stewart); he aims to earn his wings.

At the beginning of The Beat That My Heart Skipped, Thomas (Romain Duris) is presented as a nasty guy who engages in illegal real estate speculation at the expense of poor people. He clearly needs to grow. And then suddenly, an inciting incident, he crosses paths with a concert organizer (Sandy Whitelaw) who makes him want to return to the piano. This fortuitous encounter has the taste of a deus ex machina. It makes us unconsciously say: "Ah, this will help him change." One might retort that this is exactly the effect produced by the fortuitous curse that befalls the protagonist of Groundhog Day. Yes, except that it is precisely a curse that befalls Phil Connors (Bill Murray), not help. That's the whole difference between an announcement and a phone call. We know that an inciting incident is the only element of a story that has the right to come out of nowhere, one of the few moments when the author is allowed to use chance. Except... if the inciting incident phones a solution to the protagonist's problem. When Elsa (Ingrid Bergman) appears in Rick's (Humphrey Bogart) café at the beginning of Casablanca, we feel Rick's discomfort, and no one thinks it is timely. When Krogstad launches his blackmail at the beginning of A Doll's House, we feel sorry for Nora; no one thinks it will help her grow.

It should be noted that "trajectory phones" are often felt as author fantasies. "Ah, how wonderful it would be, when I feel worthless, to stumble upon the right situation or the right person who has nothing else to do but help me get better." Certainly, any story can be considered a metaphor for a personal problem of its author. But the idea is not to show it. I think an author should not put themselves on stage directly; they should hide behind their characters and situations. For example, hide behind a dramatic pitch. That's why I maintain that a character's transformation can only happen through the exploitation of a pitch. The principle is always the same: because a character seeks to achieve a dramatic goal and this generates obstacles and conflict (including trajectory conflict), then a character (or another) is led to transform.

6. FOUNDATIONS
"Before writing a single word, I must possess the character from top to bottom."
(Henrik Ibsen [44])
"From the start, I know where I'm going. (...) If you know where you're going, you can pace your story and avoid downtime."
(René Goscinny [37])
Once you have a clear formulation of your dramatic pitch, and possibly the internal trajectory of one of the characters, you have the first elements of your story's foundations. It's time to complete this work by answering the following questions. You will note that some answers are already known, as they are part of the pitch.
- What is the title?
- What is the characterization of the protagonist? Is one of the characters haunted?
- What is the inciting incident?
