- What is the protagonist's general goal? What is at stake for the protagonist?
- Who are the important characters other than the protagonist necessary to advance the action? What is their characterization?
- What are the protagonist's motivations, sub-goals, and means?
- What is the first-act-second-act transition? Is there a declaration of intent?
- Is there an arena?
- What is the nature and content of the obstacles? Is there urgency?
- What is the climax? What is the dramatic response?
- What happens in the third act?
- Is the structure enriched? If so, what are the third-act twist, second climax, and second dramatic response?
- Are there one or more general dramatic ironies?
- Are there one or more notable diffuse dramatic ironies?
- Is there a median climax? A twist? A point of no return?
- Will there be comedic elements outside of dialogue or activity?
- What are the trajectory elements? Protagonist, conflict, climax, response? Reasonable contradictor?
It is not necessary to answer these questions in the order proposed here, although it is preferable for certain elements. If, for example, you can very well find the title after defining the climax or the dramatic response, it makes no sense to think about obstacles before knowing the general goal.

A. THE TITLE
Disney Studios did not want the title "Dead Poets Society." That didn't stop the film from being as successful as we know. I don't believe at all in the myth of the good title or the selling title, even for strong premises. For the simple reason that a title quickly becomes a concept (even before the premiere) that goes beyond the mere meaning of its words, a bit like a child's first name. If I ask a cinephile to clear their mind and throw them: Bringing Up Baby, they will immediately say it's a film directed by Max Pécas or Émile Couzinet. Wrong! It's a "screwball comedy," one of the most famous in cinema history. When you know it, you no longer pay attention to its title.
It is true that some titles are a bit heavy to bear. "The Amazing Adventures of Superman" is probably more attractive to the general public than Testament of a Murdered Jewish Poet or Rodriguez in the Land of Merguez or I Bought a Hooker's Deodorant (an authentic title of a play by Jim Cartwright). But we are dealing here with extreme cases. For the rest, the myth of the good title is mainly maintained by decision-makers seeking to have power over works. The three only valid criteria for choosing a title are:
1 - that the title suits you as an author. You don't want to spend months writing a work whose title doesn't inspire you;
2 - that it is coherent, that it doesn't lead the viewer astray. If you make a serious film about the Algerian War, don't call it "The Soldiers Have Fun in the Desert";
3 - that it doesn't sell the dramatic response.
Note that taking into account the audience and the announcement that a title constitutes is not a trend born with Hollywood shopkeepers. "Shakespeare," Robert McKee [55] reminds us, "did not call his play Hamlet but The Tragedy of Hamlet, Prince of Denmark. He gave his comedies titles like Much Ado About Nothing or All's Well That Ends Well. Thus, every afternoon at the Globe Theatre, his Elizabethan audience was psychologically prepared to cry or laugh."
In short, there are no good or bad titles, only adequate or inadequate titles. Since the title matters little, you are probably wondering why it is part of the foundations. It's because finding a title contributes to giving an identity to the work in progress, to conferring it a beginning of existence. And... to give it meaning. Sometimes, you only find the title at the end. But if you can find it at this stage of development, it's a plus. At a minimum, adopt a provisional title.

B. THE CHARACTERIZATION OF THE PROTAGONIST
By definition, the protagonist is the character who experiences the most dynamic conflict. It is also, of course, the character whose story is told, with whom the viewer identifies (emotionally), and generally the one who occupies the most screen time.
Logically, the main characterization of the protagonist reflects the terms of the dramatic pitch. This seems logical, yet I have often seen a gap between the characterization and what the pitch suggests.
Three phases: conception, setup, and exploitation Whether for the protagonist or other characters, the work of characterization is threefold. First, the character must be conceived on paper, then translated into scenes. This translation work itself has two branches: the presentation of the character and the exploitation of their characterization.
When Edmond Rostand composed Cyrano de Bergerac, he probably thought that his protagonist should be brilliant, with a prominent nasal appendage, complexed, and sensitive. Cyrano is the king of panache in all areas, except in love. This is the first step, the conception on paper. At the beginning of the play, the character is discussed before he enters the scene. Some characters sketch Cyrano's portrait: exquisite, rhymer, swordsman, physicist, musician, proud, and with a nose he never removes. But all these character traits are said. Rostand knows he must show them. That's when Cyrano enters the scene. The nose is "terrible," as the playwright immediately indicates to the makeup artists. Then Rostand shows what he had some characters say. Cyrano forces an actor off stage, issues a collective challenge to the audience, provokes a troublemaker, takes offense, and fences with the Viscount while displaying wit. Quite a feat. Finally, Cyrano explains to his friend Le Bret that he loves Roxane but will never dare tell her because he is terribly afraid she will laugh at his nose. All this constitutes the second phase. The character is established in all his facets. From there, Rostand moves to the third phase, which is to exploit what he has set up. Cyrano's brilliance, panache, and complex will fuel the entire second act.
When Francis Veber wrote The Odd Couple, one can imagine that he conceived Pignon as a clumsy, romantic, and suicidal underdog. Then, Veber knows he must present these character traits at the beginning of the film. As in Cyrano de Bergerac, the character is discussed before being seen. But it's light. Christine (Anny Duperey) says: "He was very kind," and her mother (Gisèle Pascal): "Wasn't he the one who was always trying to kill himself?" Then Pignon appears, played by Pierre Richard—is it necessary to specify that a character's appearance plays a significant role in characterization? Pignon is writing a suicide note. When he learns he has a son and that Christine thought of him to find him, he cries, makes a blunder, and enthuses: "I have no job, my wife left me, I live with my mother who makes my life impossible. I have no plans, no future, everything is blocked, ruined, isn't it wonderful?!" The character is thus established where others might have lazily relied on Pierre Richard's established persona. Then Veber exploits the characterization he has set up. He does so largely by contrast, as he has taken care to place a dominant, assertive, and brutal character, Lucas (Gérard Depardieu), opposite Pignon. We thus see Pignon being victimized, worrying like a mother, crying with a desperate thug (Roland Blanche), and, of course, opposing Lucas on how to conduct the investigation.
In this chapter, we will focus only on the first phase, which is part of creating the foundations. The second and third phases of characterization work will be addressed in the next chapter.
Phase 1: Character conception
Start by giving your protagonist a name. It's like the title of the work; it gives the character a beginning of existence. Preferably, give them a first name that fits the context and that you like. As with the title, you don't want to project yourself for months onto a character whose name you despise or hate.
Then, give them an age, a physical appearance, and, if you can, a costume. We know how much a costume can help an actor find their character. You can do your work and all those who will collaborate on its creation a service by providing some indications on this subject. The costume represents the character's way of dressing, the kind of clothes they wear, but not only that. Beyond the wardrobe, the costume includes hairstyle, posture, certain physical traits, the type of makeup, etc. Marie-Anne Chazel [21] recounts that she entered the skin of Zézette (wife X) the day she put on her character's dental prosthesis (Santa Claus is a Bastard).
Next, imagine the main characteristics of your protagonist: their qualities, flaws, skills, wounds, demons, dramatic need, possible ghost (cf. below), secret dream, fixed ideas, what they hide, etc.
Don't be afraid to give your protagonist one or two flaws. It will make them more human and, if you take care to make them experience conflict, will never prevent the viewer from being interested in them. Tom Fontana [31] reports that when he was writing Oz, Chris Albrecht, his boss at HBO, told him: "I don't care if the characters are likable as long as they are interesting." This sentence should be plastered in large letters in all the offices of all fiction managers on the planet. N.B. The principle also applies negatively to opponents: give them qualities. Unless you are addressing 4-year-old children, opponents should not embody pure evil.
Distinguish psychological flaws that handicap the character themselves from moral flaws that harm others. In Casablanca, Rick has a moral flaw; he is selfish and without compassion. He also has a psychological flaw: he no longer believes in love. In Read My Lips, Clara (Emmanuelle Devos) is a social doormat. In One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, McMurphy (Jack Nicholson) is a rebellious libertine. In Amadeus, Salieri is envious, hypocritical, and villainous. In Gone with the Wind, Scarlett (Vivien Leigh) is proud, self-centered, and manipulative. In Dexter, Dexter (Michael Hall) has a gigantic psychological flaw: he feels no emotion, and a gigantic moral flaw: he kills impulsively. In House, House (Hugh Laurie) is drug-addicted, misanthropic, and without compassion.
the repertoire is filled with intellectually unappealing but emotionally sympathetic characters.

Be mindful of moral flaws. It is even more imperative to create conflict for an amoral or immoral protagonist than for one without moral flaws. Rick, McMurphy, Scarlett, Dexter, and House experience a lot of conflict. If you forget this fundamental rule, you end up with characters that are intellectually and emotionally unappealing. Like Henry (Michael Rooker) in Henry: Portrait of a Serial Killer or Jaonovici at the beginning of Once Upon a Time in France.

Three traits maximum  
Don't burden yourself with lengthy characterizations. Aim for the stereotype to which you add a nuance. Too many characteristics kill characterization. In life, a human being can be rebellious, greedy, selfish, idealistic, intolerant, funny, cowardly, home-loving, and sensitive all at once. But in a dramatic work, there's no room to address all these traits. If you assign them to a character, you'll either drown and the character will end up bland or incoherent, or, without realizing it, you'll only retain two traits, three at most, and not address the others.

This tendency to characterize characters by filling ten pages of traits probably comes from acting schools. They often offer a long questionnaire to help actors prepare a role. It includes questions like "What are your favorite dishes?" or "Did you have a difficult birth?" I think it's much more useful for an actor than for a writer. Because an actor doesn't start from scratch. They already have a role. Hopefully, the role is well-written and coherent. So, the questionnaire allows them to create and enrich the character from existing elements. Whereas the writer starts from nothing. The page is blank. If they start answering all these questions, in my opinion, they get lost. And this is especially true when starting a story. Over time, the character will develop. They will also develop when embodied by an actor. But I think it's better to start with a small handful of important traits.

N.B. In my definition, the role represents everything in the text concerning a given character, which is therefore offered to actors to play. The character is the whole fictional human being of which the role only reveals a few fragments, a few symptoms. The role is thus the visible part of the character-iceberg.

Not only can a character not be rebellious, greedy, selfish, idealistic, intolerant, funny, cowardly, home-loving, and sensitive all at once, but there are about thirty-six ways to be rebellious, thirty-six ways to be greedy, thirty-six ways to be selfish. In short, it's not enough to decide on two or three traits of character; you must also make them specific.

If your character is rebellious, does their rebellious spirit manifest politically or socially? Are they rebellious only in their profession (an avant-garde artist, for example) or also in their personal life? Are they aware of having a rebellious spirit? If so, do they justify their rebellion with an ideology? Do they have a problem with authority, whatever it may be, or do they just dislike being directed? Do they have a problem with institutional limits or all limits, including those of nature? Do they rebel on principle, for any reason, for trivialities? Do they have only a spirit of contradiction? Is their rebellion static—closed, obtuse, refractory—or dynamic—they easily revolt and even provoke, inciting opposition? Are they rebellious towards certain people in particular and not at all towards others? Are they rebellious towards their era because they are visionary? I'll stop there, but you see that if you're not precise, your character will be impossible to pin down.

The ghost  
A ghost is a past event that haunts a character. It's a strong element of characterization. One of the most well-known ghosts in the repertoire is that of Sophie (Meryl Streep) in Sophie's Choice. Upon arriving at Auschwitz, a doctor forced her to choose which of her two children would be exterminated immediately. Lioubov (The Cherry Orchard) also has a ghost. Her son drowned at the age of seven.

It's not necessary for there to be a ghost in a story. And it's not necessary for the ghost to belong to the protagonist. In Spellbound, the protagonist is Dr. Petersen (Ingrid Bergman) while the haunted character is John Ballantyne (Gregory Peck). In Chinatown, the protagonist (Jack Nicholson) has a small ghost (more mentioned than detailed) but it's mainly Evelyn (Faye Dunaway) who has suffered a major trauma.

Of course, a ghost is not designed to be decorative. If you imagine a ghost for one of your characters, you must exploit it. First, in the characterization. Part of the actions and attitude of the haunted character must be linked to their ghost. Often, this also involves creating a mystery around the ghost and waiting for the best moment, often the end, to reveal it. But there are exceptions. Lioubov's ghost is revealed in the first act of The Cherry Orchard. It helps us better understand the protagonist, the reason for her exile, and her relationship with her property. Citizen Kane offers another interesting exploitation. We see Kane's foundational trauma from the beginning, but it's only at the end that we understand it was his ghost.

The wound and its elastic  
As we've just seen, the ghost is a trauma. It is more of an isolated and often melodramatic event. The wound refers more to the educational flaws received by your character over time.

The notion of elastic deserves attention. An elastic is an emotional process that often brutally connects a present experience to a past experience (often a wound). A character is in contact with someone or is experiencing an action when suddenly they feel a surge of emotion. In life, people who don't know the principle of the elastic tend to believe that the emotion is generated by the present situation when in fact, it is due to this process which, like a powerful temporal elastic, has reactivated a childhood wound or brought us back to our childhood, often to a painful moment of it. When Proust dips a madeleine in tea and suddenly remembers his Aunt Léonie with happiness (Swann's Way), he benefits from a positive elastic. Unfortunately, elastics are far from always happy.

An essential characteristic of elastics is to produce a disproportionate reaction to a present event. For example, one bursts into tears or enters into excessive rage. Or a panic fear—phobias fall under the principle of the elastic. It's a reaction that can be characterizing in fiction. If you wish to exploit it, you will need to conceive the corresponding wound of your character.

The "backstory"  
The ghost, wounds, and elastics are part of the protagonist's past, what the Anglo-Saxons call the "backstory." It is an important element of characterizing characters, especially the protagonist. Knowing their backstory is the best way to give the impression that there was a life before your story, that your protagonist doesn't come from nowhere.

The evil double  
In Dramaturgy [51], I extensively develop the notion of internal obstacle. It's an element of the protagonist's personality that generates obstacles on their path. Hamlet's hesitations prevent him from avenging his father (Hamlet). Jimmy Ringo's reputation as a sharpshooter (Gregory Peck in The Gunfighter) prevents him from leading a peaceful life. Howard's clumsiness (Ben Miller in The Worst Week of My Life) prevents him from being accepted by his in-laws.

Don't confuse the internal obstacle with the internal conflict, even though both are elements of characterization that deserve attention. An internal conflict is a conflict experienced by a character (protagonist or not) and results from an opposition between two traits of said character. Alceste, the protagonist of The Misanthrope, suffers from a nice internal conflict: he is madly in love with a socialite whose flaws annoy him and should make him flee. But "reason does not govern love." Pinocchio, examined in chapter 5, offers a fine example of a character constantly torn between two contradictory desires: the reality principle and the pleasure principle. An internal conflict can appear during the action, but here, I want to talk about the internal conflict that is part of one of the characters from their entry into the story, which gnaws at them and thus constitutes an element of their characterization and not a type of conflict specific to the action. In The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde, Dr. Jekyll, who is the main character of Stevenson's novel but not its protagonist, is confronted with a brutal and embarrassing double, Mr. Hyde. If Jekyll were a pure and innocent scientist unwittingly embarked on an unfortunate adventure, this conflict would not be an element of characterization. But we learn towards the end of the novel that Jekyll has long been attracted to vice, that he already has this internal conflict within him. It's even what led him to conceive a potion meant to dissociate his soul.

I'm not talking about simply disturbed characters either. In these cases, one can consider that their neurosis/psychosis is exploitable as an internal obstacle. I'm talking about characters who have a duality, two antagonistic characteristics, who suffer from it, and who, when they are aware of it, fight to overcome it (generally, to make one of the traits win). Because, of course, when a character possesses an internal conflict, the story will take care of staging it.

Thus, many stories exploit the protagonist's internal conflict. In Psycho, Marion (Janet Leigh) is plagued by guilt even before stealing the $40,000. Note that Norman (Anthony Perkins) faces a similar problem. He fights against a parental figure that sometimes takes control of his mind. Is Kane (Orson Welles) a humanist or a tyrant (Citizen Kane)? Both, my general. In fact, he knows it. Doesn't he say to Thatcher (George Coulouris) at the beginning of the film: "The problem is you're talking to two people. (...) As Charles Foster Kane, shareholder, I agree with you: Charles Foster Kane is a scoundrel. His newspaper should be closed. (...) Besides, I am the editor of The Inquirer. As such, it is my duty to ensure that the good workers of our community are not robbed by a bunch of profiteers." Finally, let's mention Rick (Humphrey Bogart) in Casablanca, whom Captain Renault (Claude Rains) suspects of being an idealist behind his cynical shell.

Does a trajectory protagonist necessarily experience an internal conflict? Often, they are not aware of their trajectory flaw. They are selfish, cowardly, childish, and nothing else, before becoming, after a series of trials, altruistic, courageous, or mature. But when one has a trajectory flaw, thus a lack, an unconscious part of oneself challenges and pushes us to grow. One can consider that the opposition of these two facets constitutes an internal conflict.

You don't have to burden one of your characters with an internal conflict. But you see that such a characteristic can enrich a character, in addition to adding a certain type of conflict to your story. To be stored in our toolbox and pondered.

The relationship with others  
A human being, real or fictional, does not exist in the absolute, immutable, regardless of the circumstances, regardless of the person they are facing. The character's relationship with others is an important trait of characterization.

Nina Leeds, in Strange Interlude, is a good example. She is not the same in front of the five men in her life: her father, her two husbands, her son, or her lover, and, moreover, she does not expect the same things from them. The 20th Cavalry also contains a remarkable example. Colonel McStraggle, who commands the fort, has a very specific relationship with his son who has the misfortune of serving under his command. He is stricter with him than with the other soldiers because he is terrified of favoritism. One cannot say that Colonel McStraggle is strict in the absolute; he is especially so with his son. This relationship thus teaches us two traits of the Colonel: a general trait—he is afraid of being caught in a fault—and a specific relational trait—he is strict with his son, to the point of injustice.

In your characterization work on paper, imagine your protagonist facing the important characters in their life.

Avoid unintentional sexism  
If you are a chauvinist and proud of it, this paragraph will shake you. Perhaps you'd prefer to skip directly to the next part. If, on the other hand, you advocate equality between girls and boys, as well as between women and men, then you should be interested in this section. Because an author can be sexist without realizing it. Even a woman can be chauvinistic. It is enough to reproduce the many patriarchal ideas that have circulated in all societies for a long time and can be acquired unconsciously, including by reading novels or going to the cinema, as storytelling is a powerful cultural vector. Thus, a majority of well-intentioned works of fiction convey subtle sexism. If only by the gender of the protagonist (very predominantly male).

In 1985, in the comic strip Dykes to Watch Out For, a female character explains that she only watches movies that meet the following criteria: 1 - there are two identified female characters, 2 - they have a dialogue scene, 3 - in which they do not talk about a man. Since 1985, these criteria have been known as the Bechdel-Wallace test, named after the author of the comic strip, Alison Bechdel, and a friend of hers, Liz Wallace, who gave her the idea to create a test based on this character's reflection. It is an interesting source of inspiration to avoid sexism in a work of fiction. But it's not the only one. You can also:

- Ask yourself, for each character, if they need to be male. The answer is more often negative than one might think;
- Avoid having only one important female role (the Smurfette syndrome). Distribute plenty of roles to women and not just those of the mother and the whore: judge, surgeon, astronaut, researcher, mechanic, etc.;
- Not have only female characters with a top model physique or/and a great and pure soul or/and surreal power;
- Not have only female characters under forty or over seventy. Between the roles of young lover and grandmother, many actresses notice a long tunnel in our Western cinema. As the actresses of the MFA (Actresses and Actors of France Associated) say, from the age of fifty, women acquire a superpower on screen: they are invisible! Is it because as they mature, many women gain strength and independence, and that scares many men?;
- Avoid female characters all serving the male protagonist;
- Avoid the helpless victim who serves as a support for the villain's (and the author's) sadism;
- Avoid clichés. Not all women are poor at DIY. Not all Native American women are wise and ecological. Not all women want to have children. Unfortunately, in this area, the list of clichés is long;
- Avoid treating rape like a cold. If you include a rape in your story, first question its absolute necessity. Then, if you maintain the rape, do not treat it lightly. Ask yourself how to show it. In fiction, some rapes show a guilty complacency. Finally, do not show a woman who is raped and then moves on. Rape always has painful and long-term consequences. In a particularly harsh episode of Outlander (episode 1.15), a man (Sam Heughan) is raped. The authors don't stop there. The repercussions of the rape are explored in subsequent episodes. I hope this accuracy is not due to the fact that the victim was a man.

If you feel concerned about this subject, you will find other advice on the internet. But, for me, the most important advice is this: create characters who are fighters. This also (and especially) applies to female characters who are not protagonists. Not necessarily strong, powerful, or sympathetic women—which is also good—just women who do not let themselves be confined to a status of victims or trophies, who steer their own course and fight, in their own way, with their means. I think this is the condition for creating beautiful female portraits and for telling our daughters: you can do it just as much as the boys.

1. The following works can also serve as inspiration: Lysistrata, One Thousand and One Nights, Donkey Skin, Tartuffe, A Doll's House, The Caucasian Chalk Circle, Pippi Longstocking, Eve, The Miracle Worker, Yoko Tsuno, Autumn Sonata, Victor Victoria, Steel Magnolias, Alice, The Thorn Birds, Thelma & Louise, The Story of Qiu Ju, Mulan, Not One Less, Sur mes lèvres, Desperate Housewives, Martha Jane Cannary, Tangled, Philomena, Happy Valley, Mustang. These are just a few titles. Everyone will extend this list as they see fit.

C. THE INCITING INCIDENT  
By definition, an inciting incident is an event that breaks a routine or balance and prompts a living organism to react. There are all sizes, major, local, semi-local, tiny. We will talk here about the big inciting incident, the one that will launch the action.

Two types of inciting incidents  
Inciting incidents can be classified into two main categories: those that do not depend at all on the future protagonist and those that depend, directly or indirectly, on the future protagonist. The first category concerns sudden chances, accidents, unexpected inheritances, etc. In the second category, I distinguish:

- The result (therefore the climax) of an action undertaken before: considerable lottery win, birth of a child, etc.;
- The trial sent by Heaven. The trial resembles an accident from nowhere but feels like this accident does not fall on the future protagonist by chance. It is thus a sudden chance but with a metaphorical function. When Phil Connors (Bill Murray) finds himself stuck in Punxsutawney reliving the same dreary day of his life indefinitely (Groundhog Day), he is the victim of a curse that will allow him to transform. From the start, we feel he deserves it, that it's "well done for him," as they say;
- The last straw that breaks the camel's back. Imagine a woman who believes she wears a rare perfume and feels a certain pride. She starts encountering other women with the same perfume. She holds back until one day, exasperated, she finally sets out to find a unique perfume for herself alone. What triggers this objective is the moment the vase overflows. Similarly, it's the accumulation of difficulties with his deaf and blind daughter that finally convinces Captain Keller to hire an educator (The Miracle Worker). The inciting incident of The Purple Rose of Cairo is the moment when one of the actors (Jeff Daniels) of the film Cecilia (Mia Farrow) is watching steps out of the screen and joins her in the theater. We understand he does it after seeing Cecilia several times. Here too, he has stuck psychological stamps until one day, unable to hold back, he decides to meet Cecilia.

I invite you to become aware of the category in which your inciting incident falls. Conversely, if you do not already know the inciting incident, you can start by asking yourself if you want it to be completely independent of the future protagonist or not. The meaning is obviously not the same. If, for example, the inciting incident is a car accident, the future protagonist can be the victim (cf. The Sucker) or responsible (21 Grams).

The different categories mentioned above can give the same objective with the same protagonist. Imagine that your protagonist's objective is to quit smoking. When one has an addiction, one does not wake up one morning deciding to quit. An event is needed to trigger this will. It can be an isolated event dependent on the future protagonist (an aortitis followed by medical advice), independent of the future protagonist (they witness a death due to tongue cancer), or it can be the last straw, the end of a psychological stamp collection. Every morning, the character wakes up coughing up their lungs. One day, they decide that's enough and resolve to quit.

Include the incident in your story?  
Another important topic of reflection: do you want the inciting incident to be part of the story? It's rare, but it happens that it is absent. That is, the inciting incident has occurred, in one of the four forms mentioned above, but before the start of the story. This generally requires you to refer to it in the first act. That said, the inciting incident being a powerful and precious dramatic knot, I advise you to include it in the story.

If you choose not to include it and if your protagonist already has their general objective from the beginning of the story, plan a few scenes before revealing the objective in question. In other words, include a first act. The second act will begin when the audience knows (consciously or unconsciously) what the protagonist's objective is. If you do not proceed this way, you risk that your protagonist's objective will not be perceived as the general objective of the action but as a routine objective. Because a story can very well start with your future protagonist having a strong objective. This is the case in The School for Wives. From the first scene, we understand that Arnolphe's objective is to marry Agnès. But it is not his objective to
second act. The same principle applies in Alien or Sunshine. From the start, the respective crews of these two films have an objective: to bring a cargo of ore back to Earth for one, to reignite the Sun for the other. After the inciting incident, a new objective is launched.

In Cyrano de Bergerac as in The Apartment, the inciting incident is not shown. When the story begins, the protagonist, Cyrano or Baxter (Jack Lemmon), has already met the love of his life (Roxane or Fran (Shirley MacLaine)), has already fallen in love, and already has the goal of being loved. But he hasn't taken action yet. He waits for the right opportunity to act: the arrival of Christian for Cyrano, a promotion for Baxter. This opportunity can be seen as a mini-integrated inciting incident. As if being loved was the goal of the first act and seducing the goal of the second. Or one might consider that, regarding the grand goal of seducing the beloved, the protagonist has set a first sub-goal: waiting for the right opportunity. In Baxter's case, the opportunity is even provoked by him.

A shock
Whatever its nature, the inciting incident is an event that forces the future protagonist to take action. Therefore, conceive the inciting incident as a shock. A death, an accident, an inheritance, a love at first sight are classic inciting incidents. This dramatic knot must be powerful enough to disrupt the life of your future protagonist and motivate them to embark on a long second act.

Multiple and manifold
Be careful not to have several independent inciting incidents. Any event, big or small, that is not routine can serve as an inciting incident. If, for example, the inciting incident is the opportunity for a broke couple to win a large sum of money, do not include a scene just before where the husband is fired (as in The Box). Because the firing is already an inciting incident. Instead, show that the couple has been struggling to make ends meet for some time. Another solution is to place the parasitic inciting incident before the story begins. There was indeed a firing, but it is part of the past. No matter if it's a recent past. Start the story after the firing and have the protagonist mention it at the right moment. A third solution is to link the two inciting incidents by a causal effect. Because the husband is fired and available, he is offered a large sum of money.

At the beginning of The Sting, Hooker (Robert Redford) and Luther (Robert Earl Jones) con a Doyle Lonnegan (Robert Shaw) employee. Lonnegan retaliates by killing Luther. Hooker decides to take revenge on Lonnegan. It is only at this point that we enter the second act. So, we have two inciting incidents: one for the antagonist (the initial con) and one for the protagonist (Luther's death). Fortunately, one is a consequence of the other.

Why don't two independent inciting incidents work? First, because you are only allowed one "accident" at the beginning of a story, one chance occurrence. Two accidents in a row resemble a coincidence. It feels like the author is arranging events. Second, because two independent inciting incidents should logically generate two independent objectives. Which makes for one objective too many. And if they generate the same objective, we return to the first point: too much coincidence, too much authorial convenience.

D. THE GENERAL OBJECTIVE
Logically, the objective must be the same as in the dramatic pitch. How many times, in workshops, have I seen a different objective in the foundations than what appeared in the pitch! I have already made this point above regarding characterization, and it applies, of course, to all elements of the pitch, but it is especially on the objective that I notice discrepancies. They can be due to carelessness. More often, they are the symptom of (still) uncertain thinking.

Action and result
The word "objective" has two meanings: the action itself and its result. When declaring their objective, a protagonist will rarely state what they aim for: "I want revenge," "I want a job contract," or "I want Noémie's love." They will rather say: "I want to get revenge," "I want to find a job," or "I want to seduce Noémie." Because they know that to achieve a result, one must first act. This is why examples of objectives are always action verbs: survive, take revenge, win, find, seduce, save, etc.

Often, when the protagonist declares their objective in terms of action, the audience understands what the objective in terms of result should be, that is, what the protagonist is aiming for. If a character does everything to be loved by another character, we have a pretty good idea of the moment that will prove they have achieved their objective. When Hamlet declares that he will avenge his father, we guess that this equates to obtaining Claudius's death. And if we are not sure, we do not doubt that we will be able to spot the moment when the protagonist has achieved their objective.

But when the result is not obvious, it is necessary to explain it to the audience. Take the example of a character whose general objective is to be a rock star. There are several ways to be a rock star, several criteria. Is it to release a first album? To sell it in more than x thousand copies? To sign a contract for five albums? To get four stars in a specialized magazine? To have at least three groupies at their doorstep every night? To be admitted into this or that microcosm? In It's a Wonderful Life (1946), George's (James Stewart) objective is to achieve great things. A bit vague. Quite quickly, we understand that he wants to build works of art. Ironically, he will never leave his hometown, but that will not prevent him from accomplishing great things on another level than the one he had in mind.

In short, if the objective embraces too many possibilities and you do not say more about the criteria your protagonist imagines, the audience embarks into the unknown. You must clarify the objective.

An inseparable trio
I remind you that there is very often a close link between the inciting incident, the characterization of the protagonist, and the objective (and even obstacles and climax). The respective protagonists of Ikiru, Haut les cœurs! and Breaking Bad have the same inciting incident: they are told they have cancer. But since they do not have the same characterization, it does not produce the same objective. The first (Takashi Shimura) stops working and fights to build a children's park downtown. The second (Karin Viard) wonders if she should keep the child she is carrying. The third (Bryan Cranston) starts manufacturing and selling drugs to secure his family's future.

Your pitch must convey this coherence. Too often, I read pitches in which I do not see a logical link between the inciting incident, protagonist, and objective.

How to find the general objective
If you cannot determine your protagonist's objective, to find a concrete formulation that encompasses your entire second act, there are two ways to find the objective. But, first of all, you must clearly distinguish between dramatic objective and trajectory objective (cf. pages 16-17 and chapter 5).

The first way to find the protagonist's general objective is to start from the central conflict. Imagine you extract the essence of your second act, what is the representative conflict? What does your protagonist struggle most to do? Answering these questions should help you determine their general objective. The second way is to start from the climax. Provided you know it, of course. Since the climax is contained in the germ of the objective declaration and is the protagonist's ultimate attempt to achieve said objective, it necessarily gives a good idea of the latter. If I show you only the climax of Raiders of the Lost Ark or Cyrano de Bergerac, assuming you know nothing else, there is no doubt that you will guess what the protagonist's objective was. Do the same with your story if you know the climax.

A way to be original
Even if loving, taking revenge, saving the world, keeping a secret, finding someone are omnipresent objectives in the repertoire, you can imagine a rarer objective. Finding a different objective is one of the ways you have to create an atypical work. Of course, once again, the objective will also depend on the characterization of the protagonist and their inciting incident.

Not only is an uncommon objective a way to inject some originality into your story, but it will never prevent you from making a noble work, contrary to what some claim. To be convinced, just look at the loads of flops produced with the noble objective of saving the world. Getting apologies, asserting revolutionary theories, teaching a teenager to communicate, escaping social and emotional misery, can give magnificent works (The Story of Qiu Ju, Life of Galileo, The Miracle Worker, Read My Lips). I have already discussed this in chapter 4.

What if the protagonist has two antagonistic objectives?
Whether your protagonist starts with an internal conflict or not (cf. above), they may quickly find themselves with two antagonistic objectives of equal intensity. They want love but also money. They want to become better but also harm someone. They want to have a career and, at the same time, be a good father to their children. Their heart swings between two people. Etc. If you treat both objectives on the same level, the audience will be divided, they will not know what you are telling, and it will be uncomfortable.

A solution is to make one of the two objectives an obstacle for the other. But be careful, the choice is not indifferent. Because, depending on whether you choose one or the other, it does not give the same story. Let's take the example of love versus money. Imagine, in the first case, a character who seeks to seduce someone and is hindered by their greed. You have one type of story. Now imagine a character who seeks to make money but the love in their heart puts obstacles in their way. You see that with the same internal antagonism, we can obtain two different story directions.

A complementary solution is to make one of the objectives conscious, and the other unconscious. In this case, it is best to take as the conscious objective the one that will fail and as the unconscious objective the one that will succeed. Why? I would like to say that it is because our unconscious often knows better what is good for us, but the real reason is that the unconscious is powerful. It seems more credible to me that it is the one that prevails.

The unconscious objective can be treated as a trajectory objective but not necessarily. In The Toy, Perrin (Pierre Richard) has the conscious objective of making a film and the unconscious objective of regaining his fiancée's love (Miou-Miou). The two objectives clearly oppose each other because the film that the romantic Perrin is shooting is a pornographic film whose production distances him from his partner. However, Perrin's unconscious objective is not a transformation objective. It is just an antagonist objective to his dramatic objective.

Treating an objective as an obstacle is also useful if you tend to give too many objectives to your protagonist and create subplots for them. I have seen this process in authors who are afraid of not filling their second act. They give their protagonist plenty of actions to accomplish, to the detriment of the unity of action. The solution is to keep one and only one general objective and treat all other objectives as obstacles. Imagine, for example, a salesperson who has a bad habit of alienating customers. His boss gives him one last chance: the protagonist has three months to calm down and prove himself. Otherwise, he will be shown the door. Here is an objective that is enough to fill a second act. You do not need, in addition, to add a divorce for the protagonist to manage, parents moving, a teenage daughter running away, neighbors filing a lawsuit, etc. Unless you turn these actions into obstacles for the first objective. The divorce, the parents, the daughter, the neighbors can occupy the protagonist, push him to the limit, and thus prevent him from proving himself at work.

The essential conditions
I remind you that it is not enough to give your protagonist a unique general objective. Your task will also consist of:
- making this objective known (or understood or felt) to the audience (cf. "First act-second act transition" below);
- motivating the objective (cf. "Stakes" and "Motivations" below);
- making the objective difficult to achieve (cf. "Obstacles" below);
- endowing your protagonist with a fierce determination to achieve their objective.

Legitimate and illegitimate dramatic objective
There is a type of story, quite common, in which the audience feels, from the start, that the dramatic objective is not legitimate and that a positive dramatic response is not desirable. We therefore hope that the protagonist will fail but not only that. We also hope that they will learn a lesson from it. From the beginning of the second act of Casablanca, we hope that Rick (Humphrey Bogart) will not manage to remain neutral and thus will overcome his selfishness. From the beginning of the internal second act of Citizen Kane, we feel that Kane (Orson Welles) will not manage to do as he pleases and we hope that he will grow from this failure. Same for Perrin (The Toy), above, with his porn film. We do not wish for him to succeed and hope he will find his girlfriend again.

This means that you can very well give your protagonist an illegitimate dramatic objective. This type of story works. But when you determine your protagonist's dramatic objective, it is imperative that you are aware of the effect this objective will have on the audience: hope or disapproval. Basically, this comes down to knowing whether you give your protagonist reason or not to have such or such objective.

E. THE STAKES
The stakes, a powerful factor of identification, are what the protagonist has to lose or gain at the end of the action. Nine times out of ten, whether in life or in fiction writing, people confuse stakes with the objective. And this for a simple reason: the expression "The stakes" has become synonymous with "The most important" or "What counts." "The stakes are to save the planet." "The stakes are to win the next match." "The stakes," explained this firefighter in a TV report, "are to stop the forest fire before it reaches the house." He was actually talking about the objective. The stakes, in this case, were the house itself. When you make a bet, the stakes are not to win the bet, the stakes are precisely what you put at stake: money, a good meal at a restaurant, etc. In these examples, the confusion between stakes and objective is relatively harmless. But sometimes it costs dearly.

Confusing stakes with motivation or stakes with objective is indeed the best way to get confused, to deprive oneself of reflection on the stakes (the real ones) and to miss the mark. In The Finishers, a fifty-year-old (Jacques Gamblin) agrees to run an Ironman triathlon carrying his paraplegic son (Fabien Héraud) during the race. If you define the stakes as having to finish the race on time, you might think you have an exciting action. For the protagonist, finishing an Ironman under these conditions is an extremely difficult objective to achieve. But the stakes are not the objective. In the specific case of this story, the stakes are identified by answering the following question: "What does the protagonist lose if he does not finish the race or does not finish it on time?" And, unfortunately for the interest of the film, the answer is: nothing. Because, even before the start of the race, the protagonist has already gained the essential - in this case, he has already regained his son's affection. Note that the answer could not be null. By running this Ironman, the father could lose his self-esteem or the new relationship he has with his son or serenity (versus guilt). But these stakes are not exploited in the film. The authors should have conducted a reflection on the stakes instead of believing they were safe because their protagonist had a difficult objective to achieve.

The stakes can therefore be life, survival, health (physical or mental), a child, a family, a job, power, a seat, a career, a sum of money, a property, rights, the future, supremacy, credibility, honor, reputation, dignity, freedom, appearances, a relationship, a friendship, balance, well-being, peace of mind (versus fear or guilt), etc.

Be aware of your protagonist's stakes. And, if possible, do everything to increase the stakes. If, for example, you want to tell the story of a Russian conductor who is hired to conduct a concert and who requests that the soloist be a young woman he has lost touch with but with whom he has family ties, do not do like the authors of The Concert who first make us believe that the soloist in question (Mélanie Laurent) is the conductor's daughter (Aleksey Guskov) to later reveal that she is only the daughter of a friend of the conductor. If the conductor is indeed the father of the soloist, there are more stakes for everyone, the father, the daughter, and the audience. This simply makes the story stronger, more exciting, and... more meaningful.

Conflicts without stakes
Is a dynamic conflict necessarily underpinned by stakes? It is tempting to think so. It seems logical that a living organism undertaking a difficult action necessarily has something to lose or gain in the operation. And yet, some conflicts give the impression of the opposite. This is the case with routine conflicts like psychological games. A violent couple's argument, for example, identical to yesterday's argument and tomorrow's, serves more to maintain a toxic balance than to allow one of the two parties to escape it. And since this is unfortunately often effective, the stakes perceived by the audience are low.

There are also few stakes in investigation stories. Not because they lack conflict. One can even imagine a very difficult investigation, at the end of which the investigator fails. But because the investigator often has little to gain or lose in the operation. Even their pride can come out unscathed. An investigator knows that their success rate cannot be 100%. In investigation stories, there is often a strong stake for the victim's relatives. Unfortunately, they are rarely the protagonists. The first season of Broadchurch is eloquent on this subject. At the beginning of the series, I identify with the mother of the murdered child. She is devastated, and I share her pain. Thanks to Jodie Whittaker, who plays the mother superbly and has two or three poignant scenes. What interests me then is not knowing who killed. The resolution of the mystery does not excite me. I want justice to be done. And above all, for the mother to grieve, find peace. This feeling is slightly reinforced by the fact that, as long as the culprit is not caught, the child cannot be buried. Unfortunately, the mother is not the protagonist. As the series progresses, her character fades in favor of a classic investigation.

Another case. Imagine an individual whose objective is to find a job and who goes through a job interview. It does not go smoothly. The recruiter asks embarrassing questions, casts doubts on their CV, etc. On their side, the protagonist defends themselves, argues, shows their motivation. In short, there is a nice balanced dynamic conflict. And there is clearly a stake: a job. Is that enough? Perhaps not. If the author forgot to make us understand how important this job is, if we do not feel the protagonist's despair, the last chance aspect, the scene will seem to lack stakes. All this to say that conflict may not suffice. Sometimes the stakes need to be made explicit.

In summary, if there is conflict, there are necessarily stakes for the characters experiencing the conflict but not necessarily in sufficient quantity and not necessarily for the audience. It should be noted that the reverse is also possible: a lot of stakes but little conflict. For this, it is enough to motivate an objective well and then forget to put obstacles.

Staging the stakes
Often, the stakes are quite clear in the scenes that present the life routine, the inciting incident, and the protagonist's objective. But not always. It may be a good idea to think about creating a stakes scene. This is one of the functions of the first scene of Where Is the Friend's Home?. Among other things, the teacher (Kheda Barech Defai) reminds his students that homework must be done in a notebook under penalty of expulsion. This scene serves to motivate Ahmad's (Babak Ahmadpoor) future objective: to return his friend's lost notebook. The protagonist could lose a friend, who could outright lose his schooling.

The action of Lagaan is set in India in 1893. Peasants come to ask the local raja (Kulbhushan Kharbanda) to exempt them from taxes ("lagaan" in Hindi) for the coming year, due to the drought that has devastated their lands. The raja refuses because he is tied hand and foot to the English. They, under the authority of a certain Russell (Paul Blackthorne), then make a proposal. If the peasants beat them at cricket, there will be no tax for two years. However, if they lose, they will have to pay triple. Bhuvan (Aamir Khan), the young peasant leader, hesitates to take up the challenge. Russell raises the stakes: no tax for three years throughout the province if the Indians win. Bhuvan accepts. Then the film's authors offer a second scene, in which Bhuvan defends his position to his entire village and reminds them of the importance of the stakes. Finally, a little later, it is Russell who must explain himself to his superiors, as they find the bet scandalous. It is decided that if Russell wins, nothing more will be said. If he loses, he will have to pay the tax out of his own pocket and will be transferred to Africa. Needless to say, after these three scenes, everything is clear.
What follows is highly suspenseful. Note that the authors don't just support the protagonists' motivations; they also do so with the character who creates obstacles. The same approach is used in The Bridge on the River Kwai, where the main opponent, Colonel Saito (Sessue Hayakawa), has strong motivations: if the bridge he's having built by his prisoners isn't finished on time, he has no choice but to commit suicide.

Reflecting on the stakes always seems necessary to me, even with classic objectives where the stakes are implicit. We all need love, work, security, yet finding love, a job, or security can be more important for some than for others. In A Time for Drunken Horses, Ayoub (Ayoub Ahmadi) seeks to earn money. His harsh survival conditions in Iranian Kurdistan are enough to legitimize his goal. But Bahman Ghobadi strengthens his protagonist's motivation: Ayoub wants to pay for an operation for his deformed and sick brother (Madi Ekhtiar-dini). If Ayoub doesn't find money, his brother will die very soon. In short, in the "Stakes" entry of your foundations, make it clear, in one way or another, how important it is for the protagonist to achieve his goal. Go beyond knowing what your protagonist has to lose or gain; ask yourself what he will experience and do if he fails. "He loses his honor" is too vague. Imagine the consequences.

Unconscious dramatic stakes and trajectory stakes

I just talked about dramatic stakes. This is what motivates the dramatic goal. These stakes can very well be conscious. This is the case in the examples of Lagaan and A Time for Drunken Horses above. The protagonists are fully aware of what they can gain or lose in the operation. This is also the case in The Merchant of Venice. Antonio is quite aware that he could lose a pound of his flesh. But sometimes the stakes are not as clearly conscious for the protagonist. When the stakes are immaterial (freedom, honor, friendship, etc.), they can motivate the protagonist without him realizing it.

Be careful, just because the dramatic stakes can be unconscious doesn't mean they should be confused with what might be called "trajectory stakes." These stakes, conscious or unconscious, motivate the dramatic goal. And the trajectory stakes, necessarily unconscious, will motivate the trajectory goal. Just as all trajectory goals could be summarized as growing or awakening, all trajectory stakes could be summarized as a part of humanity. It is indeed a facet of his humanity that a trajectory protagonist has to lose or gain in his possible transformation. I believe that the trajectory stakes are always evident to the audience. This is not the case with the dramatic stakes, which are discussed in this chapter and which I invite you to mature.

Stakes for other characters

Stories in which the protagonist is a witness, not an actor, of conflicts with strong stakes often fall into a trap because, although the protagonist makes it a personal matter, it is not he who has the most to gain or lose, but other characters. I see this problem in works like The Visitor, Welcome, Call the Midwife, Goya's Ghosts, Intimate Enemies, The Year My Parents Went on Vacation. These works are sometimes appreciated because they denounce humiliations or scandalous living conditions. But, in my opinion, humanism is not enough to make a story captivating. Once you've found your intention (see chapter 2), eliciting emotional and not just intellectual identification from the audience is greatly beneficial.

First act stakes

The stakes I just mentioned are second act stakes. Sometimes the author makes us understand how much it costs his character to set a particular goal. In other words, the author stages a first act stake. Imagine, for example, an extremely home-loving character. Something happens that urges him to leave his slippers. You feel that the character is torn. Finally, he decides to go on an adventure. You can understand that it costs him to adopt such a goal. In this case, he must sacrifice his little comfort and tranquility. This is a first act stake. That said, once the second act has begun, you can't keep reminding us that your protagonist embarked on the action reluctantly or that he lost a lot by setting a goal. The first act stake can therefore be interesting to show the protagonist's motivations or characterization. In no way can it replace the second act stake.

Characters other than the protagonist

A good part of the work of characterizing secondary characters is identical to that concerning the protagonist. We have seen that the choice of protagonist is not arbitrary. It is made according to the meaning you want to give to your story. The same should apply to other characters who must all serve the action in one way or another. A beautiful character that pleases you but serves no real purpose must be ruthlessly eliminated (and carefully preserved for another work).

Create useful secondary characters, either because they logically belong to the protagonist's universe, because they advance the action (obstacle or aid), or because they combine the theme. In particular, imagine which characters will push your protagonist the most to his limits, make him choose, and reveal his theme. Try to have the equivalent of Stanley Kowalski in A Streetcar Named Desire. Through his primitive strength, authenticity, suspicions, and intransigence, Stanley twists the knife in Blanche's wound, the protagonist.

In The Journey of Mr. Perrichon, there is a debtor character whose purpose may seem unclear at first glance. It is Majorin, a bitter type who borrows 600 francs from Perrichon while hating him. In fact, it's a thematic subplot that mirrors Perrichon's. Majorin can't stand being a debtor. Just like Perrichon, who prefers to serve than to receive a service. Moreover, at the end, when Majorin returns the 600 francs, Perrichon—who has understood and changed—says: "And to think I was like him." The little Perrichon-Majorin subplot thus serves to enrich the theme and create contrast.

The importance of the goal

"I will fight to keep my little job," says Krogstad, the main opponent in A Doll's House, "as if it were a matter of life or death." The goal, stakes, and motivations are important elements of characterization. Generally, the question doesn't arise for the protagonist, who necessarily carries a general goal. This is not the case for secondary characters and, even worse, tertiary characters. Too often, secondary characters hang around without a determined purpose, and their utilitarian side is glaring.

Give a goal, conscious or unconscious, to all your secondary characters (see chapter 1). It's the best way to give them existence. Not only must a secondary character have a goal, but unlike the protagonist, he has the right to have several during the action. That said, if you give a secondary character a single general goal, you have a better chance of making him identifiable and even credible. The fact that he is allowed several goals does not authorize you to make him flit about. Always unlike the protagonist, a secondary character has the right not to have obstacles on the road to his goal(s). It's even advisable since the more conflict you make a secondary character experience, the more he risks overshadowing the protagonist.

John Truby [83] cites the example of Dorothy's companions (Judy Garland) in The Wizard of Oz. The lion (Bert Lahr) would like to have courage. The scarecrow (Ray Bolger) would like to possess a brain. And the tin man (Jack Haley) desires a heart. In reality, these three characters have more of a conscious dramatic need than a true goal. Because they do nothing to achieve their respective goals. In a way, their main character trait is wishfulness, softness. In short, not only must you give one or more goals to your secondary characters, but you must also make them active to make them exist.

In my opinion, the only two three-dimensional female characters in all of Francis Veber's work are Christine (Miou-Miou) in We Will All Be Famous and Christine (Anny Duperey) in The ComDads. It's no coincidence that they both have a strong goal that they do everything to achieve. They are fighters (see above). In We Will All Be Famous, Christine seeks to discourage her fiancé (Pierre Richard) from making a pornographic film because she thinks he's getting lost in it. She is present throughout the second act, and those who know the film know she doesn't spare her efforts. In The ComDads, Christine is the protagonist of the first act. She wants to find her runaway son (Stéphane Bierry), travels to Nice to investigate, and doesn't hesitate, to achieve her goal, to manipulate two former friends (Pierre Richard, Gérard Depardieu) by telling each that he is the father.

A matter of coherence

The characters in a story have their own lives, whose history you must know (see chapter 1). This is true for all your characters, including the most secondary ones. Even if you don't tell your story in order, even if you show only a few elements of this or that secondary character, even if you have a unique omnipresent protagonist and don't make a choral work, even if you hide certain narrative elements from the audience, as an author, you must know where all your characters come from, what their inciting incidents are, their goals, and their motivations. Without you necessarily realizing it, this will give truth and credibility to your characters and coherence to your story. Not to mention that it will greatly help the actors who will have to embody these characters.

False ally and false adversary

John Truby [83] mentions two types of characters that deserve attention: the false ally and the false adversary. The false ally is a character who starts by helping the protagonist and turns out to be a source of obstacles. The false adversary is the opposite character.

Be careful, in both cases, you must play the surprise card (for the audience as well as for the protagonist) rather than that of mystery or dramatic irony; otherwise, the adjective "false" is not justified. Thus, unlike Truby, I don't think Rosencrantz and Guildenstern are false allies in Hamlet. Because we know from the start that they are spying for Claudius. For the audience, they are immediately perceived as sources of obstacles. And even for Hamlet, who quickly sees through their game. On the other hand, Mrs. Linde is a nice false ally in A Doll's House. The same goes for the Nurse in Romeo and Juliet. Or the mayor (Thomas Mitchell) in High Noon, who receives an ironic "Thank you!" from the protagonist (Gary Cooper). Stella, Blanche's sister, is a local false ally in scene 4 of A Streetcar Named Desire.

Note that the least interesting false ally is the traitor. A beautiful false ally is a character who creates obstacles for the protagonist not because he turns his coat (out of venality, revenge, or perversion) but because his personal interests lead him to stray from the protagonist's path and hinder him.

For obvious reasons, the false adversary is more delicate to handle than the false ally. Because the false adversary ends up transforming into an aid. You must therefore justify it. This is what, for example, the authors of The Man Who Knew Too Much do with Mrs. Drayton (Brenda De Banzie). Or Hergé with Wolff in Explorers on the Moon or the authors of Tangled with the horse Maximus. But if you don't do preparatory work, you risk creating the same kind of disappointment as that produced by Colonel Landa (Christoph Waltz), the Jewish hunter in Inglourious Basterds. For much of the film, he embodies a magnificent villain, calm, smiling, and ruthless. And then when he has one of the protagonists (Brad Pitt) at his mercy, he turns his coat. Surprise, certainly, but deus ex machina. If you have to choose, it's better for the false adversary to be less surprising but more logical.

The Harry Potter series, which is full of false allies and false adversaries, offers over its entire duration a very beautiful false adversary. I won't say more for those who don't know, but know that he generates obstacles, that the revelation of his role is well brought, that everything contributes to making this false adversary particularly successful, up to the actor who plays him in the film adaptation.

Don't confuse the false ally with the temporary ally. In The Good, the Bad and the Ugly, Blondie (Clint Eastwood) travels a bit with Tuco (Eli Wallach) and then with Angel Eyes (Lee Van Cleef) because it's in his interest at the moment. But he knows that at the end of the road, they will become adversaries again. In these cases where one of the characters acts as a circumstantial co-protagonist, there is no surprise.

One of the reasons why Hannibal Lecter (Anthony Hopkins) is such a fascinating character in The Silence of the Lambs is that he is both an ally and an adversary. He helps Clarice (Jodie Foster) find the serial killer she's looking for, and at the same time, he puts her in a bind since he takes the opportunity to escape. He should also put her in danger when she starts revealing intimate elements about herself, but this announcement is unfortunately not exploited (see explanations in Dramaturgy [51]). That said, we can't say that Hannibal Lecter is a false ally or a false adversary. Because the protagonist and the audience are warned from the start of the role he is supposed to play. The same goes for Gustavo Fring (Giancarlo Esposito) in Breaking Bad. He is both an ally and an adversary of the protagonist, but there is no surprise. The adjective "false" does not apply to him either.

An adult vision of humanity

All characters who reveal themselves to be something other than unwavering support or persistent opposition for the protagonist will help you give an adult dimension to your story. Whether in life or fiction, we like to adore or hate. It's so comfortable. Individuals who have great qualities but also major flaws are often embarrassing. What to think of them? In which box to put them? How to love someone who has such big flaws? Yes, but how to hate someone who still has such beautiful qualities? Have you noticed that when a dear friend plays a dirty trick on you, it's more comfortable to break ties completely than to continue seeing them? In short, we often adopt a Manichean attitude towards others, similar to that of a 4-year-old child towards fairy tale characters. Creating nuanced characters who do both good and evil is like giving an adult image of reality.

Duos and trios

Main or secondary characters sometimes come in pairs or trios. If you adopt this formula, especially take the opportunity to characterize them differently. Don't do like the authors of The Sons of the Desert or Three Men and a Cradle. Do rather like the author of The ComDads or No Exit.

If the main opponent is a trio, take advantage of it to make two similar characters and one different. This third element can even turn out to be a false adversary. Giving the same characterization to three antagonistic secondary characters is unnecessary; two are enough. Symbolically, it even makes much more sense when the opponents form a duo of twins (see Lady and the Tramp, Desire Under the Elms, or King Lear).

The case of the four Dalton Cousins in Lucky Luke deserves comment. From a narrative point of view, three bandits would have sufficed, but the real Dalton Brothers were four—five, in truth, but only four became criminals. Morris therefore respected his historical sources in Outlaws. It is interesting to note that the Dalton Cousins are four but have the characterization of a trio: Joe is the most vicious and clever, William and Jack are almost identical followers, while Averell is deeply gluttonous and stupid.

Character and amalgam

In real life, if you are robbed one day by an individual belonging to a visible minority, you will tend to fear most men of the same type afterward, without distinction. It's unfair and irrational, but that's how the human brain works. It's an animal protection. This kind of amalgam also operates in fiction. The characters in a story are often perceived by the audience as archetypes (not in the Jungian sense of the term, rather in the sense of prototypes). Again, it's unfortunate, but you must take it into account. If, for example, the only villain in a story is homosexual, the audience will interpret it as the author's point of view: "Homosexuals are all villains, and even, all villains are homosexuals." This is how the controversy around Basic Instinct arose, in which the criminal is bisexual. You must be aware of this phenomenon and be cautious. In any case, at a minimum, make your characterization match your thoughts and values. Which brings us back to the statements in chapter 2.

On the number of characters

I invite you not to include too many characters unless it's the subject of the work. If, in literature, one can say that the great basic story is a human being facing himself, in dramaturgy, two human beings facing each other are both necessary and sufficient. One can tell The Stranger in literature or comics. In theater or cinema, it's more difficult. Moreover, the very rare theatrical or cinematic works with a single character still have dialogues. The interlocutor can be on the phone (The Human Voice) or in the audience (The Double Bass). When Chuck (Tom Hanks), in Cast Away, finds himself on a deserted island, he creates an imaginary companion using a volleyball. In Jeremiah Johnson, the protagonist (Robert Redford) goes to live alone in the Rocky Mountains. Apart from a sequence in the middle of the story where he settles with a Native American woman (Delle Bolton) and a boy (Josh Albee), we understand through the use of ellipses that solitude is his daily lot. Nevertheless, not five minutes of film go by without him encountering a Native American, a trapper, a soldier, or a settler, and exchanging with them.

In 1928, Murnau [58] declared: "One of my dreams is to make a film in a single room (...) there would be nothing to distract us from the drama taking place between a handful of human beings." When we read these words, we think of Strindberg (Miss Julie, The Father, etc.) or his spiritual son: Bergman (Scenes from a Marriage, The Silence, Persona, Autumn Sonata, etc.). But they are not the only ones. Works in which two humans face each other are numerous, varied, and quite often successful: Antigone, Kiss of the Spider Woman, The Bidochon, Célimène and the Cardinal, The Cat, The Navigator's Cruise, The Odd Couple, A Strange Place to Meet, Duel, Hell in the Pacific, The Odd Job, Green Card, Strangers on a Train, Sleuth, Misery, The Bread of Friendship, For No Good Reason, Rain Man, The African Queen, The Savage, The Sun Above the Clouds, A Special Day, Planes, Trains and Automobiles, etc.

With two human beings, sitting across from each other, in a hotel room or a restaurant, there is plenty to write a beautiful two-hour work. But it's not easy to exploit a situation, to do milking, and since most authors are afraid to face this difficulty, they add characters, sets, tricks, and gadgets. Better to have few well-exploited characters than a parade of silhouettes.

Motivations, sub-goals, and means

After "Where were you on the night of the crime?" and "You have a choice," the third most present dialogue in the repertoire is probably "Why...?" : "Why are you asking me that?", "Why didn't you tell me anything?"... "Why are you chasing me?" Frank (Henry Fonda) essentially asks Harmonica (Charles Bronson) at the end of Once Upon a Time in the West. I'm not sure that fiction aims to teach us to clarify mysteries or to prove that free will exists. However, I think one of the essential goals of fiction is to allow us to discover the backstage of the human psyche. And this is undoubtedly one of the reasons why works whose outcome we know still manage to arouse interest. Certainly, we know that Joan of Arc ended up at the stake, but we want to know why and how. Yes, we know that So-and-So ended up killing his wife and children, but we want to understand his motivations. This is why The Program disappointed me. I knew Lance Armstrong had doped. I wanted to understand his motivations. Why does he embark on this organized fraud? What pleasure, what pride can he derive from winning races by cheating? How can one look in the mirror when reaching that point? Unfortunately, the film doesn't answer these questions. It's just a cold sequence of facts.

In every story, there are several levels of goals. There is first what could be called level zero. It's the protagonist's general goal, the one that defines the second act, thus the action. Oedipal's general goal is to find Laius's murderer (Oedipus Rex). Indiana Jones's general goal is to retrieve the Ark of the Covenant (The Adventures of Indiana Jones).
The Raiders of the Lost Ark. Cyrano's general objective is to woo Roxane (Cyrano de Bergerac).

Upstream of this general objective are higher-level objectives that can be described as motivations. Conscious motivations and, if we dig deeper, unconscious motivations. Oedipal is motivated by the pain of his people suffering from the plague. More profoundly, he needs to discover his true identity. Indiana Jones obeys the government that has given him a mission. But he is also motivated by adventure. He is addicted to the adrenaline that treasure hunts provide. Cyrano wants to be loved. He would also like to forget the nose that burdens him, to be loved for his soul and nothing else. In other words, he would like to no longer be tormented by this complex, to finally have peace of heart. You will note that all these motivations are emotional. Whether in life or fiction, humans sometimes explain their motivations. Most often, it is rationalization. If you give intellectual motivations to a character, make sure that emotional motivations are felt behind them.

Downstream of the general objective, we find sub-objectives and sub-sub-objectives. One of Oedipal's sub-objectives is to obtain insight from Tiresias. A sub-objective of Indiana Jones is to retrieve a medallion that will allow him to precisely locate the Ark. A sub-objective of this sub-objective is to prevent the evil Nazi (Ronald Lacey) from torturing Marion (Karen Allen). Cyrano's general sub-objective is to use Christian, and one of his sub-sub-objectives is to write letters on Christian's behalf.

As we can see, the objective unfolds like a set of Russian dolls, with the general dramatic objective as the intermediate doll. We return, once again, to the fractal principle.

This reflection on the different levels of objective can be useful. Because sometimes an author confuses the general objective with its motivation. This can lead to having a general objective that is too vague, not specific enough, or not respecting the unity of action. Regarding this last point, you know that the general objective must be unique. It is one of the essential factors of the unity of action. It is equally important that uniqueness applies to the protagonist's conscious motivation. Two different conscious motivations risk giving a whimsical image of the protagonist and damaging identification.

What is the difference between stakes and motivation? A stake always serves as motivation. The stakes in The Old Lady's Money are Peppino's (Alberto Sordi) financial comfort, his children's education, his daughter's health (Antonella Di Maggio), and his sister's safety (Luciana Lehar). These stakes strongly motivate Peppino to earn money against the old billionaire (Bette Davis). However, a motivation is not always a stake. A character can be motivated by an emotion or a super-objective (see above). In The Sting, Hooker (Robert Redford) wants to take revenge on Lonnegan (Robert Shaw) who had his partner killed. He is motivated by anger. A super-objective of Phil Connors in Groundhog Day is probably to return to his well-armored life in Pittsburgh and no longer have to deal with these "hicks." Of course, there is always a link between stake, emotion, and super-objective.

The reasonable contradictor of the dramatic protagonist

The protagonist's motivation contributes to characterizing him and always influences the spectator's empathy. If a character's objective is to rob a bank to pay for a bone marrow transplant for his little sister, he will not be perceived the same way as if his objective is to rob a bank to buy drugs.

That said, you can give questionable motivations to your protagonist and make us understand that you disapprove of his objective. A protagonist has the right to go astray, to have a wrong objective. We talked about this earlier. In this case, I suggest creating a character who thinks like the spectator. This is the role of Kent/Caius in King Lear, Sganarelle in Don Juan, M. Jourdain in The Bourgeois Gentleman, Christine (Miou-Miou) in We Will Have Seen Everything (see page 157), Sally in The Nightmare Before Christmas, or more discreetly, Sam (Dooley Wilson) and Carl (S.Z. Sakall), the two confidant employees of Rick (Humphrey Bogart) in Casablanca. The principle is the same as for the contradictor of a trajectorial protagonist (see pages 192-193). Creating a reasonable character in relation to the protagonist has several advantages:
1 - it facilitates the spectator's adherence who does not think that the author approves of a protagonist with questionable motivations;
2 - it is an exploitation of dramatic irony, diffuse or frank;
3 - it allows explaining the protagonist's motivations.

In general, an intellectual (but not emotional) identification is created with a disapproving character: we feel sympathy and understanding for him, but rarely empathy since he is not the protagonist and experiences little conflict.

Specific sub-objectives for an active protagonist

The reflection on sub-objectives forces you to make your protagonist active, thus avoiding a classic trap, that of the passive protagonist. Because it is not enough for a protagonist to have a great objective, he must also desperately seek to achieve it. He must be active or reactive. With conflict and stakes, the protagonist's determination is a powerful factor of identification.
