The choice of sub-objectives also depends on the characterization of the protagonist. Faced with conflict, some choose flight, others confrontation. Others still choose manipulation. A protagonist can choose an inappropriate sub-objective at first, realize it during the story, and change tactics.

Collateral sub-objectives

Think of a type of sub-objectives that are a bit special: those that do not directly depend on the general objective but are consequences of the action undertaken by the protagonist. Often, when a character starts an action, he sets an entire universe in motion, breaking certain balances. He must then manage not only his original objective but also what he has set up or disrupted. This adds obligations to him. In adventure films, for example, the protagonist may have the general objective of finding a treasure or a missing person. Very quickly, he has the collateral sub-objective of saving his life. In many comedies, the protagonist has the collateral sub-objective of hiding something from other characters.

H. THE FIRST ACT-SECOND ACT TRANSITION

The first act-second act transition is the moment when the action begins. It is generally at this moment that the protagonist's objective is known or perceived by the spectator.

When there is a declaration of objective, the first act-second act transition often follows immediately. In Hamlet, Hamlet clearly says: "Only your command (N.B. to avenge myself) will live in the book of my memory" (1/5). Then he makes Horatio and Marcellus swear to say nothing and begins to feign madness. Hamlet has just taken action. In Pygmalion, Higgins says: "I will make a duchess out of this street urchin." Immediately, he orders Mrs. Pearce to wash Eliza. In The Great Escape, the Allied leader (Richard Attenborough) gathers all the camp leaders and launches his grand project: to make two hundred and fifty prisoners escape. We even have a declaration of sub-objectives: build three tunnels, dig to such a depth, pretend to garden, prepare papers and travel documents, etc. Immediately, the co-protagonists get started. Two of them place an order with the scrounger (James Garner).

Sometimes, the protagonist does not clearly declare his objective, but the spectator understands it unambiguously. In Tootsie, an artistic agent (Sydney Pollack) explains to Michael (Dustin Hoffman) that he has become unmanageable as an actor and that no one wants to work with him anymore, neither on Broadway nor in Hollywood. In the next shot, we discover Michael disguised as a woman in the streets of New York. There is no doubt for the spectator that the second act has just begun. Note, in this example, that the objective is actually declared by the agent but in the negative: "No one will hire you."

The first act-second act transition is a dramatic knot too often neglected by authors, even though it is an extraordinary tool for audience participation. This moment is always felt as a trigger in the spectator's unconscious who says to himself: "Ah, there it is, it's started." The declaration of objective, explicit or implicit, is nothing less than the greatest preparation of an entire story - the greatest payoff being the climax. When an objective is declared, a dramatic question is automatically created in the spectator's mind. Suspense is launched. It is absurd and even a mistake to deprive oneself of a declaration of objective or a scene of transition from the first act to the second act. This will strengthen your story and... the audience's participation.

I. THE ARENA

We talked about it in chapter 4, the pitch often contains the arena. It is the environment in which the protagonist will evolve during the second act. But it cannot just be a setting intended to be pretty or original. It must be an omnipresent and above all meaningful setting. In short, the arena is a specific universe (place + time) that tells something.

Attention, the arena that counts is the one in which the protagonist will live the action, therefore the one of the second act. It is not necessarily the environment of his routine life. In Time After Time, H.G. Wells (Malcolm McDowell) lives in 19th-century England. But the film's arena is 1979 San Francisco. In some action films, the first act's arena is the training camp, and the second act's arena is the field of operations.

If your protagonist moves from an office today to a living room, from a living room to a street, from a street to a restaurant, from a restaurant to an office, you do not have a spectacular arena. Yet, it is worth thinking about. Even if your arena is mundane, you cannot tell a story that takes place in any office, any street, any restaurant. Your arena must be specific. This will give unity to the work, a personality to the story, and preparation elements to pay off. In short, it will add meaning and connection to your story. The arena also allows:
- to create contrast. In Jaws, the shark's arena, the open sea, echoes that of humans, the seaside resort;
- to add a symbolic dimension. King Kong also has two arenas that respond to each other: the wild jungle of Skull Island and the urban jungle of New York;
- to make the subject original. This joins the sometimes documentary or even spectacular dimension of the arena. In the TV series City of Men, the arena is both a place (a poor and violent favela) and a way of life, exotic and frightening for spectators who are lucky enough to live in comfort. Some theater or opera directors stage classics by changing the arena. Macbeth will thus leave his medieval Scotland to land in the contemporary office of a multinational, and Aida will move from the Egypt of the pharaohs to an Orwellian futuristic society. This often has an artificial side because the text was not written for another arena, so it does not exploit it;
- to characterize certain characters. Brochant's luxurious apartment (Thierry Lhermitte) in The Dinner Game is a significant setting - which Veber exploits. The Nautilus in Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea is closely linked to the personality and history of Captain Nemo, a misanthrope who finds peace only at the bottom of the sea, far from humans;
- to reinforce the sensation of evolution. A number of TV series are set in a time of change (Boardwalk Empire, Mad Men, Rome...). Downton Abbey exploits this atmosphere of the end of the aristocratic reign a lot. An evolving arena tends to push characters to do the same.

Exploit the arena

An arena must be exploited, even milked. As Hitchcock [41] said about espionage in The 39 Steps, if you set your film in Switzerland, you must use chocolate, clocks, banks, and mountains. Otherwise, you can stay in New Jersey. "It must be more than just a backdrop. You have to try to use all these local elements dramatically."

We can easily imagine how to exploit an original arena when it represents an environment little known to the spectator. But how to exploit a mundane arena, known to all? Half of the "fish out of water" type stories, for example, have an unusual arena (an insane asylum in One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest or an Amish community in Witness). But when it is a foreigner who lands in an environment we know well (cf. E.T. or the second part of Crocodile Dundee), the arena is unusual for the foreigner but not for the spectator. This can nevertheless lead to exploitation if care is taken to stage the gap between the "fish's" manners and the environment in which he evolves. In Time After Time, for example, H.G. Wells (Malcolm McDowell) arrives from 19th-century London in 1979 San Francisco. He orders a hamburger in a fast food restaurant and discovers their fries with amazement in front of a table neighbor who is dismayed. The same principle applies in 3rd Rock from the Sun, whose arena is our contemporary human terrestrial society. Nothing very original. Except for the protagonists who are extraterrestrials with human appearances and who react in a shifted way to everything that makes up our life, from cars to disposable tissues, from menstruation to mortality.

Before even being exploited, it is possible that your arena requires research work if it is set in the past or on the other side of the planet, or even creation work if it is a fictional arena. Science fiction authors often do considerable work to create and present their arena.

Some arenas logically derive from the subject: a peasant village in The Seven Samurai, a dilapidated and isolated sawmill in the Vosges in The Big Mouths, a submarine during World War II in Das Boot, a lost town in Pennsylvania in winter in Groundhog Day.

Sometimes, the arena is more than an original setting. It is significance. The arena of the second part of Life Is Beautiful (1997) is a concentration camp. It is an arena all the more important because it is the subject of dramatic irony. The father (Roberto Benigni) makes his son (Giorgio Cantarini) believe that the arena is a summer camp.

The sub-arena

An arena can include one or more sub-arenas. These are moments in the story that have a specific and original setting. In Ben-Hur, the general arena is the Near East at the beginning of our era with Roman domination, slavery, beliefs, etc. There are several sub-arenas: the Roman galley, the valley of the lepers, etc. In The Walking Dead, the arena is North America after a violent epidemic that has turned the dead into zombies. The authors must have quickly realized that they could not have their protagonists wander in the countryside for several seasons. Quite quickly, they offer sub-arenas: a penitentiary, a secured neighborhood but governed by an autocrat, a den of cannibals, a hospital run by police officers, an ecological neighborhood, each of these sub-arenas having very specific rules of life. Like the arena, the sub-arena must be defined and then exploited.

J. OBSTACLES

Unless the pitch already gives an idea, it is time to think about the type of obstacles your protagonist will encounter. As I develop at length in Dramaturgy [51], the most adult and enriching obstacles are internal or internally originated obstacles. So, take an interest in all the obstacles that can be generated, directly or indirectly, by the protagonist himself. A first track is to exploit his flaws. Another possible obstacle is the co-protagonist, when there is one.

An apparently purely external obstacle can be perceived as internally originated if we feel that this obstacle is deserved. This is the case of the curse that falls on Phil Connors (Bill Murray) in Groundhog Day. At first glance, he is not directly or indirectly responsible, but we understand that there is a reason for this ordeal to affect him and not someone else. The same process in Casablanca. When Ilsa (Ingrid Bergman) enters his café, Rick (Humphrey Bogart) curses fate: "Of all the gin joints in all the towns in all the world, she walks into mine." We know 1 - that fate sometimes does things well, especially when it is called the author and is competent, 2 - that it serves Rick right. He needs this ordeal to grow.

Imagine an element that makes your protagonist's world collapse. Often, it is intimate: his convictions, his motivations, his beliefs. Imagine something that twists the knife in your protagonist's wound. Plunge your protagonist into an unprecedented situation, imagine an ordeal he has never experienced in his life.

Consider a countdown

Many stories work without urgency, but this phenomenon is so effective that one of the recommended tricks for Hollywood screenwriters is called "Start the clock." It consists of introducing urgency into a scene or sequence that lacks nerve. It can be a bomb that will explode at a certain time (Dr. Strangelove, Fail Safe, 24 Hours (season 2) or WarGames) or an antagonist who seeks to reach the same objective before the protagonist (cf. the endings of The Shooting Star and Strangers on a Train). It can also be more subtle. For example, in a scene where a character must announce something delicate to a second character, you can add a train, which leaves in two minutes, or a third character who will soon arrive and must not hear anything. It can even be funnier. At the end of A Fish Called Wanda, Archie (John Cleese) tries to catch fugitives. He is in a hurry. But he doesn't know where they went. He inquires with Ken (Michael Palin) who stutters and can't get the information out. To put him at ease, Archie takes it upon himself and assures Ken that he has all the time in the world!

As we can see, this process can be used on a local scale, in a scene, semi-local, in a sequence, or throughout the action. That's why it can be a good idea to think about it at the foundation stage. In A Distant Neighborhood, the protagonist has four months to prevent his father from abandoning his family. This urgency covers a good part of the second act of the manga.

What is called the "villain" can also be an external obstacle of internal origin. This is, in my opinion, the condition that makes the most beautiful villains. Speaking of villains, we all remember Alfred Hitchcock's famous quote [41]: "The more successful the villain, the more successful the film." At the end of his interviews with Truffaut, Hitchcock nevertheless modifies this precept to: "The stronger the evil, the more relentless the struggle and the better the film." I generalize further: the better the quality of the obstacles (external but also internal), the better the work will be.

You are not obliged to have a villain (nor an antagonist, cf. below). Many successful stories have fierce opponents, but they do not necessarily do evil (Creon, for example, in Antigone). That said, if you want to have an unbearable villain, you can be inspired by the following models:
- Iago in Othello,
- the Church in The Life of Galileo,
- Bruno Antony (Robert Walker) in Strangers on a Train,
- Harry Powell (Robert Mitchum) in The Night of the Hunter,
- Max Cady (Robert Mitchum) in Cape Fear (1962),
- Tullius Detritus in The Zizanie,
- Nurse Ratched (Louise Fletcher) in One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest,
- Jack the Ripper (David Warner) in Time After Time,
- Annie Wilkes (Kathy Bates) in Misery,
- Peyton Flanders (Rebecca de Mornay) in The Hand That Rocks the Cradle.

These characters are indeed villains, in the sense that they are both sources of obstacles for the protagonist and that they do evil. Including Nurse Ratched. At first, Nurse Ratched mainly gives the impression of being strict. But the terrible scene where she symbolically castrates Billy Bibbit (Brad Dourif) leaves no ambiguity about her capacity to do evil. You will note that they have several common characteristics: 1 - they are the antagonists of their respective stories. That is to say, they concentrate all the external obstacles in their person. N.B. Once again, having an antagonist is not an obligation in a story. The sources of obstacles can be diverse. In North by Northwest, for example, the CIA is as responsible for Roger's (Cary Grant) misfortunes as Vandamm (James Mason). In A Doll's House, Nora has two very different opponents: Krogstad and Helmer. But, by giving the impression of making the central conflict a personal matter, an antagonist can be an interesting option.
2 - They are not primitive brutes or psychopaths like the shark in Jaws or Amon Goeth (Ralph Fiennes in Schindler's List). They are not overtly evil in a vulgar way. They can be smiling, honey-tongued. They even sometimes have charm. And also, very importantly, they have understandable motivations;

3 - They are inventive and clever. One could even say, in the cases of Iago, Max Cady, Tullius Destructus, and Peyron Flanders, that their creativity has something diabolical about it. If you combine characteristics 2 and 3, you see that a well-crafted villain can even inspire admiration;

4 - And above all, they do not attack just anyone. Even if none of the corresponding protagonists deserves to suffer so much, they do not have their villain on their back by chance. We return to the external obstacle of internal origin. In short, design your villain as the sworn enemy of your protagonist, as the ideal person to twist the knife in their wound or as their Mr. Hyde side, as the embodiment of their shadow. This last characteristic is magnificently represented by Bruno Antony.

One last criterion that deserves attention is the ruthless nature of the opponent. This "quality" is illustrated by most of the villains mentioned above and even more by Porter (Lionel Barrymore) in It's a Wonderful Life (1946), the HAL 9000 computer in 2001: A Space Odyssey, the truck driver in Duel, and the killer robot (Arnold Schwarzenegger) in Terminator. These villains give the impression that it is impossible to pity them and that they will go to the end of their interests (or programming) unyieldingly. They are steamrollers.

K. THE CLIMAX

You must conceive the climax as the dramatic knot that provides the dramatic answer. Until the climax, the protagonist has still not reached their goal but is still trying. At the end of the climax, the protagonist no longer has a goal, either because they have achieved it or because they have decided to abandon it. Once the climax is over, we logically enter the third act.

The climax is often a very conflictual and spectacular scene. If the second act had to contain measured and dynamic conflict, this is even truer of the climax.

But the most important criterion is to conclude the action. And even to resolve it. I said earlier that the statement of the objective is the biggest setup of a story. The climax is its biggest payoff. All well-written stories are there to prove it. If there is one author, among others, who has particularly understood this, it is Michael Arndt, the screenwriter of Little Miss Sunshine. In most stories, we know that the climax will conclude the action, but we do not know what exact form it will take. As soon as Little Miss Sunshine begins, we understand that the climax will be the beauty pageant in which Olive (Abigail Breslin) is entered. When the contest begins and we see more professional contestants one after another, our concern for Olive increases. We fear she will embarrass herself and wonder how her screenwriter and she will manage. We might even fear that the author will use a deus ex machina. I will not say more to avoid spoiling the discovery of this delightful climax for those who have not yet seen the film.

Another announced and remarkable climax is that of Flashforward. The triggering incident of the series is a moment of two minutes and seventeen seconds during which all of humanity loses consciousness, and each individual sees what their future will be in six months. An FBI investigation begins. Of course, it lasts six months, and the climax is the moment when everyone, including the audience, checks if the events glimpsed during the blackout occur as expected. We hope for some characters, we dread for others. A great moment, treated in the last two episodes of the series.

The climax is also often a moment of all or nothing, a sort of ultimate attempt to achieve the goal. It can also be a moment of choice, sometimes even staging a Cornelian dilemma.

Let's examine the climax of Cyrano de Bergerac, which possesses all the aforementioned qualities. At the end of Act I, we understand that Cyrano's goal is to be loved by Roxane, but he fears she will laugh in his face. This declaration announces the climax. Unconsciously, the audience imagines a scene in which either Cyrano seduces Roxane or he finally understands that he will never succeed. The scene in question arrives at the end of Act IV. By writing poems and hiding behind Christian's appearance, Cyrano has managed to make his soul loved, to make Roxane say she would love Christian even if he were ugly, disfigured, grotesque. Cyrano hesitates to reveal everything. But he feels that it is now or never. He finally decides to tell the truth when, as the ultimate obstacle, Le Bret stops him and whispers something in his ear that silences Cyrano forever. This interruption is not a stroke of fate, like an inopportune phone ring in a bad script. In other words, it is not a diabolus ex machina. It is the direct consequence of what Cyrano has set up. Christian has uncovered Cyrano's little game, understood that Roxane does not love him, and has just thrown himself headlong into the raging battle. He is brought back dying. Cyrano then understands that he can no longer speak. Edmond Rostand—who has well understood the principle of a climax—makes him say "It's over" three times, a few dozen seconds apart. Cyrano clearly abandons his goal. End of the second act.

L. THE DRAMATIC ANSWER

With the choice of the protagonist and their goal, the dramatic answer is a crucial tool for giving meaning to a work. Depending on whether the protagonist achieves their goal or not, the story says different things. It is no coincidence that in Hollywood, the question of the "happy ending" is the subject of many discussions. It even gave rise to a film, The Player, which offers a happy but ironic dramatic answer: the protagonist (Tim Robbins) is a Hollywood producer and a murderer, and he gets away with it.

N.B. In general, the dramatic question interests both the protagonist and the audience. Both want to know the dramatic answer. However, it sometimes happens that it is given to one (the audience, of course) and not to the other, as in Citizen Kane or Waiting for Godot. It is up to you to see what makes the most sense for your project.

The vast majority of works offer a dramatic answer. Life Is a Long Quiet River is an exception. The protagonist is Mme. Le Quesnoy (Hélène Vincent). She learns, at the beginning of the story, that one of her children was replaced by another shortly after birth. The one she thought was her daughter (Valérie Lalande) for about ten years is not her biological daughter. This is the triggering incident. Mme. Le Quesnoy is devastated. She then adopts her natural son, Momo (Benoît Magimel), who has been raised in a way quite different from his siblings. This, of course, does not go without friction. Will Mme. Le Quesnoy manage to cope? We will never know. At the end of the film, Momo goes to summer camp, but nothing indicates what will happen when he returns.

Did the screenwriters have something to say? Perhaps not. Certainly, it is not an absolute necessity. Even if, as we saw in Chapter 2, films of "pure entertainment" sometimes convey a point of view, one can write a story for fun. Except that Life Is a Long Quiet River is not an adventure film or a thriller. It poses a fundamental problem, that of nature versus nurture. It is not forbidden to joke about it, quite the contrary, but one must not confuse humor with carelessness. I would have liked to know what the authors had to say personally on such an important subject.

Some will point out that the most painful thing is not ignoring the authors' point of view in Life Is a Long Quiet River, but that the story stops in the middle. The two things are closely linked. Deep down, the audience knows that a story that ends has meaning. And this need for meaning is acute in humans. It is what makes a story without an end unpleasant. In Six Characters in Search of an Author, Pirandello shows that this need can even be felt by characters. The protagonists' drama is not knowing their end. Their life has no meaning.

In short, the mere fact of putting "positive" or "negative" in the entry reserved for the dramatic answer will give an additional dimension to your story and help you know where you are going.

If you plan to provide a dramatic answer in two stages, you are ready to adopt an enriched structure, a process I address a little further down.

M. THE THIRD ACT

In my definition, the third act is the transition between the end of the action and the end of the work. It gives an indication of what the characters will become. Sometimes, it allows for the resolution of a dramatic irony or a subplot that was not resolved in the second act.

If you do not have an enriched structure, your third act should be quite short. Count five or ten minutes. Shakespeare knew well that once his second act (dramatic) was over, it was time to conclude. In Hamlet, the second act logically ends with the death of Claudius (located in the logistical Act V). At that moment, Hamlet has avenged his father, he has achieved his goal. In what follows, we have the death of Laertes, Hamlet's last recommendations to Horatio, Hamlet's death, the announcement that Fortinbras will take power, the announcement of the death of Rosencrantz and Guildenstern, and the assurance that Hamlet's memory will be honored. And all in five minutes!

The three acts

I remind those who have not read The Dramaturgy [51] that my conception of the three acts is not the same as that of most Anglo-Saxon theorists. My definition of the three acts is simple and logical, and even better, it is natural: before the action, during the action, after the action. If you have an action—it is recommended to have one (human and difficult, cf. page 13)—then you necessarily have three acts.

first act	second act	third act
_._,	*,-

triggering incident

climax

For reasons that continue to elude me, Syd Field [28] and all his spiritual children divide the action into two distinct parts: what they call the second act + the first half of what they call the third act. That is, they place the resolution of the action (the climax) in the middle of their third act. The only "interest" of this formula is to advance the end of the second act, thus making it shorter. As if by magic, there is no longer an 80-minute desert to fill. Of course, it is an illusion. It has never made the action easier to create. It only postpones the difficulty. And, as we saw with the analysis of Groundhog Day (pages 108-109), it can even trap the author.

N.B. I am talking here about the only act that matters: the dramatic act. Many American screenplay theorists, and not the least, confuse logistical acts with dramatic acts. They tell you that Four Weddings and a Funeral is a film in five acts. In five logistical acts, yes, but not at all in five dramatic acts. Like the vast majority of stories in the world, Four Weddings and a Funeral contains three dramatic acts. More developments on this subject in Chapter 5 of The Dramaturgy [51].

The enriched structure

You may find that your subject needs a first dramatic answer (at the end of a first climax) before being restarted and providing a second dramatic answer (at the end of a second climax).

first act	second act	third act



triggering incident

climax no. 1
t.i./c.d.c.
climax no. 2


In this case, it is rare for the two answers to be the same. Certainly, it can be entertaining to end on a positive note and then surprise the audience by saying: "Ah, you thought the hero had won, but in fact, the monster is still alive, it's back for a round" and provide a second answer, also positive. However, ending the second act with a negative dramatic answer and inviting the audience to stay attentive for a while longer to provide a second negative dramatic answer seems very dark and depressing. In short, most often, the second answer is the opposite of the first. And most often, it is first negative, then positive. This is the case in The Cameraman, E.T., The Apartment, The Treasure of the Red Rackham, It's a Wonderful Life (1946), etc.

A plot twist preceded by a crucial moment

If you choose the enriched structure, you will need to think about a plot twist that restarts the action at the beginning of the third act and a second climax that will provide the second dramatic answer. Two important rules:

1 - The plot twist—which acts as a triggering incident for the third act—cannot come out of nowhere. You will therefore have some preparatory work to do to make it plausible (and not predictable, of course). In truth, if you have to choose, it is better for this dramatic knot to be plausible than surprising. If I call it a plot twist, it is to avoid telegraphing the enriched structure. The audience should not feel that you are going to restart the action;

2 - If there is one scene not to forget or botch in the configuration of the enriched structure, it is the moment between the end of the second act and the plot twist of the third act. You must clearly show that the action is over. If the first dramatic answer is positive, the protagonist savors their success. If the first dramatic answer is negative, it must be obvious to the audience that the protagonist has abandoned their goal and has begun to come to terms with it. In other words, you must make it clear that we have entered the third act before launching your plot twist.

I insist heavily: do not be vague about the end of the second act. For a while, the protagonist no longer has a goal, either because they believe, and we with them, that they have achieved it, or because they believe, and we with them, that they will no longer be able to achieve it. I write "and we with them" because at this point at the beginning of the third act, you do not have the right to dramatic irony (neither frank nor diffuse). If you make the audience understand that the dramatic answer is not what the protagonist perceived, you are telegraphing the enriched structure.

Thus, if you want to give the receiver the impression, at the end of the second act, that "all is lost"—to use an expression that has been fashionable lately in the world of screenwriting—it must correspond to a clear and convincing abandonment of the goal. An ambiguous "all is lost" does not serve as a first climax and is perceived only as one more obstacle, possibly stronger than the others.

The entire Battlestar Galactica series offers an enriched structure. In the tenth episode of the last season (4), the protagonists reach their goal (Earth) but a big disappointment awaits them. They then abandon their goal. Everyone is depressed. Before restarting the action, the authors devote an entire episode (No. 11) to this moment of floating.

Finally, imagine what the third act of the third act will be. It can convey meaning as much as a simple third act. It can even partly justify the use of the enriched structure. It is logical, for example, that two opposing dramatic answers generate different reactions. This contrast can be significant.

N. MAJOR DRAMATIC IRONIES

At the foundation stage, you must think about the major dramatic ironies that will run throughout the second act, or at least a good part of it. Present them in the order of appearance in the story.

It is possible that one or more dramatic ironies are already inherent in your subject. If, for example, you want to tell the story of aliens who take human form to discover Earth (3rd planet after the Sun), you automatically have two major dramatic ironies: 1 - we know that the protagonists are aliens, the other characters do not; 2 - we know earthly customs, the protagonists do not. In these cases, be aware of them. But sometimes, no dramatic irony is imposed by the subject.

Victim and protagonist

I distinguish two major categories of dramatic irony: the one where the victim of the dramatic irony is also the victim of the conflict (i.e., the protagonist) and the one where the victim of the dramatic irony is a character other than the protagonist.

As examples of the first category, which I call "bomb under the table," we find:

- The Apartment: we know that Fran (Shirley MacLaine) is Sheldrake's (Fred MacMurray) mistress; Baxter (Jack Lemmon), the protagonist, does not;

- Jean de Florette: we know that Papet (Yves Montand) and Ugolin (Daniel Auteuil) have blocked the spring; Jean (Gérard Depardieu), the protagonist, does not;

- The Night of the Hunter: we know that Harry Powell (Robert Mitchum) is not a priest but a dangerous criminal; the two co-protagonist children (Billy Chapin, Sally Jane Bruce) do not;

- Oedipus Rex: we know that Oedipus himself is the murderer he is searching for; Oedipus, the protagonist, does not;

- Othello: we know that Desdemona is faithful and that Iago is scheming everything; Othello, the protagonist, does not;

- The Government Inspector: we know that Khlestakov is not an inspector; the governor, his family, and his deputies, the co-protagonists, do not;

- The Towering Inferno: we know that the fire has started on the 81st floor; most of the guests, the protagonists, do not.

And as examples of the second category, which I call "secrecy":

- Amadeus: we know that Salieri, the protagonist, is plotting Mozart's downfall; the latter does not;

- Some Like It Hot: we know that Josephine (Tony Curtis) and Daphne (Jack Lemmon), the protagonists, are men disguised as women; the other characters do not;

- Cyrano de Bergerac: we know that Cyrano, the protagonist, loves Roxane and that he is the true author of the letters Christian sends to the young woman; Roxane does not;

- Rear Window: we know that Jeff (James Stewart), the protagonist, is spying on his neighbors; Thorwald (Raymond Burr) and the other neighbors do not;

- The Great Escape: we know that the allied prisoners, the co-protagonists, are preparing an escape; the Germans do not;

- City Lights: we know that the protagonist, the "millionaire" (Charles Chaplin) who helps the blind flower girl (Virginia Cherrill), is just a tramp; the flower girl does not;

- To Be or Not to Be: we know that the protagonists are not spies or German officers but Polish actors in disguise; the real Nazis do not;

- Life Is Beautiful (1997): we know that Guido (Roberto Benigni), the protagonist, and his son Giosuè (Giorgio Cantarini) are in a concentration camp and not a recreational place; Giosuè does not.

different exploitations

As you can see, the first category considerably reinforces the protagonist's victim position. Not only does Jean de Florette struggle to make his farm prosper, but he also does not know that his spring has been blocked. Not only does Othello struggle to resist the jealousy that burns him, but he also does not know that Iago is manipulating him. That said, if this first category of dramatic irony adds conflict for the protagonist, it does not sink them. Because the prospect of the obligatory scene creates hope for the audience. We think that it is enough for the dramatic irony to be resolved for the conflict to disappear. The second category of dramatic irony, on the other hand, concerns the strategies devised by the protagonist. It tends to make the latter more active.

One might think that the protagonist is more responsible for what happens to them in the second case. This is certainly true on a conscious level. But it happens that a protagonist is a victim of dramatic irony because, unconsciously, it suits them to ignore a too painful truth. In a way, they are responsible for their ignorance. The most emblematic example is Oedipus (Oedipus Rex). It is up to you to see if your protagonist could not be a victim not of ignorance that would fall under the external obstacle but rather of ignorance that would fall under the internal obstacle, an ignorance that suits them well.
In the first category, as we have just seen, the spectator has only one desire: for the irony to be resolved, for the protagonist to finally learn what they were unaware of. In the second category, it is generally the opposite. We hope the Germans won't discover the tunnel (The Great Escape). We hope no one will realize that our protagonists are disguised as women (Some Like It Hot). But since spectators unconsciously know that dramatic irony is almost always resolved, their hope turns into fear. We dread the moment when the Germans will discover the tunnel. We wonder what the victims will think when they discover that our protagonists are men disguised as women.

A sense of urgency
The management of time is also different in the two categories. Certainly, the ironic question always arises in the same way: "Will the victim discover, when and how, what they are unaware of?" It is the "when" that is not managed in the same way. In the case of bombs under the table, we want the resolution to happen as quickly as possible. We want Jean to discover that the source is blocked (Jean de Florette) or for Othello to understand that he is being manipulated by Iago (Othello) before the irreparable is committed. In the case of secrets, it is the opposite; we want the dramatic irony to be resolved as late as possible. We hope that Jeff (James Stewart) will have time to prove Thorwald's (Raymond Burr) guilt before he discovers he is being spied on (Rear Window). We hope Cyrano will have time to woo Roxane with his soul before she learns he is the author of Christian's letters (Cyrano de Bergerac).

As we can see, some dramatic ironies contain a form of urgency. Put yourself in the place of the Greeks hidden in the Trojan horse. As long as the horse is not anchored in the city, they worry about the discovery of the deception. The Greeks must enter the walls of Troy before the Trojans understand that this giant sculpture is a trap. In The Great Escape, the prisoners must dig their tunnel before the Germans discover the truth. The difference here with classic cases of urgency is that we do not know exactly when the dramatic irony will be resolved. We just know we have to hurry.

In short, be aware of the type of dramatic irony you are using, as it will allow you to exploit it optimally.

It's the exploitation that makes dramatic irony
Of course, on this general basis, many authors show nuance and subtlety. First, some works mix "bomb under the table" and "secrets." In North by Northwest, Roger (Cary Grant), the protagonist, is unaware that Kaplan does not exist. But Vandamm (James Mason), the main opponent, is also a victim of the same dramatic irony. Vandamm also ignores that Roger is not Kaplan. In A Doll's House, Nora, the protagonist, does not realize she is infantilized by her husband, while her husband is unaware that she has secretly taken out a loan. As we can see, Nora is doubly responsible for what happens to her.

Next, some dramatic ironies are exploited in a non-classical way. In Oedipus Rex, we want Oedipus to learn the truth, and at the same time, we dread the resolution because we know it will wound him. In Amadeus, we do not dread the moment when Mozart will understand that Salieri, the protagonist, seeks to harm him because, even if we understand it, we do not approve of the protagonist's goal. In Johnny Got His Gun, the doctors are unaware that the protagonist (Timothy Bottoms) has a consciousness and is not a vegetable. We do not dread the resolution, quite the opposite. In City Lights, we dread the moment when the florist will understand that her "millionaire" is just a tramp, and at the same time, we wish for it because we want her to know what Charlot has done for her. In A Patch of Blue, we do not dread the moment when the young blind girl (Elizabeth Hartman) will learn that she has befriended a Black man (Sidney Poitier).

In short, it is not enough to determine if you want your protagonist to be a victim of dramatic irony or not; you must also have an idea of how you will exploit it. The idea is to add conflict and meaning. Ask yourself, for example, what would happen if the victim knew. Would it change anything about your protagonist's actions?

The ironic management must reinforce the dramatic question
As soon as you install dramatic irony, you pose an emotional question in the unconscious of the receiver (cf. formulation above). This question, which I call "ironic," runs in parallel with the dramatic question. In Jean de Florette, part of the action is devoted to Jean's (Gérard Depardieu) attempts to cultivate his land and make his farm prosper. Another part, intertwined with the first, deals with his ignorance of Papet's (Yves Montand) and Ugolin's (Daniel Auteuil) wrongdoing and the consequences of this ignorance. Logically, there is a link between the ironic question and the dramatic question. We cannot imagine Papet and Ugolin blocking a source and then Jean de Florette arriving at Bastides Blanches to learn piano or write his memoirs. In The Great Escape, which, I remind you, belongs to the "secrets" category, it is even more evident that the two questions, dramatic and ironic, reinforce each other. We wonder if the protagonists will manage to escape and if the Germans will discover the escape attempt. Or, if you prefer, if the protagonists will hide their attempt from the Germans long enough. We return to the time management mentioned earlier.

Beware of the ironic protagonist
When you install dramatic irony, automatically, by projection, you confer the status of protagonist to the victim of the dramatic irony. When victim and protagonist are one and the same, it poses no problem, quite the opposite. However, when the victim of the dramatic irony is not the protagonist, you must be vigilant. The best way not to get trapped is to ensure that your dramatic irony will add conflict to your protagonist, even if they are not the victim of the dramatic irony.

A classic example of this delicate situation is the story of feigned love. The protagonist aims to seduce someone but is not sincere. They do it for revenge (The Discreet), to earn money (Heartbreaker), or to win a bet (The Great Maneuvers). Opposite the protagonist, there is often an important character, a victim of significant dramatic irony. If this character occupies only part of the story (like Charlotte and Mathurine in Don Juan, Rosette in On Ne Badine Pas Avec L'Amour, or Mme de Tourvel (Michelle Pfeiffer) in Dangerous Liaisons), the danger of identifying with them is low. But if they are almost as present as the protagonist, there is a risk that we will pity them for being manipulated and feel empathy for them - this is what happens, in my opinion, in Heartbreaker. The author then offers the spectator two subjects of identification. They must make a choice. Otherwise, the spectator's posterior finds itself between two chairs, and it is not pleasant.

In cases of feigned love stories, it will happen, for example, that the protagonist not only struggles to achieve their goal but also ends up falling in love (The Discreet, The Great Maneuvers). Another classic solution to prevent the spectator's heart from wavering: make the victim of the dramatic irony unsympathetic and deserving of being manipulated. This is the case, for example, in Rear Window. At no time is the spectator tempted to pity Thorwald (Raymond Burr). And yet, a voyeur is poking their nose into his business.

You will find another enlightening example in Evaluating a Scenario [50] with the comparison between The Tall Blond Man with One Black Shoe (1973) and its remake Buddy Buddy.

MAJOR DIFFUSE DRAMATIC IRONIES
Diffuse dramatic irony is omnipresent in dramaturgy. If the question arises at the foundational stage, it is only to help you become aware of what your spectator might feel or guess at certain stages of the story.

An inexperienced author might hesitate, for example, to show a protagonist congratulating themselves on achieving their goal in the middle of the story, fearing that the spectator will no longer be interested in what follows. Tell yourself that the spectator is often smarter than that. When, in Jaws, fishermen catch a big shark and the entire Amity community rejoices, with the protagonist (Roy Scheider) at the forefront, the spectator senses that it is not the right shark. The authors provide no clues, but the spectator guesses it. Consequently, they do not rejoice with the protagonist, their attention does not wane, and they patiently await the dramatic knot that will reignite the action. This is an exploitation of diffuse dramatic irony.

These cases of absence of conflict made interesting by diffuse dramatic irony gave rise to a delightful gag at the beginning of Meet the Fockers. Greg (Ben Stiller) and Pam (Teri Polo) head to the airport to catch a flight. A stranger starts by giving them his taxi. Then all the lights turn green. At the airport, a check-in counter opens in front of them, avoiding a long queue. They are assigned Business Class seats because there are no more seats in Economy. In short, the deus ex machina sequences follow one another at full speed. A bad script would lament the lack of conflict. A screenwriter lacking confidence would fear writing a scene where everything goes too well. It is evident that it is a nod. The authors are simply announcing that it will not last and that the rest will be conflictual. And to do this, they rely on diffuse dramatic irony.

One of the privileged domains of diffuse dramatic irony is related to the trajectory question. Our perspective often makes us perceive that a character needs to grow and that the upcoming trial or the goal they have chosen will allow them to evolve. We discussed this in the previous chapter. This is how we will agree with the reasonable contradictor without having certain information that they are defending the right position.

That said, at the foundational stage, it is rare to fill in the "Diffuse Dramatic Irony" entry. In other words, if it does not jump out at you, do not waste energy and move on to other entries.

THE GREAT DRAMATIC KNOTS
As we have seen, the two greatest dramatic knots of a story are the inciting incident and the climax. Third comes the declaration of the objective (or the transition from the first act to the second act). Other dramatic knots deserve reflection at the foundational stage.

The mid-climax
The big dramatic knot that sometimes divides the second act will logically impose itself if you tell a story in two phases. First, your protagonist must escape, then they must do everything to avoid being caught (cf. The Great Escape). In Jaws, there needed to be a moment that forced the mayor (Murray Hamilton) to finally recognize the magnitude of the problem, close the beaches, and finance the shark hunt. The death of the boater on his red boat (Ted Grossman) fulfills this function. It is the mid-climax of the film. From this scene, we clearly enter a new phase of the story. In the middle of Rambo, Rambo (Sylvester Stallone) manages to corner the sheriff (Brian Dennehy) who harasses him, points out that he could have killed his deputies instead of just injuring them, and asks him to let it go. Will the sheriff give up? Cut. The army is called in to catch the protagonist. The antagonist has just escalated, and the pressure increases.

But there are more subtle, less expected mid-climaxes. The end of Act I of Waiting for Godot, when the boy first comes to announce that Godot will not come, is a mid-climax. From that moment, we do not see the rest in the same light.

In other words, even if your story does not impose it, consider the possibility of having this type of dramatic knot. It will help you reignite the action at a moment when you risk a lull. In the middle of The Fugitive, Kimble (Harrison Ford) revisits in a dream images of his wife's murder (Sela Ward). We see him performing CPR on his wife and fighting against a one-armed man. This crucial scene clarifies a mystery - Kimble is indeed innocent - and launches the major sub-objective of the second part: finding the one-armed man.

The mid-climax can also open the protagonist's eyes to their tactical errors. Before the mid-climax, the protagonist is not doing well. After, they adopt more effective means or sub-objectives. However, be careful not to conclude the action. The mid-climax should not be a climax located in the middle of the film, followed by an interminable third act. It should be a sub-climax located in the middle of the film. It should launch the protagonist on a new sub-objective or shed new light on the action. If you risk giving the spectator the feeling that the action is over, it is imperative that you thoroughly prepare the action that will follow. This is what George Bernard Shaw does in Pygmalion. Higgins' objective is to transform Eliza into a duchess. He succeeds during an evening, in the middle of the play, in a scene that looks very much like a climax. But Shaw has taken care to repeatedly prepare a fundamental question, moreover related to his subject: what will become of Eliza if he manages to transform her? The second part of Pygmalion addresses this question.

If you have a major dramatic irony, you may have a ready-made mid-climax. Because the installation or resolution of a major dramatic irony is an important dramatic knot that can be placed in the middle of the story.

Twist(s)
A twist is a surprising dramatic knot for the spectator. It can also be surprising for the protagonist or other characters in the story, but it is not a necessity. The important thing is to surprise the receiver. This is why, if an installation of dramatic irony can be treated as a twist, a resolution of dramatic irony is never a twist. It is often a surprise for the ex-victim but not for the spectator because the obligatory scene has been announced.

The twist has two major interests:
1 - it breaks the narrative monotony. Indeed, because it shakes the spectator like a small electrocution and changes the perception of the story, the twist constitutes a break;
2 - it pleases the spectator. I see two explanations for this. First, the twist brings a touch of realism. Because real life is not predictable, it is full of surprises, sometimes good, often bad. Second, the twist is perceived by the spectator as a way for the author to say: "We are partners, I take you into account." And this message is priceless.

For these reasons, I strongly invite you, at this foundational stage, to think about surprises of this kind.

Twist and red herring
Let's distinguish two types of twists:
1 - the dramatic knot that the spectator does not expect, quite simply. In episode 4.5 of Dexter, we follow with the protagonist (Michael Hall) a particularly odious serial killer named Trinity (John Lithgow). Suddenly, Dexter and we discover with surprise that Trinity has a wife and children, that he is like Dexter, a good family man. The last image of Planet of the Apes (1968) or the revelation of the doctor's identity at the end of The Monkey of Hartlepool also fall into this first type of twist;
2 - the dramatic knot that the spectator expects even less because you have taken care to lead them on a false trail. Laura, The Sting, and Les Diaboliques are partly famous for this kind of twist.

If the first type does not require preparation work (the famous false trail), you must nevertheless ensure that the spectator does not guess it. Now tell yourself that today's spectators are unconsciously trained in the mechanisms of dramaturgy. Every twist therefore requires careful management. Hitchcock uses a clever process in Strangers on a Train, at the moment when Guy (Farley Granger) goes up to Bruno's father's house. To prevent the spectator from guessing the twist, the filmmaker stages a small danger (in this case, the presence of a threatening dog on the stairs), thus a small suspense, which prevents the spectator from thinking and guessing the approaching twist.

Different forms
We readily imagine the twist as emanating from the structure rather than the characters. But the twist can concern a character whose new facet or motivation we discover with surprise. When an ally becomes an obstacle and thus reveals themselves to be a false ally, there is surprise. The surprise linked to characterization is a form of twist that authors do not think about enough.

More classically, the twist can be the installation of dramatic irony. At the beginning of The Last Metro, the authors tell us twice that Lucas Steiner (Heinz Bennent) fled France to escape the Nazis. Suddenly, we discover that he is in Paris, hidden in the cellars of his theater. It is both a surprise (preceded by an emphasized false trail) and the beginning of a long dramatic irony. Similarly, when we learn in North by Northwest that Kaplan is an imaginary agent invented by the CIA, we have both a twist and the installation of significant dramatic irony (and also the clarification of a mystery).

Unequivocal
Avoid ambiguities at the moment of resolving the false trail. In other words, the twist must be clear and massive. If there is a doubt, you sprinkle the surprise with mystery, and the spectator is lost.

In truth, even before the resolution, mystery and surprise often make a very bad mix. If you want to create a nice twist, above all, do not put mystery. You must present a reality without the slightest doubt, without the slightest ambiguity. And suddenly, boom! surprise. The resolution of a mystery is a clarification, not a surprise. It can almost never be surprising (cf. more developments in chapter 8 of Dramaturgy [51]).

It should also be noted that the great end twists have become rarer lately. This is perhaps due to the disappointment produced by some of them. But it is mainly due to the fact that we have entered the era of the "spoiler." What is nothing more than a scoundrel waste is considered by some as a way to feel superior to others. Thus, in this era of so-called "social" networks, twists are often spoiled. This is especially true for end surprises. Take this into account and think rather of mid-action twists.

The point of no return
As its name suggests, the point of no return is a moment that gives the impression to the spectator that a return to normal is now impossible. The protagonist's life can never return to what it was before the beginning of the action. Think about what could be the point of no return in your story. The interest of this dramatic knot is to reinforce the consequences of the action undertaken, therefore the stakes and the dramatic question. Because, from the point of no return, the protagonist loses an option. In a way, they can only move forward. In short, the point of no return increases the audience's participation.

The two most radical points of no return in the repertoire are those of Faust and The Matrix. Deaths, murders, revelations, love at first sight, adulterous sexual relations can be points of no return. The transition from the first act to the second act of Festen and the declaration of the objective of Lagaan are also beautiful examples. But there are more subtle ones. In The Dinner Game, it is the moment when Brochant (Thierry Lhermitte) accepts Pignon's (Jacques Villeret) help after a moment of hesitation. Pignon has already worsened Brochant's back pain, he has already made a blunder on the phone with Brochant's mistress, but he has not yet ruined his life. We feel that by betting on Pignon's ability to help him, Brochant is putting his finger in a gear from which he will not be able to emerge unscathed.

Another amusing but exceptional example: the point of no return in Back to the Future 3. It is not a moment in the story but a specific place materialized by a windmill along a railway. As Doc (Christopher Lloyd) explains, before this point, the locomotive - which must push the time machine to 140 kilometers/hour - can still be stopped. After this point, it will be too late to turn back, it will either work or break. The point of no return is therefore spatial and not narrative. Of course, it is exploited at the moment of the climax. At the moment when the locomotive passes the spatial point of no return, a narrative point of no return is created.

In most of the examples above, the point of no return coincides with a moment of choice. But it is not an obligation. Clyde Barrow's (Warren Beatty) first murder in Bonnie and Clyde is clearly treated as a point of no return. Yet it takes place in...
the heat of the moment, Clyde doesn't really have time to weigh the pros and cons or think about the consequences of his action. The car accident that destroys all the evidence in the middle of 'Beyond a Reasonable Doubt' is a powerful point of no return, but the protagonist (Dana Andrews) has no control over this dramatic knot. It's an unfortunate accident (and a huge external obstacle) in which the protagonist isn't even involved.

I don't think it's a good idea to create too many points of no return. Firstly, because you'll scatter yourself and likely dilute the impact of the true, major point of no return. Secondly, because the point of no return is meant to prevent the protagonist from returning to their starting situation. Logically, one well-designed point should suffice.

Emotional Times

This is a type of dramatic knot that authors don't think about enough. I'm talking about all those moments, sometimes small, sometimes immense, that change a character's emotion. An emotional time is necessarily a dramatic knot, even if it's tiny or subtle. Conversely, a dramatic knot isn't always an emotional time. This is quite evident in action films. But it's also true in more delicate narratives, where characterization plays an important role. In the middle of 'The Apartment' (N.B. Warning, I'm revealing an important plot point), Baxter (Jack Lemmon) is depressed and bitter. He has just discovered that the woman he loves, Fran (Shirley MacLaine), is sleeping with their boss. And, on top of that, in his own home! This discovery is a resolution of dramatic irony—because we already knew—and a huge emotional time. While drowning his sorrows, Baxter is unaware that Fran has just attempted suicide in his apartment. He returns home. There are then two dramatic knots in the scene, two new resolutions of dramatic irony. In the first, Baxter discovers that Fran is in his apartment, asleep on his bed. This doesn't change Baxter's mood. He still resents her and asks her to leave. In the second resolution, Baxter realizes that Fran has overdosed on sleeping pills. This dramatic knot radically changes his emotion. He was bitter; he suddenly becomes caring and worried. The second dramatic knot thus also serves as an emotional time.

Oedipus Rex provides another example, instructive in several ways. In the middle of the play, Oedipus begins to fear the worst: could he be the murderer of Laius? Then an old Corinthian messenger arrives announcing that Polybus is dead. For Oedipus, who believes Polybus was his father, it's a huge relief. The Corinthian's news thus serves as an emotional time for Oedipus. However, it's not at all an emotional time for the audience; it doesn't change their perception. Because, as with the example from 'The Apartment' above, there is dramatic irony. Since the scene with Tiresias, we know that Oedipus is the murderer he seeks. This knowledge prevents us from rejoicing with Oedipus when the Corinthian arrives. Note that in chapter 11 of 'Poetics' [02], Aristotle precisely cites this dramatic knot as an example of what he calls a "peripeteia." Yet the word is sometimes imprudently translated as "plot twist" or "reversal." It's clear that it's neither in Oedipus Rex. The testimony of the old Corinthian messenger may be a reversal for Oedipus but surely not for the audience, as they are ahead of the characters.

At all stages of development (from foundations to dialogue continuity), I invite you to imagine the emotion each of your characters is in and to ensure that emotional times are there to justify it. At the foundation stage, it can be interesting to imagine the major dramatic knots that will also serve as emotional times for the protagonist.

Q. COMEDY

Don't ask yourself about comedy at this stage of the foundations to determine if you have a sense of humor or if you want to write a comedy. Hopefully, you already know where you stand on these questions. The foundations are not the place to think about the little gags or witty lines that will brighten your story. However, ask yourself what general discrepancy (on a character trait or a situation) could bring a dose of humor. In short, ask yourself which characters can serve as clowns (in the broad sense), what surprises or dramatic ironies can be exploited comically, what failures or exaggerations can lead to a smile.

You can also ask yourself what type of comedy you wish to use: demeaning, demanding, or compassionate? Do you want to mock a character harshly, like Molière with Tartuffe (The Tartuffe) or René Goscinny with Detritus (The Discord)? Or rather correct them through mockery, like Molière with Orgon (The Tartuffe) or Danny Rubin and Harold Ramis with Phil Connors (Bill Murray in Groundhog Day)? Or even laugh at them in a supportive way, like Molière with Dorine (The Tartuffe) or Rodolfo Sonego with Peppino (Alberto Sordi in The Old Lady's Money)? For more details, I refer you to chapter 9 of 'Dramaturgy' [51].

R. TRAJECTORIAL ELEMENTS

If you have worked on the trajectorial question using chapter 5, you have already started filling in this entry.

Trajectorial Protagonist

It's not enough to determine which character will change in your story and what their trajectory will be. It's also a good idea to think about the historical reasons for their initial personality. Where does this guilt, selfishness, lust, softness, etc., come from? It's not impossible that the answer will end up being part of the story. In the middle of Casablanca, for example, we understand, thanks to a flashback, why Rick (Humphrey Bogart) is bitter and no longer wants to commit. You see that a link is possible here with the protagonist's ghost.

Trajectorial Objective

When you know the trajectorial protagonist's path, you know what their trajectorial objective is. It's a variation of "growing," "gaining humanity." If your trajectorial protagonist is venal, their trajectorial objective will be to become selfless. If they are naive, their objective will be to become clear-sighted. Etc.

The Reasonable Contradictor of the Trajectorial Protagonist

Let's focus on this crucial character we've already discussed in chapter 5: the reasonable contradictor. Earlier, we saw that they could disapprove of the dramatic protagonist regarding their objective or motivations. Here, it's about disapproving of the trajectorial protagonist's attitude, highlighting their need to grow.

You don't have to have a character who tells the trajectorial protagonist off at the beginning of the story and whom the audience morally and/or intellectually agrees with. Nevertheless, you'd be wrong to deprive yourself of it. The reasonable contradictor is an extremely practical tool. It actually serves to designate the trajectorial protagonist in the eyes of the audience. And it helps to launch a trajectorial question.

One day, during a conference on this subject, a participant asked me if it was really necessary to have a reasonable contradictor, if it wasn't enough to show the trajectorial flaw of the trajectorial protagonist and let the audience draw conclusions. And he cited 'The Beat That My Heart Skipped,' in which, indeed, there is a trajectorial protagonist (cf. page 129). I went back to watch the film and didn't take long to find a reasonable contradictor. It's the representative of the Right to Housing association (Serge Onteniente), who protests against Thomas's (Romain Duris) actions at the beginning of the film. In fact, the presence of this contradictor is logical and almost inevitable. Whether in life or fiction, when an individual has a flaw, they often receive remarks from their surroundings or people they encounter. Especially if their flaw creates harm for others. I'm not far from thinking that by merely showing a character's trajectorial flaw, especially if they are not the protagonist, one risks not making the audience understand that this character has the potential to grow and that the story will offer them the opportunity. So, one risks not creating a trajectorial question.

The reasonable contradictor can be the protagonist (Woody vis-à-vis Buzz in Toy Story or Juror No. 8 (Henry Fonda) in 12 Angry Men), a secondary character (the Talking Cricket in The Adventures of Pinocchio), or even the antagonist. 'Time After Time' offers a nice example. At the beginning of the film (set in 1893, in London), H.G. Wells (Malcolm McDowell) expresses his utopian views: in three generations, there will be no more war, crime, or poverty. His friend Stevenson (David Warner) is surprised by his naivety. According to Stevenson, we live in a gigantic charnel house, and humanity hasn't changed in two thousand years. After a time travel, the two men find themselves in 1979, in San Francisco. In the middle of the second act, Stevenson—who is none other than Jack the Ripper—shows Wells the world's violence by channel surfing on a television. Humanity has clearly made no progress since 1893. This scene is a painful revelation for Wells, his trajectorial climax. And the reasonable contradictor of the protagonist is none other than the story's antagonist. Note that while we agree with Stevenson on his analysis of reality, we disagree with the delight he takes in it. Jack the Ripper isn't right on all fronts!

The Trajectorial Conflict

Imagine how you can push your trajectorial protagonist to their limits. Imagine what could make them suffer enough to abandon a personality trait they've been carrying for some time and have learned to live with. Your trajectorial protagonist lives in a cesspool, sure, but they've installed curtains, air conditioning, and an internet connection. It will take a serious jolt to convince them to leave the pit.

I remind you that the notion of trajectorial conflict is also interesting when strictly no character changes. If you want this lack of change to make sense, it will be beneficial to stage conflicts and situations that could or should have allowed a character to change.

The Trajectorial Climax

The trajectorial climax is the dramatic knot that triggers the transformation of one of the characters. If you've created a trajectorial question (cf. previous chapter), you necessarily have a trajectorial climax. So imagine the moment when the character who changes goes from this to that. And imagine where in the story you will place it. At the same time as the dramatic climax? A little before? A little after? At the moment of the inciting incident? In the middle of the story, at the median climax?

Engaging in reflection on the trajectorial climax forces you to imagine how you will show that your character has changed. You can't just have them say it (as in Tootsie, cf. page 104). The character who has grown must undertake something that proves they are no longer quite the same. This is what I called "phase 4" in the previous chapter.

Think that the trajectorial climax is often the place of free will. That is, the moment when a character faces a dilemma and makes a choice that commits them. A choice made consciously and responsibly. This is one of the reasons why I think McMurphy (Jack Nicholson) doesn't transform in One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest (cf. page 82).

The Trajectorial Response

In general, when working on a character's internal trajectory, it's for them to awaken. In other words, the trajectorial response is likely to be positive. But, as I just mentioned above, it can make sense for you to set up the trials likely to change a character and yet, despite everything, it doesn't suffice to make them move. In Waiting for Godot, Beckett plunges his protagonists into boredom and frustration. And yet, in the end, they are still just as lost. Cf. also the example of the 11th man below.

S. THREE EXAMPLES OF FOUNDATIONS

To illustrate the principle of foundations, my first impulse was to produce the foundations of one or more known works. Except that I already do this elsewhere (in 'Dramaturgy' [51] and (one day) in 'Exemplary Dramatic Stories' [52]), and especially, one doesn't read the construction grid of a work to which images and actors have already given flesh the same way as that of a work in progress. It seemed all the more interesting to reproduce the foundations of projects not yet realized as these can constitute an excellent alternative to the synopsis demanded by so many decision-makers. You are therefore invited to observe, with the three examples that follow, that the work of foundations gives a good idea of the upcoming story and even its tone. The first project is the work of Corinne Klomp, the second of Didier Emotte. Their rights are, of course, protected. I thank them both for allowing their publication. The third project belongs to me. I'm not comfortable giving my own work as an example, but many students have assured me that it was enlightening. Partly because it offers an enriched structure.

You will notice that these foundations all fit into two or three pages. One of the goals of the foundations (like the scene-by-scene) is to have an overview of the project. I strongly encourage you to remain concise. The more you fatten the foundations, the more this overview fades.

Finally, I specify that the examples given below are not first drafts. Don't be impressed if they seem complete and accomplished.

The Foundations of 'Model Couple'

Title: Model Couple

Dramatic Pitch: The owner of a matchmaking agency—who gives his own marriage as an example—discovers that his wife is cheating on him and does everything to save face.

Protagonist: XAVIER, 37 years old, quite rigid and concerned with appearances. Hard worker. The Felicitas matchmaking agency is his "baby." Controlling with everyone, including his wife.

Arena: a matchmaking agency.

Inciting Incident: Xavier discovers that his wife GAËLLE has a lover (JEAN-FRANÇOIS).

Objective: save face.

Stakes: precisely face. If Xavier doesn't achieve his objective, he will feel a deep sense of shame. He will feel worthless.

Important characters other than the protagonist:

- GAËLLE, 32 years old, Xavier's wife. Malleable, she has been shaped by her husband, his demands, and his passion for the agency. Now she wants to take flight and finally adopt a child with a man who would love her for who she is, not for what she represents.

- JEAN-FRANÇOIS, 35 years old, Gaëlle's lover. Very natural, with a booming laugh and a mover's physique, he hides an extreme sensitivity under his apparent nonchalance. He suffers from his wife's indifference towards him and wants to be finally loved in return. He has some maturity to gain.

- CLAIRE, 35 years old, Jean-François's wife. She doesn't care about her appearance or what people say, and above all, she has given up on love. She married Jean-François after a romantic disappointment. Deep down, she is not happy and desires to love again. But is she capable of taking the risk of suffering again?

- MRS. VARNIER-DUVAL-LE GUEN, 70 years old. A widow with a grandmotherly appearance. The wife of one man but married three times already. Employed as a mole by a competing agency of Felicitas, she seeks the flaw in the model couple Xavier/Gaëlle. Along the way, she might say goodbye to her widowhood.

Sub-objectives and means: in the short term, prevent his clients and competitors from learning that he is cuckolded and in the long term, get his wife to break up with her lover. For this, Xavier manipulates Jean-François's wife, Claire, to re-seduce her husband.

First act-second act passage: disguised objective declaration: jokingly, Xavier tells a former client what his objective would be if he knew he was being cheated on.

Internal obstacle: Xavier's own schemes, his budding love for Claire.

External obstacles: Gaëlle, who is very much in love with Jean-François; Jean-François, who lacks discretion; Mrs. Varnier-Duval-Le Guen who works for a competing agency and has infiltrated among the agency's clients; Claire, who doesn't care about being cheated on.

External obstacle of internal origin: Claire falls in love with Xavier.

Climax (dramatic and trajectorial): the party for the agency's tenth anniversary. Xavier first prevents Mrs. Varnier-Duval-Le Guen from revealing everything, then publicly declares that his marriage is a sham and that he loves another woman: Claire.

Dramatic response: negative.

Third act: Xavier declares his sincere love to Claire; together, they start a new agency, specializing in marital counseling, and Xavier offers his services to the couple Gaëlle/Jean-François, who were somewhat mistreated in the second act.

Dramatic ironies: 1 - we know that Xavier is cuckolded, his clients, the mole, and his competitors don't; 2 - we know there is a mole among the agency's clients, Xavier and Gaëlle don't; 3 - we know that Xavier manipulates Claire to re-seduce her husband, Claire, Gaëlle, Jean-François, and Mrs. Varnier-Duval-Le Guen don't.

Diffuse dramatic ironies: 1 - we feel that Xavier doesn't have the ideal behavior to save face; he's weaving the rope to hang himself; 2 - we feel that Xavier is falling in love with Claire.

Plot twist: Mrs. Varnier-Duval-Le Guen is a mole.

Median climax: Claire falls in love with Xavier.

Trajectorial protagonist: Xavier, a slave to his work and the judgment of others.

Trajectorial objective: to be authentic.

Trajectorial conflict: his budding love for Claire, the realization that he no longer loves Gaëlle, the cruel realization that his marriage is a sham, the awareness that others' opinions and appearances are tyrannies.

Trajectorial climax: cf. above.

Trajectorial response: positive.

The Foundations of 'The 11th Man'

Title: The 11th Man.

Dramatic Pitch: A young girl very talented in football fights to be recognized as a professional player in the men's league and fully live her overwhelming passion.

Protagonist: CLAUDE RETZ, at 6, 13, 15, 20, and 23 years old, a child then a teenager then a dynamic and determined young woman. Claude is trapped by the idealized representations her father gives her of her mother, a brilliant activist admired by all, who died giving birth to Claude. For Claude, life is a battle, and one must fight to get what they want. For her, nothing is impossible.

Arena: the world of football, Belgian and European, female and male, amateur and professional.

Inciting Incident: After a lost football match, Claude (13 years old) is scapegoated and kicked out of the boys' team of which she is the only girl.

Objective: To be considered a true professional player in the men's league 1 championship.

Stakes: the recognition of Claude's father, a great football enthusiast. Women's football being despised, Claude imagines that the only way to exist in her father's eyes is to shine in men's football.

Important characters other than the protagonist:

- CHARLES RETZ, from 38 to 55 years old, Claude's father. A steelworker with a strong character. Committed unionist. Passionate about football, he is a supporter of Standard Liège (professional club) and president of FC Ougrée (amateur club). Charles is a straight and honest man, attached to the noble values of sport. He knows the professional football world well and doesn't like it much.

- GEORGES RETZ, from 18 to 35 years old, Claude's older brother. A well-known professional player with a chaotic career (drugs).

- FRANCK MARCANTONI, 40 and 50 years old, coach, former international goalkeeper and world champion. A football legend. Sentenced to community service for match-fixing. He's rather an honest man who made a mistake. He loves football, the real one, the one on the field, but his professional career has disillusioned him in this area. Wants to redeem himself.

- ANDREAS PRONTI, 52 years old, unscrupulous player agent, very skilled in business and renowned in the field. Cunning, intelligent, Pronti considers players as cattle.

- TIGRAN MANDORALLEN, 22 and 25 years old, brilliant professional football player, captain of Standard Liège. Very proud of his Scythian culture, he considers himself a warrior of life, a survivor. He likes women who resemble him: warriors. He is stubborn and slightly macho. Claude intrigues and attracts him because she is different from all the footballers' wives he has met.

- HORST SANTANDER, 55 years old, president of the G-14 (very powerful association of the richest football clubs). Intelligent and unscrupulous man of the apparatus, he defends the football business lobby. For him, a woman in D1 would be a nightmare. At no cost will he let Claude finish a season, let alone win a championship.

Sub-objectives and means:

- in the short term (13 years old): reintegrate the boys' team;
- recruit a great coach;
- in the medium term (20 years old): lead her team to win the Cup;
- find sponsors;
- in the long term (23 years old): obtain her professional men's football license;
- last at least one season in a top three division 1 club.

Objective declaration: After her boys' team's defeat, Claude (13 years old) sees herself holding a trophy and being carried in triumph by men. Following this vision, she declares to her father that she wants to become a professional in the men's league.

Internal obstacles: Claude's sense of omnipotence, her integrity, her physical limits. Claude is more enduring than men but less powerful and less fast.

External obstacles of internal origin:

- Charles, who stops supporting Claude when he sees she's going too far;
- the professional football world (players, agents, press, etc.);
- Horst Santander.

External obstacle:

- the professional football world (players, agents, press, etc.).

Climax: For the League 1 championship final, Claude forms a semi-amateur team with players willing to play with her. Charles and the team's doctor try to dissuade her from playing. With the doping products she's taken, she's not in shape. Not to mention that Santander will never let her do it. Claude persists. The amateurs are dominated, but Claude scores a goal that saves honor. She collapses in stoppage time, poisoned by a player in Santander's pay. Proud to have obtained her license, Claude dies in her father's arms.

Dramatic response: positive.
Third Act: a few years later, Charles, Tigran, and Franck gather at Claude's grave, who has become a symbol of beautiful play. But even though Santander has been fired, the world of football hasn't changed.

Dramatic Ironies:
1 - In the first half of the film, we know Claude is a girl disguised as a boy, but many characters are unaware.
2 - We know Pronti is a swindler with the worst intentions, but Claude is unaware.
3 - We know what Santander is plotting, but Claude and others are unaware.

Diffuse Dramatic Ironies:
1 - We sense that Claude is playing with fire and going too far, but Claude doesn't realize it. This diffuse dramatic irony is linked to the trajectory question.
2 - We sense that Claude's mother wasn't the saint Charles praises, but Claude doesn't suspect it. This diffuse dramatic irony becomes clear (to Claude's detriment) when we learn the truth about her mother's death.

Midpoint Climax: during an important match, Claude's deception (a girl disguised as a player) is revealed. She must completely change strategy.

Trajectory Protagonist: Claude, in omnipotence.
Trajectory Objective: to lose her will to power.
Reasonable Opponent: Charles.
Trajectory Conflict: various frustrations, health concerns.
Trajectory Climax: the beginning of the dramatic climax, when the doctor advises Claude not to play. She faces a choice. She could stop in time, but her omnipotence is strongest.
Trajectory Response: none. She dies before we know if she has changed.

Foundations of Culbuto
Title: Culbuto.
Dramatic Pitch: A chronically clumsy, hypersensitive man falls in love with an insecure theater actress. He battles his own sensitivity and an unscrupulous director to win her over.

Protagonist: MAXIME, 37, has been dyspraxic since childhood. It's an incurable neurological disorder affecting psychomotor skills. In Maxime's case, it's random. He can succeed in what his brain commands or fail.
