With no training or skills, he receives welfare and earns money by lending his body to science: he tests drugs at the Clinical Investigation Center (CIC) of several hospitals. Maxime is also extremely sensitive. While he often plays the clown unintentionally, he can't stand being laughed at.

Arena: the theater of a regional cultural center.
Inciting Incident: at the hospital where he tests drugs, Maxime falls for a new nurse. Her name is Natacha. He quickly learns she's not a nurse but an actress. She was interning to learn a role and is currently performing in a comedy play (an adaptation of The Inspector General).

Objective: to win Natacha's heart.
Stakes: true love. Maxime's love life is limited. He has relationships, but his sensitivity often shortens them. He has no family left. His social and emotional life is reduced to passing flings, CIC researchers, and a lab mouse he saved from euthanasia.

Important Characters Other Than the Protagonist:
- NATACHA, 30, semi-professional actress, very insecure about her acting skills, seeks recognition in others' eyes, especially her father's. She wants to know if she's meant to be an actress.
- HANS, 57, director. Very charming. Confidence bordering on megalomania. His life is intertwined with theater. He had a success in Avignon 30 years ago. Since then, he's been struggling. He's ready to do anything to make theater history. Admired by Natacha.
- ANTOINE, 40, lab technician, head of the CIC. Has an affection for Maxime without pity. Hopes to beat Maxime at chess one day.
- GELULE. White mouse serving as Maxime's confidante.

Sub-objectives and Means:
- invite Natacha (to a restaurant, for her birthday, to rehearse),
- get hired in Hans's troupe,
- create a play about a love-struck clown and seduce Natacha through the play,
- convince Hans to stage this new play,
- ensure Natacha gets the main female role in the play,
- help Natacha on stage.

First Act-Second Act Transition: Maxime asks Antoine to organize a tête-à-tête dinner for him and Natacha.

Internal Obstacle:
- Maxime's clumsiness and hypersensitivity,
- the terror of rejection.

External Obstacle with Internal Origin:
- Hans. He is fascinated by what Maxime does with his body, forces him to perform on stage, and uses him to create the play of his life. Maxime was a lab guinea pig, Hans makes him a theater guinea pig;
- Natacha hates mice, even white ones;
- due to Maxime's actions, Natacha falls into Hans's arms.

Climax: during a rehearsal of the new play, Maxime is so convincing in his role that Natacha changes the text and kisses him. Maxime is overjoyed. But he is disillusioned when he realizes Natacha was carried away by her actress's impulse. The theater hasn't influenced life as Maxime hoped.

Dramatic Response: negative.
Third Act Twist: Maxime agrees to a single performance of the new play to allow Natacha to perform in front of an agent.
Third Act Climax: Maxime helps Natacha on stage. She realizes she loves him and proves it to him on stage during the performance. Second Dramatic Response: positive.
Third Act of the Third Act: Maxime has found his calling: clown-magician for children in hospitals.

Dramatic Ironies:
1 - We know Maxime is in love with Natacha, Natacha and Hans are unaware;
2 - We know Maxime isn't an actor clowning on stage but a chronically clumsy person who controls nothing, the audience is unaware;
3 - We know Natacha is starting an affair with Hans, Maxime is unaware;
4 - We know Hans has come to understand Maxime's feelings for Natacha, Maxime is unaware.

Twists: 1 - Natacha isn't a nurse but an actress, 2 - Natacha isn't an actress but a hairdresser.

Midpoint Climax: Maxime discovers Natacha is dating Hans. After a moment of dejection, he decides to win Natacha over using Hans's domain: theater.

Point of No Return: Maxime is removed from the list of healthy volunteers because he exceeded the legal quota. He has no choice but to find another way to earn a living.

Comedy: created by Maxime's clumsiness - especially on stage where he steals the show -, by exploiting dramatic ironies, by some characters' dismay at Maxime's antics, and by the ridiculous obsessions of the troupe's actors.

Trajectory Protagonist 1: Maxime, hypersensitive.
Trajectory Objective 1: learn to laugh at himself.
Reasonable Opponents 1: Juliette (one of Maxime's flings), Antoine, Marie-Claude (nurse at the CIC).
Trajectory Conflict 1: the audience's mockery, humiliating for Maxime; the mirror effect of Natacha who, in her own way, also values others' opinions.
Trajectory Climax 1: Natacha opens Maxime's eyes, who accepts to exploit his clumsiness on stage and eventually enjoys it.
Trajectory Response 1: positive.

Trajectory Protagonist 2: Natacha, lacking self-confidence.
Trajectory Objective 2: stop seeking recognition in others' eyes.
Reasonable Opponent 2: Maxime.
Trajectory Conflict 2: Hans's manipulation, the realization due to Maxime, and especially a catharsis on stage.
Trajectory Climax 2: Natacha reveals her wound on stage and heals it.
Trajectory Response 2: positive.

T. A STEP TO MATURE
Before moving to the next step, I recommend taking a break, letting the foundations mature. This doesn't prevent you from jotting down scenes that seem necessary for the action. Only when the foundations seem solid and mature can you move on to milestones and fill in the scene-by-scene.

I remind you that the foundations can take several months of work. Some authors tend to quickly answer questions and move on. It's risky. One can't say they're helped by decision-makers who consider that what can be typed by their secretary in half an hour requires neither time nor talent to conceive. Consequently, it doesn't deserve substantial remuneration. It's a grave mistake that costs everyone dearly.

7. CHARACTERIZATION

"The way of acting is the way of being."
(Lao-Tseu [49])
"There will be character if words or actions reveal a determined choice."
(Aristotle [02])

At the foundation stage, you designed your protagonist and main characters. That was the first phase of the characterization work. Alongside the development of the scene-by-scene, which will be discussed in the next chapter, it's time to move on to the next two phases of characterization work.

Second Phase: Staging Characterization
It's quite possible that, in the first phase of characterization work, you imagined situations showcasing your character and even wrote some scenes, characterizing of course. These scenes may never be part of the final text, but they allow you to grasp the character. In a way, you've mixed the first and second phases.

Major Tool 1: Conflict
To convey a character's characterization to the audience, there aren't many ways, you have to put said character in a situation, in action or reaction. What enlightens us most about what a character is, is their behavior in a given situation, preferably conflicting for them or others. In The Remains of the Day, for example, Stevens (Anthony Hopkins) continues to serve his master instead of going to his dying father's bedside. At the beginning of Gone with the Wind, Scarlett (Vivien Leigh) agrees, out of romantic spite, to marry a suitor who doesn't interest her (Rand Brooks). In Titanic, at the height of the disaster, the attitudes of Cal (Billy Zane), Ismay (Jonathan Hyde), and Molly (Kathy Bates) reveal a "determined choice," to use Aristotle's expression [02], which characterizes each of them. For memory, Cal pretends to be the father of an abandoned girl to get into a lifeboat. Bruce Ismay - who is none other than the CEO partly responsible for the disaster - discreetly boards a lifeboat reserved for women and children. And later, Molly - who is a nouveau riche despised by the old aristocracy - insists that her lifeboat go back to rescue survivors swimming in the icy water.

Major Tool 2: Abnormality
Be careful, however, the way of reacting under pressure is an essential element but not sufficient. A second important factor is necessary: the character's way of acting or reacting must be different from the norm. If your character behaves like 95% of the audience would in the same situation, they resemble thousands of people and therefore lack specificity. If, for example, your character runs away when they hear "Fire!", sure, they react in a conflict situation but do nothing characterizing. While everyone rushes to the exit when "Fire!" rings out, Garfield, on the other hand, brandishes sausages skewered on a stick (Garfield). In Some Came Running, a writer, Dave (Frank Sinatra), reads one of his stories to Ginnie (Shirley MacLaine), a kind but not very bright young woman. When he's finished, Ginnie expresses her enthusiasm for the text and her admiration for Dave's storytelling ability. For her part, she feels worthless. A normal writer would thank the young woman for appreciating his prose and possibly try to cheer her up. Dave does none of that. On the contrary, he attacks Ginnie and accuses her of not understanding his story. In Cleaning Up, Blanche (Blandine Pélissier) hesitates between preventing a drop of coffee from staining her carpet and stopping her depressed friend (Sandrine Dumas) from jumping out the window. A normal human wouldn't hesitate for a second. Blanche is so neurotic that she chooses cleanliness.

You might say that abnormality, while certainly characterizing, tends to generate comedy. This isn't always true. Dave's aggressive bitterness isn't funny. Neither is Scarlett's sentimental suicide. Consider Danny (Charles Bronson) in The Great Escape, who refuses to advance in the tunnel he built and prefers to return to prison, so claustrophobic is he. Or Jeannine Schwartz (Emmanuelle Bach) in episode 3.4 of A French Village, who is so upset about losing a cake contest to a "Jewess" (Hélène Seuzaret) that she makes a scene and creates a big discomfort. Even more eloquent, in the series Hannibal (S1, ep.11), a serial killer (Eddie Izzard) disembowels a still-living doctor (Raul Esparza) and extracts his organs. A blogger (Lara Jean Choroscecki) is beside him, but not as an accomplice, as a forced witness. Any normally constituted human would tremble, scream, cry, vomit, faint, or look away. The blogger, however, watches the scene coldly. Her abnormal lack of reaction is extremely characterizing and, believe me, there's nothing funny about the scene.

Major Tool 3: Contrast
In other words, one of the major tools of characterization is contrast. Contrast, as we've just seen, between what the character does and what most normal people would do in life. But also contrast between what two characters in the story do in the same situations. The Companions, mentioned on page 134, provides a fine example throughout the film. We can also cite Blueberry in the first volume of his adventures (Fort Navajo) or Gustavo Fring (Giancarlo Esposito) in season 2 of Breaking Bad. Charlier introduces the cavalry lieutenant Blueberry just after characterizing a first lieutenant named Graig. The latter is straight-laced while Blueberry, dressed in civilian clothes, is cheating and drinking in a gambling den. In the first season of Breaking Bad, Walter (Bryan Cranston) seeks a dealer to sell the methamphetamine he makes. He associates, with difficulty, with a brutal psychopath named Tuco (Raymond Cruz). The appearance, in the second season, of Gustavo, a discreet, polite, and refined drug lord, contrasts sharply with Walter's first associate.

Setting Up the Routine of Life
Often, the second phase of characterization work involves presenting the characters in their routine of life. At the beginning of Othello, the scene where Othello is both tasked with defending Venice's interests against the Ottomans and asked to justify his relationship with Desdemona allows us to know the protagonist. At the beginning of Casablanca, Rick (Humphrey Bogart) claims he doesn't stick his neck out for anyone, but the authors don't just rely on his words. They clearly show Rick's neutrality with the announcement of Ugarte's (Peter Lorre) arrest, which Rick does nothing to prevent. Another example of masterful presentation: Carla (Emmanuelle Devos) in Read My Lips. Small details, like the coffee cup regularly forgotten on her desk by colleagues, allow us to understand that no one pays attention to her. But Carla has an uncommon ability: she can read lips. This character trait will serve her well later. Cleverly, the authors present this ability as a source of suffering: in the cafeteria, she reads the obscenities her colleagues say about her.

Sometimes, it's not one or two important characters the author must present but half a dozen. At the beginning of Stagecoach, in ten minutes, Dudley Nichols and Ernest Haycox manage to characterize an alcoholic doctor (Thomas Mitchell), a crooked banker (Berton Churchill), a strict wife (Louise Platt), a distinguished gambler (John Carradine), a cowardly salesman (Donald Meek), and a lady of easy virtue (Claire Trevor). To do this, there aren't many ways, they put them in situations. Of course, they are defined by only one or two character traits each and are not far from stereotypes. But it's clear, economical, and it magnificently launches the story. At the beginning of The Pigeon, Capannelle (Carlo Pisacane) looks for a pigeon, that is, a sucker willing to go to prison in place of a crook (Memmo Carotenuto). Capannelle addresses a first person (Renato Salvatori) who refuses. Together, they think of a second person (Tiberio Murgia) who also refuses. And so on until the gang finds the pigeon (Vittorio Gassman). By doing this, the authors present one by one the five future co-protagonists of the story, each in their routine of life. Other instructive examples can be found at the beginnings of The Chase, The Grand Maneuver, and the mini-series Battlestar Galactica (2003). This last example deserves attention. The authors present a dozen important characters for the continuation of the action. It's noteworthy that they do this over about forty minutes (for a three-hour mini-series). In other words, take your time if you have to introduce many characters.

Don't Tell, Show
At the beginning of The Decline of the American Empire, the characters keep saying "I, me...". "I am this," "I think that." In my opinion, not much is retained. First, because the information is told and not shown. Then, because a person is rarely objective about themselves. So, either what they say isn't quite right, or if they tell the truth, they're too lucid and therefore... they sound false.

At the beginning of The Court of Songs (1942), while the credits display the actors' names over the image of their respective characters, a voice-over describes the characters. Eighteen are paraded with their succinct characterization in just over a minute! How could the authors seriously think the audience would retain anything? The following (i.e., the beginning of the film) could have - should have - done characterization work so the audience understands who is who and the relationships between the characters. Unfortunately, this isn't sufficiently the case. The authors must have assumed the audience had already grasped everything during the credits.

Sometimes, the indigestible accumulation concerns a single character. At the beginning of A Ghost, Jean-Jacques Sauvage (Louis Jouvet) enters the empty room of a certain François, whom he doesn't know. "But I do know him," he says to François's father (Jean Brochard), "he resembles his room: dapper (the camera shows ties), generous (we see loose money), tormented (a skull), a bit childish (a toy), disorganized (the room is messy), romantic (Goethe's Werther) and sentimental (a flower in Werther)." Needless to say, not all the characterization elements concerning François are captured by the audience. For two reasons: it goes too fast and it's told instead of shown. The authors might have thought it was enough to illustrate the dialogues with images to show things. It's giving too much power to the image. There is something far more effective than the image to show: action.

This scene from A Ghost is reminiscent of the cliché seen a thousand times in cinema where a character reads the hero's file and lists their exploits. It's found in Raiders of the Lost Ark, Casablanca, Fort Navajo, The Grand Illusion, The Man Who Would Be King, Lawrence of Arabia, The Matrix, Birdman of Alcatraz, One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, and almost all war or adventure films in the world. This type of scene usually occurs at the beginning of the work (except, oddly, in Lawrence of Arabia). Generally, we mostly retain that our protagonist is a rebel or a genius, which we didn't doubt. And we prefer to see them in action rather than hear about it. At the beginning of The Dirty Dozen, Reisman (Lee Marvin) contests the content of his file and adds that, anyway, he's more interested in results than "embellishments."

In Young Sherlock Holmes, the first meeting between young Sherlock Holmes (Nicholas Rowe) and the future Dr. Watson (Alan Cox) is somewhat like the scene in A Ghost. Just after meeting, Holmes has already deduced half a dozen characteristics about Watson. Like in A Ghost, it doesn't teach us much.
doesn't teach us much about Watson, but unlike in 'Un revenant,' it teaches us something about Sherlock Holmes' deductive mind.

Examples from 'The Decline of the American Empire,' 'The Court of Songs,' and 'Un revenant' clearly show that it's not enough to determine a character's traits; you must also effectively inform the audience. When Truffaut writes about Hitchcock's cinema that "everything said instead of shown is lost to the audience," he exaggerates. You can indeed say an event without showing it. "Bunny has disappeared" or "I won the lottery," for example. However, Truffaut is entirely correct regarding characterization elements. Stating a character trait is pointless, whether in a Hitchcock film or any other story. It's useless to say François is vain and tormented ('Un revenant'); you must show it and show it well. Otherwise, the audience won't retain the information. Being a competent characterologist is necessary but not sufficient to be a good playwright.

Crafting the first appearance Among the many ways to introduce a secondary character at the beginning of a story, one method is to have other characters talk about the character in question first, then carefully craft their first appearance. Molière applies this principle in 'Tartuffe.' During acts I and II (out of five), Tartuffe is mentioned constantly, but he is not seen. He only enters at the beginning of act III in a famous scene. His very first action is to hand his handkerchief to Dorine, saying: "Cover that bosom which I cannot behold"—and not "Hide that bosom" as is often heard.

The same principle is found at the beginning of 'The Toy,' with the character of Rambal-Cochet (Michel Bouquet), at the beginning of 'A Fistful of Dollars,' with the character of Ramon (Gian Maria Volontè), at the beginning of 'A Man for All Seasons,' more so in the film than in the play (Thomas More), with the character of King Henry VIII (Robert Shaw), and in the first album of the 'Tyler Cross' series, with the character of Spencer Pragg.

Note: It is not necessary to provide information about the important character before they appear; it is enough to make us understand that they will play a major role in the story. The idea is not to say what a character is—I just explained that it's not effective!—the idea is to arouse the audience's curiosity, to make them want to know the character. However, of course, you must place the character in a significant situation when they enter the scene. Moreover, if you choose to proceed this way, you should be creative for this first appearance because you will have created an expectation. If you write for cinema, you cannot simply ask the director to film the feet first and then pan up the body to reveal the character's face, as is often seen in macho films.

It should be noted that this narrative technique rarely concerns the protagonist because it is quite logical to present the protagonist at the beginning of a story without too much delay. This did not prevent Edmond Rostand from using the method at the very beginning of 'Cyrano de Bergerac,' as we saw in the previous chapter (pages 133-134).

When to introduce an important character? The example of 'Tartuffe' above is a tour de force and an exception in the repertoire. Even though Tartuffe is not the protagonist, he is an important character in the story. He is even the main character. Yet he only enters the scene in the middle of the play. Generally, important characters other than the protagonist are introduced in the first act or at the beginning of the second act. It makes sense. A character is important because they drive the action.

There are exceptions. Kurtz (Marlon Brando) in 'Apocalypse Now,' DeSalvo (Tony Curtis) in 'The Boston Strangler,' Lilly (Catherine Spaak) in 'The Easy Life,' the ambassador (Mogens Wieth) in 'The Man Who Knew Too Much,' Nihad in 'Incendies,' Roberta (Kathy Bates) in 'About Schmidt,' Frank Miller (Jan MacDonald) in 'High Noon,' or Lyle (Harry Dean Stanton) in 'The Straight Story.'

In all these examples, the late entries are justified by the subject. In fact, the rule that should guide you is: unless the story suggests otherwise, introduce your important characters early in the story.

Third phase: exploiting characterization In 'Braquo,' Théo (Nicolas Duvauchelle) is presented as a drug addict. He drowns his despair in cocaine and alcohol. It's a characterization element, even a foreshadowing. We expect it to be useful. Unfortunately, the authors stop at the second phase. At no point in the first season does Théo's drug addiction impact the action. Nor does the action alter his addiction problem. Of course, it's disappointing. Similarly, in 'A French Village': De Kervern (Patrick Descamps) is characterized as an alcoholic, but if he weren't, the series wouldn't be affected. Conversely, in 'The Secret in Their Eyes,' Sandoval's (Guillermo Francella) alcoholism serves the action. It's because of this addiction that the protagonist's (Ricardo Darin) investigation is closed, and it's thanks to it that the protagonist escapes a gruesome execution. Similarly, McClure's alcoholism in 'Blueberry' often causes problems for the protagonists (for example, in 'The Man with the Silver Star').

Characterization must be exploited. The examples of 'Cyrano de Bergerac' and 'The Companions' on pages 133-134 illustrate a classic case of exploiting characterization. The author has presented their protagonist, then shows us what it leads to in action, what consequences such or such a character trait can generate. Logically, the protagonist's characterization guides their choice of sub-objectives and creates obstacles for them. In 'Indiana Jones and the Kingdom of the Crystal Skull,' Indiana Jones (Harrison Ford) finds himself trapped in quicksand. To get him out, his co-protagonist (Shia LaBeouf) hands him a six-meter-long snake, a python type. It was clearly established in the first episode of the series ('Raiders of the Lost Ark') that Indiana Jones is ophidiophobic. He therefore refuses with disgust to grab the snake offered to him, risking dying in the quicksand. It's an exploitation of Indiana Jones' characterization. One can only regret that it is just a nod and doesn't have longer-term conflictual consequences. As the protagonists are being chased at that moment, it would have been elegant if Jones' ophidiophobia slowed them down and they were captured because of this internal flaw.

Suggestions, tricks, and sources of inspiration Before embarking on characterization work, you can ask yourself what type of characterization excites you, what type you want to write, and even, why not, what type you feel capable of writing. Staging puppets doesn't require a particular effort in characterization. It just requires not twisting psychological plausibility for the sake of structure, which is a common trap for plot weavers.

However, if you want to present well-developed characters, you must, in my opinion, respect the following principles.

1. Know your characters well. We've seen that this doesn't mean knowing what their grandmother drank for breakfast. Transactional analysis provides excellent tools for defining a character. Knowing their life plan, their two or three favorite psychological games, and the roles they play in them can be very useful. Life, our friends, our neighbors, family members are also great sources of inspiration. We can guess that the authors of 'A Family Resemblance,' 'My Big Fat Greek Wedding,' or 'Saturday, Sunday, and Monday' were inspired by characters they knew to create these delightful family portraits. In his journal, Tolstoy explains how he combined his cousins and relatives to create the characters in his novels. Finally, testimonies are also valuable sources, whether through books, documentaries, or face-to-face. One of the beauties of being a fiction writer is being led to immerse oneself, for the time of writing, in the minds of people one would never think of associating with. It's a richness we share with actors.

2. Know, at any moment of the action, what is happening in the heads and hearts of your characters. This rule applies to all characters, including villains or tertiary characters. First, it's the only way to be truthful. Then, each individual seeks above all to satisfy their personal needs. Putting yourself in each one's shoes means being able to justify their actions, in their own system. This ties into the next rule, well known to authors.

3. Love your characters, all your characters, including the least lovable ones. The word "love" here doesn't mean "want to be loved by" or "feel love for," but more deeply, "understand," "accept." Unconditional love, the most precious commodity in the world, is simply given by accepting the other. "Humanity," Chaplin reminds us in the epigraph to the long version of 'A Woman of Paris,' "is composed not of heroes or traitors but of men and women (...). The ignorant condemn their faults, but the wise have compassion for them." A good trick to love a character is to identify their wounded child. This also works in life. This rule is particularly useful for villain roles, antagonists, or main opponents. You can prove a character wrong, but you must never condemn them. It's up to the reader or viewer to do so, if necessary. In other words, an author must be the advocate of each of their characters, not their judge.

4. Be interested in your characters' journeys as if they were the protagonist. In a way, they are. Every character is the protagonist of their own story, no matter how small. This suggestion ties into rule 2, it already forces you to put yourself in the character's shoes, probably more than is generally done with secondary or tertiary characters. It can also lead you to consider your story from another angle, at least temporarily. A small but delightful moment in 'Shakespeare in Love' perfectly illustrates this principle. Shakespeare (Joseph Fiennes) and his troupe have begun rehearsing a new play called 'Romeo and Juliet.' All the actors are gathered in a tavern to celebrate. A prostitute (Patricia Patter) asks Ralph (Jim Carter), the actor playing the nurse, what this new play is about. "Well, you see, there's this nurse..." begins Ralph. In other words, Ralph sees things from his own perspective. It is often enriching for an author to see things from the perspective of each of their characters, no matter how small.

5. Do not be inconsistent. That is, make it feel that behind these bits of characterization, there is a real human being, complex, almost ambiguous and contradictory but nevertheless coherent.

6. Show a facet clearly, then add nuances. Imagine an old maid living by the sea. One day, she sees boys swimming naked in front of her house. Outraged, she goes to the police station and files a complaint. The police merely suggest the youths swim a little further away. But the next day, the old maid returns to complain: she still sees the swimmers. The police ask the impudent ones to move away radically. We have just presented a character, with a character trait. And we could leave it at that. Isn't it more interesting and perhaps more human to find that our old maid returns to the police station three days later, furious, because by climbing onto her roof, and with a good pair of binoculars, she can still see the nudists? Haddock, who has never hated the Castafiore more than in 'The Castafiore Emerald,' takes on a new dimension when he momentarily adores her (for the duration of a panel) because she promptly throws another intruder (Lampion) out. Similarly, Colonel Fitts (Chris Cooper), violently homophobic in 'American Beauty,' when he reveals his homosexuality. In 'The Last Metro,' Marion (Catherine Deneuve), actress and theater director, drinks and eats calmly on the night of a premiere. Lucas (Heinz Bennent), her husband, can't believe it. He is terribly anxious. Marion reassures him, relaxed. Then the authors add information: Marion leaves her husband and rushes to the bathroom to vomit.

The play 'La Cage aux Folles' contains one of the most beautiful secondary characters in the repertoire (unfortunately often omitted from productions): Mr. Languedoc. Mr. Languedoc is a traditional butcher, "cut from the same cloth." He arrives when Georges is laboriously trying to masculinize Albin, his partner. "It's God who sends him," says Georges, delighted to show Albin a man, a real one. Languedoc begins by explaining a recipe in a virile manner before drinking a schnapps in one go. Georges is overjoyed: what a male in all his splendor. Then Mr. Languedoc recounts how the meats in Bruegel the Elder's paintings revealed him to the world of butchery. He starts talking about painting and colors with increasingly graceful gestures, more and more feminine, and ends by saying: "They never found Fragonard's pink again." Disappointed, Georges shows him the door. In a few minutes, Jean Poiret manages to nuance a short character by creating conflict and humor. It's masterful.

In short, without making a character one thing and its opposite, adding nuances or showing that they are prey to contradictions is a way to enrich and humanize them.

7. Whether for the protagonist or secondary characters, imagine their prism. That is, their way of perceiving things in life, their value system, their professional bias. The prism should be felt in what the character does or says. In 'Panic,' the butcher (Max Dalban) makes butcher's comments. He judges people by their meat consumption. The prostitute (Lira Recio) has prostitute's concerns. She rejects the tax collector's (Guy Favières) remarks because, in other circumstances, he dares to demand taxes from her. In 'The Imaginary Invalid,' Thomas Diafoirus, a doctor, proposes to Angélique, his betrothed, to attend a woman's dissection. In the same ultra-romantic vein, in 'Taxi Driver,' Travis (Robert De Niro) takes the young woman he is courting (Cybill Shepherd) to see a porn film! In 'Day for Night,' Joëlle (Nathalie Baye) and Bernard (Bernard Menez) take advantage of a car breakdown to make love. As a result, they arrive late at their workplace (the film set). Liliane the script girl (Dani) smiles when she sees Joëlle arrive and says: "That polo doesn't match." In 'Angel,' two English valets (Ernest Cossart, Edward Everett Horton) discuss the political situation in Europe in 1936. They mention with disgust the French delegation whose representative at the League of Nations doesn't even have a valet. There is still hope that war will be avoided because the Soviets, surprisingly, were properly dressed in Geneva. Finally, let's not rush things, the Russians still dip their bread. In 'Security Arms,' Felix (Tony Randall) has been robbed. He describes the two suspects to a policeman (Al Molinaro): "One of them had mud on his shoes and a tomato stain on his shirt. The other needed a good haircut."

8. For villains, avoid those who do evil because it amuses them (with a diabolical laugh to boot!). The best villains have their reasons. Some even do evil thinking they are doing good or without realizing it. Bud's father (Pat Hingle) in 'Splendor in the Grass' and Janice's parents (Bill Dean and Grace Cave) in 'Family Life' are excellent examples. They wonderfully illustrate the classic "It's for your own good" of parents who prevent their children from becoming independent.

If you're stuck on a character 1. A very useful trick: draw inspiration from the repertoire. There are hundreds of successful characterizations in the repertoire. Draw and mix. Such a character will be a kind of Professor Calculus, another a kind of Antigone, yet another a kind of Michael Scofield (Wentworth Miller in 'Prison Break'). And immediately, your characters come to life, you see them better.

However, I do not advise imagining a specific actor in the role. It's a dangerous crutch. First, because a great role should be able to be played by anyone (competent). Second, because relying on an actor's image confines them to what they have already played. Actors are already victims of this labeling. Third, because you are never 100% sure the actor will accept the role.

2. If you're stuck on a character you don't like, it may reflect something you don't like in yourself. Accepting this part of you—a difficult task, I admit—can help unblock the character by ricochet.

3. Sometimes, replacing the character with a completely different one (older, of the opposite sex, with other character traits, etc.) and seeing what it gives can also help. Even if it means returning to the first character under better conditions.

4. Finally, an extremely profitable trick that I had the opportunity to apply successfully: imagine you have in front of you a renowned actor to whom you want to sell your character. The actor being very sought after, they have the luxury of refusing roles that have no interest in their eyes. What will you say to convince them to play the role? Go ahead, you have five minutes—think about recording yourself. Inevitably, you will characterize the character, and very probably present the most attractive aspects, even if it's a villain or a secondary role. You may be surprised to notice a gap between the summary you will make to this imaginary actor and what you have actually put in your work. You will only have to align your actions with your words.

The role of the actor If you write for theater or audiovisual, there is an important condition missing in all that precedes for the character to be successful: the role must be well played, that is, played by a good actor, well directed. Of course, we are stepping out of the author's skills here. However, it is not useless for an author to have notions of dramatic arc or, at least, to know what an actor needs to play.

Small roles, for example, are often delicate to play. The actor is asked to start a scene, or to enter it without always knowing where their character comes from, and to deliver two or three lines. The author can considerably support the actor by specifically writing for them the dialogues that precede their text, and even better the few scenes that precede their text, even if it will never appear in the final result.

It is probably no coincidence that an impressive number of playwrights and directors are or have been also actors. We all know actor-authors (Allen, Chaplin, Aeschylus, Molière, Shakespeare, Welles, etc.) and authors better known as such but who have sometimes acted (Cassavetes, Polanski, Renoir, etc.) not to mention actors turned authors (Alberto Sordi, Agnès Jaoui and Jean-Pierre Bacri, Sean Penn, etc.). What is less known is that Bergman, Forman, Mizoguchi, and many others started as actors. This does not mean that an author must necessarily be an actor to have talent, nor that an actor is necessarily able to compose dramaturgy, but it can help.

I invite all playwrights to take an acting workshop and to direct at least once in their life.

8. SCENE-BY-SCENE

"First operation: the plan with the division of the three main parts." (Carlo Goldoni) "The pearls do not make the necklace, it's the thread." (Gustave Flaubert) "I had a big event per episode. (...) It's like a backbone. The image that comes to me is that of a Christmas tree. People don't care about the trunk. They only see the balls, the lights, and the garlands. But you need a trunk. We kept referring to it." (David Chase) "By plan, I mean the developed succession scene by scene of the entire play from its beginning to its end. (...) Once my plan is finished, I go over it and ask each scene what it serves, if it prepares or develops a character, a situation, finally if it advances the action." (Eugène Labiche)

You have well planned your story, you have coherently filled the foundation entries, you are beginning to possess your characters. It is time to move on to the next step, the scene-by-scene (also called "sequence of scenes" or "sequencer"). This consists of feeding the second act by designing the broad lines of each scene but also and especially the way these scenes follow one another. I speak of the second act because, generally, at this stage of the process, the scenes that make up the first act are known. These are the scenes that come most easily. They are also the scenes that illustrate the pitch.

The most important but the most neglected The scene-by-scene is the place of structural work, one of the most precious parts of creating a story. "The most important is the arrangement of facts into a system," wrote Aristotle twenty-four centuries ago. "Most people think that screenwriting is just dialogue," explains William Goldman, "(...) The reality is that the most important contribution of a screenwriter is structure."
compared to dialogues, Paul Schrader [72] states: "Structure is infinitely more important."

Why is it so important to structure your narrative? I see several reasons. First, because structure is a factor of plausibility. If life is governed by causal relationships, we expect the same in fiction. Then, because structure satisfies our need for meaning and unity. In "What Do You Say After You Say Hello?" Eric Berne [04] explains that "most people start to feel uncomfortable as soon as they face an unstructured period of time." He concludes that "time structurers are among the most sought-after and best-paid members of any society." Structure has a third quality: it represents the most powerful language of the narrative, ahead of the language of actions, gestures, or dialogues. More meaning is generated, and more durably, by creating links between scenes than by producing dialogues.

This language of scenes involves cerebral persistence. When, for example, you install an emotional question in the mind of your receiver, they keep the question in a corner of their subconscious throughout the unfolding of the narrative. As long as you take care to feed this question in a measured way until its resolution, you create meaning and connection, and you make your receiver want to continue following you. This is also the last major quality of structure: it allows you to hook the receiver and maintain their deep interest.

Observers have not only stated that structure is the most important thing, but they have also noticed that it is the least shared skill, that "those who start in poetry are capable of finesse in expression and characters before knowing how to arrange the system of facts" (Aristotle [02]). How is it that the most important part of creating a narrative is also the most neglected? Again, several reasons. First, it's invisible. The work of structure is the submerged part of the iceberg. Authors are like David Chase's [20] spectators; they don't see the trunk in a Christmas tree, only the garlands. Second, it's difficult. "The genius who arranges incidents seems rarer than the one who finds the right speeches" (Diderot [25]). Consequently, among those who try, many struggle. Third, for most authors, structuring is not fun. It's so much more fun to come up with ideas for garlands, images, actions, dialogues, or scenes rather than ideas for sequences. Yet a scene is only beautiful if it has a function in the structure. Without that, its beauty is only superficial.

I see many works whose authors have dispensed with reflection or work on structure. Some launch a strong premise and imagine what could happen. Oh, this could happen, and then that, and then this. Let's explore, explore, without ever conceiving a backbone. I see adapters of novels or mangas who delay the inciting incident to 45 minutes, sometimes 60 (out of 90 minutes of film!). And who omit the declaration of the objective even though it exists in the original work. I see authors who ask the receiver to trust them. "Follow me, I know where I'm going." They take their starting point, their endpoint, and imagine all the scenes that more or less allow them to move from one to the other. Basically, they string pearls. Except they don't have a thread. Well, there's something better than asking the receiver to follow you blindly; you can offer them a guiding thread. Precisely by launching emotional questions and structuring your narrative. It requires courage, humility, generosity... and skill.

If you are convinced of the interest of this work and have solid foundations, you can start by placing the major dramatic knots of your narrative on an axis representing it (see pages 176-177). If your foundations include a mid-climax, a point of no return, a trajectory climax, one or more plot twists, the installation and resolution of major dramatic ironies, place them on your axis, even if it's approximate. You will thus find yourself with the first slice of what we will call the "milestones."

A. MILESTONES AND SEQUENCES

You will understand that filling the second act is not about padding. It's about giving body and substance to the heart of the narrative, and it's far from an easy operation. As an author, I have always been frustrated not to find in narratology treatises assistance for one of the biggest problems facing playwrights and screenwriters: how to fill that big eighty-minute hole, how to leap into the void? The trick of many theorists to shorten the second act and lengthen the third (with two very different parts) doesn't solve much. The hole is shorter, but first, it still needs to be filled, and second, it only postpones the problem. I ended up adopting a very practical net that consists of dividing the second act into sequences or even, more simply, into groups of scenes. To remain flexible, let's say the number of these sequences can be between two and eight.

Sequences can be designed based on the protagonist's sub-objectives since that's their very definition. But one can also first conceive what I call the "milestones," which are basically the major dramatic knots of the second act. By determining the milestones, you will similarly arrive at the sequences since there is often a sequence between two milestones. It's up to you to see if you prefer to focus on the posts or the intervals. Personally, I find it more practical to first conceive the posts, that is, the milestones. Partly because there isn't always a sequence between two milestones. Sometimes the interval between two strong dramatic knots is just made up of scenes that lead from one milestone to another and not a group of scenes underpinned by a sub-objective.

Note: Some theorists divide the second act into two parts: a descending part - the protagonist is doing worse and worse - and an ascending part - the protagonist bounces back - or the reverse depending on the nature of the ending (success or failure). The two parts are separated by the mid-climax. Like the milestone method, this method can help build the second act, but it doesn't suit all narratives. Even if this structure is clearly seen in some contemporary American films, I advise against applying it systematically. Adopt it if it suits your subject. You will note that it is not incompatible with the milestone method, the climax and the mid-climax being the two greatest milestones of the entire second act.

The milestones of "North by Northwest"

In terms of fractal structure, "North by Northwest" is a model (see Exemplary Dramatic Narratives [52]). The film is divided into seven very easily identifiable sequences. All the scenes in the film without exception belong to one of the sequences, which is very rare. Indeed, as I express above, it happens that some parts of a narrative are composed of a set of scenes not linked by a sub-objective. I invite you to watch or rewatch the film written by Ernest Lehman and Alfred Hitchcock to better understand the following analysis. When we think back to "North by Northwest" and its bravura pieces, we are not far from listing the film's milestones: Roger's (Cary Grant) kidnapping, the drunken driving, Townsend's (Philip Ober) murder at the United Nations, the CIA meeting that informs us Kaplan doesn't exist, the airplane trap, the auction, Roger's assassination by Eve (Eva Marie Saint), and finally the chase on Mount Rushmore. More or less, these are the film's milestones. Here is the entirety with the objectives or sub-objectives of Roger that result from them, that is, the objectives or sub-objectives that Roger adopts after experiencing the milestone - and not during it; the sub-objective that counts is the one that unifies a sequence, not that of the milestone itself.

1. Roger's kidnapping. It's the film's inciting incident. Objective: regain freedom. Sub-objectives: escape, convince that he is not Kaplan.
2. Drunken driving. Sub-objective: prove his innocence.
3. Roger's humiliation by the fake Mrs. Townsend (Josephine Hutchinson). Objective: clarify this mystery.
4. Townsend's murder at the UN. Sub-objective: escape the police.
5. The CIA meeting that explains Kaplan doesn't exist. Roger's sub-objective: discover what he doesn't know.
6. The scene where we discover Eve works for Vandamm (James Mason). Sub-objective: avoid traps and discover what he doesn't know.
7. The airplane trap. Sub-objective: understand Eve's role.
8. The auction. It's the climax. Roger is out of trouble, he has put himself under police protection, he no longer has an objective.
9. The airport scene where Roger learns Eve is a double agent. It's the third act's plot twist that reignites the action - we are dealing with an enriched structure. Roger's new objective: help Eve divert Vandamm's suspicions.
10. Roger's assassination by Eve. It's the climax of the sixth sequence. In the third act of this sequence, Roger learns Eve must leave with Vandamm. New sub-objective: prevent Eve from leaving.
11. The chase on Mount Rushmore. It's the climax of the third act, followed by a very short third act.

Nothing tells us that Alfred Hitchcock and Ernest Lehman placed these milestones on paper before filling the sequences as David Chase [20] explains having done on "The Sopranos." Hitchcock [41] states that at the beginning of the writing, Townsend's assassination at the UN was the film's inciting incident. The two screenwriters must have experimented with their milestones, trying different combinations. In any case, the result is impressively rigorous. Not only is the film punctuated with great moments and divided into sequences, but the authors make the effort to systematically have all his sub-objectives explained by the protagonist. It seems difficult to believe that this work was done solely by instinct. There is consciousness in the construction of "North by Northwest." In short, if you want to write a plot-driven narrative, study Hitchcock and Lehman's screenplay closely.

Milestone and sub-objective

Following each milestone, imagine what sub-objective it induces for your protagonist. They necessarily have one because a milestone acts as a local inciting incident. It's even an essential criterion. If you imagine a twist that doesn't serve as a local inciting incident for the protagonist, you may have a strong dramatic knot, but you don't have a milestone. If it doesn't create a new sub-objective, a milestone can reinforce the general objective or the previous sub-objective, that is, confirm the protagonist in their quest.

The first act-second act transition being the first milestone of the second act, the resulting sub-objective is generally the easiest means the protagonist adopts to achieve their objective. It's logical. A living organism first aims for the simplest, most obvious, least costly. Of course, in dramaturgy, it never works. At the end of the first sequence, the protagonist is forced to find a solution that requires more investment. Or it partially worked, but they must manage unforeseen consequences. Hence new sub-objectives.

Ironic sub-objective

The sub-objective can be linked to an ironic question if the milestone is the installation of a dramatic irony of which the protagonist is a victim. We have just seen two examples above with milestones 5 and 6 of "North by Northwest." Milestone No. 5, for example, is both a plot twist - we learn, surprised, that Kaplan doesn't exist - the clarification of part of the mystery - we understand why spies could have mistaken Roger for Kaplan - and the installation of a major dramatic irony - we now have information that neither Roger nor Vandamm possesses. Since Roger is a victim of this dramatic irony, he cannot have a sub-objective related to this information that he is unaware of consciously and unconsciously. It is therefore we who, by projection, give him a sub-objective related to the ironic question: discover what he doesn't know. Even if this scene doesn't change Roger's general objective and sub-objectives, it changes our perception of the entire sequel. And it changes the way the authors exploit the obstacles. When, for example, Roger seeks to see Kaplan - whom we now know doesn't exist - and Eve - whom we know works for Vandamm - organizes a meeting for him, pretending she just had Kaplan on the phone, our feeling partly depends on this milestone.

Parenthesis on the synopsis

By definition, a synopsis is a summary that provides an overview of a text or narrative. It is clearly more useful to decision-makers than to authors. Many decision-makers (especially in television and comics) require a synopsis before committing to writing - and even sometimes before paying a single cent, as if writing a synopsis didn't require time and skills. A harmful practice, of course.

Let's distinguish two types of synopses. The half-page synopsis, a maximum of one page, which summarizes the first act quite copiously and then gives an idea, in a few lines, of what the second act will be. It can even omit the ending to preserve suspense. This type of document is more of an expanded pitch. And then there's the true synopsis, several pages long, which summarizes everything that happens in the order of the narrative but in a literary style. This type of synopsis should be more of a presentation tool intended for professional organizations (once the screenplay is written) than a creation tool. It cannot be written correctly in a few days before doing substantial construction work. Writing the synopsis of a screenplay that doesn't exist is like designing the trailer for a film that hasn't been shot. As a result, when written at the beginning of the project, it often gives a poor idea of the film's potential. Not to mention that it can handicap the author who struggles to depart from it. The foundations could and should advantageously replace the synopsis. When I read well-written foundations, I see a narrative.

Yes, but then, what to do with decision-makers who demand a synopsis because it's convenient for them? First, ask for a contract and a check to write it. Second, design the foundations of your narrative and its milestones before adapting everything into a synopsis. That's why I'm talking to you about the synopsis now, at a time when you know your milestones. Even if, once again, the ideal is to write the synopsis once the screenplay is finished. It's often an enriching exercise. It's another way to have an overview of your narrative.

Some authors spontaneously write a synopsis from the start, without being asked. I see an explanation for this. Even if it's difficult to find the twists that lead from the beginning of the narrative to its end, at least when writing the summary of a story, one is in the narration, in the concrete. One can let oneself go with the flow of writing. The foundations have a "disadvantage": they require reflection and planning. They demand a certain power of abstraction - even if, as I wrote on page 52, they do not exclude spontaneity, creativity, emotion, and pleasure.

Final reflection on the synopsis, if you want or need to write one, write a condensed version of the narrative, not a condensed version of the story. If you want to summarize "Casablanca," for example, don't start with Rick's (Humphrey Bogart) and Ilsa's (Ingrid Bergman) adventure in Paris. More or less, your synopsis should respect the order of the narrative.

B. THREE EXAMPLES OF MILESTONES

The example of "North by Northwest" above serves more to show that a work can be divided into sequences than to familiarize you with the work of milestones. When I write "The airplane trap" and nothing more, I rely on your knowledge of the film to understand and see what it's about. As with the foundations, it is more enlightening to propose the milestones of a work you don't already know the embodiment and imagery of. Here are the milestones of "Couple témoin," "Le 11e homme," and "Culbuta" with the corresponding sub-objectives.

Again, don't be intimidated. If you start designing the milestones of your narrative, your first draft won't be as accomplished. There will probably be fewer milestones. The sub-objectives won't all be obvious. You may even not have a precise sub-objective for some sequences. In short, you'll have gaps, uncertainties. It's normal.

The milestones of "Couple témoin"

1. XAVIER, head of the Félicitas matchmaking agency, discovers that his wife GAËLLE has a lover. It's the film's inciting incident.
2. Jokingly, Xavier tells a former client of his agency what his objective would be if he knew he was being cheated on. It's the first act-second act transition. Xavier's general objective: save face.
3. Gaëlle understands that Xavier knows about her affair with JEAN-FRANÇOIS. She proposes a deal: she plays the exemplary couple game, in exchange, he turns a blind eye to her affair. Xavier pretends to accept. Xavier's sub-objective: break the relationship between Gaëlle and Jean-François.
4. MME VARNIER-DUVAL-LE GUEN, a client of the agency, is a mole sent by a competing matchmaking agency. Xavier's sub-objective related to dramatic irony: discover what he doesn't know.
5. Gaëlle discovers that Xavier is trying to break her relationship with Jean-François. She leaves the marital home. Xavier learns that Jean-François is married (to a certain Claire). Sub-objectives: hide the breakup with Gaëlle and manipulate CLAIRE to win back her husband.
6. Claire falls in love with Xavier. It's the mid-climax. Sub-objective: discourage Claire.
7. Xavier unconsciously falls in love with Claire. Xavier's dramatic sub-objective: understand what is happening to him and renounce this love. Trajectory sub-objective: accept his feelings.
8. To dissuade Claire from leaving her husband, Xavier bluntly tells Claire that he, Xavier, doesn't love her. Claire is devastated. Trajectory sub-objective: make amends.
9. Xavier discovers that Mme Varnier-Duval-Le Guen is a mole and that she has photos of Xavier and Claire that could raise suspicions. Sub-objective: prevent Mme Varnier-Duval-Le Guen from causing harm.
10. Instead of returning to Jean-François, Claire breaks up. Jean-François proposes to Gaëlle to start a new life with him. Gaëlle accepts. Xavier's sub-objective: get Gaëlle not to leave him immediately and to keep up appearances until the party planned for the agency's tenth anniversary.
11. The agency's anniversary party. Xavier neutralizes the mole. Despite everything, he publicly declares in front of his former and new clients that he is playing a comedy: his couple is far from being a model, and he loves another woman, Claire, present here, whom he asks to marry him in front of everyone. It's the climax. Xavier abandons his objective. End of the second act. Third act objective: start a new agency with Claire, specializing in marital counseling.

The milestones of "Le 11e homme"

1. CLAUDE, 6 years old, discovers the world of football through the passion of her father (CHARLES) and her older brother (GEORGES). She is amazed.
2. CLAUDE, 13 years old, despite being a formidable footballer, is kicked out of her team (composed of 95% boys).
3. Claude declares to her father that she wants to become a professional football player in the men's championship. It's her general objective. First sub-objective: regain her place in the boys' team.
4. Claude learns that she will soon reach the age limit for mixed teams (15 years). Sub-objective: find a way to stay with the boys.
5. With her team's complicity, Claude pretends to be a boy in the eyes of the official bodies. Sub-objective: not to be unmasked.
6. Eight years later. CLAUDE, 22 years old, still successfully passes for a young man. During the Belgian Cup final, Claude's identity is revealed. The team is disqualified. It's the mid-climax. Sub-objective: find another way to integrate men's football.
7. Claude convinces a major club to hire her. She is talented, and for the club, it will be huge publicity. Sub-objective: be accepted by her father, her teammates, and the FIFA management, embodied by HORST SANTANDER, very hostile to the presence of a woman in League 1.
8. Claude starts doping. Ironic sub-objective: stop before it has serious consequences.
9. Claude and TI GRAN, a player on her team, fall in love. Objective of this secondary plot: make their love last.
10. Santander manages to accuse Claude of cheating. Claude is arrested. The press goes wild. The players renounce the young woman. Except Tigran, but Claude doesn't know it. Dramatic sub-objective: get out of this nightmare. Ironic sub-objective: discover that Tigran supports her.
11. In exchange for her renunciation of football, Santander offers to clear Claude's name. Claude cracks and accepts. Ironic sub-objective: discover that Tigran supports her.
12. Claude understands that Tigran hasn't abandoned her. Sub-objective: play one last match. If she succeeds, she will obtain her definitive license.
13. Charles begs his daughter not to play. She is putting her health at risk. Not to mention that Santander will never let her do it. But Claude is determined. Dramatic sub-objective: play the match. Ironic sub-objective: stop before it's too late.
14. Ligue 1 Championship final. Claude obtains her license but dies from general poisoning.

The Milestones of Culbuto

1. MAXIME meets NATACHA. Love at first sight. This is the film's inciting incident. But Maxime doesn't yet have his overall goal. Sub-goal: test the waters.

2. Maxime asks ANTOINE to help him invite Natacha to dinner. This is the transition from the first act to the second act. Maxime's overall goal: win Natacha's heart.

3. The dinner is a disaster. Sub-goal: find a way to be close to Natacha. Sub-sub-goal: get hired at the theater by HANS as a medical advisor.

4. Maxime replaces one of the actors, steals the show on stage, and is humiliated by the character played by Natacha. Sub-goal: never go on stage again.

5. Natacha refuses another date with Maxime. Sub-goal: find a solution to be near Natacha.

6. Natacha and Hans fall into each other's arms. Maxime's sub-goal linked to dramatic irony: discover that it will be even more difficult than expected and understand Natacha's emotional issues.

7. Hans decides to stage a new play with Maxime in the lead role. Maxime's sub-goal: seduce Natacha as quickly as possible before the humiliations worsen.

8. Maxime discovers that Natacha is dating Hans. This is the mid-climax. Sub-goal: seduce Natacha using the theater.

9. Hans hasn't chosen Natacha for the female role in the new play ("Culbuto"). Maxime's sub-goal: get the role for Natacha.

10. Hans realizes Maxime is madly in love; he gives the role to Natacha and changes the ending of "Culbuto." Maxime's sub-goal linked to dramatic irony: discover that Hans is using him.

11. Maxime understands that Hans knows and is challenging him through the play. Sub-goal: give it his all and win Natacha's heart against Hans.

12. During a rehearsal, Giulena (played by Natacha) falls for Culbuto (Maxime). Initially euphoric, Maxime soon realizes that Natacha likes him but doesn't love him. This is the climax. Maxime abandons his goal. End of the second act.

13. Maxime agrees to play "Culbuto" only once. This is the twist of the third act. Maxime's new goal: help Natacha find out if she's meant for the theater.

14. Premiere of the new play: Maxime agrees to exploit his clumsiness, Natacha experiences a catharsis and falls into Maxime's arms. This is the climax of the third act.

These three lists were developed after a lot of writing and rewriting. Don't try to come up with a dozen milestones right away. Start with a small half-dozen. Others may come during the writing process.

C. FROM MILESTONES TO SCENE-BY-SCENE

When you have enough milestones in the second act, you can move on to developing the scene-by-scene. With this advantage, you no longer have to fill a second act of sixty or eighty minutes but sequences of ten or fifteen minutes. The work consists of finding the scenes that allow you to move from one milestone to another and/or develop a constructed sequence.

_The "what," a priori or a posteriori

Once again, the scene-by-scene work is a structural task, a syntactic work from one scene to the next. You must therefore focus on the meaning created by the sequence of scenes. In other words, the scene-by-scene is the place of the "what." What matters is knowing what each scene essentially conveys. The question of "how," of how to convey the content of each scene, will be addressed when we move on to the treatment (chapter 9).

I recognize that it is very difficult to stick to the "what" when writing a scene-by-scene. The exercise is too cerebral to be practiced perfectly. It is very tempting to elaborate and add details. If you can't be 100% synthetic, do a first draft as it comes to you and then summarize each scene. The scene-by-scene work can be done a priori or a posteriori.

You will probably feel that this scene-by-scene work stifles your imagination. But keep in mind that the scene-by-scene is the only and unique way to work on the structure. To the point that I sometimes return to a very sparse scene-by-scene when a finished text, with dialogues in full, has structural issues. If you are able to let your imagination run wild and write the scene-by-scene a posteriori to work on the sequence of scenes, then no problem. But if you can never summarize your scenes synthetically, neither before nor after writing them, you can only hope that your imagination and spontaneity are naturally... well-structured!

A skeletal scene-by-scene

Each scene in the scene-by-scene must be reduced to its bare minimum. This is the only way to express what is essentially learned from it. In an ideal scene-by-scene, scenes are summarized in two or three lines. Again, they explain what is learned but do not tell what happens. For example, you will simply write: "Mrs. Dupont is greedy," nothing more. How we learn that Mrs. Dupont is greedy will be the subject of the treatment.

This discipline makes the scenes necessary. It often happens that an author elaborates when their scene is not essential. Charming little details give the impression that the scene is useful when at best, it is interesting. The necessary and the interesting are two very different notions. A good scene should therefore be easily summarized in a few lines. If a scene cannot be summarized in less than two or three lines, it's a bad sign.

Moreover, not getting into adaptation details allows you to keep an open mind. When you develop the details of a scene, you often get used to them, without realizing that there might be a better way to convey the information.

Many Anglo-Saxon screenwriters create their scene-by-scene on index cards the size of a business card, each card representing a scene. This forces them to indicate only the essential of each scene. A similar method consists of using Post-its (one per scene, also) and sticking them on a board to have an overview of the story. William Goldman [33] takes the discipline even further: "I write a list of scenes. 30, 40, 80 scenes. In fact, they are not scenes but key words. 50 words, for example, each word being meant to remind me of a scene that advances the story. Since we are dealing with structure, this principle is crucial."

From general pitch to local pitch

In a good scene-by-scene, each scene should be able to be presented as a local dramatic pitch. The only difference with a general pitch is that each scene must summarize both the action and its result. Do not write "Alex tries to get money from his ex-wife" but "Alex manages to get money from his ex-wife" or "Alex fails to get money from his ex-wife." In other words, do not launch a mini-suspense with each scene. Local dramatic responses, positive or negative, count to grasp the meaning created by their sequence.

To fill the second act

First of all, to fill the second act, put conflict in the way of your protagonist, do not let them advance peacefully, make them struggle (with love). In other words, the majority of local dramatic responses we just talked about should be negative.

The thematic subplot

What I call a "thematic subplot" is formed when the same secondary narrative element appears three times or more and takes on a symbolic dimension. Be careful, it must be a secondary element. If it is about World War II in a film set during World War II, you are not dealing with a thematic subplot but with the main thematic plot. A thematic subplot can be linked to an animal (a cat in 'The Flowers of War'), an object (a book in 'La Reine Margot,' a hat in 'Ninotchka,' a pebble in 'Departures'), an activity (practicing English, scuba diving, smoking in 'No Man's Land'), a theme (money, bioethics, racism, etc.), a secondary character (a florist, a taxi driver, etc.).

The thematic subplot uses the preparation-payment mechanism. But that is not its only criterion. In 'The Man Who Knew Too Much,' a song ('Que Sera, Sera') is planted at the beginning of the film and pays off magnificently at the climax. However, this does not make it a thematic subplot because the song only appears twice. 'The March on Rome' contains a beautiful thematic subplot.

In 1919, Umberto (Ugo Tognazzi), a modest farmer, discovers the fascists' program printed on a poster. It talks about allowing freedom of the press, returning land to the peasants, abolishing noble titles, etc. Seduced, Umberto joins the fascist movement. As he goes through his journey, he discovers that the fascists hardly respect their program. He begins to cross out the articles that are violated. Until all the entries in the program are crossed out and Umberto makes the gesture of wiping himself with the poster. This subplot is triply thematic: 1 - it concerns an object that reappears several times, 2 - the object in question directly refers to one of the film's themes (the birth of Italian fascism), 3 - the object symbolizes the character's evolution.

Be very attentive to thematic subplots because they sometimes arise without realizing it. However, from the moment you have created a thematic subplot, you must understand what it symbolizes, exploit it, and resolve it - basically, by putting a payoff at the end. Note that it is quite possible for a thematic subplot to arise later in the writing, at the time of the treatment or even the dialogue continuity.

Organic evolution...

Two essential principles must be respected in the second act. First, avoid a series of sketches. Scenes must logically follow one another and respond to each other. The scene-by-scene is the place of general milking - when the treatment will be the place of local milking. It is by exploiting existing elements, by whipping up the few eggs you have in your basket that you will create a knitting effect and give your work an organic dimension. Consciously or unconsciously, authors like spectators know that structure is important (cf. above). Consciously or unconsciously, authors (not spectators) know that it is also one of the most difficult languages to master. As a result, some pretend to offer structural work by avoiding the difficulty. First structural avoidance: false knitting. You take three pearl necklaces and mix them. Most series episodes are constructed this way. In 'Friends,' you have three plots: A, B, and C. A typical episode of 'Friends' is constructed like this: A1-B1-C1-A2-B2-C2-A3-B3-C3-A4-B4-C4. In 'Desperate Housewives,' the principle is the same with four plots instead of three. Second structural avoidance: the quest narrative. Police or not. It is very easy to structure, you start with the crime then you follow with the investigation, clues, interrogations and you end with the resolution of the mystery. Needless to say, in both cases, there is only an illusion of structure.

There are several ways to give a story an organic dimension. The most powerful consists of launching emotional questions (dramatic, ironic, or trajectory) at the beginning of the story and proposing only scenes that lead to their resolution. The receiver will have these questions in a corner of their subconscious (cerebral persistence, cf. above), will perceive the function of each scene, will be torn between hope and fear, in short, will participate.

You can also create an organic dimension through a subplot. In 'The Pigeon,' one of the co-protagonists, Mario (Renato Salvatori), is attracted to Carmela (Claudia Cardinale) who is kept locked up at home by her brother Michele (Tiberio Murgia). To get the young woman to open up, Mario takes advantage of the absence of her guard and pretends to be him: "It's Michele, I forgot the key." She falls for it and Mario discovers that Carmela is charming. Thirty minutes later in the film, Mario returns to the charge. He imitates Michele again but this time, of course, the young woman's reaction is different: she plays along. A quarter of an hour later in the film, it's the real Michele who has genuinely forgotten his keys. Carmela opens with pleasure, convinced it's Mario, and gets scolded. You will notice that this subplot is built on the principle of the triad A-A-N. These three scenes scattered in the middle of the second act of 'The Pigeon' undeniably create a link. And even a double link. There is first a link between the three scenes since they develop a subplot. But there is also a link between these three scenes and the other scenes that surround them. This subplot acts as a binder. Another quality of this subplot: its elements have repercussions on the main action. We see Mario interested in Michele's sister in a previous scene, at the beginning. Then, after the triad, Michele seeks revenge, which pushes Mario to abandon his co-protagonists. And finally, in a sixth scene, Michele entrusts Carmela to Mario in case the burglary goes wrong. Skillfully, these six scenes are thus sprinkled between the 8th and the 77th minute of the film. If you chain several scenes of a subplot at once, you give the impression that you have abandoned the main action and are telling something else.

In 'Kung Fu Panda,' Master Oogway summons his chamberlain, Shifu, to tell him he had a vision: the terrifying Tai Lung escaping from his prison and sowing terror in the valley. Immediately, Shifu orders the goose Zeng to run to the prison where Tai Lung is locked up and further strengthen security. Oogway then comments: "It is on the path where one believes to avoid it that one often meets one's destiny." And indeed, Zeng's arrival in the high-security prison has the perverse effect of allowing Tai Lung to escape. If Shifu had not feared Tai Lung's escape and had not sent Zeng, Tai Lung would not have been able to escape. Story enthusiasts will recognize a mechanism, already at work in the myth of Oedipus, sometimes called a "self-fulfilling prophecy." By creating these causal relationships in their story, authors create a link between scenes.

In short, think about creating causal relationships and evolution effects from one scene to another. This consists of exploiting to the maximum everything that is planted at the beginning of the story, to pay off in scene x + 10 what was used in scene x. For example, by reusing secondary characters. No scene should give the impression that it constitutes a parenthesis that could be removed without altering the rest of the story. Moreover, your protagonist should not get rid of their obstacles to move on to the next one. It is by getting rid of their obstacles - either by overcoming them or bypassing them - that the protagonist creates new obstacles for the future, as if they had set off an avalanche. Finally, remember that a long dramatic irony can also serve to unify a series of sketches.

... and crescendo

The second important principle to respect is to reserve the strongest conflicts for the end. That is, to build a crescendo. This can consist of proposing more and more obstacles or conflicts, in quality as well as in quantity, but it can also be a relationship between two characters that gradually degenerates. When a character accumulates resentment stamps and fills their vase, you clearly feel a rise towards overflow. It's a form of crescendo. 'Alien,' 'Invasion of the Body Snatchers,' 'Rambo,' and 'The Towering Inferno' offer four beautiful examples of crescendo in the category of plot stories. 'One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest' is a fine example in the category of character stories. The further we go in the second act, the more the institutions that MacMurphy (Jack Nicholson) faces respond with violence. 'Mustang' also offers a magnificent crescendo. The film tells how five sisters (Günes Nezihe Sensoy, Daga Zeynep Doguslu, Tugba Sunguroglu, Elit Iscan, Ilayda Akdogan) suffer the weight of traditions in Turkey. The further we go, the more confinement is imposed on the girls. They are veiled, computers and phones are confiscated, traditional teaching replaces school, the girls are forcibly married one after the other, the house is increasingly barricaded, and the consequences become tragic.

The combination of evolution and crescendo creates a "snowball" effect, giving an impression of escalation that is both logical and rewarding for the viewer. It starts with a tiny match and, little by little, the house is on fire. Or, if you prefer, it starts with a sigh and ends with an orgasm. 'The Chase' offers a beautiful example of the first metaphor (the fire).

::>'<!\! res sum ig_n ourJ c_èn_e-_à_-_sc_è_n_e

1. Plan as little exposition as possible and spread it over the entire work. We will return to this in chapter 10.

2. Think about providing rest periods in the middle of the action. For two reasons: a - to reinforce, by contrast, the strength of the obstacles, b - to give the viewer hope that the protagonist can achieve their goal. A rest period can be a gag, a moment of joy or happiness, a comedown scene, but also a local success, that is, a scene where the protagonist achieves their local goal.

3. Do not maintain a sustained pace throughout the second act, vary the effects, avoid monotony. For this, you have several tools: surprise, dramatic irony, false ally, mid-climax, subplot, etc. Frantisek Daniel had a beautiful image about this. A story, he said, should not make you think of "... and then... and then... and then..." but rather "... suddenly... moreover... and then... consequently... two years before... except that... all of a sudden... and then... meanwhile... nevertheless..."

The principle of contrast, which I refer to here (in 2 and 3) has a huge advantage: it keeps the receiver's attention. Living organisms tend to adapt to a situation, even negative. After a while, they no longer experience it as negatively as at the beginning. Thus, a viewer gets tired of constant conflict, even if this conflict is powerful. To stay alert, their brain needs relief, variations, ups and downs, bright moments, and dark moments. This contrast work can be done at all stages of creation, from writing to editing (in the case of cinema), through imaging. But the strongest breaks will be those that are inscribed in the scene-by-scene.

4. Start simple, with few characters and clear situations, and gradually complexify. Do not do like Molière in the first scene of 'Tartuffe' or like those soap operas that present fifteen characters from the first episode - and keep them in the following ones.

5. If you want to convey narrative information at a moment in the story, avoid designing a scene just for that. See if the information can be inserted into an already existing scene. In short, kill two or three birds with one stone. Beyond three strokes, you risk a lack of unity in the scene.

On excess

Do not hesitate to go very far. Dare, impress yourself! If you find an idea that makes you say: "Oh no, it's too much, it will never pass," you might be onto something. Maybe. Some claim that in all circumstances, excess is insignificant. This is probably true in life, where truth is often found in the middle, in moderation. Even if, in terms of excess, life often surpasses fiction - we will come back to this. But it is manifestly false in art. Great authors, from Shakespeare to Almodovar, from Molière to Hitchcock, through Brecht, Lubitsch, or Bergman, are excessive. And, manifestly, it has worked for them.

In 'The Heart is a Lonely Hunter' or 'Oedipus Rex,' the protagonist blinds himself. In 'The Unknown,' Alonzo (Lon Chaney) has both arms amputated to marry the woman he loves. In 'The Ballad of Narayama,' Orin (Kinuyo Tanaka) does not hesitate to break her teeth to appear older. In 'The Sex of Angels,' Meo (Paolo Ferrari) agrees to be castrated. In 'Desire Under the Elms,' a mother kills her infant. One could accumulate examples, especially in comedy. Because it is the treatment that goes the furthest. If you think about it, who would dare, in life, to change their sexual identity to get a job (cf. 'Some Like It Hot,' 'Tootsie,' or 'Victor Victoria')? Or do what the protagonists of 'To Be or Not to Be' do?

If you should not hesitate to be excessive from time to time, you must nevertheless avoid being so all the time. Going very far means starting from a point, generally normal, to arrive at another, paroxysmal. Otherwise, due to lack of contrast, you get overexcited and hysterical works.

Excess is surely one of the secrets of the success of American cinema2. If excess causes Americans, and sometimes the rest of the planet, many problems, it is clear that, in the field of cinema, it is a formidable asset. Truffaut [84] understood this well. Despite his realistic temperament, he declared wanting to film paroxysms.
