2. The other secrets, in my opinion, are having a very developed Free Child and having understood and assimilated three truths from the birth of cinema: a - it is a business for the story, b - it is a very powerful cultural vector, c - it is also an industry.
Some might say there are more measured authors: Taniguchi, Kiarostami, Sarraute, Chekhov, etc. It's interesting to note that they can afford this because they are rare. They are the exceptions that prove the rule. Moreover, the restraint of these authors might only be apparent. Characters unable to live in the present moment (The Three Sisters), who tear each other apart over a simple intonation (For No Good Reason), who seek someone to help them commit suicide (The Taste of Cherry), or who wait for their father to die to take an interest in him (My Father’s Journal), are they really that measured? Excessive doesn't mean agitated and spectacular. Character traits can be as exaggerated as situations. And then, as we've seen, all comedy is excess. That of Jerry Lewis, obviously, but also that of Jacques Tati or Eduardo De Filippo.

Comedy Technique
Precisely, if you're writing a comedy or your story contains comedic elements, it's advisable to work on it from the scene-by-scene stage. This will be the best way to achieve situational and character comedy, which is infinitely more desirable than relying solely on slapstick and jokes, which can always be added later. Even silent film clowns, who started their careers with sketches, understood the need for structural work when they moved to feature films. In my mind, there is situational comedy when the comedy is created by the context. In other words, the gag—the element that makes you laugh—is necessarily a payoff. When, in Life is Beautiful, Guido (Roberto Benigni) translates the German corporal's (Hannes Hellmann) instructions in a completely fanciful way, it's situational comedy. It can even be produced by dialogue, a payoff dialogue in this case. Osgood's "Nobody's perfect" in Some Like It Hot is situational comedy.

For character comedy, the gag must be linked to the characterization of a character. In The Dinner Game, Pignon (Jacques Villeret) pretends to be a Belgian producer to find out where his host's (Thierry Lhermitte) wife is. He makes his interlocutor believe he wants to acquire the rights to a novel to adapt it into a film. He gets so caught up in his role that he forgets his mission. The highlight is when he hangs up. He still doesn't know where his host's wife is, but he's proud to have secured the rights.

Mockery, Displacement, and Detachment
Other suggestions for creating comedy.
1. Mock your characters. Comedy is inherently mockery. Be careful, the word "mock" is not to be taken in the sometimes pejorative sense it is given. There are several forms of mockery. Mocking others doesn't necessarily mean taking them for fools who understand nothing. Sure, one can mock someone to humiliate or devalue them. But one can also mock someone to help them improve. "The duty of comedy is to correct men by entertaining them," explains Molière in the first placet of Tartuffe. Or mock with compassion, to console the other. Yes, yes, console them. Compassionate mockery is a way of saying to the other: "Welcome to the club, we are all brothers in misfortune." It's a solidarity mockery.
2. Stage failure, make your characters stumble. Schematically, serious drama can be considered as overcoming a series of increasing obstacles. The protagonist doesn't reach their goal immediately but gets closer. In comedy, the first wall is already too high for the protagonist. Not only must they try another, but it gets worse. Staging comedic failure is telling the characters: "Give up, you're ridiculous." And since, inevitably, the characters don't give up right away, as they don't always realize they're ridiculous, they give us plenty of material for comedy.
3. Show that the characters believe in it. They have trivial obsessions, grotesque limitations, but it absorbs them. In other words, they have no perspective on themselves or what happens to them. This rule also applies to actors, who must play comedy as seriously as possible, staying at face value. Actors who play humorous situations with a wink to the audience, as if to say "See how funny I am?" or "Don't be fooled, I'm not that stupid," spoil a good part of the show.
4. Create distance, detachment. Distance (spatial or temporal) concerns two people: the author and the spectator.
4a. Creating distance for the author means not taking oneself seriously, nor life, nor others. It also means mocking one's work, mocking the characters and situations one has invented.
4b. Creating distance for the spectator means avoiding suffering. When you watch fall videos, have you noticed that some make you burst out laughing while others make you wince? Those that make you wince always involve falls where you fear the victim got hurt. Certainly, comedy implies conflict. Inevitably. Comedy thrives on human limitations, hence life's difficulties. But one cannot laugh at a conflicting element if one feels compassion for the victim of the conflict, if one identifies with them. To laugh, one needs detachment.
4c. Maintaining the spectator's distance also involves creating one or more dramatic ironies (frank ones). It's no coincidence that dramatic irony is omnipresent in comedy.
5. Create displacements. Along with detachment, it's the second magical ingredient of comedy. Generally, this process is not conscious. Authors with a sense of humor do it without realizing it. But it can be useful to know the principle. When writing The Fugitives, Francis Veber had a scene where the protagonists go to find a doctor to treat one of them. He didn't find it funny. And for good reason: the doctor acted normally. Until one day Veber had the idea to replace the doctor with a veterinarian, creating a comedic displacement. If Veber had thought "displacement" when he was stuck on his scene, he might not have been stuck for weeks.
5. Exploit everything, up to the topper. Milking, this tool that involves exploiting what you've set up, making the most of everything, often leads to comedy.
More developments on comedy in chapter 9 of Dramaturgy.

Fiction or Reality, Which Surpasses the Other?
It's often said that "fiction surpasses reality." Uh... or is it rather the opposite? I think fiction has the obligation to surpass average reality, that of "normal" people or "normal" situations, and it often succeeds. However, it struggles to be as strong as the extreme cases of life. To be convinced, just look at news stories or the hilarious Darwin Awards, which annually reward the most extreme and stupid real situations (www.darwinawards.com).
For example, the farmer who seals his head in a plastic bag to avoid being stung by bees and ends up suffocating. Or the man who comes to pick up ID photos, suddenly decides to rob the photographer, and flees, forgetting his photos on the counter. Or the alcoholic who, after a fight, arrives at the emergency room with a bloody stomach. He's so violent that they have to tie him securely to a stretcher. He then grabs his lighter to try to burn the straps, Rambo-style, only to set his stretcher on fire and end up in the burn unit. A few years ago in Eastern Europe, a fortune teller announced to a man that he or his son would soon die. The man committed suicide out of fear that the prediction would target his son.
This incredible story—as they say—is true. It's clear that a "normal" man who, like most people, would place very limited trust in a fortune teller's predictions, wouldn't make a good fictional character. He'd be too moderate. But does that mean the one who committed suicide would work? As soon as characterization, action, or a series of circumstances seem exaggerated, either the spectator doesn't believe it, or the author must prepare it to make it plausible, thereby diminishing its impact. And once again, reality proves stronger than all the world's fictions. Sometimes, the only solution is to warn at the beginning of the work: "Based on a true story."

If You Want to Deconstruct
In recent years, the trend has been towards deconstructed narratives. The beginning is placed at the end and vice versa, or there are endless flashbacks, or everything is mixed up. The motivations seem diverse. Deconstruction can be imposed by the subject and produce beautiful successes. This is the case, in my opinion, with Memento. Some authors may also want to play with the spectator. They offer us a puzzle and ask us to participate. This is the case with 21 Grams. All this seems commendable to me. But deconstruction sometimes resembles a smokescreen, an admission of impotence, or even a sign (often relevant) of a lack of confidence in their narrative—we've already discussed this on pages 45-46.
Two remarks about deconstruction. Before deconstructing, you must construct. Before mixing, you must have something to mix. In other words, finish your scene-by-scene before blowing everything up like a puzzle. Moreover, consider that the highest degree of comedy, namely situational comedy, accommodates very poorly with deconstruction. Probably because it's better to place a payoff after its setup than the reverse. Imagine The Dinner Game, City Lights, or To Be or Not to Be out of order; it would be a mess.

There is a highly instructive film on this subject, Three Burials, written by one of the masters of deconstruction: Guillermo Arriaga. There are two very distinct parts in the film. The first half follows the completely fragmented principle of 21 Grams. It seems a bit gratuitous—or a bit systematic for those who have already seen Amores Perros and 21 Grams—probably because there are fewer important characters than in those two films. As for the second half of Three Burials, which offers fewer strong dramatic knots and is more subtle, it's symptomatic that it's told in order. When you start delving into the intimate and the big twists become scarce, it becomes difficult to mix everything up.

Although for Your Eyes
To conclude on the scene-by-scene, I strongly advise against having it read by anyone. Or at least, by someone who knows nothing about your project. Because a rigorously written scene-by-scene is very dry and impossible to understand for a reader discovering the project. Reserve your scene-by-scene for those who have followed the development from the beginning: your co-authors if you have any, the members of the writing workshop you participate in, possibly your producer.

THREE SCENE-BY-SCENE EXCERPTS
First Act of the Scene-by-Scene of Witness Couple
1. XAVIER, 37, and GAËLLE, 32, run a matchmaking agency and proudly display their couple. They select clients based on their genuine romantic motivations. Then they help them enhance themselves. To please, one must also appear.
2. A 70-year-old widow, MME VARNIER-DUVAL-LE GUEN, registers with the agency hoping to find her fourth husband.
3. In a month, Xavier and Gaëlle will celebrate both their 10th wedding anniversary and the agency's 10th anniversary. Xavier is in control, and Gaëlle obeys.
4. Gaëlle pretends to Xavier that she's taking pottery classes. In fact, she has a lover, JEAN-FRANÇOIS, 35, a jovial and somewhat wild man.
5. Xavier and Gaëlle showcase themselves as a model couple and host a dinner-meeting in front of clients. Xavier gives a lecture on trust. Gaëlle plays along. The act is well-rehearsed.

6. Xavier discovers that Gaëlle's pottery is "Made in Taiwan." He doesn't understand. And the trust? Why did she make him believe she was taking classes if she's not interested?
7. Xavier discovers that Gaëlle has a lover. He is devastated.
8. Xavier is determined to keep up appearances. While waiting to win Gaëlle back.

First Act of the Scene-by-Scene of The 12th Man
1. CLAUDE (6 years old) discovers the world of football with wonder alongside her father, CHARLES RETZ (41 years old). On the field, GEORGES (18 years old), Claude's brother, is the star of the day and Charles's pride. The coach is FRANCK MARCANTONI (40 years old).
2. Claude has been motherless since birth. She wishes to be as admired by her father as her brother Georges is.
3. Charles is a metallurgical unionist, president of FC Ougrée, an amateur football club. He lives in the memory of his late wife, a passionate advocate of the union struggle. Claude drinks in her father's words about this woman he adorns with all qualities and whom she never knew.
4. Claude has become a football fan. She manages to join the group of boys in her neighborhood and plays football with them.
5. Claude is now 13 years old. She has become the best player in her club, she is extremely passionate and aware of the limited future that women's football can offer her.
6. Franck Marcantoni, former coach of Georges turned internationally renowned coach, is sentenced to prison and community service for match-fixing.
7. As a footballer, Claude has obvious technical and tactical qualities. But her pride and need to control everything play tricks on her.
8. Following a defeat, Claude is unjustly kicked out of her boys' team. Only BRUNO, her childhood friend, supports her. Some boys display machismo. The age limit for mixed teams (15 years) is soon reached for Claude.
9. As club president, Charles refuses to intervene to reinstate his daughter. It's up to her to fight if she wants to rejoin the boys' team. That said, Charles thinks Claude should join women's football.
10. Disheartened, angry at the world, Claude has a vision: she plays in a packed stadium before being carried in triumph by a male team, a trophy in hand.

11. Claude declares to her father that she wants to become a professional player, which is impossible in the women's league. Charles doesn't believe it at all.

First Act of the Scene-by-Scene of Culbuto
1. Introduction of MAXIME LAVILLE, 37 years old. He suffers from a motor regulation disorder since childhood, making him extremely clumsy, randomly. He is also very sensitive and cannot stand being laughed at. This has already cost him more than one romantic relationship.
2. Maxime earns his living as a healthy volunteer at the Clinical Investigation Center (CIC) run by ANTOINE (40 years old). He lives alone in a dilapidated studio with a lab mouse (GÉLULE).
3. At the CIC, Maxime meets NATACHA, 30 years old, a new nurse. Love at first sight.
4. Maxime is attracted to Natacha and, at the same time, afraid of suffering.
5. Maxime learns that Natacha is not a nurse but an actress.
6. Maxime goes to see Natacha at a cultural center where she performs an adaptation of The Government Inspector. He returns several times. The audience is sparse.
7. Maxime meets HANS, the director, 57 years old, confident and charming.
8. Maxime has a plan: invite Natacha to dinner, tête-à-tête, but without revealing his feelings.
9. To afford the chic restaurant, Maxime participates in a new test protocol in Switzerland.

THE TREATMENT

"You must first sketch a general outline, then introduce episodes and develop."
(Aristotle)
"... the story of the screenwriter who racked his brains to concisely show how a forty-year-old and his wife were no longer in love. He finally found it. The couple enters an elevator. He keeps his hat on. At the next stop, a woman enters the elevator. The man immediately removes his hat. That's exemplary cinematic writing. I would have made it four pages. What this guy found takes a few seconds."
(Raymond Chandler)
As we've seen, the scene-by-scene allows you to work on the overall structure of the story. Once the scene-by-scene is not only completed but matured, you can move from the general to the local. I invite you to do this work in two stages. The first stage will concern the "how" (with the treatment) while the second stage will be devoted to the nuances, details, and plausibility brought by the dialogues (with the dialogue continuity).
The first step of the adaptation consists, for each scene, in determining the best way to convey its content. What conflict, what situation, what character, what setting, what payoff, what activity will best show that "Mrs. Dupont is greedy"? This results in an enriched scene-by-scene called "treatment" or "non-dialogue continuity." As this latter name indicates, this stage does not include dialogue, or very little.

LOCAL FOUNDATIONS
We've seen that most scenes in the scene-by-scene can be presented as a local pitch. In the transition to the treatment, you can do foundational work for each local pitch as you did for the entire work. I concede that it might be tedious to conceive all the local foundations for each scene. But I strongly encourage you to find at least the setting, the protagonist, the central conflict, and the climax of each scene. And, if possible, one or two meaningful activities. And all this before writing a single line of treatment.

Examples of Transition from Scene-by-Scene to Treatment
Imagine your main characters are young students and your scene-by-scene indicates this: "Charlotte asks Kevin not to dump her. He insists and tells her he might come back to her if she changes." A lazy screenwriter would show Charlotte going to find Kevin on the street or at the end of a class. She would ask him not to dump her. They might have a conflictual exchange. And Kevin would end up saying: "I might come back to you if you change." In this case, the transition from scene-by-scene to treatment adds nothing, and everything relies on the dialogues. Now imagine Charlotte and Kevin are in a German class at a certain distance from each other. Charlotte gets caught by the teacher passing a little handwritten note. She asks Kevin not to abandon her. In front of the whole class, the teacher forces Charlotte to translate the note into German. Charlotte complies. Then the teacher forces Kevin to respond. In front of everyone and in German, Kevin suggests Charlotte signal him when she has changed. I'm sure you can be even more creative, but at least in this second configuration, 1 - an arena has been exploited, 2 - Charlotte has shown resourcefulness, and 3 - conflict has been added, which probably made her character more endearing. In short, the scene has gained by transitioning from scene-by-scene to treatment.

Let's take another example: the first appearance of an important character in The Toy. In the scene-by-scene, it would read: "François Perrin discovers President Rambal-Cochet, whom he has heard so much about: a stinking CEO feared by all his employees." In treatment version: "In the courtyard of the Rambal-Cochet factory, tables have been set up on trestles for an open-air lunch. The employees have already started eating because the CEO has announced he would be late. Suddenly, his car stops at the back of the courtyard. Everyone stands up respectfully. He is escorted to his seat, which is at the end of the table. Without a word, Rambal-Cochet sits down and, instead of pulling his chair closer to the end of the table, grabs the board and pulls it a good meter towards him, causing confusion among the diners. Then he eats, satisfied, indifferent to others, while the diners juggle plates and glasses without daring to complain." This insane gesture characterizes Rambal-Cochet in seconds as no succession of dialogues could.

Final example, from Parenthood. In the scene-by-scene, one might simply read: "Susan announces to Nathan that she's leaving him." An unworthy screenwriter would conceive a classic breakup scene with lots of dialogue, possibly a raised voice and some tears. Lowell Ganz and Babaloo Mandel exploited the characterization of the characters, which is not ordinary. Indeed, Nathan (Rick Moranis) and his wife Susan (Harley Kozak) are trying to make their 4-year-old daughter (Ivyann Schwan) a little genius. But Susan finds that the child is not well-balanced and that Nathan is going too far. She begins to detach from her husband's educational principles. Problem is, he's so busy that she has trouble talking to him about it. One day, while Nathan is having their daughter recognize chemical symbols on cards, Susan offers him a new set of cards. Very interested, Nathan starts reading them: 1 - This is, 2 - the only way, 3 - to get, 4 - your attention. Nathan protests: their daughter is well above this kind of cards. Then he continues reading: 5 - I'm leaving you. Nathan looks at his wife, stunned and incredulous, and says: "You're leaving me?!" Susan shows him one last card: 6 - Yes.

You understand, moving from "what" to "how" involves, among other things, exploiting and even milking what you've set up, particularly the arena and the characterization of your characters. It also involves being creative. An exercise given in some screenwriting schools is called "I'm pregnant." It involves writing a scene in which a woman announces to her partner that she's expecting a child from him. The scene must not resemble the thousands of similar scenes already existing in the repertoire. The first volume of the series The Old Geezers contains a delightful one. Using a treasure hunt, the future mother leads the future father to discover a puppet in a drawer, but he doesn't understand the metaphor and only worries that his underwear has disappeared from the drawer.

Double "How"
In the transition from scene-by-scene to treatment, it may happen that you don't ask yourself how to convey this or that narrative element but rather how a character will go about achieving their local goal. It's not just you who must be creative, your character must be too.
One of the dramatic knots of "Some Like It Hot" could be summarized as follows: "Joe, disguised as a Shell company heir, takes Sugar on Osgood's yacht and seduces her." No need to say more in a scene-by-scene. Now put yourself in the shoes of Billy Wilder and I.A.L. Diamond. Their mission is to find the best way to handle this dramatic knot. They must first respect the situation and the characterization of the characters. The essence of the situation lies in the fact that Joe is not a billionaire at all and does not know the yacht on which he has taken Sugar. The essence of Sugar's characterization was established at the beginning of the second act. She explained to Joe (then disguised as Josephine) that she was tired of men who take advantage of her, grope her, and always end up taking money from her. Since Sugar is already half-conquered by Joe, one can easily imagine what a weak or lazy screenwriter would have done with this scene. Here is what Wilder and Diamond proposed. It's a summary of the scene as it might appear in a treatment.

"Joe takes Sugar on Osgood's yacht using a motorboat. But since he doesn't know how to use it, he goes in reverse. On the yacht, which he awkwardly passes off as his own, Joe makes Sugar understand that he is heart-disabled due to emotional trauma. He has tried everything to cure his handicap: Freud, Balinese dancers, everything, but in vain. Sugar is touched. It makes her want to help Joe. She kisses him to see if it has any effect. Joe pretends to be insensitive. Sugar insists. Joe eventually gives in."

In the middle of "In Her Eyes," a policeman (Ricardo Darin) and an investigating judge (Soledad Villamil) try to make the presumed culprit of a heinous sexual crime (Javier Godino) confess. The man is tough. How to credibly push him to confess? The scene-by-scene could simply indicate: "Irene and Benjamin make Gómez confess," but when it comes to the treatment, a satisfying "how" must be found. The authors found a trick that involves Ericksonian hypnosis. Irene arrives in the office where Benjamin is interrogating Gómez. She sizes him up and asserts that he cannot be the rapist-murderer. Because Gómez is clearly a runt, he couldn't have had the strength and especially the sexual power to do what was done to the victim. Gómez can't stand these attacks on his virility and ends up, after brooding, betraying himself.

No dramatic question before the inciting incident. Since conflict is the indispensable tool for any good narrative, it must also be included in the first act, even when the action hasn't started. The operation is particularly delicate before the inciting incident. You need to present static rather than dynamic conflicts. Routine rather than exceptional conflicts. And resolved conflicts. Without this last condition, you risk establishing a strong objective before the protagonist's general objective, which can confuse the viewer. For the same reason, be careful with all dramatic knots, even trivial ones, that disrupt the future protagonist's routine life and may give the viewer the impression that they've encountered the major inciting incident.

Resolving a local conflict means not posing a long-term dramatic question. The protagonist of the scene abandons their objective at the end of the scene. Either because they have achieved it or because they give up on achieving it. If, for example, two characters try to convince each other in a first-act scene and fail, it's better to end with: "Oh well, to each their own" than with: "It won't happen like that!" In the latter case, it's understood that one of the characters hasn't given up their objective, and it already gives direction to the story. It can be risky.

The two emotional questions that can be launched before the inciting incident are the trajectory question if there is one—the "need" before the "want," as our American friends say—and the ironic question, if it doesn't launch another action than the one planned. When two questions of this type are posed, the receiver unconsciously knows that they still need to learn the dramatic question to embark on the story.

Favor activity. Don't hesitate to spend time searching for meaningful activity. Don't keep saying: "The dialogue will take care of it." In theater, many playwrights delight in words and leave it to directors to handle the activity. Playwrights would benefit from being less verbose, more visual, and also more descriptive, meaning doing a minimum of staging on paper—without going so far as to indicate on which step a character should be, as in some texts by Feydeau.

Exploit your local arenas. If a scene can take place in a hotel room, why set it at the top of the Eiffel Tower? Of course, it's always nice to vary the settings. The viewer is sensitive to it. But the setting can seem gratuitous or, worse, distract from the scene. The ideal is to find the setting that adds something to the scene. In other words, if you insist on the top of the Eiffel Tower, you must exploit it. In "When Harry Met Sally," there is a scene where Harry (Billy Crystal) listens to a friend (Bruno Kirby) recounting his troubles. It's a pure dialogue scene (like most scenes in the film) that Nora Ephron chose to set in a stadium. The two friends are sitting in the middle of the crowd, in front of an American football game. From time to time, a wave of ola shakes the stadium: the crowd stands and sits, raising their arms. Mechanically, Harry and his friend do the same without conviction, while continuing to talk, creating an amusing discrepancy.

Sometimes, the setting is imposed by the scene. This is the case in the "Some Like It Hot" example above. Joe made Sugar believe he owned Osgood's yacht, he promised to take her there. The scene cannot take place elsewhere. But sometimes, the setting remains to be found. The scene between Harry and his friend (When Harry Met Sally) could take place anywhere. Rather than doing like Nora Ephron, choosing a stadium and gently exploiting it, imagine a setting that will bring additional conflict to your protagonist. Like the language lab, in the scene between Charlotte and Kevin above.

B. AVOID DEUS EX MACHINA AND CONVENIENCES
A deus ex machina is a narrative element that comes to help the protagonist and gives the unpleasant impression of appearing out of nowhere. In reality, the viewer senses that this element doesn't come out of nowhere but from the author's hat, making it even more unpleasant. In a broad sense, a deus ex machina is any convenience that suits the author.

Managing the aids and solutions for the protagonist is a significant part of our activity. There are several ways to avoid a deus ex machina. The most radical solution is to ensure that the help comes from the protagonist themselves and not from outside. Any protagonist worthy of the name finds within themselves the resources to get out of trouble.

That said, unless you're called MacGyver (MacGyver), a protagonist can't do everything, provoke a solar eclipse, resurrect their neighbor, or master nuclear fission overnight. Here are some techniques to avoid conveniences. Note: Some can be combined.

1. Preparation. Prepare the help. Preparation is the great antidote to deus ex machina. In the middle of "The Great Dictator," the little Jewish barber (Charles Chaplin) is in a bad spot; Tomanian soldiers are about to hang him. He owes his salvation to the last-minute intervention of a Tomanian officer, Schultz (Reginald Gardiner). Deus ex machina? Not quite. Because the barber had saved Schultz's life at the beginning of the film. In "The Temple of the Sun," the eclipse that saves the protagonists was prepared by Hergé. Tintin finds a newspaper clipping announcing it and requests that his execution be scheduled for that date.

Even more radical: the magic potion of Asterix—which, to say the least, often helps our protagonists—is part of the very premises of the series. René Goscinny even had the intelligence to make it the subject of the first three albums. In "Asterix the Gaul," the Romans try to discover the secret of the magic potion. In "The Golden Sickle," Asterix and Obelix seek an essential tool for making the magic potion. And in "Asterix and the Goths," the protagonists strive to find the druid who makes their magic potion and who has been kidnapped by the Goths. After these three albums, the magic potion becomes an obvious fact that no one would think to question. It will be noted that Goscinny then endeavored to tell stories where the magic potion is unnecessary (cf. "Asterix in Britain," "Asterix and the Cauldron," "Asterix and the Normans," "The Soothsayer," "The Great Divide," etc.). Because he knows that, magic potion or not, it's not enough to help his protagonists; obstacles must also be placed in their path. Gene Roddenberry had the same problem with the all-powerful technology he had set up for Star Trek.

Don't just prepare the help. You must also make this preparation discreet, in other words, not create a phone. Then, when the preparation pays off, the protagonist must not let themselves be saved without doing anything. Make them active and continue to make them experience conflict.

2. Inversion. Put the help before the viewer is aware of the problem. In "Three Days of the Condor," Turner (Robert Redford) returns home. He is stopped at the foot of the building by his concierge (Carol Gustafson), who reveals the presence of individuals in his apartment.

Turner guesses that the individuals in question mean him no good and decides to flee. He thus avoids an obstacle thanks to the concierge. But this help is not felt as a deus ex machina because it occurs at the very moment we learn there is an obstacle. It is clear that if we had been informed of the threatening presence of the killers in the apartment before Turner returned home, the concierge's intervention would have been less well accepted.

Another trick, in "A Knight's Tale," William (Heath Ledger), a roofer by trade, pretends to be a knight to participate in tournaments. Problem, he will need letters of nobility proving his status. If the author puts a public writer on his path capable of forging documents, we have a nice deus ex machina. In the film, Brian Helgeland creates the encounter between William and a man named Chaucer (Paul Bettany) at a time when there is no conflict to resolve. Only afterward do we learn that Chaucer is a writer, and it is he himself who later informs the protagonist that he will need letters of nobility proving his lineage over several generations. He then offers to take care of it in exchange for clothes, food, and protection.

Of course, this principle of inversion cannot be systematic, as it amounts to answering the local dramatic question at the moment it is posed. We cannot resolve all conflicts before they even arise, otherwise, nothing happens.

3. Justification by the past. Go into a flashback that explains where the help will come from and justifies it. Be careful, it is important to show that the help is the consequence of elements planted earlier. In "The Secret of My Success," the process doesn't work. Bradley (Michael Fox) is in a dead end. Very short flashback: a few days earlier, his mother (Elizabeth Franz) gave him something to get by, just in case. Too easy, the viewer thinks. It's as if the mother had foreseen that her son would need her. Episode 3.2 of "Battlestar Galactica" uses the process more effectively. At the beginning, several co-protagonists are about to be executed. A card announces: "One hour earlier." Then starts a ten-minute flashback that describes what happened on the sidelines of the main action and justifies the presence of a rescue team at the time of execution.

4. From bad to worse. Put the protagonist in a situation as terrible or even more terrible than the one from which you just saved them, or which at least seems more terrible while in fact, it will be easier to resolve (for the protagonist as well as for the author). In other words,

make it seem like your character is doing like those ships, in the Strait of Messina, that threw themselves on the rocks of Scylla to avoid the Charybdis whirlpool. If, for example, our protagonist collapses from exhaustion in the middle of the desert—cf. the end of "The Tiger of Bengal"—the unprepared arrival of a caravan of kind merchants at the beginning of the next episode (cf. "The Indian Tomb") is a deus ex machina. But the unprepared arrival of a helicopter full of villains who are delighted to get their hands on our hero to torture him is a disguised deus ex machina.

In "The Frozen North," the protagonist (Buster Keaton) is in a canoe filling with water and about to sink. Suddenly, a submarine surfaces where the protagonist is and thus saves his life. But the submarine is actually a target on which an army of destroyers is about to fire! In "A Yankee Named Blueberry," Blueberry is accused by Northerners of being a double spy. He is to be executed at dawn. He is saved by Harriet, his ex-fiancée, but not because she still loves Blueberry. On the contrary, she wants to find him to avenge her father's death and kill Blueberry with her own hands.

In "Some Like It Hot," the murder of Spats (George Raft) by Little Bonaparte (Nehemiah Persoff) can be seen as a deus ex machina since it removes the threat of Spats from the protagonists. But the protagonists witness this second massacre (after that of the inciting incident) and are still being chased, this time by Little Bonaparte's men.

5. Announced danger. Variant of the previous principle, introduce a dramatic irony (diffuse or frank) on the help to let us understand that the deus ex machina holds unpleasant surprises. It may solve the problem in the short term, but danger lurks.

6. Refusal of help. Another variant of the "From Charybdis to Scylla" principle, make the protagonist categorically refuse the convenience. Their first reaction must be: "Never that!" or "No thanks, especially not!" First, we will understand that the deus ex machina does not delight the protagonist and is therefore also a source of conflict for them. Second, we will guess that they will eventually adopt it, which will soften its effect.

7. Humiliating. Yet another variant of the "From Charybdis to Scylla" principle, make the rescue be experienced as an obstacle. In "Gotham Central," which narrates investigations conducted by Gotham City police officers, Batman's interventions are vexing for the protagonists when they can't manage on their own. "I hate that we need him," says Inspector Marcus Driver in "Dead Robin 3" (Gotham Central episode 35).

8. Comedy. Exaggerate the situation to lean towards comedy. Because it better supports deus ex machina than serious treatment. Improbable successes, for example, are much more acceptable in a Buster Keaton film than in a dark adventure film. The gag of the axe in "Jumanji," described in "Dramaturgy," is a humorous way to disguise a deus ex machina. The albums of the "Imbattable" series, in which Pascal Jousselin plays with the layout to "justify" the exploits of his superhero, exploit a comedic spring.

9. Attachment. Make sure the viewer wants so much for the protagonist to get out of trouble that they become ready to accept all wonders. (Note: I reveal the ending of "E.T.") At the end of the second act, the extraterrestrial dies. We are almost as devastated as young Elliott (Henry Thomas). When E.T.'s chest lights up again, it must be recognized that it's a little miracle. We forgive this convenience to the authors because our childlike heart cannot bear for the story to end with the death of a beloved character and the infinite sadness of three kids. It will be noted that, very cleverly, Melissa Mathison creates a little dramatic irony at the moment the miracle occurs: Elliott does not realize that E.T.'s heart has revived, he begins to leave the place. Instead of saying to ourselves: "A bit easy, the miracle," we think: "Oh no! Elliott is going to leave E.T. to suffocate in his refrigerated container." And we wish for a quick resolution of the dramatic irony.

10. After the action. Make the convenience intervene in the local third act. In "The Bridge on the River Kwai," the commando's radio no longer works. Shears (William Holden) gives up trying to make it work—thus abandoning his local objective and entering his local third act—and kicks the radio in frustration, saying everything is rotten. This gets the radio working again. Hergé uses the same principle in "The Red Sea Sharks" to make the "Ramona's" radio work. In "The African Queen," the miracle of the rain occurs in the general third act, after the protagonists have abandoned their objective and are ready to die. Rosie (Katharine Hepburn) has even said her prayer.

11. Cheat. Make the help appear as the voluntary intervention of one of the characters. At the beginning of the first season of "Six Feet Under," the Fishers are prey to a large group (Kroehner) that wants to absorb their family funeral business. To this end, Kroehner has bought an old mansion located right across from the Fishers' offices. They plan to open a funeral home there to compete with the Fishers. At the end of episode 1.3, the mansion in question is on fire, and everything suggests to the viewer that it was Claire Fisher (Lauren Ambrose) who set the fire. As a result, Kroehner gives up their "takeover" and the police conclude it was an accidental fire. In the following episodes, we discover that Claire had nothing to do with it, so it was indeed an opportune help. A bit easy, then, but the impression of deus ex machina was avoided at the time of the events.

12. Red herring. In episode 1.6 of "The Americans," the protagonists (Soviet spies (Keri Russell, Matthew Rhys) installed incognito on American soil) are kidnapped and tortured. Their identity has been discovered, they are in a very bad spot, and one might legitimately wonder how they will get out without the help of a deus ex machina. Plot twist: it was an operation conducted by the KGB (thus, their own bosses), who wanted to check that their agents were reliable. The intense conflict was therefore a red herring. If the red herring is credible, at the time of the plot twist, the viewer does not think it's cheating. They are even delighted.

13. Enormity. Save your protagonist with a gigantic (but nevertheless credible) plot twist that changes the characters' motivations. At the end of "Apocalypto" (Note: What follows reveals an important moment of the film), Jaguar Paw (Rudy Youngblood) is wounded and pursued by two enemies (Richard Can, Carlos Ramos), determined to finish him off. All three emerge onto a beach and suddenly stop, stunned: Spanish caravels are anchored in the bay, and conquistadors are preparing to land. The event is so crazy for these Mayans that Jaguar Paw's two pursuers abandon their objective, allowing the protagonist to slip away.

14. At the beginning. Finally, if all the above hasn't helped you, at least put the deus ex machina earlier in the story. It won't solve the whole problem, but it's better to have help at a time when the viewer feels there will still be obstacles ahead than at a time when they know it's over.

C. THREE TREATMENT EXCERPTS
Same principle as for the foundations and the scene-by-scene, here is the first act of the treatments of "Couple Witness," "The 11th Man," and "Culbuta."

If you compare the scene-by-scene and the treatments, you will note that some scenes from the scene-by-scene have become several scenes in the treatment. This is normal. Sometimes, when moving from "what" to "how," it is necessary to break down the scene into several parts. The eight scenes of the first act of the scene-by-scene of "Couple Witness" have thus become twelve scenes in the treatment. The nine scenes of the first act of the scene-by-scene of "Culbuta" have increased to sixteen scenes in the treatment.

First act of the treatment of "Couple Witness"
1. In his office at Félicitas, his matchmaking agency, with walls decorated with photos presenting him happily with his wife, XAVIER, 37, serious and impeccably dressed, explains to a FUTURE CLIENT the philosophy of his agency: the agency's clients, nicknamed the B.A.D. (Soon To Be Two), are selected based on their real romantic motivations. Then they are helped to showcase themselves. To please, one must also appear. Xavier details the client's "romantic" resume, carefully questioning him about his dry or busy periods. The client hopes to soon be part of the B.A.D., as the seriousness and reliability of Félicitas are well established. Together, Xavier and the client criticize online dating sites that are unreliable and encourage secrecy.

2. Meanwhile, in her office at the agency (with the same photos of her model couple and, on a shelf, a row of clay jugs of dubious taste), GAËLLE, 32, pretty and well-groomed, has a new B.A.D. sign her contract: MRS. VARNIER-DUVAL-LE GUEN, a 70-year-old widow with a grandmotherly appearance. The old woman, after a makeover, will participate tonight in her first dinner with other new recruits. Mrs. Varnier-Duval-Le Guen is excited: will she meet her fourth husband? Together, Gaëlle and Mrs. Varnier-Duval-Le Guen criticize online dating sites that are unreliable and encourage secrecy.

3. In the meeting room of their agency, also decorated with photos of the couple witness taken over the years and bearing the mention "Realized without trickery," Xavier and Gaëlle prepare the invitation cards for the big anniversary party of their ten years of marriage and the ten years of the agency, which will take place in a month and will bring together former clients (the M.A.D., Now Two) and the B.A.D. Absorbed in his task, Xavier gives orders to Gaëlle and becomes impatient. It must be perfect. Used to her husband's demands, Gaëlle reassures him: everything will go well, as usual.
4. In a secluded corner of a floral park, Gaëlle, wearing dark glasses and a scarf over her hair, secretly meets her lover JEAN-FRANÇOIS, 35, with a booming laugh and the build of a mover. With a conspiratorial air, Jean-François hands his mistress her traditional alibi, a clay jug similar to those decorating Gaëlle's office. Then he gives her a pretty sweater in bright colors with a sweet note pinned to it. Happy but stressed, Gaëlle immediately tears up the note: what if Xavier found it! Mockingly, Jean-François retrieves the pieces of paper and swallows them, making them disappear forever.

5. Xavier and Gaëlle's bedroom is located just above the matchmaking agency. The room contains no photos of the couple. The decoration is simple and impersonal. Xavier tries to button his charcoal gray suit pants. In vain. He resolves to put on another pair, in a lighter shade. Then he descends the stairs and heads...

6. ...to his office at the agency. He lifts his leg to the height of a poster of his couple to compare the color of his pants to that in the series of photos: slight difference in shade. Xavier seems very annoyed. Gaëlle enters, radiant, wearing the colorful sweater given by her lover. Xavier chokes: what is this outfit? She must wear the traditional cream blouse from the photos! It's impossible to disappoint the B.A.D. Gaëlle protests then capitulates. Her husband then notices the new pottery she holds in her hand and sincerely marvels at his wife's rapid progress. She replies that she's becoming more and more addicted and leaves, slamming the door.

7. In the meeting room of the Felicitas agency, Xavier and Gaëlle, at aperitif time, display themselves as a model couple in front of about ten B.A.D. with a studied appearance, including Mme Varnier-Duval-Le Guen, transformed. According to a ritual unique to each dinner meeting, Xavier draws a paper from the pottery made by Gaëlle to launch the evening's debate. Tonight it falls on: "Living together, living better?" Transported by the subject, Xavier expresses his conception of the couple—trust, communication, honesty, etc.—without a glance at Gaëlle. She plays along with ease, supporting her husband's words or involving the B.A.D.

8. It's late. The B.A.D., then Gaëlle, have long gone to bed. In the meeting room, Xavier is still working on the preparations for the ten-year celebration. Exhausted with fatigue, he gets up, grabs his wife's pottery, and inspects it with an admiring whistle. Pottery in hand, he leaves the room and heads heavily toward Gaëlle's office. He places the pottery on the shelf next to his wife's other works. Suddenly the shelf collapses. Xavier is devastated, but miraculously, only two pieces of pottery are broken. Panicked, he begins to pick up the pieces to repair the damage. He freezes upon discovering the label "Made in China" on one of them. He searches for other clues among the debris, in vain. With a hard face, he grabs an intact piece of pottery and lets it fall coldly to the floor. A piece marked "Made in Taiwan" bounces off his shoe.

9. Xavier opens the bedroom door and turns on the light, waking Gaëlle. In a tragic tone, he announces that he has broken three jugs. Will she forgive him? How many hours of work has he destroyed? Gaëlle reassures him between yawns. She'll make more jugs, she has quite the technique now! Then she buries herself under the covers, leaving her husband frustrated.

10. Gaëlle, wearing a scarf and dark glasses, meets Jean-François at the floral park. He offers her a new piece of pottery. Xavier, who has followed his wife and spies on them from afar, doesn't understand what's happening. Suddenly, Gaëlle gives Jean-François a small kiss. Xavier can't believe it. Jean-François responds with a deep kiss, and Gaëlle lets him. Xavier collapses, devastated.

11. Xavier and Gaëlle are in bed, backs turned. They wish each other a weak goodnight. Seized by a sudden impulse, Xavier tenderly takes his wife in his arms. In a sleepy voice, she asks if he has broken another jug. Deflated, Xavier immediately lets her go.

12. In his office, Xavier looks drawn from a sleepless night. Distracted, he examines the invitations for Felicitas' ten-year anniversary presented by a former client, RAOUL VAUTRIN, in his fifties and somewhat roguish. Raoul is "very happy" with his wife. In two years, not a single argument, not a single infidelity. Despite himself, Xavier asks: not even a suspicion? After a moment of hesitation, Raoul answers no. The conversation drifts to the theme of adultery, discussed by the two men jokingly. Finally, Xavier reveals to Raoul what his strategy would be if Gaëlle cheated on him: first, he would save face; second, he would quietly work to break the affair and ensure everything returns to normal.

First Act of the Young Man's Treatment

1. CLAUDE RETZ, 6 years old, accompanies her father CHARLES (41 years old) to a football match. Her father is a fervent supporter and her older brother, GEORGES (18 years old), is one of the players on the field. It's a final day, the stadium is packed. The noise scares Claude. She is alone, sitting between her father's legs, who shouts his encouragement. Annoyed by Claude's crying, Charles finally lifts her onto his shoulders. Claude then discovers football with wonder. She takes in the spectacle, sees the vast field, the streamers, the flares, the stadium colored by the supporters' clothes. Suddenly Georges scores a goal a few seconds before the end of regulation time, giving victory to his team. The atmosphere turns delirious. Charles exults with the stadium.

2. The players celebrate the title on the field invaded by supporters. The coach FRANCK MARCANTONI (40 years old), moved, publicly thanks the whole team. The hero of the day, Georges, speaks and dedicates his victory to his deceased mother. Claude can read in her father's eyes the admiration he has for his son.

3. Back home, Claude asks her father questions about her mother. But Charles doesn't have time. He reminds Claude that he is the president of a metallurgists' union and a small amateur football club (FC Ougrée). He has work, appointments, obligations. Claude insists, she wants her father to talk about the mother she never knew. Charles gives in and paints an idealized portrait of her mother, a fighter who struggled in the union for workers' rights and never gave up, no matter the difficulty. Claude drinks in her father's words.

4. Sitting on a bench with her doll, Claude watches a street match between the sons of workers from the Ougrée neighborhood. Claude broods, no one wants her on the team. During an unfortunate shot, the boys lose the ball. As they are ready to abandon the game, Claude tears the head off her doll and offers it as a replacement ball. But she demands her presence on a team. The boys agree.

5. Seven years have passed. CLAUDE, 13 years old, plays on a boys' team, in her father's club. She knows more than him about strategy and tactics. She gives him advice for the next match, crucial for the championship's continuation. Charles knows his daughter won't be able to play with boys much longer. He talks to her about her future in women's football. Claude wants nothing to do with it. She wants to play with the boys to the end. She analyzes women's football: no resources, no professional players, so no future for her.

6. As Claude is on her way to the decisive match of the season with her father and brother Georges, they learn on the radio that Franck, Georges' former coach turned internationally renowned coach, is sentenced to prison and community service for match-fixing.

7. Football match. Charles poorly coaches his team and doesn't follow Claude's advice, sitting on the bench. Moreover, an opposing player, TIGRAN MANDORALEN, poses significant problems for the team. FC Ougrée is set to lose. Charles agrees to follow Claude's advice and lets her onto the field despite Tigran's presence. Some spectators think bringing in a girl isn't the right idea to counter such a player. Some Ougrée players share this opinion. Despite this, Claude shows obvious technical and tactical qualities. Thanks to her game intelligence, she turns the situation around. She is omnipresent and directs everyone. As the score is tied, she cleverly earns a penalty, which she pays for with a blow to the ankle. Claude insists on taking the penalty despite her injury. She misses it, and her team loses the match.

8. In the locker room, the boys scapegoat Claude for their defeat. Only BRUNO (13 years old), captain, and childhood friend of Claude, defends her. But the boys no longer want Claude on the team. They believe she has reached her limit. Claude is kicked out.

9. Claude seeks her father as club president. She wants her place back. Charles categorically refuses to grant her any special treatment. If she wants her place back, she must fight. That's how her mother would have reacted. That said, at 15, mixed-gender play will no longer be allowed. Charles believes it's time for Claude to move on to women's football. Claude refuses.

10. Alone in her room, crying and raging against the world, Claude has a vision: she plays in a grand stadium packed with people, then is carried in triumph by a men's team, a trophy in hand. Claude dries her tears. The vision disappears.

11. The next morning, Claude announces to her father that she has chosen her path. She has decided to become a professional player in the men's championship. It's the only way to fully live her passion. Charles thinks it's a foolish idea. He lists the enormous difficulties it represents, even legally. But Claude is determined: there was a first female astronaut, a first female fighter pilot, she will be the first professional female football player among men.

First Act of the Culbut Treatment

1. MAXIME (35 years old) is in a laundromat. Physically, he looks like an average man. He even has a certain charm. He just has a bandage on his eyebrow and another around a finger. He asks a young woman, JULIETTE (33 years old), to help him put detergent in his washing machine because he's afraid of spilling it everywhere. Juliette smiles.

2. Juliette and Maxime make love. He can't find the target. It's clear this isn't their first time. Juliette bursts out laughing. Maxime gets offended.

3. A bag on her back and a vanity case in hand, Juliette stands in the doorway of Maxime's studio. She explains, sorry, that she's fine with drinking from chipped glasses or getting mustard on her occasionally. But there's one thing she can't do, and that's stop herself from laughing when he messes up. It's beyond her control. Maxime defends himself: he's not forbidding her to laugh. It's just that it's not funny. Juliette tells Maxime that his real handicap isn't what he thinks. Then she gives him a little kiss and leaves. Maxime stands there, thoughtful. GELULE, a white mouse, approaches him, as if to console him.

4. Maxime goes to the Clinical Investigation Center (CIC) at the Lyon University Hospital where he seems to know everyone. Opening the door towards him, he hits himself in the face. He immediately looks around to see if anyone saw him, then continues on his way.

5. At the CIC, Maxime is received by ANTOINE (40 years old), a pragmatic lab technician who offers him three protocols as a healthy volunteer: a dewormer, an antihistamine, and a series of phytotherapy tests. Maxime chooses the latter because it pays the most. He will have to stay in the hospital for observation.

6. Maxime is in a hospital room reading The Pharmacist's Daily. He receives a visit from two nurses, one of whom he doesn't know. MARIE-CLAUDE (50 years old) introduces NATACHA (30 years old) who is there for the week. Maxime is both intrigued and wary. Trying to get out of bed, he falls in front of the two nurses. Marie-Claude bursts out laughing, much to Maxime's dismay, while Natacha holds back and helps Maxime up.

7. In the pediatric section of the hospital, Maxime sees Natacha entertaining sick children with a balloon act. He is charmed.

8. Natacha takes Maxime's blood pressure. He watches the young woman as his heart rate accelerates.

9. In his well-kept studio but with traces of various disasters, Maxime talks about Natacha to Gélule. He is moved.

10. Maxime plays a game of chess with Antoine. He gives Antoine codes so that Antoine manipulates Maxime's pieces for him. Maxime tries to get Natacha to take care of him in the next protocol. Antoine tells him that Natacha is gone. She's not a nurse but an actress. She was at the hospital to prepare for a role she is soon to play in the theater. Currently, she's acting in another play: The Government Inspector by Nikolai Gogol.

11. Maxime finds the contact details of the Marcel Pagnol Cultural Center where Natacha is performing.

12. Maxime goes to the Cultural Center and buys a ticket to see the play. Attendance is very low. Maxime enters the theater as if entering unknown territory. In the following weeks, he attends several performances of The Government Inspector. The play amuses him, but it's Natacha who fascinates him. She plays Maria, the governor's daughter who becomes the inspector's fiancée.

13. One day when Maxime returns to see the play for the umpteenth time, the performance is canceled because one of the actors is unavailable. HANS, the director, 57 years old, confident and charming, offers the few spectators a refund, an invitation to return to see the play, and a drink with the whole troupe. Natacha thus encounters Maxime, who pretends to be there for the first time. Natacha introduces Maxime to Hans, saying only that he's a black belt in pharmacology. She introduces Hans to Maxime, saying he's a black belt in theater. Hans takes Maxime for a doctor and asks for advice for chronic back pain. Maxime recommends a medication.

14. Maxime explains to Gélule that he's going to invite Natacha to dinner and listen to her.

15. Maxime asks Antoine to give him a new test protocol because he needs money to invite Natacha to a fancy restaurant. Antoine refuses. Not only will Maxime exceed his legal quota, but it could be dangerous for his health. Maxime doesn't insist.

16. Maxime goes to Geneva to another laboratory and asks DOCTOR FISCHER for a test as a healthy volunteer. Fischer gives him a medication for kidney stones. Maxime ensures it won't prevent him from listening. Because he needs to be all ears in the coming days. Fischer thinks it surely won't make him deaf. Maxime is reassured. Everything is in place. All that's left is to take action.

10. THE CONTINUITY OF DIALOGUE

"After moving from theme to acts, from acts to scenes, from scenes to dialogues, you have a ready script."
(Billy Wilder [94])
"I am terribly talkative out of concern to keep the character true."
(Jean-Michel Charlier [19])
"If the scene doesn't work, it's not by injecting a lot of words that you'll improve it."
(Stirling Silliphant [73])
"The terrible events (...) will be exposed on our stage in two hours."
(prologue, Romeo and Juliet)
Dialoguing a scene is perhaps the most spontaneous part of a playwright's work. While the previous steps alternated between reflection and spontaneity, this one allows you to fully dive into writing to get a first draft. Then, you return to your work to analyze and refine it.
I've seen many authors eager to move on to dialogues, as if they felt they were finally writing for real. I maintain that the work of foundations, scene-to-scene, and treatment also involves writing and creation. Conversely, I've seen some authors less comfortable with the dialogue stage than with all the previous ones. Perhaps because they were about to finally have a "finished product" and imagined their first draft had to be perfect. They found themselves blocked.

A. THE DIALOGUE
The most important function of dialogue, far ahead of all others, is to make the story believable, to create an illusion of reality. For the simple reason that putting human beings into action requires making them communicate through speech. Exceptions to this rule are rare and shouldn't necessarily be sought in what was called "silent" cinema. Take 99% of feature films in silent film history—I exclude short films and documentaries—and you'll see they're filled with dialogues. And often with very meaningful dialogues. The "Yes, I see now" of the florist (Virginia Cherrill) at the end of City Lights or the "Sorry if I'm a bit late" of Buster (Keaton) in the middle of The Cameraman testify to this. In short, it's not because dialogues are given to read or guess rather than hear that silent film characters didn't speak like the vast majority of human beings do.
The great exception of silent cinema is The Last Laugh, which is more of a challenge than a rich and complex narrative. Moreover, there are dialogues in The Last Laugh, they just aren't presented as intertitles. It's up to the viewer to guess the little that's said. In sound films, one can cite The Spy, which follows the tribulations of a solitary scientific spy, The Naked Island, which tells the minimalist story of a family surviving in difficult conditions, Quest for Fire, whose action takes place before articulated language, Themroc, set in the 70s but where characters grunt like savages instead of speaking, and The Last Battle, set in a post-apocalyptic world where the (few) humans have lost speech. As we see, to do without dialogues, one must have a subject that justifies it. Otherwise, it can quickly become grotesque and prevent the viewer from entering the story. The last four examples give us an eloquent indication: when we refrain from making humans speak, we assimilate the characters to animals with animal concerns (survival, reproduction).
This function of dialogue (making fiction plausible) is so obvious that no one pays attention to it, but it can be useful to be aware of it. It can relieve some of the pressure regarding dialogue and prevent others from seeking THE dialogue that will dazzle everyone and be remembered in dictionaries.

Other functions of dialogue
In addition to making the story believable, dialogue also serves to:
1 - characterize the speaker, more in the content of the dialogue than in the way of speaking. The most classic characterization dialogue is where the character gives information about themselves or others. But there are less direct dialogues that teach us a lot about the characters without seeming to;
2 - illustrate the relationships between the speaker and other characters;
3 - provide information on what the speaker desires, thinks, or feels. States of mind can be expressed directly, as in classical theater or comics;
4 - advance the action. That is, generate or convey obstacles; prepare or pay off an element; set up, exploit, or resolve dramatic irony; recount past events (exposition, cf. below); support or contradict a piece of activity, etc.;
5 - create a link. Generally, within a scene, dialogues logically follow one another. It's a factor of unity.
All these functions are, of course, compatible. N.B. You will find, in La dramaturgie [51], an entire chapter dedicated to dialogue. It details the functions stated here and explores the dialogue tool.

Dialogue technique
Technically, dialogue must also:
1 - be clear and understandable for the viewer;
2 - be comfortable for the actors. Read your dialogues aloud. Better yet, act out your dialogues. You'll do everyone a favor;
3 - be specific. Prefer "I could use a vacation" to "I need a break" or "I'm fed up." Prefer "My dream right now is ten toes in the sand and a piña colada straw lazily hanging from my open mouth" to "I could use a vacation." Of course, the right dialogue will depend on the context. Prefer "My kingdom for a horse!" to "I'm in a bad spot" if your protagonist is on a battlefield and has some wealth (Richard III);
4 - seem alive, that is, "realistic," especially in cinema and television. Of course, what we call realistic dialogue is, in fact, an artifice, simply less artificial than poetic, romantic, symbolic, or other dialogues. In life, people hesitate, search for words, repeat themselves, don't finish their sentences, don't always say what they meant, etc. Even Woody Allen, whose dialogue is slightly stammering, doesn't speak in his films as he would in life. His dialogue is very crafted, respects the unity of action, and doesn't say more than necessary.
Omnipotent dialogue, paraphrase, and underlining
However, dialogues must avoid:
1 - carrying everything on their shoulders. "I'll sort that out during the dialogue" is one of the great mottos (more or less conscious) of the novice author;

2 - diluting information. In classical plays, characters didn't just give their opinion, they expressed it in every possible way. They paraphrased. This resulted in paragraphs of alexandrines where they explained themselves at length—real tunnels for actors and spectators, except for the enlightened amateurs, of which I confess I am not one. But the authors had an excuse: it was poetry (and surely, at the time, a form of spectacle, a feat). Today, conditions have changed. Audiences have evolved, shows are omnipresent, and dialogue has become almost "realistic." For these reasons, I think authors should be more concise;

3 - saying what the audience is supposed to understand on their own, making explicit what is implicit, in short, underlining. A classic example of underlined dialogue: "Obviously, you didn't understand anything!" If it's obvious, there's no need to say it. Thank you, life offers a remarkable explicit dialogue. Camille (Charlotte Gainsbourg) says to Joëlle (Anouk Grimberg): "Do you feel the emotion passing?" As if a clown said to his audience: "Did you see how funny it is?" Moreover, in a scene from Under the Biggest Tent in the World, while we clearly see the spectators laughing, a little girl adds: "I'm having so much fun, mom." Later, the spectators exclaim, admiring the acrobat's feats. A spectator comments: "He's amazing." At the beginning of A Heart in Winter, we see two luthiers (Daniel Auteuil, André Dussolier) assembling a violin without speaking. And Daniel Auteuil's voice-over feels obliged to specify: "Maxime and I understand each other without speaking." Do Sauter, Fieschi, and their audience need a voice-over to understand each other?

Often, underlining concerns the psychology of the characters. They are verbally too lucid about themselves or others. It's not about rationalization or self-justification, which are not underlining and instead betray a hidden meaning. It's about making them say what we are supposed to understand about them, the core of their thoughts, their true unconscious motivation. It's a classic pitfall in dramaturgy. It is partly due to the difficulty of conveying information through action or activity. Underlining is an easy solution.

B. EXPOSITION
Dialogue is sometimes used to have one of the characters tell a story. This is what I call "exposition" (chapter 11 of Dramaturgy [51]). Because it is literary in essence and not dramatic, exposition quickly becomes indigestible. Moreover, when it occurs at the beginning of a story, it often feels like it's addressed directly to the receiver and not to one of the characters. It should therefore be used sparingly. Ideally, it should be avoided altogether. But this is not always possible. An author often needs to inform the audience of events that occurred before the story began and does not always have the means to show them. If these exposition pieces are very short, it's not a problem. But if they are quite long, the only way to make them digestible is to wrap them in conflict (serious or humorous).

The content is conflictual
Or the very content of the exposition is conflictual, and the mere act of narrating it creates conflict for at least one of the characters in the scene. This is the case in Hamlet when the Ghost tells how he was killed. It is also the case for the entire second act of Oedipus Rex, which is essentially made up of exposition, rendered conflictual by dramatic irony. It is also the case in The Workshop, which contains very beautiful examples of conflictual exposition. In scene 5, Simone, whose husband was deported and has not yet returned—we are in 1947—recounts her dealings with the administration and the atrocious and absurd scenes of which she is the protagonist. The exposition is moving because it is conflictual.

The content is not conflictual
Or the content of the exposition is not in itself conflictual, but the narrator character or their interlocutor experiences conflict. A Doll's House offers a very fine example. Nora has done something behind her husband's back. We need to know what it is, but there is no reason for Nora to tell just anyone, quite the contrary. Even to her friend Christine, Nora cannot reveal her secret outright. Very cleverly, Ibsen has Christine provoke Nora. Christine claims to be a liberated woman, criticizing Nora's comfort. Piqued in her pride, Nora then spills the beans to prove to Christine that she too has done unusual things for a woman. Moreover, since these things are secret and likely to cause conflict, our interest is doubly engaged.

Another classic example: one of the characters tells a story, and their interlocutor does not believe them, which creates conflict. This is the case in the very first scene of Peer Gynt, where Peer tells an incredible story to his mother, who is not fooled. The scene (and, by the same token, the play) thus begins with a conflictual dialogue that prompts us to pay attention to the exposition, to listen closely: "You're lying, Peer." In An Italian Straw Hat, the authors combine the two: Fadinard tells a slightly conflictual exposition—his horse ate a hat—to Vézinet, who is deaf.

An alternative: the dilution of exposition
Hergé was very skilled at conveying exposition. He knew that text, whether description or dialogue, quickly becomes indigestible in comics. He used various devices to dilute his information. In The Calculus Affair as well as in The Red Sea Sharks, he uses Haddock and his penchant for the bottle to animate a long discussion, with Topolino (pages 25-26) or Oliveira (pages 23-24). To recount the progress of the investigation in The Castafiore Emerald (pages 48-50), he shows us a television report and, to make it entertaining, has it broadcast by Tournesol's experimental television. Fundamentally, the tools are always the same: conflict, humor. However, it should be noted that this kind of trick might not work in cinema or theater. The spectator would be interested in the image and would miss a good part of the exposition.

Hitchcock does it at the beginning of The Trouble with Harry: a woman is murdered, and the information is conveyed in small pieces across different media. It's original, but what do we retain from this parade of images and short scenes? In comics, the problem doesn't arise because reading can be doubled. Often, a comic book reader first looks at the images and then returns to the text.

Blake Snyder [75] proposes a similar idea he calls "The Pope in the Pool." The expression comes from a project Snyder encountered where a good dose of exposition was delivered while we saw the pope doing laps in the Vatican pool. I find this technique very fragile. The risk of using an unusual image to throw in important information is that the spectator will be intrigued by said unusual image—"Oh! There's a pool in the Vatican," "Why isn't it cross-shaped?" "The cardinal in the background is quite fashionable with his little swimsuit"—and miss the essential of what they need to hear. Let's be clear, the general idea is not to get rid of exposition or sabotage it. The idea is to find an effective and digestible way to convey necessary information to the spectator.

More or less successful methods
Dramatists have used various tricks to convey exposition: the Greek chorus, chatty servants, the friend or confidant returning from a long journey are classics, not always very successful. Guards or watchmen are also particularly appreciated (cf. Hamlet, The Infernal Machine, or Roberto Zucco).

Chekhov uses a method of his own: his characters undertake a sort of self-examination, soliloquize, remember. Only Chekhov's incredible talent for characterizing his characters allows this unusual kind of exposition. It is not recommended for anyone not named Anton Chekhov.

The first scene of Tartuffe is an exposition scene. And it is conflictual. Unfortunately, Molière imposes on us, from the outset, six characters who also talk about two absentees (Orgon and Tartuffe), and it must be said that this scene is extremely indigestible. Many of the information it conveys is lost to the spectator.

Two: basic rules and a trick
Include only the bare minimum of exposition necessary for understanding the story. You would be surprised to find that the spectator quickly understands the essence of a situation without much historical material. If, for example, the spectator needs to understand a character's motivations, they do not require knowing all the circumstances that led this character to be who they are and feel what they feel. If we had to recount all the education the protagonist received before starting the action, we would never finish. In short, you have an interest in eliminating any exposition element that is not absolutely essential or that will become clear during the story.

Do not put all the exposition at the beginning of the work. It is often very beneficial to postpone it to later and, if possible, to the moment when it has the maximum impact, where it can constitute an interesting dramatic knot. Eve offers a magnificent example. Towards the end of the film, Addison DeWitt (George Sanders) uses the information he has on Eve (Anne Baxter)—which is then revealed to us—to force her to stay with him. Be careful, however, not to make a mystery of what needs to be exposed. It's a delicate balance. If you give information too late that the spectator feels is necessary for understanding the story—as in Sophie's Choice—it is the participation of said spectator that suffers.

A trick worth considering is to place the exposition after a strong dramatic knot, at a moment when the spectator is necessarily attentive and, at the same time, needs to digest what they have just experienced.

C. GENERAL SUGGESTIONS
Scenes without conflict
Not all scenes, without exception, need to contain conflict. The protagonist can perfectly achieve a local goal without encountering obstacles. In scene 4 of act IV of Tartuffe, Elmire's goal is to hide Orgon under the table. She encounters no obstacle, as Orgon does not resist. Often, scenes without conflict do not contain the protagonist of the work. They serve to announce obstacles for the future. In scene 2 of act II of Hamlet, the king and queen aim to convince Rosencrantz and Guildenstern to spy on Hamlet. They have no obstacle. Rosencrantz and Guildenstern might hesitate to spy on a childhood friend, but they do not.

It is clear that the authors had no interest in developing conflict in these two scenes. The entirely legitimate desire to put a lot of conflict in a work should not lead you to put it everywhere, for any reason. If conflict were exploited every second, the action would take forever to advance. However, at the risk of boring the spectator, it is evident that a scene without conflict must be short. This is the case in the two examples cited.

Local evolution and crescendo
In general, it is easy to chain the gestures and dialogues of a scene, thus creating evolution. This contributes to respecting the local unity of action.

However, establishing a crescendo within a scene does not come as spontaneously and requires that the scene be rigorously constructed before being written. Certainly, one can do without it. It is even likely that a work in which all scenes are written on the same mold (protagonist-goal-obstacles with a crescendo leading to the climax) gives a somewhat tedious feeling of systematism. That said, the more constructed the scenes are, the more comfortable the writing is for the author, and the more the spectator enjoys it.

It may be a good idea to speed up the pace at the end of the second act by shortening the scenes and, if possible, by reducing dialogues and adding more activity. At the end of Intolerance, which tells four stories in parallel, Griffith moves from one story to another more and more quickly. Tightening the action can also consist of focusing on an increasing or decreasing number of characters. This is the case, of course, when characters disappear one after the other (cf. Alim, Battle Royale, Ten Little Indians, or The Bridge) or turn into rhinoceroses (Rhinoceros). But it is also what Renoir and Spaak do in The Grand Illusion. As the action progresses, we become more interested in a small group of characters. In The Builders of Empire, the story of a family grappling with a relentless curse, the action tightens according to three criteria: 1 - the family moves to increasingly smaller places, 2 - they lose objects and furniture each time, 3 - its members disappear one by one. This type of process helps reinforce the impression of a crescendo. The same effect can be created by proceeding in reverse, by introducing more and more characters, as at the end of The Awful Truth.

A resourceful protagonist
If the protagonist decides to do something, the idea must come from them and not from their surroundings (newspaper, television, event, friends, etc.). When in trouble, one has imagination. If the idea is provided by an external event, the spectator does not think that fate sometimes does things well, they rather feel the author's intervention placing their pawns at the right moment and tossing their protagonist around. If the idea is provided by another character, it seems too easy. Or, add conflict. A classic trick is to have the protagonist refuse the idea, which, of course, they are forced to adopt later.

When the author betrays themselves
Unless the subject requires it, avoid giving the same character trait to several characters. It can pass if it's naivety or clumsiness. But if you have several sexist, cynical, bitter, angry, racist characters, the spectator will feel that it is you who are transpiring through your characters, and chances are they are right.

Lucky Luke's Fiancée offers a spectacular illustration of this principle. It is an accumulation of misogynistic clichés. Even Lucky Luke joins in: "Marriage is a lottery," "Marriage is a charming mistake that two human beings make together," "A woman worries about her future until the day she marries. A man worries about his future from the day he marries." In short, the author throws in all the aphorisms they found in the dictionary of quotations under the heading "Marriage." We thus discover a Lucky Luke who is afraid of women. Why not. It could have been an interesting characterization element to exploit. But when we see that all the characters are in the same state, we quickly realize that this primary sexism can only come from the author themselves. In short, if you suffer from misogyny, racism, or homophobia, and if you think it deserves to be addressed, reread yourself or have someone else read you. Check that your tendencies do not transpire.

The montage-sequence with parsimony
In the case of cinema and television, do not overuse series of images or series of short scenes sometimes called a "montage-sequence." This tool is generally used to condense an action that repeats or unfolds over a long period. Even when it doesn't turn into a music video, the montage-sequence tells something, but it tells it from a distance. The spectator does not participate. They even feel that they are being addressed directly. Not to mention that the montage-sequence is sometimes a symptom of the author's impotence. Or a sign that the author wants to cram a lot of information into their story.

Finally, know that, from a purely practical point of view, the montage-sequence often costs a lot. Because it requires moving an entire technical team to different sets to record only a few seconds of film.

The permutation trick
