As I have already had the opportunity to say, I believe that an author possesses in their unconscious the ideal story. The difficulty is to give birth to this ideal story. Among its characteristics, the unconscious does not know negation. If you say, for example: "No, Haneke and Tarantino are not sadistic neurotics," your interlocutors' unconscious will not hear the negation and will associate the names Haneke and Tarantino with the words "neurotics" and "sadistic." For the same reasons, it is better to tell a child "Remember to take your bag" than "Don't forget your bag." It gives better results.

All this to say that you may have a good idea for your story but not situate it correctly. If you feel something is wrong, take two narrative elements, reverse them, and see what it gives. Instead of having the triggering incident happen to this character, have it happen to another. Instead of giving this goal to a character, give them exactly the opposite goal. Instead of putting this scene at the end, put it at the beginning. Instead of creating a new character to generate an obstacle, use an existing character. Instead of having a character say a dialogue, put it in the mouth of the character opposite. Instead of a character hating another character, have them adore them. Instead of the son experiencing a particular conflict, have it be the father. Instead of a character knowing a secret, have them ignore it. In short, you keep part of the idea and radically change its insertion into the project. The possibilities for permutation are endless. Try, simulate, dare the most absurd inversions. You will be surprised at how often this technique solves the problem.

I beg you
Avoid the drunkenness to allow one of your characters to say or do something they certainly wouldn't do if they were sober. Avoid the too-realistic nightmare from which your protagonist wakes up saying, "Phew! It was just a bad dream." More generally, avoid any dream scene. Avoid the phone call that interrupts the characters at an important moment or at a moment that suits you. Avoid the radio, television, or newspaper to give your protagonist an idea (cf. above). Avoid the character who says to another: "We need to talk." Spare us the endless brawl from which the belligerents emerge with intact metacarpals. Spare us "I don't know why I'm telling you all this." Spare us the villains who laugh heartily after threatening your protagonist. Spare us the characters who are lucid about themselves or others. Spare us the character who orders a drink in a café, pays for it, and leaves without drinking it. Spare us the blow to the back of the neck that knocks out a character as if by magic. Renounce, in the stage directions, characters who blush or pale. N.B. Today, there are lists of clichés to avoid on the internet, especially in the field of science fiction.

Questions of plausibility
Plausibility issues are tricky because they are not all of the same nature. It's a delicate balance. Sometimes, an author dwells on an implausibility without realizing that the spectator will never notice it. Sometimes, they gloss over it and leave big holes in their story. The classic trap is to adopt an idea because "it exists in life." Let's recall Boileau's famous saying [10] on this subject: "The truth can sometimes not be plausible." In life, we know there is coherence, even if we don't always have the elements to understand. In fiction, this coherence must be staged. Or make believe it exists. "The role of the poet," says Aristotle [02], "is to say not what actually happens but what could happen in the order of the plausible or necessary."

When you have a plausibility problem, a good trick is often to lay it out in the open, turning it to your advantage, that is, making a strength of its weakness. This is what the authors of I Confess should have done. The film tells the story of a priest (Montgomery Clift) who receives the confession of a murderer (Otto Hasse) before being accused of his crime. And who remains silent. Truffaut and Hitchcock [41] agree that the film poses a problem: the public cannot believe that a man is so bound by the seal of confession that he refuses to declare his innocence. And Hitchcock concludes: "The film should not have been made." To this somewhat radical solution, an alternative consisted of creating a character who could justify the protagonist's motivations. This is a classic trick when something true is not plausible: just introduce a skeptic into the story, to whom another character gives the necessary arguments. This gives the spectator a reference point, who then says: "It's unusual but possible." In other words, a skeptical character defuses the spectator's skepticism. Certainly, in I Confess, the
the problem is delicate. The only people who know the priest's secret are the murderer, his wife (Dolly Haas), and the viewer. But nothing prevented the priest from confiding in another clergyman.

Alongside this, there are cases of implausibilities that pose no problem. The anachronisms of George Bernard Shaw are among them. In Saint Joan, nationalism and Protestantism are clearly mentioned, even though at the time (1431) they did not exist, at least not consciously. In The Birds, one of the characters present during the bird attacks is, coincidentally, an ornithologist (Ethel Griffies). As Hitchcock says: "I could have shot three scenes to make her arrival plausible, but those scenes would have had no interest. (...) Let's be logical: if you want to analyze everything and construct everything in terms of plausibility, no fiction script would withstand this analysis."

A proof with nothing less than Citizen Kane. At the beginning of the film, the old Kane (Orson Welles) is alone and bedridden in a vast room. He murmurs "Rosebud," drops a snow globe, and dies. The door opens, a nurse (Renee Godfrey) enters and confirms the death. Did she hear the word "Rosebud"? It's unlikely. The door was closed, the word was whispered, and she had no reason to be right at the entrance of the room, ear pressed against the door. It was the breaking of the snow globe that caught her attention. In short, the word "Rosebud" was not heard by any character in the film. Nevertheless, a few scenes later, everyone knows about it. At the end of the film, far too late, the butler (Paul Stewart) claims he was present in the room when Kane said "Rosebud" before dying. We don't know if the butler is lying to seem interesting or if he's telling the truth, but one thing is certain, we didn't see him at the beginning of the film. And if he was in the room, what was he doing there? Why didn't he approach Kane to assist him? I suspect Welles and Mankiewicz started their story with an inconsistency. But few notice it because the viewer heard the word "Rosebud." An attentive or critical mind might wonder how the journalists know, but they cannot doubt that Kane pronounced the word.

Length of scenes and works

There is no absolute rule regarding the length a scene should have. But if we took the cinematic repertoire to find an average, we would probably arrive at a duration close to two minutes. Of course, it depends on the needs. A scene with two characters is necessarily shorter than a scene with ten. Some transition scenes are very short, others (the bathroom cleaning in Psycho or the chariot race in Ben-Hur) much longer. The great confrontation between Eva (Liv Ullmann) and her mother (Ingrid Bergman) in Autumn Sonata lasts seventeen minutes. In theater, scenes are more verbose, thus longer than in cinema.

But there is a certain logic that most scenes should be neither too short nor too long. Too short, they prevent the author from developing and exploiting the local action. A work composed solely of very short scenes works poorly. The viewer barely has time to settle in before having to move on. Too long, a scene can become boring, either because it is verbose (the first scene of Family Plot or the last of A Doll's House), or because it does not respect the unity of local action (scene II/6 of The Cid). To this, we must add a fundamental consideration: physical length and psychological length are two very distinct notions. It's like real temperature versus perceived temperature in meteorology. We've all seen five-minute short films that lasted an eternity, while two-hour feature films prevented us from noticing the time passing. That's why a scene, like an entire work, should last as long as necessary, knowing that, most of the time, two minutes are enough for a scene and an hour and forty for a work. Strindberg, who was concerned about not boring the audience, endeavored not to make Miss Julie last more than ninety minutes. This is also the attitude of Woody Allen and Francis Veber, whose many films do not exceed this duration. At one time, the length of an act in theater depended as much on dramatic necessities as on the lifespan of candles. Since the advent of electricity, Hitchcock estimated that the duration of a film should take into account the endurance capacity of a human bladder.

"It is necessary for a play to be short," says Peter Brook. "After two hours of performance, I get restless and impatient. Shakespeare himself uses the formula 'Two Hours' to talk about the duration of his own works." Shakespeare is not the only one. In The Discourse of the Three Unities, Corneille affirms that "the representation lasts two hours."

Since we are talking about duration, a few thoughts on rhythm. Unless you want to bore the receiver or create a contemplative atmosphere, the first logical rule is to get to the point. By respecting the unity of action, cutting the superfluous, starting scenes as late as possible, ending them as soon as possible, and achieving multiple goals at once.

Another element sometimes related to the question of rhythm: ensuring that each scene leads to the next. Not only is it desirable that what precedes prepares what follows, but if each of your scenes makes you want to know the next, you hold your receiver. As our old friend Aristotle said, "it is very different to say 'this happens because of that' and 'this happens after that.'"

Contrast is also a way to give rhythm to a story. If all your scenes are full of nonstop adrenaline, you risk tiring the audience. Alternating strong scenes and moments of rest or digestion is a way to give relief, thus interest, to a story.

Of course, in cinema, editing plays an important role in the rhythm domain. The editor spends a good part of their time removing parts of scenes as well as parts of shots. Their contribution to rhythm is major.

Running a time trial

Do you know how cyclists are advised to run a time trial? The rule is simple. They must start strong, accelerate, and finish with a flourish. I think we should do the same with a story. The beginning must be impeccable, the middle solid, and the end powerful.

Why take care of the beginning? First, to make the rest easier to write. Many elements are planted in the launch. Then, to not give readers or viewers an excuse to drop out. The beginning must be gripping and indisputable.

"Gripping" means it arouses interest, already making you want to turn the pages. "Indisputable" means there are no implausibilities or inconsistencies. The first act must be fluid. Zero snags.

Why take care of the middle? Because it allows you to transition from the beginning to the end, thus justifying it correctly. It's in the middle that trajectory conflicts occur. It's also often in the middle that the key scene slips in. Finally, taking care of the middle is about giving pleasure to the receiver and maintaining their attention.

Why take care of the end? Because it brings meaning. And because it's the last element that marks the receiver, their last memory of the work. The end has a particularity that no other dramatic node of a story has: the receiver has time to digest, mature, distill it because there is nothing behind it. The end is therefore the ideal place to create an impact.

The first ten minutes

A representative of the Writers Guild of America West told me in 2015 that his guild registered about 70,000 works per year, the vast majority of which were scripts. It's monumental. Especially since not all screenwriters submit their work to the WGA. All in all, this means that about 200 new scripts arrive in Hollywood every day, 365 days a year. Thus, some interns or assistants go home on weekends with twenty or thirty scripts to read. The task is inhuman. But rest assured, Hollywood has found several radical ways not to have to read scripts to the end and quickly dismiss them. Inventing an excuse to say no has become one of the major skills of decision-makers. The three main criteria are layout, genre, and immediate attractiveness. If your script is not formatted correctly, trash! If it does not respect the constants of a codified genre, trash! And if your first ten minutes do not win the reader's approval, they won't even get to the beginning of the second act, trash!

Does this mean your first ten minutes must be devilishly sexy? For some, it has indeed become a tacit rule in the United States. Of course, the beginning of a story should not be dull, vague, or awkward. But is it necessary to dazzle right away? If you're writing an adventure film like James Bond or Indiana Jones, it's understandable. Not so much because they are popular entertainment works but rather because the first act of a story is partly devoted to the routine life of the future protagonist, and the routine life of carnival spies and treasure hunters is always spectacular. But why apply this rule to all types of stories? When examining the repertoire, we find many major works that do not meet this condition. At the beginning of Seven Samurai, bandits decide not to attack a village they have already ransacked in the past so it can recover before the next raid. An impatient author would probably have started the film with a good big spectacular attack. Hashimoto, Kurosawa, and Oguni felt they didn't need it. Despite all the esteem I have for In Her Eyes, 12 Angry Men, Miracle in Alabama, Groundhog Day, or One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, I don't find their first ten minutes breathtaking. Sometimes, like in Amélie, The Apartment, or Once Upon a Time in the West, you could even say they drag on.

It must be acknowledged that these examples are not recent. To be precise, they date from before Syd Field, who declared in 1979 in Screenplay: "As head of the story department at Cinemobile Systems, I was always 70 scripts behind. (...) I read so many boring and poorly written scripts that I could tell from the first ten pages if the script was properly structured. (...) I had too much to read to waste my time finishing a script that didn't work." This last sentence is paradoxical. How can you know a script doesn't work if you haven't read it all? Syd Field's answer: "I didn't have time to hope the author had done their job." We are anchored in a new era. Scripts are ten, a hundred, maybe a thousand times more numerous than in the 60s. Not only did Syd Field clarify the ruthless cut-off of decision-makers, but also, studios and box offices are no longer run by rock children but by business school graduates. We have no choice but to take this into account.

That said, I am convinced that the public does not demand that a story start hysterically. The viewer knows that an author needs time to set up and launch their action. They only start getting impatient after twenty or twenty-five minutes. Besides, that's the time Syd Field declared he gave to scripts: "I gave the author 30 pages to set up their story. If it wasn't done by then, I grabbed the next script on the pile." My suggestion is that you show professionalism and make people want to read from the first page. But without trying to dazzle, without forcing things. If prostituting oneself may allow being read by interns to the end, I'm not sure it's a winning choice in the long term.

What to put in the first act

The first act's function is to launch the action. As we saw in chapter 4, it must therefore present the elements of the pitch: arena, main characters, routine life, inciting incident, declaration of objective, and an idea of the obstacles to come. Sometimes, the first act sets up a major dramatic irony. But not always. A major dramatic irony can also be set up at the beginning of the second act or even in the middle of the second act (using the median climax, for example). If one of your characters transforms, the beginning of the first act (before the inciting incident) is the ideal place to present the trajectory protagonist (often with their reasonable contradictor).

I advocate a simple, clear, precise, and plausible beginning. With as little mystery as possible. I find that mystery is a poor way to hook the receiver. I advise against using it to start a story. Why? Because mystery creates intellectual curiosity and not emotional curiosity. When you set up a dramatic question, a trajectory question, or an ironic question, you launch uncertainty and suspense. We know the answer will be yes or no - she reaches or doesn't reach her goal, he transforms or doesn't, she learns or doesn't what she doesn't know - but we are torn between hope and fear. Whereas with a mystery, there is zero suspense. We just want to know the info we don't know. Who killed? Why did he have this accident? Who is this strange character? What are they talking about? Why are they acting this way? What are their motivations? Zero emotion. We know we'll get the clarification and we don't particularly fear it. We just wait for it to arrive so we can finally fully participate. Of course, I'm talking about important mysteries over time. At the very beginning of a story, there is necessarily a bit of mystery since we don't know everything about the characters and their universe. But that mystery can (and should) be resolved very quickly.

Thus, starting a story by launching a big flashback rarely seems like a good idea to me. For several reasons. First, we try to hook the receiver with mystery and not with an emotional question. Second, as the receiver tends to unconsciously put events in order, they quickly forget they saw a future element at the beginning. And if they don't forget, it's because that element gave information that spoils the rest. I am convinced that one can be brilliant and original by putting scenes in order.

I know that starting with a flashback and/or mystery is a classic in cinema. Examples abound. Despite this, I invite all those who think that withholding information and "mystifying" things are good ways to hook a viewer, I invite them to rush to the first season of Happy Valley. Zero mystery. Plenty of conflict. Dramatic ironies galore. Result, an ultra-powerful first episode. Giving all the information (except those that would spoil surprises) does not deflate suspense or sell the ending. It's even the opposite. It's the best way to make the viewer participate.

Exemplary conclusions

The authors of Cyrano de Bergerac and City Lights know that it is not enough to provide the dramatic answer to end a story. When great dramatic ironies have been set up, they must also be resolved. This is what they carefully do in their third act.

It has been said a lot that Waiting for Godot has no end. That's false. Just because there is no end for Vladimir and Estragon doesn't mean there isn't one for Beckett or his viewer. At the second appearance of the boy, we understood: Godot will never come. Beckett knows the information has been conveyed. He is right to stop his play at this moment.

At the end of the second act of Tootsie, Michael (Dustin Hoffman) has decided to stop the deception of his disguise. He takes advantage of the live recording of an episode, awaited by all of America, to reveal that his character, Dorothy, is a man. It's one of the most beautiful climaxes in the repertoire. First, because it is conflictual and even explosive (humanly). Second, because it resolves all the plots, those of Dorothy as well as those of Michael. Third, because it doesn't just resolve the dramatic question, it also resolves the film's great ironic question. Fourth, because everyone is present. All the important characters in the film are either on the set or in front of their television. Fifth, because the characters' reactions are presented in crescendo, from the least important to the most important, from the technicians to Julie (Jessica Lange). Sixth, because this climax gives meaning to the story.

If you are attentive to endings or if you wish to end your story with an ending that will leave an unforgettable memory, you will find beautiful examples in the following works, in addition to Cyrano de Bergerac, Waiting for Godot, and City Lights mentioned above: Apocalypto, Blow Out, Bombón: El Perro, Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid, Citizen Kane, In Her Eyes, the 6th episode of the 1st season of Dexter, The Easy Life, Little Miss Sunshine, Manon of the Spring, Memento, Miracle in Alabama, About Schmidt, No Man's Land, Planet of the Apes (1968), Fail Safe, The Sixth Sense, Titanic, It's a Wonderful Life (1946), Life Is Beautiful (1997), Ikiru, One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest, the 4th episode of the 1st season of The Walking Dead. Without revealing too much for those who haven't seen these works, we will notice that they are all human-scale stories and that there is a significant part of misfortune or failure in these endings. Most are not happy endings. But all are moving or/and meaningful payoffs. You will also notice that these are not hooks, as found at the end of an episode. I am talking here about conclusions. Some are more or less open, but at least what was set up has been resolved.

On the dramatic text

A dramatic work must describe what is seen and heard significantly on screen or stage, no more, no less, and in as neutral a style as possible. You must therefore avoid poetic or literary effects. Only dialogues are entitled to poetry. Also to be avoided: your comments on what is happening, sentences - whether in descriptions or dialogues - that indicate what the viewer is supposed to understand or feel. Cross out, for example, all sentences that contain "one guesses that" or "one understands that" and manage to make the acts, actions, images, sounds convey the information you want to pass on.

Stylistic effects and comments are terrible traps. Certainly, they greatly facilitate the reading of a dramatic text, but they disappear when the story moves from text to representation. And with them, either a tone or important information that has not been taken care to convey otherwise may disappear. It is true that a well-written script (i.e., without literary effect) is quite a difficult product to read, even more so than a play which generally contains fewer pictorial descriptions (or stage directions). A dramatic text is received with the cortical brain and the left hemisphere, while its representation involves much more the limbic brain, which is the seat of emotions. Hence this terrible handicap for dramaturgy: plays and scripts are poor models.

In 1983, Patrick Bokanowski, starting from an idea by painter and researcher Henri Dimier, according to which the eye is not only a receiver but can also be an emitter, imagined most seriously a machine that would allow filmmakers to record directly on a sensitive medium (perhaps living matter, microorganisms) the images that form in their brain, enabling them to produce a true model of the work they have in mind. "I don't believe the idea of this device is crazier than the prediction of the telephone, radio, computer, etc.; what I'm sure of, in any case, is that it will be more practical for making films." The day this machine exists, authors will be able to produce models as effective as an architect's or musician's model. But we are not there yet.

Some cynics suggest, once a script is finished, adding stylistic figures, humor, atmosphere, poetry, in short, all sorts of literary elements that will make the script more pleasant to read and give it better chances with producers and professional organizations. In the United States, it has become a convention. Scripts are allowed to have what is called a "voice." It is even sometimes recommended. From my point of view, there is voice and voice. I find it wonderful that a story has a voice if the term refers to the personal universe and specific narration of an author. On the other hand, if the voice concerns the very style of the script, I find it dangerous. Because, once again, that voice will disappear once the script is put into images and sounds. I have read scripts with a voice (like that of Lethal Weapon, for example). I found that the authors were being clever, trapping themselves and everyone else. I maintain that it is a gigantic mistake and that there is much better to do: that readers and decision-makers finally learn to evaluate a dry dramatic text. Or hire competent readers.

On the presentation of the text
Since a dramatic work is not easy to read, you should make it as fluid and readable as possible. Thus, in the case of a screenplay, avoid technical indications (close-up, tracking shot, etc.). This makes reading very tedious and, moreover, it is not the screenwriter's job to conceive the film's breakdown. Furthermore, there is a strong chance that the director will not take such indications into account, so it is more clever to suggest a staging effect through an appropriate description. If, for example, you write:

"A fly lands on a hand. The hand swats the insect. It belongs to a 40-year-old woman with flaming hair...," it is clear that the scene is not filmed, from the start, in a long wide shot. In any case, is it really significant for the story whether a scene is shot in a fixed shot or a circular tracking shot?

Also to be avoided: pages divided vertically, with one column for the image and another for the sound. A human brain cannot read two things at once. It must make a choice. The author might as well impose the order of reading.

A final little tip for screenplays. Repeat the setting of the heading because many readers, unfortunately, do not read the headings. Example:

EXT. CHAMPS-ELYSEES - NIGHT

It is night. The Champs-Elysées are illuminated for Christmas. Charlotte and Kevin walk hand in hand...

Remember that before being used as a plan by a team of craftsmen and technicians, a dramatic work is read by decision-makers (producers, actors, financiers). They are the ones you must seduce first. Of course, the best way to seduce them is to tell a great story. In other words, to ensure that your plan contains a great narrative. But the readability of the text itself deserves attention. Everything must be done to make reading your story an enjoyable experience. But, once again, without cheating or selling your soul.

Second draft of the statement of intent: When you have revised your text twenty times and feel it can be sent to professionals, you may be asked for a statement of intent. Often, this contains two parts: a first part dedicated to meaning - the loop is then closed with my suggestion on page 39 - and a second part dedicated to staging.

I have never been comfortable with the statement of intent. It seems to me to essentially cultivate the laziness and lack of confidence of decision-makers who do not know how to evaluate a screenplay and need a text explanation. Yet a story must stand on its own. No statement of intent will be distributed to spectators at the end of the performance to put words on what they have just seen. The statement of intent can even become a trap when it allows the author to convey meaning that he has not managed to transmit through the story itself. On the other hand, it must be recognized that some elements do not come across in reading. I just spoke about the representation of the finished story, yet we are dealing with a text. Basically, it lacks everything concerning staging.

That is why, in my eyes, a statement of intent should primarily discuss what the author envisions as imagery, staging, sound, and embodiment by the actors. It can also be a good idea to provide references, titles. This allows your readers to understand what type of story you have in mind. It is especially useful in comedy. That said, it is a double-edged sword. If you propose a mediocre screenplay by referencing a masterpiece of the repertoire, it will not prevent your reader from noticing that your text does not measure up to its model. The statement of intent will not change that.

D. EXCERPT OF DIALOGUE CONTINUITY

There are plenty of examples of dialogue continuity on the internet - while examples of foundations, milestones, scene-by-scene, and treatment are nonexistent. I still propose a sample of a dialogue scene, taken from Culbuta, with the type of layout often demanded by decision-makers.

A bit of preparation. The protagonist, Maxime, is extremely clumsy and extremely sensitive. Antoine runs a Clinical Investigation Center where Maxime regularly tests medications to earn a little money. During one of the tests, Maxime fell in love with a new nurse named Natacha. We are still in the first act.

INT. ANTOINE'S OFFICE AT CIC - DAY

Maxime and Antoine are in front of a chessboard. Maxime has the black pieces.

MAXIME
CfS.

Antoine takes one of Maxime's two black knights and moves it. Then he plays one of his white pieces.

MAXIME
For the next test, I'd rather it wasn't Marie-Claude taking care of me. Ta4.

Antoine moves Maxime's rook.

ANTOINE
Did something happen?

MAXIME
No, no, but uh... I...

Antoine moves one of his pieces.

ANTOINE
You prefer to try someone else.

MAXIME
That's right.

ANTOINE
(smiling)
Natacha, for example. She's quite a looker, huh?

MAXIME
No, no, not at all!...
Well, yes, for example... E3.

Antoine moves one of Maxime's pawns.

ANTOINE
Is it already over with Juliette?

MAXIME
_ She annoyed me.

Antoine doesn't seem to approve.

MAXIME
Would you like to be constantly reminded that you sweat a lot?

Antoine doesn't insist. He moves one of his pieces.

ANTOINE
That's how I ended up here.

Maxime doesn't understand.

ANTOINE
During my medical studies, I was looking for an effective deodorant. I applied for tests. I liked the principle.

Maxime smiles.

MAXIME
So, if you had protruding ears and suffered from chronic aerophagia, you'd work in aeronautics.

Maxime makes a fart noise with his mouth. Antoine nods, smiling with a falsely complicit air.

ANTOINE
Do you want to see Natacha again or do you prefer to make fun of me?

MAXIME
Yes, sorry.

ANTOINE
Well, she's gone. That'll teach you, Zigoto.

MAXIME
To another department?

ANTOINE
She was a placebo nurse. You didn't see it coming.

MAXIME
She was what?

ANTOINE
An actress.
(Maxime's astonished look) She came to do an internship to prepare for a theater role.

MAXIME
(thoughtful) Actress... Db8.

Antoine looks at the game and realizes he has lost.

ANTOINE
Ah, damn! I should let you move your pieces. That would give me a handicap.

The scene is not major. However, I did not feel exempt from constructing it. Maxime has not yet decided to take long-term action. He still has a local objective: to see Natacha again. Obstacles: he doesn't want to reveal himself, he alienates Antoine, and, above all, Natacha is not immediately accessible; she has left the hospital. Climax and twist: she is not a nurse but an actress. The characterization of the characters is exploited: their relationship; Maxime's clumsiness, sensitivity, and intellectual vivacity; Antoine's professional deformation, expressed in his choice of vocabulary. The last dialogue, in the third act, is a little pun that refers to one of the film's major themes. The scene teaches us that Maxime is not the only one handicapped. Antoine also has a problem. The film's message is that we are all handicapped in one way or another. The question is what we do with our handicap.

11. SPECIFIC WRITINGS

"I would be unable to tell you the story of Daphne Du Maurier's The Birds. I only read it once, quickly."
(Alfred Hitchcock [41])
"If Peau d'âne were told to me, I would take extreme pleasure in it."
Jean de La Fontaine [48])

The progressive work proposed in this book (meaning - pitch - foundations - milestones - scene-by-scene - treatment - dialogue continuity) works for all types of writing. It works very well for adapting an existing source, whether it is a literary work, a news item, or a historical episode. Or even for preparing a film remake. It works very well for writing for children. It works very well for creating a short story. In addition to everything that precedes, here are some suggestions that take into account the specificities of each medium or format.

A. ADAPTING LITERARY WORKS

In La dramaturgie [51], I recall the fundamental difference between stories written to be read and stories written to be seen and heard. And I invite authors not to adapt a novel that does not contain enough drama. The mere fact that a literary work inspires images in its reader does not justify it being made for these "image arts" that are theater, cinema, or comics. If you still want to adapt a short story or a novel, some guidelines and common-sense rules can be proposed.

1. Start by questioning your motivations. If you want to share your enthusiasm for a book, you should not adapt it for theater or cinema; you should encourage people to read it. But one can be sensitive to the point of view or one of the points of view of the novel. The first step is to identify what attracts you and the reasons for this attraction.

2. Be aware that a novel is generally much too long to make a two-hour dramatic work. Accept the idea that you will not be able to put everything into images and that significant cuts will be necessary.

3. Be aware that images alone are not enough to make a long-format dramatic work, whether in theater, cinema, or comics. It also requires a plot and conflict.

4. Alfred Hitchcock, Frantisek Daniel, and others suggest reading the book once or twice and then putting it aside, only to return to it much later when the foundations of the work are well advanced. The idea is to start imagining a dramatic story from what you remember of the novel.

5. Choose, as the protagonist, the character who experiences the most dynamic conflict and who allows the chosen point of view to be conveyed. Preferably choose dramatic conflicts, that is, conflicts that can be represented by actions. If the novel tells the story of a deaf-mute poet who questions himself because he cannot find inspiration, it is wise to give up. If you do not offer a clear subject of identification to the viewer, you end up with a film like Atonement or Tamara Drewe. Lovely stories to read but less to watch.

6. Assign this protagonist a single general objective. If you do not respect the unity of action, you end up with a film like The Reader, which unfolds over three eras (each with a different action) and, in addition, flashforwards. Again, a beautiful story to read but less to watch.

7. Keep only the scenes or characters that constitute obstacles, direct or indirect, to this objective, that is, those that respect the unity of action.

8. Remove entire scenes from the novel rather than keeping them all, shortening them. Because David Lynch could not or did not want to do it, Frank Herbert's sprawling novel, Dune, became, on screen, a succession of short vignettes. For viewers who have not read the book, this resulted in an incomprehensible film, and for others, an accumulation of illustrations not necessarily corresponding to the vision they had of the novel.

9. Rewrite the dialogues. A good solution may be to completely forget the novel's dialogues and dialogue the treatment yourself. Then, if necessary, reintegrate some remarkable dialogues.

B. THE STORY FOR CHILDREN

In La dramaturgie [51], I speak of the nobility of writing for children and discuss its specificities. The main difference with stories for adults and adolescents lies in the nature of the recipient.

1. Do not underestimate your audience. Children are as demanding as adults. You have no interest, for example, in skimping on conflict or relying on the spectacular.

2. Consider the point of view conveyed by the work (see list on pages 44-45).

3. Involve children in live performances. Children love it. It values them, gives them power over the action, and strengthens identification, thus empathy. It is regrettable that many puppet shows are done with a recorded soundtrack.

4. Do not try to seduce adults. A successful story is one that pleased children. Of course, if you manage to include several levels of reading in the work, to give pleasure to all ages, that's even better. This is something Disney, Miyazaki sometimes, and especially Goscinny with Asterix do well. But the temptation, and sometimes the mistake, is to prioritize the adult viewer over the child. When writing for children, one must have the courage of clarity and simplicity.

5. If you start the story with a plausible, realistic situation - for example, parents who do not have enough money to feed their children (Tom Thumb, Hansel and Gretel) - do not switch to fantasy through a dream (as in The 5,000 Fingers of Dr. T). This type of story says that dreams are useful for living and sometimes that the messages of dreams deserve to be heard. But the tale included in the dream does not have the impact it would have if it were introduced without artifice. Moreover, more generally, dreams are unnecessary in dramaturgy. Because it belongs to symbolic play. A film or a play is already a dream in itself. Classic fairy tales perfectly account for the dreamlike universe without ever switching to a dream.

6. Give common, generic names to the protagonists. And even, for all characters, names that designate their function: the sisters, the king, the stepmother, the ogre...

7. For Bettelheim [07], characters must be one-dimensional, simple, and Manichean. The younger the audience, the more necessary this rule is.

8. Tell the story several times to children, like the storytellers of yesteryear, and rewrite based on their reactions.

9. Finally, spending time with children, yours or others', can be an excellent idea. It is one of the greatest schools, of life as well as dramaturgy. But it must be recognized that it is a bit more than a technical track.

Do not overuse the good fairies

A child's life is more subject to arbitrariness than an adult's life. By nature, a child cannot fully take charge of their life. That is why they accept deus ex machina much more easily, whether in the strict sense (fairies or godmothers) or in the broad sense (external aids). That is also why a story intended for children can more easily afford to chain sketches one after the other.

That said, just because a child accepts deus ex machina more easily does not mean they are desirable. At the beginning of Puss in Boots, a miller bequeaths to his eldest son a mill, to his second son a donkey, and to his youngest son a cat. Of course, the youngest is frustrated. When he has eaten his cat, he will have nothing left but to starve. But it turns out that this cat is a clever one, worthy of the commedia dell'arte valets. He makes his master pass for the imaginary Marquis of Carabas and, with a few tricks, ensures him fortune and the hand of the princess. What a beautiful deus ex machina this cat is. It seems to me to harm the moral intended by Perrault, namely that of all inheritances, know-how is the most precious. Wouldn't it be better to tell the child that the best inheritance is their own know-how rather than someone else's?

I believe that a fairy tale protagonist should not be systematically tossed around. They must also take matters into their own hands. It might not be a bad idea to teach future adults to become autonomous, responsible for their actions, and not to eternally wait for providence or the government to come and solve their personal problems. The principle of causality is highly appreciated by children. A work aimed at them should therefore strive to work on syntax and preparation.

C. THE SHORT STORY

Once again, the tools proposed in this book are suitable for a two-minute story as well as a sixteen-hour series. A short story has the advantage of forcing you to get to the point and be inventive. You need to find tricks to compensate for the lack of space.

1. Design a simple first act in three phases: the routine life of the future protagonist, the inciting incident, the first act-second act transition. Each phase can be the subject of a scene, but all three can be included in the same scene if you are limited.

2. Give your protagonist a clear and universal stake.

3. Use activity to characterize the future protagonist.

4. Design a twist. Sometimes, this dramatic knot is almost the first element you will have, even before the climax - if the two are not confused. Sometimes, you will find it last.

D. THE DOCUMENTARY

Again, I refer you to my comments on the mechanisms of storytelling applied to documentaries in La dramaturgie [51]. Basically, you need to find the human factor of your project. This mainly involves applying some fundamental principles of drama.

1. Designate a protagonist.

2. Give the protagonist an objective that allows the subject to be addressed.

3. Think about placing some obstacles in their path.

4. Do not string pearls, however spectacular they may be. Avoid the tedious alignment of sketches. Create links between the parts.

5. Be aware that the unusual does not suffice for very long to interest the viewer.

6. If you are making a scientific documentary without a protagonist, stage an inciting incident and pose a question, preferably an emotional question.

The self-produced documentary

In September 2011, I had the opportunity to view a selection of a hundred amateur French documentaries. Some flaws were recurring. Here are some suggestions for self-produced filmmakers. Professional documentarians have the right to read them.

- Mature your project. Even if you bring back images from a highly exotic trip, do not just stick them together and set them to music. Find an angle.

- Have a personal point of view. Get involved. Say "I," even with non-fictional images. You are not making an interlude to fill a gap between two programs. You must not be content to be simply informative.

- Ask yourself why you want to show others what you are filming. What is the interest? Is it only spectacular? Is it truly original? Is it instructive? Moving?

- Do not be content with having a strong, original, or spectacular subject. You must also exploit it, structure it, stage it. It is the form that will capture the viewer's attention.

- Arouse the viewer's curiosity with a hook (an inciting incident, in the broad sense). More generally, structure and dramatize. Beginning, development, end.

- An off-screen commentary should not be more important than the images; it should not contain more interesting information than what you show.

- Your film should not give the impression that it was financed by a tourist office or a company.

- Have your project read if there is a prior written presentation. Once edited, show your film. In short, ask for feedback and learn to receive it.

- Do not rely on the viewer's admiration. They are sensitive to spectacular images - and even then, it only lasts for a while - but they do not care about the difficulty you had in capturing certain images.

E. THE SERIES (TV, COMICS, WEB)

I read here and there that the principle of the three-act structure (dramatic) cannot be applied to the series because, often, there is not a main action but several plots that span many episodes. How does this prevent the use of the classic structure? Unless, once again, logistical acts are confused with dramatic acts, I maintain that the three-act structure is one of the fractal forms of storytelling, and even one of its most important motifs, so it works on all scales and is indispensable in all formats, including the series. A series is a sum of three-act structures, simply richer than the sum present in a unitary story.

The first constraint, if you are writing for an already existing series, is to respect the bible. That is, the document that specifies all the recurring elements of the series.

Here are some suggestions if you are creating a series.

1. Find a general objective for the entire series, even if it is very long. Too many soap operas are started without the authors or producers knowing where they are going. As a result, the series are rather ended by the level of audience than for dramatic reasons.

2. If there are several parallel plots, choose a general objective for each plot. We have already addressed this subject in the chapter dedicated to the pitch (see pages 69-70).

3. Determine a local objective for each episode. The protagonist of a soap opera episode may not necessarily be the general protagonist of the series. If there are several plots, the ideal is to choose one predominant for the episode and simply keep the others alive.

4. Put a hook at the end of each episode.

5. If there are several plots, make sure they intersect as often as possible, one constituting obstacles for the other. This reinforces the unity of the series. To do this, you can proceed as follows. You designate each plot by a letter: A, B, C, etc. Then you design the milestones of each plot. This gives several scenes, which you number from 1 to x. You can then fill in a two-entry table. This gives something like this:
Then, you try to align the scenes of one plot with those of another. Each scene has its protagonist, who can serve as an antagonist for the neighboring plot's scene. This can result in, for example: A1 = E2, B4 = O5, or even C12 = O12 = F9, etc. The series "Un village français" does this brilliantly during the first four seasons. In episode 4 of season 2, for example, one of the plots involves a Jewish family crossing the demarcation line. Along the way, their smuggler (Dan Herzberg) encounters a poacher (Aubert Fenoy). A clue leads the smuggler to realize that his wife is cheating on him. He is so shocked that he sabotages his mission. Thus, a subplot creates an obstacle for another subplot.

The principle I describe here is useful for series with a strong ensemble cast. However, it is not sufficient. If you decide to follow the plots of many characters, you must choose a clear main protagonist and maintain unity of action. This is brilliantly achieved in the first season of "Spartacus" (main protagonist: Spartacus (Andy Whitfield)), "The Wire" (main protagonist: McNulty (Dominic West)), and "Un village français" (up to season 4 inclusive; main protagonist: Daniel Larcher (Robin Renucci)). We discussed this on page 62.

This method is also useful for constructing a unique work containing several subplots, that is, an ensemble work in the style of "The Chase" or "Children of Paradise."

12. FUNDAMENTAL REQUIREMENTS
"I was primarily looking to tell a story... and to tell it clearly."
(Hergé [40])
"Clarity is the most important quality."
(François Truffaut [85])
"I realized that I hadn't been simple enough, clear enough."
(Ingmar Bergman [03])
"It is essential that the audience is perfectly informed of the elements at play."
(Alfred Hitchcock [41])
"An absurd marvel holds no charm for me."
(Nicolas Boileau [10])
"I had the immense joy of entertaining others while entertaining myself."
(René Goscinny [38])
"I spend more time rewriting than writing."
(I.A.L Diamond [24])

I will not revisit what this book has advocated since the first pages, namely that it is preferable to proceed in order and construct the parts like a seamstress. And even before all that, one must be autonomous (cf. page 10). Instead, I want to propose a few precepts to keep in mind at all times, consciously and even unconsciously if possible, when writing. They can be seen as lighthouses in the night of authors, lighthouses whose flame must be constantly maintained. Or better yet, as automatisms, reflexes that eventually flow in the blood of narrators without them realizing it. As you will see, some advice reinforces points already made in previous chapters. These are important reminders in my eyes.

In "Dramaturgy" [51] and in my workshops, I sometimes give the impression of being obsessed with conflict. In fact, I believe that if an author puts a lot of conflict in their story, more precisely a lot of dynamic conflict, their glass will already be half full. Despite this, I will not start with this major element. There is something even more important than putting conflict in a work: one must make choices and be clear.

A. MAKE CHOICES
When starting a story, the field of possibilities is limitless. It's as if you are facing a tree with infinite branches. The freedom is absolute. The feeling can be delightful for some. Unfortunately, those who embrace too much, hold on poorly. You don't have the space to tell the exhaustive story of humanity in two hours. You must tell one specific story and only one. To achieve a unique narrative, you must make millions of decisions. And not just the major decisions that form the foundations (cf. chapter 3). Some choices are obvious and easy. For others, courage and awareness are needed. But the idea is indeed to eliminate an infinite number of branches... except one.

Making choices is often painful. Because choosing is not just about making a decision. It involves giving up everything that was not chosen. If you "choose" to adopt all the ideas that come to you and enchant you, you are not making a choice. It's actually the exact opposite.

I might be a bit heavy on this subject, but I have too often seen, in writing workshops, authors who cannot limit themselves, take responsibility, and make choices. Generally, they go in circles until the workshop ends.

This necessity of choice applies to all elements of the work and, of course, to the end of the story—which I will return to below. Except in rare cases where it makes sense to leave an open ending or to let the audience imagine the ending, you must take responsibility. Every human being necessarily has, consciously or unconsciously, a point of view on the world. An artist has the duty (and the honor) to convey their point of view through their art. Only under this condition can they create a connection with their audience and deserve to be considered an artist. It is not always sufficient, but it is at least a necessary condition.

Personally, when I go to the theater or the cinema, I don't care about agreeing with the author; I just want them to convey their point of view on life. I don't want to extrapolate and "make my own movie."

I think an author who leaves their ending open and takes a stance by explaining that it's up to each viewer to make their own movie is actually someone who doesn't know how to choose.

B. BE CLEAR
The two greatest sins of bad stories are the lack of clarity and the lack of conflict. There are others, of course, but these are the two most damaging. Clarity is the first virtue. The most brilliant thought in the world is lost if it is not transmitted and perceived correctly. It's as if it doesn't exist. Clarity does not prevent subtlety or even ambiguity. Characters and situations can be ambiguous, but not you. It's exactly like barbarism: a character can be barbaric, but it's not desirable for an author to be so.

Being clear means not hiding behind vagueness, postures, and approximations. It is therefore a courageous act. All the more courageous because some believe profound what they do not see the bottom of (as Nietzsche [59] says). As a result, they encourage you to add mystery, false notes, unresolved elements, all of which give them the impression of dealing with something intelligent. Then, being clear means quickly giving the audience a bone to chew on. And only one bone. A single triggering incident, simple or multiple. Clear motivations. A single objective. Clarity is also linked to meaning. Again, being clear and accessible does not mean being light or shallow. It's actually the exact opposite. When a rich and deep work forgets to be clear, everything is lost. Whereas a readable work, even if it says little, at least has the power to convey what it wants to say. In fact, more than depth alone, one could say that it's the product of depth/clarity that counts, a bit like the quality/price ratio in the industry.

The lack of clarity sometimes comes from the gap between what the author believes the audience knows and what the latter actually knows. This often happens with adaptations of pre-existing works. Unable to put everything in, authors remove important information for understanding the story without realizing it. But the phenomenon exists with original texts. Examples abound. At the beginning of "The Damned," Bruckmann (Dirk Bogarde) drives to a property. He talks with the driver (Helmut Griem) about characters we don't know. Moreover, the information is told and not shown.

We retain absolutely nothing from it. It's as if this scene doesn't exist. Many who have seen "The Discreet" know how frustrating the lack of clarity can be. The first nine-tenths of the film are clear and brilliant, but who understood the meaning of the last scenes? In "Insomnia," Will (Al Pacino) has trouble sleeping. Is it because he feels guilty for killing his colleague or because it's daylight at night—the action taking place near the polar circle? Both reasons are mentioned. There is one too many, but don't ask me which one.

The problem also sometimes comes from the fact that the author's thought is not clear itself. "What is well conceived is clearly stated," wrote Boileau [10] in 1674. And Chaplin [17] added: "If there is a subject so deep that it cannot be understood, it is because there is weakness in the artist's means of expression, or else that their brilliant subject was not so brilliant after all." And in an ironic version, under the pen of Goscinny [39]: "I tend to find very intelligent everything I don't understand. Every time I see an obscure film, attend an unintelligible play, or read an impenetrable text, I am deeply depressed. I am depressed because it gives me complexes. And it gives me complexes because I have always been understood." Goscinny is, of course, mocking, but many experience obscurity at face value.

"Providence," for example, begins with shots: a sign, the name of a property (Providence), undergrowth, an interior, a hand spilling a glass, and a voice-over swearing, a cathedral. Then an old man pursued in the forest by soldiers. A courthouse. A helicopter. A man (Dirk Bogarde) in a courtroom. A trial opposes him to a defendant named Woodford (David Warner). A woman (Ellen Burstyn) is in the audience, whose relationship with the trial characters is unknown. Part of the dialogue is heard over a shot of this woman. Then we see a corpse being autopsied. The hand pours a drink. Exit from the courtroom. The woman is named Sonia; she takes the defendant to a restaurant where they meet her husband, the prosecutor. He lost the trial. Then we see soldiers pushing old people into a stadium. Then Sonia takes Woodford home, ready to give herself to him. He doesn't seem in a hurry. The voice-over comments on the scene. We finally see the face of the voice-over. It doesn't resemble the autopsied body.

In short, within fifteen minutes, we are treated to a mise en abyme, flashbacks, images, nightmares, ramblings, double roles, and a voice-over without a certain owner. Is it necessary to specify that this puzzle is not of exemplary clarity? In fact, one must read David Mercer's screenplay to understand the beginning of the film. In scene 11 of his screenplay, the author provides some of the keys (and still...) in a text that appears as the perfect example of what should never be written in a screenplay: "On Mark Eddington's face, we hear the Narrator's voice-over, and we soon understand (sic!) that the scenes we are witnessing are fragments of a book the Narrator is imagining. The writer is Claud's father (N.B.: the prosecutor), a distinguished and famous old gentleman, whose body must face physical degradation, whose mind sometimes wanders, and who drinks too much. During a solitary night, he comments aloud on the film's action—that is, the book he is working on—talking to himself, evoking memories, expressing his fear of the approaching death. At this point in his work, dreaming of this novel that may be his last, he uses for his characters the real names of his son Claud, his daughter-in-law Sonia (N.B.: the woman in the courtroom), and Kevin, his natural son (N.B.: Woodford)."

"Is it such a great evil to be heard when speaking and to speak like everyone else?" asked La Bruyère [47] to Acis, the false spirit.

I just spoke about the most important clarity, the one that will allow the audience to receive your story. But there is another, the one concerning the reader of your text. Being clear also means doing everything to make your story easy to read. A simple example: don't give your characters names that resemble each other. If you have three female characters, don't call them Anne, Aline, and Alice. Certainly, when the characters are embodied by actors, there will be no more ambiguity—if the actors themselves do not resemble each other—but, in reading, it is a source of confusion.

C. BE FEROCIOUS WITH YOUR PROTAGONIST
I almost wrote "Be cruel with your protagonist," but the word "cruel" is sometimes misinterpreted. It is often confused with "sadistic," for example. In any case, you must make your protagonist experience as much conflict as possible, push them to their limits, twist the knife in one of their wounds.

Too many stories lack conflict. I see two possible reasons for this. Either the author loves their protagonist too much to harm them. In this case, they don't understand that the best way to make them endearing and even to do them a favor is precisely not to spare them. Or the author is in a fantasy. Because their own life is a nightmare, they project their desire for harmony, power, ease, or virtuosity onto their protagonist. Let's be clear, I love harmony. Yes, yes, I swear. But in my personal life. In fiction, if there is no conflict, the audience gets bored. It's as simple as that.

Once again, making a character experience as much conflict as possible implies that the conflict is both measured and dynamic. If the conflict is too strong and the protagonist can do nothing, we have a scene of torture, in the broad sense.

I remind you that conflict can take a more subtle and original form than the tedious fight, the endless shootout, or the imperishable marital dispute. A Tunisian filmmaker, Nadia El Fani, once came to a writing workshop with a very interesting scene because it presented an unusual type of conflict. Most of the time, a scene's obstacle is antagonistic. That is, it goes in the same direction as the objective, but negatively. Two people who oppose each other generally have the same concerns (convincing, being the strongest, finding first, etc., all on the same subject). Nadia El Fani's scene told the story of a revolutionary who returns to her friend's home after spending some time in prison. Surprise: her friend lives with another woman. A dialogue of the deaf ensues, the young woman reproaching her friend for forgetting her for another and the young man worrying about whether she gave him to the militiamen. This shift in conflict was all the more interesting because it occurred without the help of the preferred tool for shifts: dramatic irony.

Exhausting first time
The "fish out of water" is a well-known type of story. It involves placing a character in an environment unknown to them, forcing them to adapt, learn, and be creative. "E.T.," "Time After Time," and "One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest" offer three beautiful examples of this specific genre. Specific? What if all stories were or should be of the "fish out of water" type, in the broad sense? Not because the protagonist is projected into an unknown arena but because the action pushes them to do things they have never done. If you don't take your fish out of the water, ask it to walk in its bowl. Isn't that a good way to be ferocious with your protagonist, to push them to their limits?

Having only one father, Hamlet is confronted with an event he has never experienced (the death of his father), but it is probably also the first time he must avenge the murder of a loved one ("Hamlet"). Oedipus has certainly never conducted a police investigation, let alone one where he is the culprit ("Oedipus Rex"). Cyrano has never tried to woo a young woman through an interposed dandy ("Cyrano de Bergerac"). Anne Sullivan ("The Miracle Worker") has just graduated from her educators' school at the age of twenty. Taking care of Helen Keller is her first job. If Joe (Tony Curtis) and Jerry (Jack Lemmon) dressed as women every two months, "Some Like It Hot" wouldn't be as impactful. If Roger Thornhill (Cary Grant) were a professional spy and on his tenth kidnapping, we would be less attentive to his adventure ("North by Northwest"). It's one of the reasons why James Bond bores me prodigiously. His adventures are routine. In "Groundhog Day," Phil (Bill Murray) experiences two firsts: 1 - he must relive the same dismal day over and over again, 2 - he must love sincerely. In short, the ordeal you will throw your protagonist into must be exceptional for them as well as for the audience. Your story will then be delightful and can do without pyrotechnics, savagery, and all-powerfulness.

Being ferocious with your protagonist forces you to reveal their determination. An endearing protagonist is a character who does not give up and is even willing to go very far to achieve their goal.

D. BE RUTHLESS WITH YOURSELF
Do not be complacent. Beware, like the plague, of the old adage: "A priori, if it interests me, it will necessarily interest the public." Because it confuses your thought ("interests me") with its translation ("will interest the public"). An author always knows much more about their story than what they give to the audience. First, because you can't put everything in a work, then because you don't always put in correctly what you had in mind. It's not enough to have interesting ideas to interest the public; you also need to know how to exploit them. In short, if the translation of the thought is not adequate, the public will not be offered precisely what interested the author.

Another dangerous adage: "The essential thing is to be sincere." As if an artist or even a human being in general could do something of their own free will and not be sincere. Even Claude Chabrol, when he botches "Dr. M," is sincere. To be insincere, you need a machine gun on your belly, the Inquisition on your heels, or an abusive producer on your back. In short, the question of sincerity is a non-issue. If it's a necessary condition for producing a quality work, it's clear that it's far from being a sufficient condition.

A crucial requirement: necessity
We have already discussed the principle of necessity regarding character creation. I have seen how difficult it is for many authors to accept. It infringes on their sacrosanct freedom. I remind you that a human being's freedom is entirely relative. We will never be Monet, Molière, or Mozart, for example. We have to make do with what we have, and we don't always have the freedom—the permission, as Eric Berne [04] would say—to truly be ourselves. And then, if the author is "free" to choose their point of view and make their initial choices, they are then subject to a multitude of obligations. We just talked about this above (and in chapter 3), you cannot leave all doors open indefinitely. Of all the arts, dramaturgy is probably the most constraining, partly because it requires a lot of consideration for the audience. Of course, you can always disregard them and just please yourself. But is the freedom to disdain the public a fundamental freedom, the kind that would deserve, for example, to be included in the Universal Declaration of Human Rights? Is it even a freedom at all?

Most often, the part of works that best respects this principle of necessity is the first act. The author presents what they need to tell their story without wasting time. In this respect, the beginnings of "The School for Wives," "The Grand Maneuver," "Mr. Smith Goes to Washington," or "The ComDads" are remarkable. Unfortunately, the second act is rarely constructed with the same rigor and too often gives the impression of filler—the worst examples not being, by far, the four works above.

Sincerely a charlatan, one might add. Chabrol admitted that he didn't understand himself what he had done in "Dr. M"!
An author often struggles to let go of an idea, a scene, or a character. The fact that they are not necessary is not enough to convince him to "cut off a finger." Remember that the talent and sensitivity that found a beautiful idea will have no trouble finding another, equally beautiful and more useful. In other words, if something is cut, it's more like a lizard's tail than a finger.

A crucial requirement: economy

Economy, which involves not diluting each piece of information and condensing as much as possible, is rewarding for the audience. Its principle is directly related to the audience's mental acuity and their ability to receive a dramatic work. At the very beginning of cinema, when transitioning from one shot to another on the same action, the same thing was shown twice, at the end of the first shot and the beginning of the second, to ensure the audience wasn't confused by the editing. Today, in an era where some advertisements tell a story in twenty seconds, the audience is better educated, more receptive. Authors can say more in less time. Ellipses, unity of time, and small bits of activity, which convey in two seconds what dialogues took two minutes to express, are tools of economy. And again, this applies to theater, sometimes hampered by its verbosity.

Know the narrative off-screen

Every narrative provides a certain amount of information to its receiver. For it to be coherent, it is obvious that its author must know much more. As we've seen, this is the case with character characterization. You must know where they come from and what they have in mind, even if not everything will be included in the final result.

This is also the case with ellipses. An ellipse can only work if you know exactly what is in the omitted part. It is not a sufficient condition, but it is at least a necessary one. I've seen authors skip an event because they didn't want to tell it and end up with an inconsistency because they hadn't made the effort to see in detail how they went from A to C. At the beginning of 'Un homme à la hauteur,' Diane (Virginie Efira) meets Alexandre (Jean Dujardin) over the phone. They meet in person in a café, where Alexandre suggests Diane cancel her appointments on the spot and follow him on an adrenaline experience without telling her more. Cut. We find them in a plane, in the sky, in parachute suits, clinging to each other. And Diane screams that she's scared! But she jumps anyway. Question: how did she end up there without protesting earlier? Did Alexandre drug her? I tried to imagine the ellipsed scene. Alexandre takes Diane to a skydiving club, suggests she put on a parachute suit, get on a plane, and stand in front of him, right against him—even though they don't know each other and there is sexual tension between them. And Diane said yes? A bit light, but why not. Once in the air, to believe this scene, I miss a dialogue like "Stop! I was wrong to say yes down there." Because Diane still has the choice not to jump. I suspect the authors didn't question the content of their ellipse.

In short, you must know everything behind the scenes of your narrative, whether those backstage elements relate to events or characterizations. Your receiver will only see the tip of the iceberg, but the invisible part must exist and be solid enough to hold everything together.

E. BE SIMPLE AND EXPLOIT

For playwright and filmmaker David Mamet, a good script is a simple starting point undergoing unpredictable twists and turns. We've already talked about simplicity regarding the number of characters. Simple does not mean simplistic, just as complex does not mean complicated. Simple means the author puts few eggs in their basket and whips them well, exploits them well, makes them perform unpredictable twists and turns. However, milking is one of the most difficult techniques to master. "Being simple is difficult; the simpler you are, the more complicated you are at the same time, but in a deep way, not superficially," says Roman Polanski. And Joseph Losey adds: "Simplicity is what one can wish for the best, but it is also the hardest to achieve." As a result, most authors prefer to complicate superficially. This is how we see characters or subplots flourish that are nothing but filler. "All this great number of incidents," writes Racine, "has always been the refuge of poets who did not feel in their genius enough abundance or strength to engage their spectators for five acts (logistics) with a simple action." In 'Les compères,' Francis Veber has a golden subject: two completely dissimilar individuals (Pierre Richard, Gérard Depardieu) set out to find a teenager (Stéphane Bierry) each believes to be his son. And, of course, they continually dispute paternity. The film starts off with a bang. It's brilliant. I regret that instead of going deep into his subject—which was far from easy—and exploiting this situation full of potential to the maximum, Veber added a police subplot that weakens his film. Even though the film contains excellent moments, funny and human, there are too many eggs and not as much snow as hoped.

Another example: the truck scene from 'Raiders of the Lost Ark' is a magnificent milking scene. In the third episode of the series ('Indiana Jones and the Last Crusade'), the authors serve us the same scene again, but they lose simplicity. As if they were saying, "Oh, you liked the truck scene? Wait. This time, it won't be a truck but three tanks!" In short, there are too many eggs. The whole film is at this level: nods and escalation. As a result, it doesn't have the crystalline purity of the first episode.

Exploiting is sometimes arduous. Whipping up what you've set up requires creativity and sometimes courage. I've often seen, in my workshops, authors who are afraid of simplicity, who don't trust their project, who can't milk or who beat around the bush. They add eggs, cows, characters, subplots, flashbacks. And of course, deconstruction. They put the beginning at the end and the end in the middle.

In Chapter 4, I talked about the "What if...?" formula regarding strong premises. There is another "What if...?" among authors, which operates during writing. What if my protagonist was a surf fan? What if the villain used a milling machine instead? What if the driver said nothing? What if the neighbor had cancer? These "ideas" are sometimes escapes. The author doesn't start from a narrative problem to solve; they let their imagination wander and find dispensable ideas.

Often the avoidance is unconscious. Be at least able to sound the alarm when you notice you're overloading your boat. I am convinced that the more complicated a narrative is, the more it loses in accuracy and humanity.

Ambiguity and ambiguity

The principles of clarity and simplicity do not exclude ambiguity. But there is ambiguity and ambiguity. The latter is often a posture or a pretext not to work too hard. One thinks the audience will piece things together on their own. True ambiguity does not exist in doubt and confusion. Quite the opposite. If you want to create, for example, an ambiguous character, it is necessary to proceed step by step. You start by clearly presenting a trait of their character. And only when this trait is well established do you bring nuances, shadows, elements of doubt. Like in the example of the lady who disapproves of nudists. Putting all the colors at once is confusing ambiguity with muddle.

F. INVOLVE THE AUDIENCE

Among the works that strive to tell something, there are, roughly, two categories: those that involve the receiver and those that leave them on the sidelines. In the second case, there is a subcategory where it is clear that the author knows what they are doing, knows where they are going, but has not taken care to sufficiently inform their receiver. Generally, it is only at the end that we understand where the author wanted to go.

The first hour of 'The Lonesome West' is a routine of life, both biting and endless, presenting the antagonism of two brothers. In the middle of the play, a priest writes to the two brothers and asks them to reconcile. The climax is the (difficult) reconciliation scene. As I've already had the opportunity to write, the climax of a narrative is its biggest payoff. When the climax of 'The Lonesome West' arrives, I finally understand where Martin McDonagh wanted to go. It is regrettable that he did not take care to include in his play the biggest preparation of a narrative, namely the declaration of purpose. In my opinion, the play would have worked better if the author had designated the priest as the protagonist, with the goal of reconciling the two brothers. It wouldn't have sold the end of the work. It would have just allowed me to participate.

First tool of participation: clarity

Where we find the question of clarity, which touches all positions, from writing to casting, staging, and presentation conditions of the works. It is clearly an essential concern among great storytellers. In the 'Blueberry' series albums, Jean-Michel Charlier regularly offers topographic maps to help us locate the action. In 'The Seven Samurai,' Kurosawa shows us the village plan several times to make us understand the layout of the places. Later, Shimada (Takashi Shimura) checks off the number of bandits eliminated on a board. The same Kurosawa often quoted for saying: "A really good film is interesting and easy to understand." In 'The Man Who Knew Too Much,' Hitchcock does everything to familiarize us with a crucial piece of music for the action. The authors of 'Lagaan' are so clear that they manage to make us understand the rules of one of the most complicated sports in the world (with palm): cricket. And in addition, while entertaining us. Hats off! At the end of 'Once Upon a Time in the West,' at the time of the great final duel, the authors explain to us, with a flashback, why Harmonica (Charles Bronson) is pursuing Frank (Henry Fonda). It is interesting to note that this flashback informs the audience but not Frank. It is only when Harmonica puts his musical instrument in Frank's mouth that Frank understands who he is dealing with.

To appreciate the benefits of clarity in dramaturgy, I invite you to compare the two narratives drawn from 'Monsieur Hire's Engagements' by Georges Simenon. A heinous crime is committed by a young woman's lover. Monsieur Hire, a cultured and asocial loner, witnessed the crime but his misanthropy made him keep the evidence to himself instead of giving it to the police. The young woman knows that Monsieur Hire knows the murderer. She takes advantage of his attraction to her to make him take the blame for the crime. Blinded by love, M. Hire lets himself be fooled. In the adaptation by Charles Spaak and Julien Duvivier ('Panique'), we quickly know that M. Hire (Michel Simon) witnessed the murder and we quickly understand that the young woman (Viviane Romance) is framing him. Dramatic irony of which Hire is a victim. In the adaptation by Patrick Dewolf and Patrice Leconte ('Monsieur Hire'), we learn all these elements at the end, in the form of surprise and clarification. Not only is it less rewarding, but it prevents us from fully participating in the story, from fully understanding the characters' motivations. It lacks emotion and... participation.

Second tool of participation: preparation

Preparation is the second major tool for audience participation. All forms of preparation. The one that makes things plausible. The one that allows dramatization. The one that informs the audience about the stakes, motivations, ghost, or dramatic need of a character. The one that creates an ellipse. And then the one that consists of exploiting what you set up.

Imagine a juggler arriving in a circus ring with a basket. In his basket, everyone can see balls, tennis rackets, and torches. The juggler performs a number with the balls, then with the rackets. Then he packs up his things and leaves. What does the audience say? "And what about the torches?!" In other words, if you don't want to juggle with the torches—and it's your strictest right—don't put them in your basket, don't risk your audience seeing them.

If you consciously place an announcement in your work, it will seem logical to exploit it. But many elements in a narrative are announcements, sometimes discreet and subtle. The notions of announcement and scene to be done join the idea that an author creates expectations in the audience and that it is their responsibility to take them into account. When you start designing a narrative, you are completely free, you can open all doors. We've talked about it above. But as soon as you start making choices and reducing your field of possibilities, you can no longer do just anything. You are free to disappoint the audience, to show subversion, but it is better to do it knowingly rather than out of laziness, incompetence, or arrogance. In short, it is not degrading to think about the scenes to be done. First, because a scene to be done can very well play with the audience's expectations and surprise them. Making the audience participate and stroking them the right way are two very distinct things. But also because a scene to be done is often one of the key scenes of the work, the one where you will put your guts, the pot of intimacy and authenticity around which some circle instead of entering.

In short, always remember that 1 - all information must serve; 2 - do not promise what you cannot deliver.

Third tool of participation: dramatic irony

No need to draw a picture. The mere fact of giving the audience information that at least one character ignores is a barely veiled address to the receiver of the dramaturgy. Is it necessary to recall that the audience is fond of dramatic irony, whether to create comedy, to move, or to scare?

Fourth tool of participation: self-confidence

Imagine the following scene. A company boss summons one of his employees to an empty conference room and invites him to sit while he remains standing. The boss is accompanied by an assistant, also standing. Both look at each other knowingly. The employee, for his part, is a bit embarrassed. He goes to sit down when he realizes he has chosen a chair that turns its back to his boss. He stops mid-course and chooses another chair among the seventeen empty chairs in the room. He sits down, the chair creaks, and he finds himself on the floor. The boss thanks his employee. The latter leaves the room, disconcerted. Then the boss turns to his assistant and asks for explanations. The assistant tells him he sabotaged one of the chairs, just one out of seventeen, and that's precisely the one the unlucky employee "chose." What do we have in this scene? A bit of mystery, a surprise, and a clarification.
