Now imagine the assistant explaining his stratagem to the boss at the very beginning, even before the employee arrives, with the rest of the scene unfolding exactly the same way. An amateur author would be afraid, by proceeding this way, of giving all the cards to the audience and ruining the suspense, the pleasure, and so on. But the opposite happens! The second formula (adopted and brilliantly exploited by Francis Veber at the beginning of 'La chèvre') stages an announcement and dramatic irony. The audience certainly has many cards in hand, but they don't have all the answers. This allows them to fully participate in the action. They are even more eager to know what will happen. They are even more attentive.

If you are afraid of saying too much, you will be tempted to withhold information, to play the mystery card. Let it be clear, as long as you don't sell the dramatic answer, you can tell the audience everything. And even. Even when the dramatic answer is known, the audience can be interested in a narrative if, for example, they don't know how this dramatic answer was provided.

Already involve the reader

Involving the audience also means not falling into the classic trap of describing, in the text, what the audience should feel. For example: "Suddenly, GERALD, 42 years old, tall and endearing, enters the kitchen." Being endearing (moving, exciting, etc.) is not a physical characteristic, it's a narrative quality. It's up to you to make your character endearing and for us to be the judges. Another example: "He then has an unexpected reaction: he bursts out laughing" or "Simone reacts in a surprising way." If a character's reaction is truly unexpected, the reader will realize it without being told. Or: "You can feel that Dupont is sad." If Dupont is sad, show it, don't write it. By forcing yourself not to cheat with the reader of your narrative, you will contribute to involving said reader but also, ultimately, the audience.

G. BE DISSOCIATED

Dramaturgy has the power to reveal the thoughts, desires, and emotions of others. It is clear that an author cannot showcase the thoughts, desires, and emotions of their characters if they do not know or feel them acutely. In other words, you must know at all times what is happening in the minds of your characters, all your characters (not just your protagonist). You will tend to try to know what will happen in the mind of your audience, which is quite legitimate. But, in reality, your task is double (and heavy): you must be able to put yourself, at any moment, in the place of your characters and in the place of your audience.

H. UNDERSTAND ALL YOUR CHARACTERS

A corollary of the above, you must understand all your characters and even give them reason, each in their system. The motivation of a secondary character is never to create obstacles for the protagonist. If it is, you are manipulating puppets. A character's motivation is to satisfy their needs (including the most barbaric), which may have the side effect of creating obstacles for the protagonist.

A trick is to imagine your characters at the bar of a trial. They are accused and defend themselves. They argue. "I did what any patriotic citizen would have done in my place." "It was for his own good, Madam Prosecutor, I was so afraid he wouldn't find a job at the exit." "I was just following orders. If we start questioning the hierarchy's orders all the time, it never ends." "It's stronger than me, Mr. Judge, when I see a beautiful pair of buttocks, I can't control myself. You can understand. We're not made of wood, after all!" "I didn't think it would have such consequences, it was just to help him." "He was an apostate, Mr. Commissioner! He didn't deserve to live, God said so."

Similarly, a secondary character's motivation cannot be to provide useful information for the action. This may be their function, but it does not exempt you from giving them a goal.

Be careful, however, there is a trap in understanding all your characters and putting yourself in their place, that of conferring another character the status of protagonist, and thus losing the true protagonist. I have already pointed out this risk to you. Be aware of it and avoid making too many conflicts live for all the characters other than the protagonist. Give them goals and motivations but not necessarily obstacles.

I. DRAMATIZE

The word "dramatize" has roughly the same meaning in everyday life and in writing. It is about giving importance to things. In our activity as authors, it consists of finding the most dramatic situation to transmit or exploit a narrative element. In other words, to prepare the context to give the narrative element a setting made of conflict, stakes, and suspense.

Imagine that at a moment in the narrative, your protagonist takes drugs. You can gloss over it as if it doesn't matter much. They are offered cocaine at a party without it having consequences. And why not. But if you consider that this cocaine use is not trivial, you must value it, give it importance. For example, you will first show that your protagonist is healthy. They may even be proud not to indulge in drugs. You will show that at some point, they are overworked or depressed, in short, they are in a state that makes doping products tempting. We can imagine that they are offered cocaine and they reject it vehemently. Finally, when they take it, they tell themselves it's just for once. You can even create a character of a reasonable contradictor.

Imagine that your protagonist must discover or understand something important. A resolution of dramatic irony, for example. You can place them in a context where they will have every opportunity to react as they please. But you can also put them in a situation where they are forced to hide their emotion. You then double the conflict. This is what Brecht does in 'Mother Courage and Her Children.' When Mother Courage must recognize the body of her youngest son, she is forced to pretend it is not him at the very moment she recognizes him, lest she betray herself in the eyes of the soldiers.

Imagine Butch Cassidy (Paul Newman) and Sundance Kid (Robert Redford) riding horses in the desert. They talk about everything and nothing, their childhood, their first loves. And, at one point, between the pear and the cheese, Sundance tells Butch he can't swim. You have information that is poorly introduced. You can, on the contrary, like William Goldman in 'Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid,' put the two protagonists at the top of a cliff overlooking a...
torrent. They are cornered by lawmen. They've drawn their revolvers, ready to defend themselves, when Butch suggests jumping into the torrent below. Sundance categorically refuses. Butch insists until Sundance, cornered, admits: "I can't swim!" If you do this, you've dramatized the information.

The example of Eve given on page 270 is another example of dramatization. Eve's (Anne Baxter) past is revealed at the most appropriate moment and in a way that creates maximum conflict for her. Just when the ambitious Eve thinks she has won everything, she is caught by her lies, giving Addison DeWitt (George Sanders) the means to control her.

There is at least one thing that every worthy author dramatizes, and that is their ending. An author who knows where they are going will do everything to prepare and highlight their climax, their dramatic response, and their third act. But that's not enough. All significant elements of the story must be dramatized.

I mean "significant." It's not about dramatizing every little element. You're not going to dramatize the fact that a character is five foot nine, loves cashews, or only has sisters. If, in the middle of the action, the protagonist gets into a car, it's probably not necessary to make a big deal out of it. In short, it all depends on what you're telling.

You can even dramatize harmonious events. Imagine your protagonist experiences a series of local successes. You need to show it to advance the action, but you're embarrassed because it's not conflictual. A classic solution is to make a quick montage sequence. But you can also have these successes recounted by a secondary character who finds it difficult and who, of course, will be a source of obstacles later. In other words, your protagonist's momentary success turns into a prospect of conflict.

J. DON'T SAY, SHOW

I return to this fundamental rule, already addressed in chapters 6 and 7. It is not only fundamental, it is also famous and abundantly cited. Everyone understands that in dramaturgy, you have to show things and not say them. From this important rule, which no one disputes, things go awry. Because many conclude that everything must go through the visual and therefore through the image. Especially in cinema, which is supposedly essentially an art of the image.

Double mistake. First, in literature too, it's better to show than to say. However, in literature, there is nothing to see, everything is in the reader's imagination. Second, the best tool for showing things is not the image but the action. Of course, in theater, cinema, and comics, action partly goes through the image. But not only. Action also uses dialogue. And above all, action uses conflict, characterization, and preparation.

Even if dialogue is sometimes an easy solution, you can very well show with dialogue. When Carreidas, at the beginning of Flight 714 to Sydney, calls an auctioneer, tells him that the next sale doesn't interest him, that he already has more Renoirs and Picassos than he knows what to do with, but that if Onassis is involved, then he'll buy at any price, when Carreidas says all this, he is characterized. When the career counselor (Julie Ferrier) in Today, It's Ferrier! says in a sharp tone: "Miss, do you think you can achieve your dreams in life?!", it's nothing but dialogue and yet the characterization of the character comes through. We learn something about the career counselor that is shown and not said. It would be said if the career counselor said: "I gave up my dreams and have kept a lot of bitterness." In short, showing things instead of saying them can involve using dialogues.

Moreover, showing, even effectively, may not be enough. Imagine one of your characters is angry. Everyone agrees, he shouldn't just say "Grrrr, I'm angry!" You have to show it. So, your character will scream, flail, protest, break something, or even turn green and Herculean. You will have shown the anger but you will not necessarily have explained it. I think it's not enough to show, you also have to justify. You have to make us understand why a character is angry. By showing the damage, for example. Explaining has multiple advantages: it makes events credible, it allows the receiver to participate, it helps characterize the character, it can create a foreshadowing, and it can even allow you to do less. If we know the reasons for the anger, it can be expressed in a restrained or original way.

K. USE THE MOST POWERFUL LANGUAGES

A language is a system of signs intended to create meaning or emotion. Dramaturgy is made up of several languages that do not all have the same effectiveness. In order, from least powerful to most powerful:

- dialogue (spoken language);
- activity (language of gestures);
- characterization (language of actions);
- structure and preparation work (language of scenes).

In other words, when you want to convey information or create a particular feeling in the viewer, always ask yourself if the structure can allow it. And rely on dialogue only as a last resort.

You will have noticed that the language of images is not part of the list above. This is because in dramaturgy, whether in cinema or theater, everything goes through image and sound (in comics, through image and text). A gesture, an action, a payment are often conveyed by an image. In other words, the image can be used at all levels. This means that the visual—which obsesses some cinema theorists—can sometimes be powerful and sometimes unproductive. At the beginning of Rear Window, a tracking shot shows us the interior of an apartment. We see cameras, magazines, and a photograph of a car accident. Hitchcock doesn't just characterize the apartment's owner, he tries to tell us how Jeff (James Stewart) broke his leg. I write "tries" because in my opinion, it doesn't work very well. The image of a human being in action is much more telling than a succession of static images that the viewer must piece together.

L. CREATE CONNECTION

Have you noticed how obsessed TV presenters are with connection? They are so uncomfortable stringing pearls and jumping from one topic to another that they are filled with gratitude as soon as they are offered the slightest transition. And when they have nothing to move from a presidential election in Bolivia to the results of the Six Nations Tournament, they serve us the classic "Without transition..." I mock, but in truth, we are all obsessed with connection and structure. It's a fundamental human need.

In dramaturgy, this need is satisfied in four ways. The first connection exists in all artistic activities as soon as the artist takes care to reach out to their receiver. The second connection is the one that unites the viewer with the character they identify with. As the author projects themselves into their protagonist, we see that the character is an intermediary between author and receiver. The third connection concerns the communion that occurs between the spectators of the same performance. Theater and cinema offer rituals, religious experiences, in the etymological sense of the term "religious." Finally, the fourth connection is the one that interests us here: the organic dimension of your story. This connection between the different parts of the work is even double. It is often causal but it can also be thematic.

Give us the impression that your narrative elements fit together perfectly, that nothing is there by chance, that the life of your fictional universe makes sense and that your work breathes unity. For this, there aren't thirty-six ways, you must make the elements of the story pay off as much as possible. This must become a reflex.

A reflex that saved Andrew Stanton [78] and his co-authors in writing Toy Story. (Note: Warning, the following reveals the ending.) "The film," explains Stanton, "ends with Buzz and Woody chasing a truck to get home. They absolutely must catch this truck but their batteries are dead and it seems obvious they won't make it. The script said Woody had one last match that he uses to light a rocket that propels them into the truck. We watched this scene and realized that the audience, very aware of how films work, would immediately identify this match as the last hope that will save our heroes." Indeed, the writers had created what is called a "telephone," the worst form of foreshadowing. "At this point," continues Stanton, "the film was over before the end! So we thought Woody could indeed light the match but a passing car could blow it out! We all exclaimed: 'Great! They're lost!'" Phew. Intelligently, the writers turned the telephone into a red herring. The same principle is at work in Life is Beautiful (1997). When we see the doctor (Horst Buchholz) in the concentration camp, we are convinced he will help Guido (Roberto Benigni). It's a telephone. Fortunately, a beautiful surprise awaits the protagonist and the viewer. But back to Toy Story. Unlike Benigni and Cerami, Stanton and his co-authors nevertheless found themselves with a problem: "We loved the idea of what it would do to the audience watching the film but we had no idea how our heroes would finally get out of these problems. (...) We watched other parts of the film again and came across this scene where Sid burns a small hole on Woody's forehead with a magnifying glass and we thought that the same effect could be used to ignite the flame. Woody could remember the incident and use it." The moment Woody uses Buzz's plastic helmet is therefore a payoff of the scene with Sid. The deep connection established at that moment gives the film an extremely gratifying organic dimension.

Units

Creating a connection between the different parts of the work nurtures the sense of unity that your story exudes. After the unity of action, organic unity is the most important. Other means can strengthen the impression of unity:

- focus the action on a short period of time;
- confine the action to a handful of locations;
- deal with only one action (thus avoiding subplots);
- connect any subplots with the same theme.

Even in cases where you have a clear and unique protagonist with a clear and unique objective, it happens that they are not present in all scenes. Certainly, the omnipresence of the protagonist is a strong element of unity (cf. The School for Wives, City Lights, or North by Northwest) but you may need to stage other characters than your protagonist. A factor of connection and unity then consists in talking about them in their absence. In Act IV of Hamlet, Hamlet goes to England and Shakespeare does not follow him. We stay in Denmark but Hamlet is still the topic of conversation. In scenes 6 and 7, his letters are read. And in scene 7, Claudius and Laertes plot his death. In short, the protagonist is not physically present but he is on everyone's mind and lips.

M. BE PLAUSIBLE

The story is a symbolic representation of life. We don't ask it to copy life, we ask it to evoke it. You are therefore entitled to the fourth wall, black and white, metaphors, all the artifices, as long as they allow you to do what Shakespeare invites us to in the prologue of Henry V: fill the imperfections with thought and appeal to the power of imagination.

But the suspension of disbelief has limits. The viewer is not ready to swallow just anything and fill all the gaps. They want to believe. They need a minimum of logic and plausibility. The balance is delicate because not all viewers have the same tolerance threshold for the implausible. Some viewers of Life is Beautiful (1997) did not understand that the film should be considered a fable and did not adhere to it for this reason. Some viewers of I Confess can't believe that a priest (Montgomery Clift) who receives the confession of a murderer (Otto Hasse) is so bound by professional secrecy that he remains silent and even lets himself be accused of the crime (cf. pages 274-275). Another example: The Goat, whose project was rejected by a French producer—who had already worked with Francis Veber—because he didn't believe in the character of the chronically unlucky person.

There it is, the magic word: believe. Be the first to believe in your inventions and your fable. It may not be a sufficient condition to be plausible but it is clearly a necessary condition. Remember with Boileau [10] that "the truth can sometimes not be plausible," that between the two it is better to choose the latter and that preparation is the ideal tool to make your story plausible.

I have mainly talked about structural plausibility. But there is another area where the need for plausibility is acute: the psychology of the characters. I think you cannot rely solely on your unconscious to be coherent. Because it has its limits. The collective unconscious of the audience always knows more than our own author's unconscious on the matter. To care for the psychology of your characters, the humanities can help but life itself is also wonderful—when considered as a school and a library of infinite dimensions, and when constantly drawn from.

N. BE SPECIFIC

We talked about the importance of being specific regarding characterization (page 137), the objective (pages 146-147 and 162-163), the arena (page 167), and dialogue (page 265). In fact, this requirement is valid for all elements of your story. And at all stages. At the time of writing the foundations as well as the dialogue continuity. A protagonist, for example, cannot have as their only objective that everything goes well. It's much too vague. Who doesn't want to easily succeed in what they undertake? You must be more concrete, more precise, more specific.

Don't write: "He makes an inappropriate gesture." There are dozens of inappropriate gestures. Write: "He puts his hand on his crotch." If your character honks a horn, ask yourself what sound the horn makes. It doesn't have to be original at all costs. But it can make sense. In any case, it's always worth asking the question. In The Easy Life, Bruno (Vittorio Gassman) has a sports car with a show-off horn.

In short, be precise in everything and call a spade a spade. In addition to adding value to your story, you will do an immense service to the actors who will embody your characters as well as to the technicians (props master, sound editor...).

O. RESOLVE YOUR STORY

I return to the question of the ending, already addressed in chapter 10. Authors who consider themselves cinematic phoenixes and view the story as a pretext often have the unfortunate habit of not finishing their film. How many times do we see the end credits start in the middle of nowhere and leave us stunned by such negligence (or such artistic posture). The boldness, again, would be to tell the story well rather than cowardly shirking this responsibility.

In short, if you think you are as much a craftsman as a genius artist, constantly keep in mind that when you set up a narrative element, you announce its resolution. This is true for everything: an objective, a character, dramatic irony, a subplot, a trajectory question, etc. We have already talked about it but I insist.

Let's be clear. Announcing a resolution is making the essential partner, the viewer, feel that you are going to resolve what you have set up. It's not telling the viewer how you are going to do it, it's not giving away all the answers in advance. And, when the time comes, take your responsibilities. Don't do like Paul Thomas Anderson, in Magnolia, who drops thousands of frogs on all his characters' heads to end with a bang but who, casually, leaves several subplots unresolved.

A succinct and significant third act

Resolving your story also means not lingering in the third act, unless of course you adopt an enriched structure. Don't give the viewer the impression that you don't know what you're doing and that you're wandering at the whim of your inspiration of the moment. No Country for Old Men tells the story of a welder (Josh Brolin) who stumbles upon a suitcase full of money and decides to take it. He is pursued by a killer (Javier Bardem), Mexicans, a policeman (Tommy Lee Jones), and a sort of detective (Woody Harrelson). The welder is inventive and cautious. We are with him. Unfortunately, the end of the film is a total botch. The confrontation between the protagonist and the killer comes too early. Part of the climax is elided. After that, we follow the secondary characters for twenty minutes. The killer gets hit by a car without us knowing why. And the cop tells an uninteresting dream. Watch the last half-hour of No Country for Old Men to get an idea of what to avoid. When adapting Cormac McCarthy's novel, the Coen brothers should have known that what can pass in literature does not necessarily work in dramaturgy.

Life goes on, with its share of questions

Maximizing viewer participation and resolving what you have set up does not mean that everything must be clear and wrapped up, as if life stopped at the end of your story. You can leave something in the shadows, a detail in suspense. In a way, you will thus leave the viewer with an accurate image of life and its mysteries.

I am aware of walking on eggshells with this suggestion. Because I don't find it desirable to end a story on a thick mystery, to leave the viewer to piece things together on their own, or to propose one thing and its opposite. In Broken Flowers, Don (Bill Murray) seeks to find out who sent him a letter announcing he has a son. He comes up empty. At the end, a teenager (Homer Murray) comes to see Don but the authors don't tell us if it's his son—the fact that the teenager is played by the actor Bill Murray's own son doesn't even clarify things for the few viewers who notice. In short, it's artistic posing in all its "splendor." The authors ask us to spend ninety minutes of our lives interested in their story and they don't even have the politeness of the authors of Citizen Kane or Waiting for Godot: the one that consists of enlightening the viewer, if not the protagonist, on the main curiosity posed by the story. Same principle (but less serious) at the very end of Lost in Translation. Bob (Bill Murray) finds Charlotte (Scarlett Johansson). She whispers something in his ear but the viewer is left out, not allowed to hear. In these two examples, we are dealing with uncertainties that are too heavy and too deliberate.

It's therefore a matter of balance. Ending a story on a promise can be quite elegant. At the end of City Lights, Chaplin doesn't tell us what will become of the tramp and the flower girl. Will they live together? Unlikely, given the characterization of Charlie. At the same time, it's hard to imagine the flower girl stopping there after what the protagonist has done for her. Similarly, at the end of Some Like It Hot, we don't know if Jerry (Jack Lemmon) will settle down with Osgood (Joe Brown). At the end of Série noire, Franck (Patrick Dewaere) tells Mona (Marie Trintignant) that everything is settled when it's exactly the opposite. He has lost everything. The story is resolved, thanks to the authors, and at the same time, the ending is open. Is Franck simply lying to Mona or is he lying to himself? Has he lost his mind? How will he and Mona survive? Franck's troubles are far from over. Has Bruno (Vittorio Gassman) learned a lesson at the end of The Easy Life? He seems shaken by what just happened but will he be any less of a boor?

These four examples are very enlightening. They operate on the same principle. First, what was set up is resolved. Second, life goes on for the characters. Third, it is primarily the viewer who has learned a lesson. Fourth, the resolution is so powerful, shocking, or moving that it was surely wise to end on it and close the curtain at that moment.

P. HAVE FUN AND... ENTERTAIN US

Inventing a story is a creative act and the place of creativity in humans is a state of the self that transactional analysts call the Free Child. It is also with the Free Child that we engage in three essential activities: playing, laughing, and making love. This shows how precious this part of ourselves is and deserves to be developed. Despite this, there is a downside to this state of the self that is the Free Child. In everyday life, there are many circumstances where it is not good to be in your Free Child. When someone asks you the time, there is often better to do than to answer: "The time it was yesterday at the same time, ha, ha, ha!" When skiing down a crowded slope at full speed, you risk not having fun for long, and neither will others. In other words, there are times to have fun and there are times to be responsible. It's a bit the same when writing. An author who only pleases themselves risks being the only one having fun in the end.

This for two reasons. First, because writing does not only bring pleasure, it is also difficult and even sometimes a suffering. Then because you have to consider the other. This last point is not bothersome...
so annoying just because you have to follow rules. Even though, for the Rebel Children that artists are, it's already unbearable. It's especially annoying because the rules are difficult to apply.

Sometimes you can please yourself and others. And sometimes the two are incompatible. In short, writing involves constraints. It's so true that some authors like Dorothy Parker have gone so far as to say: "I hate writing, I love having written."

"You shouldn't turn the job of a humorist into an idyllic paradise," said René Goscinny. "There are times when it's horribly hard." Pleasure is therefore not always a criterion by which you can tell if you're on the right track. That said, if writing is only suffering and depression, that's also a bad sign. So, savor the good moments and better yet: create them.

How to have fun and entertain others

In the activity of writing, there is a source of pleasure that satisfies both the author and the audience. I'm talking about rewriting. Especially when it gives the justified impression that you're making progress on your project. Personally, I find rewriting as enjoyable as writing. With each new draft, I not only feel that my work is improving but that I'm finally holding onto something. Until the next rewrite.

For the rest, I don't believe that having fun consists of adding more eggs to your basket. Flashbacks, subplots, secondary characters. Nor in putting the end at the beginning. Unless, of course, it makes sense. In other words, anything that involves gratuitous amusement risks leaving the audience cold.

Having fun mainly involves making the most of the few eggs you have and being inventive. Every time you find an idea that excites you and makes you think: "This will delight the audience, we can share this pleasure," you're on the right track. It could be a gag, a surprise, a payoff, an exploitation of dramatic irony, a simple dialogue. Often, it must be out of sync with what is generally expected.

At the beginning of Super témoin, a police inspector comes to get Marino (Ugo Tognazzi) to take him away. Marino is sleeping. The inspector shakes him. Half-awake, Marino thinks he's dealing with a girlfriend. He mumbles to the inspector to undress and lie down next to him. And the inspector responds most seriously: "I can't. I'm on duty." I can clearly see the screenwriters rejoicing when they found this dialogue.

At the beginning of Chacun cherche son chat, Chloé (Garance Clavel) entrusts her cat to a neighbor (Renée Le Calm) and leaves Paris to go on vacation by the sea. But it's not Chloé's vacation that interests Klapisch, it's what happens when she returns. So, he shows Chloé heading to the Gare de Lyon, then a very short shot of Chloé taking a bath in the Mediterranean, then Chloé moving away from the Gare de Lyon. Chloé's vacation lasts two seconds in the film! This little shot is a delightful wink, an original ellipsis, almost a gag. Cédric Klapisch told me he conceived it during writing but only fully enjoyed it during editing.

These two examples are very punctual, almost anecdotal. Sometimes happiness comes from a masterful payoff. In general, at those moments, I feel what I call a "screenwriter's emotion." And I'm convinced that the authors also felt an emotion when they found this payoff.

What to do to have fun and be more creative? Transactional analysts would answer: cultivate your Free Child. Writing workshops often provide another answer to this question. I have often noticed, indeed, that being able to consciously name the mechanisms of the narrative allows for better exploitation and thus stimulates creativity. This incidentally confirms that awareness of the rules does not hinder the spontaneity of creation.

Don't do the same thing twice

Be careful not to repeat yourself. This will force you to find even better ideas, to be more inventive. If, for example, your protagonist is a space sheriff who must get rid of three hitmen one after the other, find a different way for each bandit. Don't do what the author of Outland does, using the same method twice to neutralize a killer. In Pirates of the Caribbean: The Curse of the Black Pearl, secondary characters go to an island to try to break a curse. In vain. I can understand their motivations, but the stakes are low because they are not protagonists. When, thirty minutes later, the action brings us back to the same island to perform the same ritual, I feel like I'm going in circles.

CHERISH COMEDY

We have already talked about comedy at the foundational stage and, especially, at the scene-by-scene stage. In The Dramaturgy, I strive to demonstrate that comedy is a noble, fair, useful, and difficult treatment (see also pages 191-192). And I advocate for authors to write not pure comedies but works that mix both serious and humorous treatments.

This does not mean that there are only comedies to write. I really like Othello, Citizen Kane, and A Bright Sky, which are not exactly wildly funny. If you don't feel like writing a comedy, of course, don't do it. But you can at least adopt two attitudes: 1 - respect comedy, rather than associating yourself with those decision-makers, intellectuals, or religious people who disdain it, 2 - ask yourself if there aren't two or three moments in your project that could allow for a bit of comedy, to add relief to the narrative and make it closer to the complexity of life.

It's not just some intellectuals, all the dictators and fundamentalists on the planet who are afraid of humor. I've seen in my workshops authors full of goodwill who were uncomfortable with comedy. I've seen these authors laugh heartily at a comic suggestion made to them only to immediately exclaim: "Oh no, I can't do that! It goes too far." For a receiver, comedy often gives an impression of lightness, whereas for an author, it can rightly give the impression of being more cruel than serious treatment.

Good dispositions

One can discuss comedy, analyze a gag, explain that comedy stages human limitations, requires detachment, and works on the gap. But can one learn to be funny? I believe so, in part. In truth, it's exactly like everything else. Of course, you need initial aptitudes but also work and training. The following few tips logically stem from the theoretical reflections proposed in The Dramaturgy, particularly in its chapter 9.

1. First, dive in. You appreciate comedy as a spectator, you are convinced of its nobility, but you don't dare to try it. You tell yourself that there are clearly people who have a gift for humor and others who don't. You're not wrong. But there are as many senses of humor as there are individuals. Just because you don't have the genius of Molière or Chaplin, the elegance of Lubitsch, or the humanity of Italian comedy screenwriters doesn't mean you have no sense of humor. A sense of humor can be explored, sought, cultivated. And then it's by writing that one becomes a blacksmith.

I will remember all my life this writing workshop where I asked participants to write a scene of dramatic irony. A screenwriter over fifty came up with a very funny scene that amused everyone. We were in tears. So was she. But not for the same reasons. She confessed to us that it was the first time in her life that she did comedy, that she made others laugh through writing. She found it magical.

2. Be the first to laugh at what you write. Of course, this is not a sufficient condition - you must even be cautious - but it is a necessary condition.

3. Don't confuse cheerful and comic. It's not by staging characters who have fun or party that you create comedy. Lightness, perhaps, but not comedy. In other words, comedy gives an impression of lightness, but it is neither lightness nor gaiety that produces comedy. On the contrary, to be funny, you have to load the boat.

4. Don't confuse zany and comic. Some authors imagine that by inventing quirky situations, characters, or accessories, they will make people laugh. I think it's more of an evasion. Since they can't be both accurate and funny, they go into delusions. At best, it becomes intellectual humor - the kind that just makes the brain smile. At worst, it's silly. In any case, it's far from character comedy and situational comedy.

5. Cultivate your Free Child. By playing, laughing, making love, entering your emotions. And by letting out a loud fart in the direction of all the gloomy people.

Take one of the peaks of nonsense, Monty Python and the Holy Grail, and ask around what the best gags of the film are. I bet that the bad faith of the Black Knight, the coconuts imitating the horses' steps, the deciphering of the graffiti ("The Grail is in the castle of aaaaaarrgh..."), the difficulties of the King of the Swamp (Michael Palin) to make himself understood by his guards, the stupidity of Sir Bedevere (Terry Jones) when he applies the Trojan horse principle will be mentioned more often than the rabbit that slaughters three knights or the police that burst into the Middle Ages. Because these gags (the first ones mentioned) have a human base, they are not totally gratuitous.

BE PATIENT AND TENACIOUS

"Hasten slowly and, without losing courage, twenty times on the loom put your work. Polish it ceaselessly and repolish it; sometimes add, and often erase." The precious advice of Nicolas Boileau applies not only to the dialogue continuity, which often receives the most care. Authors spend a lot of time polishing their dialogues because it's the visible part of the iceberg (including in theater) and because it's the easiest to do. Besides dialogues, they could work on the sets, sounds, colors, costumes, and accessories, giving them more meaning. But, above all, the initial intention, the pitch, the foundations, the milestones, the scene-by-scene, and the treatment should also be put on the loom twenty times and undergo multiple drafts before moving on to the next stage.

A good work of fiction is a rich and complex product, almost impossible to master in one go. It requires a lot of rigor and maturation. It's a long-term job. Anyone can fill a hundred pages of dialogue in three months. With very few exceptions, the result is neither done nor to be done. I think there are no bad stories, only unfinished stories. This aligns with the idea that the perfect story is indeed in the author's unconscious but that they often struggle to fully bring it to life. The misfortune of so many stories is that they are put into production too early. They are not sufficiently refined.

In his memoirs, Frank Capra writes: "Do the scenes in a film come out of a tap like running water? No. They are created. By a talent. Yes, an extraordinary talent. But they are also the result of writing and rewriting, effort and discouragement; they are the fruit of the endurance and energy of a decathlon champion. The script of Mr. Smith Goes to Washington was worthy of a gold medal for Sidney Buchman." Authors are marathoners, not sprinters.

The anxiety of the blank page...

What to do if you're gripped by the famous anxiety of the blank page? A first reflex is to leave the place where you usually write. Your desk should remain associated with the pleasure of writing. Only sit there when you're sure to write. Then, make sure it's indeed a psychological block. Writing sometimes means... not writing. A story needs maturation. I'll come back to this later. Then, if it is indeed a block, accept it. The anxiety of the blank page is normal, it happens to everyone. Writing provides moments of joy but also moments of doubt and discomfort. We've talked about this earlier. That's why you should write, even if you think it's bad.

The anxiety of the blank page is sometimes due to the author projecting themselves onto step x + 1 when step x is not finished. Thus, in my workshops, I regularly encounter authors who are afraid to start, either because they are intimidated by the magnitude of the task or because they are perfectionists. In the first case, they don't understand that the best way to climb Everest is not to focus on the summit but to start from the bottom and put one foot in front of the other. In the second case, they can't stand that the first version of the first word of the first scene is imperfect. I explain to them that there's a good chance they'll miss the first draft but that the rewrite will be brilliant. Keep in mind that the very first drafts of North by Northwest and Some Like It Hot were missed. And that rewriting is much less blocking than writing.

Another trick is to switch to another project, to write something else. Another trick is to stop writing while you still have material in your head. This way, when you get back to it later, you'll have something to start with without even thinking. Another trick is to make a list of what cannot happen in your story. By contrast, you might find what will work for your project.

You can also try altered states of consciousness. There are a thousand and one ways to reach such states. In the less dangerous genre than alcohol or cannabis and less polluting than driving, there are: walking, jogging, DIY, gardening, yoga, music, sophrology, meditation, rebirthing, self-hypnosis, or holotropic breathing. You can also meditate on a koan.

Finally, having your work read (or reading it to others) is a good way to overcome the anxiety of the blank page. Because reading reports are often mines of stimulating rewrites. I'll come back to this later.

And if none of this works, you can decide not to write anything at all for a long enough period of time. If you manage to play the game, you will have at least avoided the guilt of blocking. And if you can't help but find ideas and write a few lines, well... the page will no longer be blank.

You may find yourself not formally blocked but fundamentally blocked. You know that a certain element doesn't work, but you can't find anything better. No matter how much you write and write, find a thousand ideas, nothing works. Here are some tricks.

1. Spend time understanding what doesn't work and in which direction you should look. You know the proverb: a problem well stated is half solved. Is it a characterization problem? And if so, is it a coherence problem or rather a lack of characterization? Is it an exploitation problem? Is it a clarity problem? In short, be as specific as possible about what is wrong.

Personally, I find it very useful to write my questions in black and white. "How can So-and-so discover this or that?" "What is So-and-so's ghost?" "Does So-and-so's transformation occur before or after they reach their goal?" As if writing down precise questions on paper allowed me to free my mind and open the way to solutions. Moreover, often, when I write the questions, several possible answers follow - which I also write down - and (what seems to me) the right answer soon emerges.

2. Go to the theater or cinema, travel through the repertoire. Read or watch the best works of Shakespeare, Molière, Labiche, Ibsen, Chekhov, Shaw, Chaplin, Brecht, Lubitsch, Hitchcock, Pagnol, Wilder, Age and Scarpelli, Goscinny, Charlier, Veber, Taniguchi. They are all brilliant craftsmen and formidable teachers of dramaturgy. Our peers often help us bounce back and find solutions. You can also find solutions in works that touch you personally, even if they are not well constructed.

3. In the same spirit, you can read or reread a good treatise on dramaturgy. Yes, yes! it sometimes gives ideas.

4. Enter an altered state of consciousness. I mentioned it earlier. It's a technique that can make you want to write but can also help you find solutions.

... and procrastination

A difficulty close to that of the blank page is putting off advancing your project until tomorrow. This is particularly noticeable when writing a project without a contract, therefore without deadlines. I have a very effective trick to counter procrastination, which is to create an artificial deadline. I call a reader friend and ask when they can read my project. Often, they give me a date corresponding to their current schedule, for example, an upcoming trip or a particular weekend. I then immediately find the energy to dive into writing to be ready on time.

At the same time...

Putting twenty times on the loom also means giving yourself time, a lot of time. Of course, it's impossible to give a number, but including maturation, writing a dramatic work should take a year and a half, sometimes two or three years. It's a truth too often ignored, even by authors. Challenges like screenplay marathons, where participants are asked to write a screenplay in three days - as if dramaturgy were a fairground act - maintain this idea that improvisation and spontaneous writing can yield results in dramaturgy. There is nothing more dangerous. Dramaturgy is neither free jazz nor lyrical abstraction. Of all the arts, it is perhaps the one that requires the most time and rigor. How else to explain that painters and musicians produce hundreds, often thousands of works in their lifetime, while playwrights create only a few dozen? At the end of his life, Goethe was still rewriting passages of his Faust, started sixty years earlier. Between 1979 and 1998, Peter Shaffer rewrote the final confrontation between Mozart and Salieri six times for Amadeus. Consider also Chaplin, who shot and reshot a crucial scene of City Lights for months, thus rewriting the script on set.

Decision-makers, too, should be aware of this need for time and show more patience, demand, and generosity towards authors. Like Darryl Zanuck, whose dialogue with Ernst Lubitsch (at the time of Heaven Can Wait) Billy Wilder reports: "How's the work going, Ernst?" "Frankly, slowly but well," "I'm glad. The only thing I would have preferred to hear you say is: 'Very slowly but very well.'"

Finally, note that Boileau not only suggests multiple rewrites. He also invites us not to be discouraged. He's right. An author, even a seasoned one, who starts a personal story is always very enthusiastic, and then soon, the excitement of the start fades and gives way to discouragement, if not self-flagellation. On this subject, Frantisek Daniel had a little trick. When you have a film idea, write down your excitement on a piece of paper. You boast about it, you write why it's going to be a masterpiece. Then you put the paper away in a corner. And when you're discouraged, you take it out. I think perseverance is the most important quality to possess in this profession, far ahead of connections, luck, or even... competence. Perseverance is nourished by patience, courage, and self-confidence. At the beginning of Misery, Paul Sheldon (James Caan) puts the final touches on a novel. Then he pours himself a good glass of champagne and savors the moment. His novel is not yet published, but mentally, Paul has already put himself on the New York Times bestseller list.

Chekhov, who regularly read and corrected his colleagues' plays - Chekhov, a pioneer of script doctoring! - wrote to Gorky in 1901, lines that address all authors: "Please, finish your play. You feel that it doesn't take the desired turn, but don't listen to your feelings, they are misleading. It's normal not to like a play while writing it. It's only after that you end up appreciating it."

The old remains

In polishing and repolishing, you must be careful of what could be called "old remains." From one draft to another, you will make changes. But when you modify an element, you rarely take the time to check that everything else is still coherent with what has been modified and respects the principle of necessity. Especially since you get used to the presence of certain scenes or characters. And one day, sometimes a few drafts later, you wonder what purpose a certain element serves and realize you're dealing with an old remain.

This is not an anecdotal issue because between rewrites and cuts, an author can write three or four times more than what will end up in the finished work. It's a bit like the rushes of a shoot: there is always more material than in the final product. Because writing is also searching. You write a lot, you keep little. Richard
Curtis [23] calculated that "for each day worked on Notting Hill, twelve seconds of writing remained," at a rate of seven hours per day. That's why there are often many differences between the first and last draft. Unless the latter is full of old remnants.

Writing is rewriting — Once you have a first draft, go back and cut the beginning of the scenes. It's difficult to write spontaneously by starting a scene in the thick of things. We often need to start it gently. Knock! Knock! "Hey, hello! Come in.", "Am I disturbing you?", "No, no. Would you like something?", "Yes, a cognac. I really need it", "What's going on?", "Oh...", "No, tell me!", "... Martine left with the kids", "What?!" When reworking the first draft, start the scene with "What?!" and remove everything that precedes it. This is what Roddy Doyle does in the inciting incident of The Snapper. He begins with Dessie (Colm Meaney) saying to his daughter (Tina Kellegher): "You're what?!", "You heard me", "Good God, you're pregnant!?" This is what Horace calls "In medias res" in his Art of Poetry [43], "in the middle of things."

Another rewriting technique: put yourself in the shoes of Columbo (Peter Falk in Columbo) and reread the text with a suspicious and ruthless eye. One of the famous inspector's specialties is to put himself in the place of others (especially suspects) and spot what seems to be psychological inconsistencies. "If I had just found my partner dead, I would never think of opening my mail" (episode 1-1). "How come he didn't close the business for a few days, out of respect for his deceased brother?" (episode 3-2). "Who could have made that call, heard the victim die on the other end of the line, and not reported it to the police?" (episode 7-4). Columbo is like a normal reader/spectator who balks at anything that isn't smooth.

Once the dialogue continuity exists, don't hesitate to return to a higher scale, the scene-by-scene for example, before returning to the dialogue which represents the finest scale of the work. Dialogue continuity often reveals structural or characterization problems that can only be resolved by working on the foundations or the scene-by-scene. Back and forth between the different levels are sometimes necessary.

Reworking also involves having your different drafts read by various people or telling the story to others when the opportunity arises. It's an excellent exercise. Marcel Pagnol reports that he managed to solve his structural problems on César by telling his script to an old dying lady, too dying to wait for the film to be finished. This brings us to the next precept ("Make it read").

An incredibly fruitful trick

One day, a producer hires me to help write a subject close to his heart. I comply and provide a script. The producer reads it and tells me: "The structure is perfect but the dialogues need to be revised." He decides to entrust the task to someone else. Before handing the script to the dialogist, I review the text and add more than a hundred and fifty notes to explain to the dialogist the interest of each scene, each moment, each dialogue. I tell him why such an element is important, I explain what the characters feel. And, by doing this, I realize that some scenes don't work, that some emotional moments are missing, that some elements are unjustifiable, which leads me to correct the script.

Imagine that you are addressing a co-writer, a producer, an actor, a director. Explain to him the interest of each little moment of the text, why such a character does this or that.

This technique is productive in more than one way. First, it forces you to put yourself in the place of each character to justify their slightest actions. Then, it forces you to make each scene, each moment, each dialogue useful and necessary. And then it will push you to know all the backstage of your story and to weave the story of each of your characters, which will prove invaluable for the actors who will take on the roles.

S. MAKE IT READ

At first glance, this does not fall under the activity of an author and yet it is a crucial precept. Make your stories read, tell them, get as much feedback as possible. On all readable stages: the pitch, the foundations, the synopsis, the treatment or the dialogue continuity (but not the scene-by-scene, too synthetic). This activity is so important that I devote the next chapter to it.

T. LET IT REST

This book would be incomplete if it did not mention a common co-author to all storytellers: time. Not the amount of time necessary for the creation of a story that we talked about earlier. I mean here the time that passes. Or the time spent doing something else. It's a formidable ally. In the short term when, instead of struggling with the blank page, you let go and let the right hemisphere do the work. And in the long term. Because a story matures without being touched, or rather it is we who, by gaining perspective, mature in relation to it. Thus, an author can very well write several works at once, move from one to the other, forget one while working on another. Or even have other occupations. It is a specificity and an advantage of our profession.

13. MAKE IT READ

"You have thought about it for so long, written it so many times, in your head or on paper, that you start to be groggy, stupid, dry."
(William Goldman [34])
"I finished my drama, I would have liked to have it read to you."
(Maxime Gorki to Anton Chekhov [36])
"Every effort deserves a reward."
(a well-known but little-applied proverb)

All authors lack perspective on their work. One could broaden: all human beings lack perspective on themselves. It's related but well, let's stay in the realm of writing. Even if you write with others, there comes a time when you no longer have any freshness on your project, when you no longer see clearly. There is a simple remedy for this dangerous lack: get an outside perspective. I already mentioned it in the previous chapter but I will never recommend it enough: make your work read. I know it is scary. Many authors do not want their beautiful edifice to be questioned. Or that it be formatted. Or that their dear thought be damaged. Most of the time, these are irrational fears because quality script doctoring is benevolent and has only one goal: to help the author better translate their thought. In other words, to help the author be the artist through effective craftsmanship. But it must be recognized that, even when feedback is benevolent and professional, it is often a painful experience for authors. It is also a delicate practice because it is not easy to find a competent reader. And yet, it is in-dis-pen-sa-ble.

N.B. This chapter is also addressed to producers who do development, that is to say, who initiate or follow the writing of a story. Because they too end up lacking perspective on a text they have read, reread, and re-read.

To whom should you make it read?

The first difficulty is finding a competent reader, and even better a competent script doctor. Reading a dramatic text requires skills that not everyone has. On this subject, I refer you to Evaluate a Script [50] which, despite its title, may interest theater or even novel readers.

Do not make a decision-maker (producer, actor, etc.) read to get an opinion on the text. Of course, there is a chance that your project will one day be read by several decision-makers. I regret to say that you should expect nothing from them if you are looking for help to rewrite and make progress on your craft. For a simple (and legitimate) reason: they do not read with this goal. They read to know if your project can fit into their editorial program, their mood of the moment, their career plan, their desire for a role, their crushes, etc. In short, they have other motivations than a pure script doctor.

And, unfortunately, in addition, they very rarely have script doctor skills. In other words, whether or not they want your project, they will not be able to help you rewrite it. To be convinced, just present a project to the Advance on Receipts and, when the result of what is nothing more than a lottery is negative, request the reading reports. You will get everything and its opposite.

You need to make it read by people who agree to play the role of script doctors and who have no momentary power of life or death over your project. If, in addition, you want them to be competent - it's better - you can start now to build a network of privileged readers. Because it takes years. In literature, they are often called "beta-readers." Some internet collectives (like CoCyclics or Wattpad) offer this service to their members. But there too, some are more pointed than others.

The right profile

Some think it is better to avoid making it read by someone who knows and appreciates you. In truth, you should mainly avoid inauthentic readers. If you think the reader will not be neutral, that they will be waiting for you around the corner - whether for good or bad -, find another one.

Woody Allen and Douglas McGrath demonstrated in Bullets Over Broadway that a gangster (Chazz Palminteri) could be an excellent script doctor. Granted, the film is fiction, but what it says is true. The natural qualities of a good script doctor are not necessarily found among professionals in the microcosm.

In short, I think there is no good profile. You have to test the readers.

MAKE IT READ

What salary?

A beta-reader or a script doctor must be compensated. Not necessarily in hard cash. The evaluation can be done in the context of an exchange. Someone reads your project, you read theirs. If your beta-reader is a friend or a family member, the compensation will be in esteem, loyalty, love, curiosity, etc. But, for me, it is clear that the beta-reader must find their account in one way or another. Otherwise, they will do it reluctantly, therefore poorly. If you set out to find a competent professional script doctor, plan to leave part of your savings. Do not insult them by asking for a free reading.

A rewriting workshop

Another avenue can be very fruitful: the rewriting workshop, like Grand Nord or Plume & Pellicule (from the Dreamago association). Receiving several reading reports over a few days allows for complementary feedback and often also to spot trends. I return to the interest of writing or rewriting workshops at the beginning of the next chapter.

You are the measure

It is not easy to receive a reading report. Except, of course, if it is only positive. But this is very rarely the case. It is not easy because it is always a questioning, I mentioned it above. But it is also difficult because the feedback is not 100% accurate. Contrary to what some claim, the reader is not always right on the whole line. You have to sort the wheat from the chaff. Of course, the risk is to reject what bothers you, because it is too painful to admit, while it would be useful for your project. Sorting is a form of choice. There are also signs that do not deceive. If you clearly feel which criticism is relevant, which suggestion will be beneficial, it means you are beginning to master the coherence of your project. What to do then?

First, listen to your reader without justifying or defending yourself. Then, make a clear distinction between your reader's feelings (the symptom), their analysis (the diagnosis), and the solutions (the prescription) they may propose. If they are too much into prescription, refocus them, ask them to primarily convey what they felt. Possibly, by asking them specific questions. Did you understand this? What do you feel at this moment? Did you turn the pages? Etc. Then, take the time to digest, to decant. Do not rush to rewrite. Let your unconscious do the sorting. Then, try to distinguish between subjective and objective feedback. In general, subjective feedback is related to your reader's tastes and colors. Keep only what resonates with you, what seems right for your project. Objective feedback, on the other hand, concerns the craftsmanship of the project, therefore the universal and timeless tools of storytelling. It is, of course, the objective feedback that will be most useful to you. They are the ones that will help you better convey your thought. If, in addition, they are brought with benevolence, you will have material to keep reworking your piece.

14. SCALES

"At that time, in 1934, the companies had a very large number of screenwriters under contract. Every Thursday, we had to submit eleven pages of script. We worked on two, three, four films at the same time. We were made to move from one film to another, we were made to rewrite the scripts of others."
(Billy Wilder [94])

If you are a beginner, before embarking on writing a long work, I recommend practicing on scenes or short stories. This can be solo or in a group, within a workshop.

The stimulation of the workshop

Submitting your work very regularly to colleagues within a workshop is an extremely stimulating way to master the tools of storytelling. Basically, the principle is as follows - of course, it can be adapted. Half a dozen authors decide to meet for a few hours, once a week. On this occasion, each submits to the others the fruit of their work. This can consist of reading a scene or discussing a character, the choice of climax, the nature of internal obstacles, etc. The goal of the game being, either to do scales (cf. suggestions below), or to develop, each, a personal project. I indeed discourage collective writing. First, because you learn less by writing with others. Then because a workshop greatly helps the author to work. It is already, in itself, a semi-collective writing.

This system is very fruitful for several reasons. First, an author always lacks perspective on what they write. An outside eye often allows not to get lost, to focus their energy on the real questions. Then, it allows having a first audience. You can immediately know if an idea works, if a scene is funny, if a development is clear. When four or five people of different sex, age, culture, and religion make the same remark, the author knows right away that it is not a matter of taste, it is their work that needs to be reviewed. That is why this kind of workshop does not require the presence of a teacher. Certainly, a good facilitator will not just point out what does not work, they will say what needs to be done to make it work. But six motivated authors, who have a common language, can manage on their own. Reading a scene in front of an audience is also excellent training for any author. Because it forces them to act a little and to feel each role. And then, of course, a workshop is a formidable melting pot. It produces a lot of ideas. As long as its members make the effort to respect each other's thoughts and universe and stick to the craftsmanship aspect of writing, it can yield excellent results.

Respect for others and benevolence are essential conditions for the proper functioning of a workshop. On the right attitude to adopt within a writing workshop, I refer you to Evaluate a Script [50]. The rules proposed there are addressed to decision-makers and script doctors, whether they are professional script doctors or author-script doctors within a workshop.

Examples of scales

A good trick to start this type of workshop is to do exercises. Once a week, each author comes to read a scene written from precise dramatic constraints, and decided by mutual agreement the previous week. This allows workshop members to acquire a common language. The following list gives ideas for constraints. Of course, it is not exhaustive. You are free to invent others.

The first type of exercise consists of writing a scene containing:
- conflict. It's the ABC. It allows realizing how useful conflict is. The constraint can be made more difficult by requiring that the conflict not be too externalized, too obvious. Avoid, for example, two people arguing;
- a protagonist-objective-obstacles trio. To play the game, you must clearly choose a protagonist, their objective, and the nature of their obstacles before writing a single word;
- urgency;
- a characterization trait. Here too, to make the most of the exercise, it is necessary to play the game. That is to say, you start by deciding on the characterization trait you want to show and only then do you look for the means to show it;
- a crescendo, an evolution, and a climax. Not easy, especially to build the crescendo;
- preparation. With preparation and payoff in the same scene. The payoff can be a gesture or a dialogue;
- milking;
- dramatic irony. Of course, the most interesting is to write an exploitation scene, possibly a resolution. The installation of dramatic irony is not necessary. If it took place before the beginning of the scene, a short warning to the audience is enough;
- a comic shift;
- exposition. Playing the game of this exercise consists of first deciding on the content of the exposition. There must be a good amount, a story for example. Only then do you look for the best way to convey this exposition. Avoid the interrogation during which an individual recites their CV or tells their life story;
- a significant piece of activity;
- no dialogue. And yet, the scene must be clear and tell a story (beginning, middle, end). One of the exercises required by the Hamburg Film School from its students is to make a short film of five minutes maximum, pairing screenwriter and director, presenting a central conflict and containing no dialogue. The instruction is superb and has given rise to excellent films;
- allusive, ironic, or stupid dialogues.

All these exercises allow becoming familiar with dramatic mechanisms. To train in the syntactic work from one scene to another, you can tackle designing the foundations of a short story (cf. below). This allows working on the structure (cross arcs, dramatic knots, sequences, preparation).

The total

Another exercise consists of writing a combination of most of the aforementioned constraints. That is to say, a scene containing the protagonist-objective-obstacles trio, the cross arcs, a climax, conflict, emotion, suspense, a crescendo, evolution, comedy elements, beautiful characterization, unity of action, payoffs, milking, dramatic irony, surprise, at least one beautiful piece of activity, and good dialogues. It's possible even if it's difficult. You can possibly take up one of the scenes written for a previous exercise and refine it. I call this type of scene a "cocale..."

One might wonder if there are any in the repertoire. After all, a scene does not need to be total to exist, be beautiful, and serve the action. Without searching for long, I found two - including one in a French film!

It's the famous slap scene from Série noire, which happens to be the film's climax. (N.B. Warning, the following reveals important elements of the film.) Franck Poupard (Patrick Dewaere) has murdered two people to steal a stash. His boss, Staplin (Bernard Blier), suspects something. Franck has just made a third victim, his wife (Myriam Boyer), when dring! Staplin arrives at Franck's home. The latter lets him in, a bit stunned. Staplin sits in Franck's devastated living room: "It's really cozy here, the real Louis XV living room." Then Staplin gets down to business: "I want everything, Franck. Everything you've collected, thanks to two murders." We then enter the second act. Franck's objective is clear: keep the stolen money. Will Franck get away with it? Suspense... The obstacles follow one another. Franck first tries to make Staplin believe there is no money, that it's a "terrible love story." "Love of money!" replies Staplin who is not fooled. Franck then claims there is very little money, that it's hidden in a wasteland. He proposes to Staplin to go get it. We know Franck is lying (dramatic irony): the money is in a suitcase right next to the two men. Staplin then warns Franck: he left a letter with a friend. If Staplin disappears, the friend will give the letter to the police. Franck is stuck. He gets up, approaches Staplin, and suddenly delivers a violent punch to the stomach. Staplin takes it: "Hit... Hit if it makes you happy. To each their own. But don't forget the letter... Where's the money?" Franck shows him the suitcase.

Staplin takes everything. Franck changes his objective, he asks Staplin to leave him some money, to get by. Staplin refuses. Franck slaps him. Staplin continues to refuse and then launches this famous dialogue: "It's not that I'm bored but my cheeks are burning." And he leaves, leaving Franck defeated (third act).

The second total is extracted from One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest. We are in the middle of the film. The nine least affected patients of the ward are gathered in a therapy group led by Miss Ratched (Louise Fletcher). They are discussing Billy's case (Brad Dourif) and McMurphy (Jack Nicholson) is bored stiff. Billy has trouble expressing himself. Suddenly, Cheswick (Sydney Lassick) intervenes: "What if we talked about something else?" Miss Ratched reminds them that they are discussing
therapy. Cheswick expresses the desire to watch the baseball game McMurphy had mentioned earlier. Cheswick has never seen baseball; it might be good therapy. McMurphy then takes over to request a new vote on schedule changes. A majority of yes votes would allow him to watch his game. At this point in the scene, McMurphy's objective is announced—in fact, it's the same objective as the sequence this scene is part of. So we enter the second act. Will McMurphy manage to watch his baseball game? Suspense... Miss Ratched agrees to the principle of a new vote. One by one, the nine group members raise their hands. McMurphy triumphs. But Miss Ratched presents an obstacle: "There are eighteen of you in this ward. You need a majority to change the rule." McMurphy tries to get one more vote from the other patients in the ward. In vain. Miss Ratched declares the meeting over. McMurphy asks for one more minute and addresses Chief (Will Sampson), who doesn't seem to understand what's being asked of him. Faced with his silence, McMurphy gives up. Dramatic turn, Chief slowly raises his hand. McMurphy notices, bursts with joy, and asks Miss Ratched to turn on the television. At the same moment, a nurse leads the other eight into the washroom. A final discussion ensues between Miss Ratched and McMurphy—it's the climax of the scene. The head nurse reminds him that the vote and meeting were closed. McMurphy gets angry, demands the television be turned on but gets nothing. He abandons his goal and sits down, sullen, in front of the turned-off television. End of the second act. He broods. Suddenly (third act twist), his face lights up. McMurphy starts to comment on a baseball game as if he were watching one on the screen. The other asylum inmates stop their activities and come to see what's happening. Initially stunned to see their comrade getting excited in front of a blank screen, they are soon caught up in his enthusiasm. And soon, all the patients are shouting in front of the turned-off TV, as if they were really watching a baseball game.

This piece of activity, one of the most beautiful in the repertoire, is all at once: it's a masterful characterization trait; it's milking; it's funny; it's a big dramatic turn; it's finally the payoff of everything that preceded, especially the intense frustration experienced by McMurphy and shared by the viewer. This piece of activity is almost complete in itself.

Of course, the total is not an end in itself for a dramatic author. You can write a beautiful work without a single total. But if you conceive one, you have the right to be delighted. First, because it's rare and difficult. Then, because it's always an extremely effective scene, necessarily effective. And even more than that: totals are often classics of the repertoire. Think of the balcony scene in Cyrano de Bergerac, the boxing match in City Lights, the forgiveness scene in The Baker's Wife. These are totals (or quasi-totals). And they are moving human adventures.

The second type of exercise involves taking an existing scene, in your own work or in the repertoire, and rewriting it:
- by simply improving it. Don't hesitate to touch "untouchable" works. There's always a scene that's a bit off;
- by taking another character from the scene as the protagonist (but without changing the nature of the conflict). André Cayatte, Maurice Aubergé, and Louis Sapin did this with an entire film: La vie conjugale, which tells the story of a couple (Marie-José Nat and Jacques Charrier). Two versions were released simultaneously in 1964: Françoise and Jean-Marc, each with one of the spouses as the protagonist. It's also somewhat the approach of Tom Stoppard in Rosencrantz and Guildenstern Are Dead, which evokes the tragedy of Hamlet through the point of view of two secondary characters. Or that of Laetitia Colombani who, in the same film (He Loves Me... He Loves Me Not), offers two narratives of the same story by changing the protagonist halfway through;
- by making it funny. The chosen scene must be serious, of course. This exercise is great for grasping the mechanics of comedy and the difference between the two treatments of the same subject;
- by keeping the beginning and changing the end. Several variants. You can leave the first act intact and change everything else, or keep what you can from the beginning to reverse the dramatic response, or completely deviate;
- by changing the characterization of the protagonist without changing their objective;
- by changing other elements (the place, the time, etc.);
- by diverting it, taking the opposite approach. For example, you take a meeting scene in a deserted alley at night, as seen a thousand times in film noir, and transpose it to a deserted and sunny countryside (cf. North by Northwest);
- by shortening it to the extreme, without losing its meaning;
- by replacing certain dialogues with activity, without changing the meaning.

If you try these transformation exercises, you might be surprised at how much they lead you towards comedy. But the exercise will be all the more beneficial if you don't fall into pure parody. It's nobler to mock life than to mock the work of others.

All these exercises are far from gratuitous. First, they are scales. Scales are as necessary for the dramatist as for the musician. That's why you shouldn't be afraid to "debase" your talent by doing commercial work. You learn a lot, for example, by writing one or two sitcom episodes. Then, remember that an author creates nothing, they recreate, even if unconsciously. Working on existing scenes is fundamentally the same as creating "original" scenes from "nothing."

... and remake Most of the great classics of the repertoire (Oedipus Rex, Don Juan, Faust, etc.) are remakes. Shakespeare, for example, did not invent his subjects. Most of his plays are inspired by Plutarch or Holinshed's abundant chronicles. Hamlet would be a remake of Thomas Kyd's Hamlet, itself inspired by Saxo Grammaticus's tales (not to mention the character of Electra, of which Hamlet is a male version). The same goes for Molière, who drew heavily from Plautus, Spanish theater, or commedia dell'arte. In Observations on Comedy and the Genius of Molière [69], Luigi Riccoboni notes that "you won't find in the entire comedy of The Miser four scenes invented by Molière." Citizen Kane resembles a film made eight years earlier: Thomas Garner (including the use of flashbacks). Hergé borrowed a lot from his predecessors, from novels, theater, and cinema. Some passages, sets, and props from Destination Moon are taken from the film Destination... Moon! In Waiting for Godot, this "revolutionary" work resembles a play by Lord Dunsany, The Glittering Gate, dating from 1909, in which two tramps wait at the gates of Paradise. Dunsany is of Irish origin (like Beckett), and his play was performed at the famous Abbey Theatre in Dublin, where it is very likely Beckett saw it. All this to say that it is not the ideas, themes, or subjects that matter but how they are exploited.

Ideas matter even less because everything has already been written. There is not an infinite number of possible objectives. What changes is the framework given to these objectives and the perspective each author will bring to an already treated subject. We return to Jean-Marie Roch's words [70]: "Everything has already been written but not yet by you."

The preceding examples also confirm that a remake is not necessarily worse than its original, as is often claimed. "Same story as So-and-So's film but without the freshness of the original" has become a big cliché of critical commentary. Many examples contradict this. Apart from The Miser (remake of The Pot of Gold) and Hamlet, we can cite the following remakes that are clearly better than their original work: Some Like It Hot, The Chorus, His Girl Friday, The Man Who Knew Too Much, The Thing, True Lies, A Star Is Born, Victor Victoria. Or even My Father the Hero. The remake of My Father the Hero exploits more and better the dramatic irony of the film: we know that André (Gérard Depardieu) is not the lover but the father of Véronique (Marie Gillain)/Nicole (Katherine Heigl), many people are unaware. Thus, in the remake, André experiences more conflict.

Personal, necessarily personal
These transformation and remake exercises can have extensions. Since there is no thematic constraint but only technical, each author writes totally personal texts. It sometimes happens that an exercise scene makes you want to go further, to explore a character, for example. This can lead to a short film or a play. Moreover, an isolated scene can, one day, fit perfectly into a long narrative that initially had no connection with it.

CONCLUSION

"I like plots. I'm like everyone else, I need that. If I still go to see American films, (...) it's because they still have a little bit of their good plots."
(Jean-Luc Godard [32])
If, thanks to this book, you have progressed on your story, whatever the medium it is intended for, thank you and congratulations.
Thank you for trusting me. I hope I have been helpful.
Thank you also for wanting to play Scheherazade. In France, we lack good storytellers. It's not in our cultural DNA, as it is for the Anglo-Saxons. It's unfortunate because storytelling is a powerful cultural vector—when will the political personnel of our country finally understand this? If I enjoy listening to American storytellers, I would also like other countries, other cultures, to tell me lots of good plots. When I discovered Departures, The King of Masks, The Lives of Others, I was doubly delighted. I had just had an excellent time and, moreover, I had just discovered cultural elements I didn't know. On the occasion of the Chinese translation of Dramaturgy [51], out of courtesy, I wanted to better understand Chinese cinema. I watched about fifty films. It was a fascinating cultural journey.
Congratulations, because you are comfortable with the principle of rules.
Congratulations, because you have the humility to think that you have not been touched by grace and that talent is worked on. Here too, on the European continent, there is progress to be made on this subject!
Congratulations, because completing a story is a difficult adventure. Exciting but difficult. Now remains the task of getting your project published, in one form or another. But even if your story will never be embodied, it already exists, it is a work in itself and it has made you grow. This is another interest in putting the stories we have in our heads in black and white. They help us better understand ourselves and move forward on the path of life.
You are no longer the same, if only as a storyteller. If your project never sees the light of day, it will at least have allowed you to better write the next one. In other words, while you have your story read, rewrite it, seek producers or publishers, you can start a new project. By returning, or not, to the beginning of this book...

GLOSSARY

As specified in the preface (page 11), the language of dramaturgy is not fully codified.
