Even if you will find (in parentheses) the English translation of certain notions, the definitions that follow correspond to the concepts developed in this work as well as in Dramaturgy [51] and do not necessarily coincide with those of other theorists or those of specialized dictionaries. Moreover, I remind you that remakes, happy endings, cliffhangers, milking, sequels, script doctoring, etc., were not invented by Hollywood. They have existed in theater for twenty-five centuries.
Italicized terms correspond to glossary entries. Bold numbers refer to major definitions and developments in the book. Bold underlined numbers refer to entire chapters.
Hook (cliffhanger): unresolved dramatic knot, placed at the end of a work or part of a work (weekly comic strip, serial episode, logistical act in theater) and intended to arouse in the viewer the desire to know the rest. The hook is a form of announcement. 72, 91, 282, 295
Act, or dramatic act: one of the three narrative parts of a dramatic unit (scene, sequence, episode, work). Defined in relation to the protagonist's objective. Not to be confused with the logistical act. Cf. also Second act, First act, Three-act structure, Third act. 15, 19, 263
Logistical act: important part of a play, responding to theoretical or practical considerations (change of time or setting, or renewal of candles). Notion of act. Hamlet and Cyrano de Bergerac are plays in 5 logistical acts but in 3 dramatic acts. 19, 98, 175, 176, 210, 294, 306
Action: set of attempts by a protagonist to achieve their objective (general or local). In general, action coincides with the second act, except when the unity of action is not respected or when action is relaunched by a dramatic turn at the beginning of the third act (enriched structure). 13, 73, 74, 84, 146, 162, 165, 170, 176, 209, 267, 279, 315, 318
Activity: meaningful gesture or set of meaningful gestures, including the actor's performance. The word "act" might be more appropriate but it would cause confusion with the dramatic act. As for the word "gesture," it is a bit restrictive in its common sense: it does not encompass the actor's performance. Silence can be activity. 17, 245, 246, 247, 249, 265, 267, 293, 305, 316, 341, 343
Adaptation: operation of transforming a narrative or various documents into a dialogued continuity. The word is used in two areas: the adaptation of a novel or a news item and that of a scene-to-scene. 221, 245, 289
Announcement (advertising, foreshadowing): preparation consisting of making information predictable, and pleasantly perceived as such, by the viewer. Necessarily induces a scene to come (cf. also Hook and Telephone). 16, 125, 129, 132, 159, 187, 211, 310, 311, 315, 317, 320
Antagonist: character possessing a single objective, whose action opposes that of the protagonist and constitutes, for the latter, the only source of external obstacle. The antagonist can operate at a local level.

Quite often, a scene contains an antagonist. The antagonist is not necessarily a villain. 62, 146, 170, 171, 185, 193, 213, 296, 302
Arena: universe (place + time) meaningful for the action. 15, 53, 54, 65, 67, 99, 166-169, 196, 198, 201, 246, 247, 250, 280, 302
Aristotelian: relating to Aristotle's theories on dramaturgy as stated in Poetics [02]. By extension, relating to any dramaturgy based on emotion, emotional identification, conflict, unity of action, causality, organic narrative. For the author of this book, life itself is Aristotelian. Cf. Epic. 17, 45
Dramatic need: unresolved issue of a character, often the future protagonist, presented in the First act and addressed in the second. Often linked to the notion of trajectory objective. 80, 91, 135, 157
Bible: set of documents presenting the recurring elements (premises, characters, weapons, themes, situations, etc.) of a series. If, for example, in each episode, a particular character must die (cf. Kenny in South Park), this must be mentioned in the bible. Same if, in each episode, the inspector (Columbo) must reappear at the end of a scene and say: "Ah, one more thing!...". The bible often includes one or two dialogued episodes and the pitch of already written episodes. 294
Snowball (effect): combination of a crescendo and an evolution. 235
Thought bubble: comic strip bubble whose tail consists of several decreasing circles. Conventionally, it conveys a character's thoughts. 43
Characterization: art of creating characters. The work of characterization does not consist solely in designing the characters but also in staging their personality and then exploiting it. 15, 27, 34, 65, 74, 75-78, 122, 133-142, 156-162, 164, 165, 167, 188, 190, 196, 198, 201, 205-211, 238, 247, 264, 272, 288, 305, 307, 311, 312, 314, 316, 319, 332, 340
Catharsis: purgation of emotions produced, according to Aristotle, by listening to melodies or emotional identification with a tragedy protagonist. 123, 203
Chorality: nature of a narrative that focuses on several protagonists (each with their objective) and intertwines the plots. 62, 68-69, 128, 158, 296
Punchline ending: dramatic knot placed at the end of a work and can designate, depending on the case, a climax, a hook, or a dramatic turn (or all three at once). More than a simple wink, the punchline is often significant. 281, 293
Cliffhanger: anglicism (from "cliff" and "hang") designating a hook or a work (serial type) containing hooks. Cf. this word.
Climax: anglicism designating the dramatic knot that concludes the second act of a dramatic unit (scene, sequence, episode, or entire work), resolves the problems posed by the action, and provides the dramatic response. In general, it is also the most intense dramatic knot of the unit, that is to say, the most conflicting and/or the most spectacular. Cf. also Punchline and Scene to come. 15, 19, 34, 43, 77, 89, 107, 109, 115, 143, 148, 166, 172-174, 185, 189, 194, 197, 199, 202, 222, 223, 246, 281, 288, 308, 314, 321, 341
Median climax (mid-act climax, first culmination): strong dramatic knot, located approximately in the middle of the second act and giving the action a new coloration without concluding it. Cf. also Falcon. 185-186, 194, 197, 200, 202, 221, 222, 236, 280

1. 1. In 2011, the Official Journal of the French Republic proposed translating "cliffhanger" as "suspense." I prefer "hook," which says the same thing and is less likely to be confused with suspense.

Trajectory climax: moment when the trajectory protagonist transforms. 122, 130, 192-193, 194, 197, 200, 203, 221
Comedy: mocking treatment of human limitations, intended to create laughter in the viewer and combining detachment and shift. The mockery can be demeaning, demanding, or compassionate. 35, 56, 57, 88, 102, 112, 165, 175, 191-192, 203, 206, 237, 238-240, 241, 254, 285, 310, 325-326, 344, 345
Situation comedy: comedy whose comic nature is created by structural work. 238, 241
Conflict: difficult life circumstance, always associated (in humans) with a feeling of frustration and often associated with a feeling of anxiety. This circumstance results from an opposition between an objective and an obstacle. It generates emotion for the one who lives it (the protagonist) as for the viewer. Powerful emotional identification factor. 8, 13, 14-15, 17, 27, 109, 123, 124, 127, 148, 152, 165, 205, 232, 245, 246, 249, 267, 270, 281, 291, 297, 301-303, 313, 340
Dynamic conflict: conflict experienced actively or reactively. 14, 67, 133, 152, 172, 249, 290, 298, 302
Internal conflict: antagonism inherent to a character, which constitutes an element of their initial characterization and is often exploited during the action. 139, 148
Static conflict: conflict experienced passively. 15, 68, 249
Trajectory conflict: conflict that pushes a character to evolve psychologically. 78, 194, 197, 200, 203, 278
Dialogued continuity (script, film script, playscript): the dramatic work itself. It includes, in full, everything that will be seen and heard meaningfully on stage, on screen, or on the pages of a comic strip. Synonymous with theater text, libretto, or screenplay. 51, 191, 233, 245, 263-288, 333
Non-dialogued continuity: cf. Treatment (2).
Reasonable contradictor: character who disapproves of the protagonist (dramatic or major) and with whom the viewer agrees, thus creating an intellectual identification. 88, 89, 164-165, 185, 192, 203, 280, 313
Contrast: juxtaposition (spatial or temporal) of two dramatic elements or two feelings, one highlighting the other. Contrast is often created by preparation. In characterization, contrast can occur between what is shown of the character and the human norm. 134, 156, 167, 178, 207, 236, 237, 277
Dramatic turn (twist): particularly surprising dramatic knot for the viewer. Cf. also Surprise. 186-187, 191, 197, 202, 221, 224, 255, 288
Third act dramatic turn: dramatic knot that relaunches the action at the beginning of the third act. One of the constitutive elements of the enriched structure. 177-178, 202, 223
Crescendo: construction principle consisting of placing increasingly intense obstacles and conflicts on the protagonist's path. 15, 126, 235, 271, 281, 341
Objective declaration: cf. First-second act passage. 73, 221
Deconstruction: process of telling a story by breaking the codes of classical narration, often by leading scenes out of order. 45-46, 241-242, 307, 323
Deus ex machina: Latinism (in French, "god from the machine") designating any help or solution that appears out of nowhere (in fact, from the author's hat) and facilitates the protagonist's life. By extension, any facility. 86, 128, 159, 173, 184, 250-255, 292
Second Act: everything that happens while the protagonist tries to achieve their goal. 

Diabolus ex machina: a neologism derived from deus ex machina, referring to any gratuitous and unjustified obstacle, excluding the inciting incident and the MacGuffin.

Diagnosis: analysis of the reasons why a script-story works or does not work from a narrative perspective.

Dialogue: words exchanged by characters in a dramatic work. By extension, dialogue includes monologue, commentary, aside, voice-over.

Dialogue-payment (payoff line): seemingly banal dialogue that only takes on full meaning in its context.

Stage direction: scenic indication in a play text, equivalent to descriptions in a screenplay. By extension, sometimes called "stage direction" is anything not said by the characters in a dramatic work, including their names at the head of dialogues.

Cornelian dilemma: a conflicting situation where a character (often the protagonist) must choose between two paths for which they are responsible, and whose resolution will necessarily create conflict. Example of an external obstacle of internal origin.

Documentary: film constructed from non-fictional documents.

Drama: "action" in Greek. By extension, visual and/or auditory representation of human action. The mediums of drama are theater, opera, cinema, television, internet, video games, virtual reality, and, to a lesser extent, radio and comics. In a broad sense, drama is human action involving a goal, obstacles, and an uncertain outcome. In this sense, drama is the main driver of life.

Dramatize, dramatization: operation consisting of putting conflict, stakes, and suspense into a situation or putting stakes into the presentation of a narrative element.

Dramaturgy: technique of drama. Also synonymous with drama. Dramaturgy is written to be seen and heard, unlike literature.

Clarification: resolution of the intellectual curiosity created by a mystery. A clarification is very rarely a surprise.

Snowball effect: see Snowball.

Ellipsis: syntactic omission. We distinguish between temporal ellipsis, which means time has passed, and narrative ellipsis, which allows the viewer to imagine new narrative information.

Stakes: what a character (often the protagonist) can gain or lose depending on whether they achieve their goal. Stakes are a source of motivation for characters and can be psychological and/or unconscious. The intensity of stakes determines the strength of emotional identification and the viewer's interest in following the protagonist's story.

Arc stakes: what a character can gain or lose if they change psychologically. Generally, it is an aspect of their humanity.

Epic (theater): treatment conceived by Bertolt Brecht and Erwin Piscator, in reference to Greek epic poetry, opposing classical theater (called Aristotelian) and striving to prioritize intellect over emotion, distancing over emotional identification, artificial over truthful, juxtaposition of independent elements over the development of an organic plot. Epic theater has sparked many reactions, influenced 20th-century theater, and spawned a cohort of playwrights and theorists more royalist than the king.

Episode: one of the works composing a series.

Progression: construction principle consisting of creating a logical (organic) sequence between the successive parts of the work.

Exploitation: process of taking advantage of an element of the work, whether it is an arena, a goal, a ghost, a characterization, premises, or a situation of dramatic irony. The last phase of exploitation is resolution. Joins the notion of creativity.

Exposition: narration (oral, most of the time) of a past, present, or future event. The most literary part of dramaturgy.

Ghost: past trauma haunting a character.

Red herring: preparation intended to mislead the viewer, generally to bring about a surprise, sometimes to create contrast. A successful red herring is undetectable.

False adversary: character who initially opposes the protagonist before helping them.

False ally: character who initially helps the protagonist before opposing them.

Flashback: anglicism referring to a backward look in the narrative. This backward look is shown and not told orally.

Flashforward: anglicism referring to a leap into the future of the narrative. Like the flashback, it is shown and not told orally.

Foundations: synthetic plan of a work, consisting of all the major directions of the narrative and including the pitch, the arena, the nature of the protagonist, their goal and obstacles, the dramatic response, the three-act structure, important characters, main dramatic ironies, and main dramatic knots.

Fractal (structure): principle according to which all parts (scene, sequence, act, episode, etc.), regardless of their scale, contain the same basic pattern.

Genre: category of works. Categorical criteria can be of several natures.

Happy ending: anglicism meaning "happy ending." This is not systematically equivalent to a positive dramatic response. In Hamlet, Oedipus Rex, and Thelma & Louise, the protagonists achieve their goal, but can we speak of a happy ending? Conversely, the protagonists of Casablanca and Some Like It Hot do not achieve it, but the ending is happy.

High concept: see Strong premise.

Story: chronological succession of facts relating to a given subject, as opposed to the narrative (or plot) which is the arrangement of the facts of a story in a chosen and specific order. The same story can therefore have several different narratives. The most famous example is the Oedipus myth, of which Oedipus Rex constructs one of the many possible narratives. The word "fable" is sometimes used to refer to either the story or the narrative.

Emotional identification: process by which the viewer feels empathy for a character, merges with them through emotion, feels what they feel.

Intellectual identification: process by which the viewer feels sympathy for a character, merges with them through thought, understands what they feel. This intellectual sympathy is sometimes called "cognitive empathy."

Inciting incident: dramatic event located in the first act that, directly or indirectly, unbalances the life of one or more characters—the future protagonist(s)—prompting them to define a goal for the duration of the dramatic unit (scene, sequence, episode, or work). Sometimes called "disruptive element."

Installation (of dramatic irony): moment when the viewer learns information that at least one character in the narrative does not know. This moment is often an important dramatic knot. Sometimes called "revelation" (for the viewer).

Plot (narrative): unfolding of events related to a protagonist-goal duo. It merges with the narrative when the unity of action is respected. Sometimes synonymous with structure as in the expression "plot narrative."

Dramatic irony: process of giving the viewer information that at least one character is unaware of (consciously), thus giving the viewer an advantage over one or more characters. Dramatic irony includes three phases: installation, exploitation, resolution. Dramatic irony is formulated as follows: "We know this-that, the character does not."

Diffuse dramatic irony: dramatic irony in which the viewer feels—rather than knows—something that certain characters do not feel. Diffuse dramatic irony comes from the distance that the viewer has over the characters and situations of a work of fiction. Diffuse dramatic irony is formulated as follows: "We feel this-that, this character does not feel it."

Milestone: important dramatic knot of the second act that generally begins or concludes a sequence.

Literature: everything written to be read (aloud or silently) and not seen and/or heard. 

Villain: character doing evil and most often constituting a source of obstacles for the protagonist.

Melodrama: dramatic genre characterized by the accumulation of external obstacles, particularly the injustices of life and unfortunate coincidences. Passivity in the face of this type of obstacle reinforces the sense of melodrama.

Milking: anglicism ("milking" in French) referring to the process of exploiting an element (setting, character, situation, etc.) to the fullest, to get the most juice out of it, to make the most of it, to whip the eggs into snow. Synonymous with optimal exploitation and form of creativity.

Moment of choice: dramatic knot during which one of the characters, often the protagonist, is pushed to make a crucial decision for themselves or for the action. Sometimes coincides with the climax, the arc climax, the first-act-second-act transition, or the point of no return.

Montage-sequence: series of images and/or very short scenes intended to summarize a logical action.

Motivation: what drives a character and justifies their actions. It can be an emotion, a stake, or a goal. It can be conscious or unconscious (according to the Russian doll model). Motivation is an important notion linked to the work of characterization but also to the viewer's participation.

Means: approach used by the protagonist (local or general) to achieve their goal. Difficult to reach, it becomes a sub-goal. Poorly chosen, it is part of internal obstacles.

Mystery: process of making the viewer understand that they are missing one or more pieces of information. The formulation could be: "What are they doing? We don't know" or "What are they talking about? We don't know" or "What does this image correspond to? We don't know." Mystery creates intellectual curiosity. The resolution of a mystery is a clarification, very rarely surprising.

Dramatic knot (plot point): information or event that alters the protagonist's course and advances the action. Sometimes referred to as a beat when the dramatic knot is small. In fact, a dramatic knot can take the form of a single word, a blink of an eye, an intonation—if they are significant—up to a global nuclear catastrophe.

Intent note: document of a few pages that can accompany a screenplay and is supposed to explain the author's intentions (and sometimes the director's). The author generally explains what they wanted to say and how they see the finished film.

Objective: goal, desire, will that a character seeks to achieve or satisfy, consciously or unconsciously. The objective of a work (or general objective) is the protagonist's will that encounters and generates the most obstacles. Also called "dramatic objective."

Ironic objective: objective attributed by the receiver to the victim of dramatic irony, which consists of discovering what the victim does not know, as soon as possible—if the victim is also the protagonist of the narrative—or as late as possible—if the victim is another character than the protagonist.

Arc objective: very unconscious objective of a character (often the protagonist but not always) corresponding to their psychological trajectory, thus to their possible transformation in the work. A bit like a desire for well-being or as the specific declination of a common objective to all human beings: to grow. Linked to the notion of dramatic need.

Obstacle: element (object, character, chance, feeling, etc.) that opposes an objective, producing conflict.

External obstacle: obstacle having nothing to do with the character it opposes. Synonymous with "obstacle directly or indirectly independent of the individual."

External obstacle of internal origin: external obstacle to the protagonist for which they are nevertheless responsible, directly or indirectly.

Internal obstacle: characteristic of a protagonist (most often psychological) constituting a source of obstacles for themselves. Synonymous with "obstacle inherent to the individual."

Main opponent: character embodying the main source of external obstacles for the protagonist without necessarily being an antagonist.

Organic: which belongs to an organized whole whose components are linked to each other.

Payment (payoff): moment when an element (object, dialogue, gesture, effect, situation, etc.) takes on a particular meaning due to preparation.

First-act-second-act transition: particular dramatic knot felt as a trigger by the viewer when the action is launched. Coincides with the declaration of objective when there is one.

Character: fictional human being. By extension, any entity (animal, object, etc.) possessing fictitiously an objective, conscious or unconscious.

Main character: character corresponding to the subject or/and the title of a dramatic work. Does not always coincide with the protagonist.

Pitch: anglicism meaning "launch" and referring to the succinct sales argument of a film, launched by an author to a decision-maker or by a distributor to the public. We distinguish the one-line pitch (or logline) which summarizes the action in one sentence from the tagline pitch which resembles more a poster slogan and gives an idea of the style or atmosphere. The one-line pitch proves to be an excellent writing tool as it serves as a guideline for the author. Also called the "dramatic pitch."

Dramatic pitch (one-line pitch, logline, dramatic pitch): summary of a dramatic work in one or two lines. It is constructed from the basic elements of the narrative: arena, inciting incident, protagonist, characterization, objective, stakes, obstacles, suspense.

Arc pitch: one-sentence summary of the internal trajectory of the arc protagonist.

Point of no return: moment in the work (generally located in the first half) from which the viewer feels that the protagonist will never be the same again or cannot go back without consequences. Can be treated as an important dramatic knot.

Portrait: narrative that explores a particular character trait (jealousy in Othello) or emphasizes a character with all their facets (Don Juan).

Strong premise: enticing narrative starting point or subject with an enticing pitch. Often conceived with the formula "What if...?"

Premises: starting point of a narrative, often including the arena, characterization of important characters, and the inciting incident.

First act: everything that happens before the protagonist's goal is perceived by the viewer.

Preparation: operation consisting of making an information or event plausible, predictable, or significant. Also, the first part of this operation (setup, planting), the second being the payment (payoff). Preparation is closely linked to dramaturgical syntax.

Prescription: idea or recommendation intended to improve the narrative quality of a fiction case.

Protagonist: character in a dramatic work who experiences the most dynamic conflict throughout the work.

Ironic protagonist: victim of dramatic irony whose ignorance leads us to attribute an objective to them: to know what they do not know and/or to escape the conflicts generated by their ignorance.

Local protagonist: character of a scene who experiences the most dynamic conflict.

Arc protagonist: character in a dramatic work who has an arc objective. This can be the protagonist or another character. There may also be none in the work. Often, they change psychologically during the action. But not systematically. They may not achieve their arc objective.

Dramatic question: will the protagonist achieve their goal? This question is created in the viewer's mind at the end of the first act. It generates suspense (in the broad sense of the term).

Emotional question: question that creates a mix of hope and fear in the receiver's mind when asked. Different from an intellectual question posed by a mystery. Emotional questions can last throughout the action, and their effects can overlap.

Ironic question: will the victim of dramatic irony discover, when and how, what they do not know? This question is created in the viewer's mind when dramatic irony is installed. It can reinforce, replace, or supplant the dramatic question.

Arc question: will the arc protagonist change, and if so, in what way?

Narrative: see Story.

Rule: prescription indicating what must be done in a determined case. It can be known consciously or unconsciously. Like all languages, dramaturgy relies on rules, whether in the sense of "constants" or "guides." The word encompasses a variety of notions and various degrees of constraint.

Remake: anglicism referring to the new version of an already existing work. The remake is not a practice unique to Hollywood cinema. It has existed since Antiquity.

Dramatic response: answer to the dramatic question. It can be positive, negative, or not be (an extremely rare case, except in so-called "auteur" cinema).

Arc response: answer to the arc question. It can be positive—the arc protagonist grows—null—the arc protagonist does not change—or even negative—the arc protagonist declines.

Resolution: moment when a narrative mechanism finds its culmination after being exploited. The resolution of an action is its climax. The resolution of suspense is the dramatic response. The resolution of a mystery is a clarification. The resolution of a red herring is a surprise. The resolution of dramatic irony is an obligatory scene. The resolution of a characterization can be the arc climax and its arc response.

Feeling: what we naturally feel, what manifests in us upon receiving a work of fiction. Often the sum of an objective part and a subjective part. Corresponds to the symptom in the context of script doctoring.
Objective feeling: part of a receiver's feeling that relies on the universal or timeless mechanisms of storytelling and is therefore often shared by many other receivers.

Subjective feeling: part of a receiver's feeling that relies on personal tastes.

Life routine: an element of character development (often for the future protagonist) depicting, as the name suggests, their social or psychological life habits. The life routine sometimes reveals a dramatic need.

Screenplay (film script): the plan or narrative of a film (or a comic strip). Also called continuity dialogue. Unfortunately for screenwriters concerned with storytelling, the word refers to two different entities. The plan sometimes contains a narrative but not always. Some films have had a scenario-plan as a working tool but never a scenario-narrative because they tell nothing (or very little). It also happens that the scenario-narrative is developed in the editing room as much as on paper before shooting. This is often the case in documentaries. Another important nuance: the scenario-plan is ephemeral; it no longer exists once the film is shot. One can say, as is often heard, that it is a transitional work. In contrast, the scenario-narrative always exists in the film, like a beating heart. Its quality often determines that of the film. Therefore, while one can say that the scenario-narrative is not a work in itself (like a novel), it is nevertheless a complete work (like the music of a film). In a broad sense, the word "scenario" refers to the unfolding of a narrative or even a real event. In this sense, a theater text is the scenario of the play. Similarly, one might speak, in everyday life, of the scenario of a military action, a rugby match, or an ecological disaster.

Scene (or dramatic scene): part of a dramatic work presenting a local action. A dramatic scene can take place in several different settings, thus including several logistical scenes.

Scene to be done: any scene (or moment) expected by the spectator, which provides a certain gratification. The scene to be done is the payoff of an announcement. The climax and the obligatory scene are the most "to be done" of all scenes.

Scene-by-scene (step outline): a plan of a narrative, consisting of a succession of scenes summarized in a few lines. Also called "sequence of scenes" or "sequencer."

Aftermath scene: a moment of calm during which both characters and spectators have the opportunity to digest a scene of intense conflict. The aftermath scene is a form of payoff that contributes to the rhythm and relief of the work.

Logistical scene: a short part of a dramatic work defined by changes in setting, time, or characters. In a screenplay, logistical scenes are often numbered and designated as follows: INT. BATHROOM - NIGHT. The distinction between logistical scene and dramatic scene is similar to that between logistical act and dramatic act. Sometimes called "sequence" in French cinema.

Obligatory scene: resolution of dramatic irony, i.e., the moment when the victim of dramatic irony is finally made aware of what they were ignorant of. The obligatory scene is a particular form of scene to be done, the most anticipated of all. It is also an important dramatic knot, often a milestone. Sometimes called "revelation" (for the ex-victim, not for the spectator).

Script doctor: an anglicism meaning "screenplay fixer," referring to a professional called upon to uncover and possibly correct the flaws of a finished screenplay. The principle also exists for theater (long before 1895). In a way, anyone who finds a problem in a screenplay (or a screenplay problem in a film) and suggests a change is doing script doctoring. This can even be a character like Cheech (Chazz Palminteri) in "Bullets Over Broadway." In a restrictive sense, a script doctor is someone who has the skills to do script doctoring and is paid to do so. When the script doctor rewrites, they become a co-author. Ben Hecht, William Goldman, and Claude Sautet are three famous script doctors in cinema circles. In the United States, a distinction is made between the "script doctor" who rewrites the screenplay and the "script analyst" (or "script expert" or "script consultant") who merely provides consultation.

Sequence: a group of scenes united by the same sub-objective and often framed between two milestones. In French cinema, what is sometimes called a "sequence" is nothing more than a logistical scene. My conception of the sequence is that of the Anglo-Saxons.

Sequencer: see Scene-by-scene.

Series: a set of works possessing common elements. These are most often characters (Oedipal, Falstaff, James Bond, Columbo, Tintin, etc.), but it can also be a theme.

Sitcom: an anglicism (contraction of "situation comedy") referring to a comedic TV show with recurring sets and characters and a self-contained episode (usually 25 minutes). The word "sitcom" sometimes has a pejorative connotation. It is unjustified. As with everything, there are mediocre sitcoms and excellent sitcoms. And an excellent sitcom does more than retain the audience. Like any quality comedy, it is a work of art that physically does good, speaks of life, and conveys meaning.

Sketch: a group of scenes telling a story, often included in a collection. Closely associated with certain short films or short plays. Equivalent to a sequence in a road movie.

Soap: an anglicism referring to a melodramatic serial. The expression is an abbreviation of "soap opera," in reference to the soap brands that sponsored these serials in the USA. Also called "soap opera" in France or Canada and "telenovela" in Portugal and Latin America.

Subplot: a secondary action, possessing like any action its trio protagonist-objective-obstacles, the protagonist being, most of the time, different from that of the main action. The subplot is supposed to generate, more or less directly, obstacles for the main action.

Thematic subplot: a set of moments linked to the same theme or secondary subject treated three times or more.

Sub-objective: a semi-local objective used by the protagonist to achieve their general objective. Can correspond to the objective of a sequence.

Spectacular: anything that distracts or fascinates by its rarity. Etymologically, the spectacular imposes itself on the eye. But a sound or an idea can be spectacular. Synonymous with "sensational." Often complicit in voyeurism and the fantasy of omnipotence.

Structure: the arrangement of the different parts of a dramatic work. The notion often refers to the three acts and the main dramatic knots (climax, inciting incident, etc.). But the deepest and most powerful structuring work is a work of preparation and organic construction of the narrative.

Fractal structure: see Fractal.

Enriched structure: a variant of the classic three-act structure that consists of introducing a twist at the beginning of the third act to revive the action. The third act then proposes a second climax and a second dramatic response and has its own third act.

Three-act structure: logical division of any action, real or fictional, of a living organism: before the action-during the action-after the action. This definition does not correspond to that of Syd Field and many screenplay theorists.

Sequence of scenes: see Scene-by-scene.

Subject: specific exploitation of an idea or theme. Note: In everyday language, the word "subject" is often synonymous with "theme." But in narratology, the subject is quite close to the dramatic pitch. If you say you have a play subject and someone asks what it's about, you won't answer: "It's about barbarism" or "It deals with Dutch gastronomy." You will rather say: "It's the story of blah blah blah..." You won't be far from the pitch. The difference between theme and subject is the same as for philosophy exams. The themes are justice, art, truth, etc. The subjects exploit the themes: "Are the demands for justice and freedom separable?", "Does art transform our consciousness of reality?", "Is discussion the path to truth?" etc. A subject can therefore address several themes.

Surprise: a process that consists of giving the spectator information they did not expect. Called "coup de théâtre" when the surprise is significant. The surprise is always the payoff of a false lead.

Suspense: anguished tension, generating anxiety, experienced by the spectator and created by the uncertainty of the dramatic response. Also called "tension." There is suspense not only in thrillers and "suspense" films but in any work that poses an emotional question and exploits it.

Symptom: what one naturally feels, what manifests in us when reading a text of fiction or receiving a work of fiction. Often the sum of an objective feeling and a subjective feeling.

Synopsis: a summary of a dramatic work already written, respecting the order of the narrative and spanning between two and five pages. A practical decision-making tool but a very debatable writing tool.

Telegraphing: involuntary or clumsy preparation consisting of making information predictable and unpleasantly perceived as such by the spectator.

Emotional beat: a dramatic knot that changes a character's emotion.

Theme: idea or question addressed by a narrative. The themes of Jurassic Park are genetic manipulation, dinosaurs, omnipotence, amusement parks. A work can therefore contain several themes. Conversely, the same theme can give rise to thirty-six different subjects. The theme of The Monsters is mental monstrosity. This gives rise to twenty sketches, thus twenty subjects.

Topper: an anglicism referring to a dramatic element (often short and funny) that exploits a scene or action by crowning it. Sort of like a cherry on top.

Total: a scene that includes almost all mechanisms: protagonist-objective-obstacles, the three acts, a climax, conflict, emotion, suspense, a crescendo, evolution, comedy elements, beautiful characterization, a diurnal unit, payoffs, setting, dramatic irony, surprise, at least one beautiful piece of activity, and good dialogues.

Treatment (1): the way of handling a subject. The treatment can be serious or comic, naturalistic or fantastic, realistic or artificial (poetic, musical, symbolic, etc.), Aristotelian or epic, notions that are not incompatible.

Treatment (2) (treatment): a plan of a dramatic work, consisting of a series of detailed but non-dialogued scenes. Synonymous with non-dialogued continuity.

Internal trajectory: cleansing or transformation (moral or psychological) of one of the characters (the protagonist of the trajectory) during the narrative. This may concern the thematic protagonist but not always.

Suspension of disbelief: the attitude of a fiction spectator who leaves their Cartesian mind at the door and accepts the conventions and artifices of the work of art. The suspension of disbelief has operated in theater since time immemorial - if only with the famous fourth wall - obviously in the case of science fiction works or black-and-white films, but also more subtly in all realistic works. It is often associated with the question of narrative plausibility where it is delicate to exploit.

Triad A-A-N: a group of three elements in which the first element is repeated identically once, creating an impression of habit, before being altered at its third appearance.

Meshing: organic arrangement of the narrative in which the scenes give the impression of being interwoven. A form of milking at the structural level.

Three acts (structure in): see Structure in three acts.

Third act: everything that happens after the protagonist has achieved or abandoned their objective.

Unity: the character of what is unique. Applies to different notions: tone, style, genre, theme, treatment, characterization (character coherence), etc., and especially action, place, and time.

Unity of action: the principle according to which all parts of the work (including possible subplots) must serve a single action. Unity of action is linked to the uniqueness of the general objective and the human need for order.

Unity of place: the principle according to which the action must take place in a single location.

Unity of time: the principle according to which the action must take place within a limited time frame (from a few hours to a day).

Urgency (urgency, time lock): feeling created by the association of suspense and lack of time.

Victim (of conflict): thematic protagonist and spectator. The conflict can be serious or comic.

Victim (of dramatic irony): character ignorant of information known to the spectator.

Leftover: a narrative element that had meaning or utility in a previous version of the text but no longer does. Pure products of indispensable rewriting, leftovers handicap the work and are sometimes difficult to spot.

Voice-over: dialogue spoken by a character who does not appear on stage or on screen at the time they speak. It can be a general commentary or off-screen dialogue.
